Page 5

Mickey Andrews

The FSU Defensive Package

Florida State University

Thank you. A lot of city people have a little trouble understanding how I talk, but I can't help it. I hope all of you in here tonight are country boys. It is really good to be here. I've always enjoyed being part of a clinic. It seems strange for me to be up here talking. I feel I should be out where you are. There is so much football for me to learn. I hope I never get to the point where I think I know it all. If I do, I'll get out.

I can remember years ago when I was coaching in Alabama, every time we could get any information on a clinic, away we went. One of the things that makes coaching a great fraternity is that we are not afraid to share our ideas with each other, including the people we have to line up and play against. It is an honor for me to be here and be a part of the Nike Clinic. I know that the job Earl has done through the years has just been outstanding.

I started my college coaching career here in Kentucky. I worked for Roy Kidd at Eastern Kentucky University for a year in 1966. I coached for a year and a half in high school before Coach Bryant got me that job with Coach Kidd. I'm fortunate to have coached for that long in college. I learned a lot of football from Roy. I think he is one of the great coaches and great men of our profession. The first year I was coaching for Roy about killed me. My wife and I had just had a son. She thought I was supposed to help with rearing that little boy as she did. It didn't work that way. I was coaching offensive backs. We spent some long hours working.

I can remember the first time I went into the mountains to recruit. It was about Christmas time, and I had never been in that part of the country. There are not many mountains down in south Alabama. I was visiting a prospect. When I drove up to the house, there was a little nativity scene out in the yard. I really didn't understand everything that was there. It was a homemade deal; I guess they had made it themselves. When I got inside, I asked the boy what the three figures were outside. He told me they were the three wise men. The men were all dressed in red. I asked him why they were dressed in red. He said they were firemen. He told me that in the Bible it said, "They came from 
afar." 
I knew exactly what he was talking about, because that is the way we talk in south Alabama.

In the last 10 years I have been very fortunate to coach at Florida State. In the 90s we have been the most successful team in the country. We have lost 10 games and won 75. When something good happens, you need to find out why it did. If something bad happens, you better find out why. I think the biggest reason for our success is that we have had some great players. We have had some great recruiting years. We think we just had one this past year; only time will tell. The thing that impressed me was the way these players were willing to do the things to improve and become successful. We have had some great players come through FSU during those 10 years. We have also had some players with great ability who didn't become great players. They wouldn't get out of the comfort zone to push themselves to become great players.

We are in our off-season program now. We work at six o'clock in the morning two days a week. We have to get 10 days in before spring training. It is the 10 roughest days those guys will ever go through. It is that way by design. It is an old-time gut check for an hour. It is a time when we are trying to focus and put our guys into situations where they are going to fold or fight through it. That is the backbone, along with recruiting, that has led to our success. We work a tremendous amount on strength improvement, but at the same time we spend time working on speed and agility. We think movement is just as important as strength. We are trying to teach our kids to do things with great intensity and strict discipline. We want them to start, maintain, and finish their drills with great intensity. The thing we stress is 
never quit. 
The first thing Coach Bowden covers with the players who come to Florida State is this: "Don't ever quit."

 





Page 6

In the 14 years that I've been at Florida State, we've lost four National Championships because we didn't finish. We couldn't finish the fourth quarter. I would be surprised if there was another team in the country that outworked us this year. We had the same thing come up this year in our bowl game. We were playing for a National Championship. We looked at the results and evaluated our play. I don't think there was anything wrong with our effort. We got outskilled, out-big played, outcoached, and outfought a few times. Those are the things you want to make sure don't happen very often if you plan on being successful. We may not be in the hunt for the championship next fall. We have to replace eight starters on defense. We had a very good defense this year. Our kids did a whale of a job of fighting back and getting pride back in our defense. We will be good on defense next year, but I don't know how quick it will be. We open up with Southern Cal next year at their place. We better be pretty good when we get there.

We have a lot to accomplish in our off-season, spring, and summer practices. I really believe this. It doesn't have anything to do with X's and O's. There is a place for that kind of thinking, but you better not depend on winning games against people who are as good as you are with physical talent. It takes more than that. The thing we are trying to accomplish in spring training is to win with good people who will give great effort, play with great fundamentals, and finish the game. We try not to get to caught up with X's and O's. You win championships in the off-season. From when you put the pads away until they come out again is when you win championships. That is when you work on the mental phases of the game. That is when you work on some critical things that have nothing to do with blocking and tackling. I'm going to show you our defense. The success we had was not the X's and O's. It was our players' attitude, character, will to prepare, and will to do the things we ask them to do. It is not easy for the coach or player. If your players will pay in the off-season, that's what helps you win on Saturday. That is where the fun comes in and makes it all worthwhile. I was recruiting this year in a high school in Florida. I saw this on a coach's bulletin board. I thought it was neat and applied to what we were trying to accomplish in our off-season. It was called "The Law of Survival": "Every morning the gazelle awakens knowing he has to outrun the fastest lion to survive. Every morning the lion wakes knowing he has to run faster than the slowest gazelle or he will starve. It doesn't make any difference whether you are a lion or a gazelle. When the sun comes up, you better be running." That kind of tells the story of our off-season program.

I don't think you have to get up at six in the morning to be successful. That is not the magic we've had at Florida State. The reason we started working at six in the morning was because of the players' academic requirements. We could have worked at night, but we felt the morning was better. It makes the players get up. Also, we have a lot of kids that play other sports, and this time doesn't interfere with their practice time. I have one kid that will be a starter on the baseball team as a freshman. We have six kids that run on the track team. They go through our off-season program in the mornings and the track practice in the afternoon. The morning time gives our kids an opportunity to play more than one sport. The only time that these guys miss off-season workouts is when they have a game. Every defensive back I've had has played baseball or run track.

After we get through with the bowl game every year, we sit down as a coaching staff and list some things that we want to accomplish next year. We sit down with our players and sell them on these things. The one thing we want to sell to the player is to become a champion. If our player develops a championship attitude, we won't have to worry about him. He won't be getting into trouble.

We set down four things as goals. When I first went to Florida State, they had only one goal: That was to win the National Championship. In the first five years the closest we got was about 60th. We weren't even close. We decided to set some goals that we could reach. By doing that, we could reach other goals. We are in a state where the National Championship has been won 5 times out of the last 10 years. Miami, Florida, and Florida State have played in many other National Championship games and have not won.

 





Page 7

Our first goal is to 
win our state championship. 
We are the only team that plays both the other teams. Miami and Florida don't play each other. If we can beat both of those teams, we have an opportunity to go for the National Championship most of the time. We were fortunate to beat both teams in the regular season this year. We have been in the ACC for five years. Our second goal is to 
win the ACC Championship. 
That is important because the winner of the ACC Championship automatically gets a New Year's Day Bowl Game. How you are ranked at the end of the year determines what type of bowl game you get. We have lost one conference game in five years. Our third goal s to 
win a New Year's Day Bowl. 
If you can play in and win a New Year's Day Bowl Game, you will end up in the top four or five in the country. We have finished in the top four for 10 years in a row.

Not every player we get is a good football player. But they have been winning for us. I had Deion Sanders there. He was not a great player. I am not saying that to get any credit for his success. Deion earned what he got. He is not the most-talented guy around; he is the hardest-working guy around. That is saying a lot, because we have had some great players over the years. Hard work is the key to success. I remember that back when I was at Alabama, I was not a great player. was one of the guys who was average that Coach Bryant took and made think he was a great player. Coach Bryant had that knack. I thought I was great, and because of that I played better than I should have. That is one of the things we try to do at FSU. If you can reach the guy who is an average player and make him raise his play to another level, you can be successful.

One of the best high school teams I've seen in the state of Georgia did not have a single guy taken off that team. Valdosta has been one of the most successful programs in the country. They must have won 20 State Championships. I was recruiting and asked the coach how he kept coming up with good teams. He told me hat he coached next year's team this year. I went back and thought a lot about that. That is the thing we do on defense. We play ever single guy we can get ready o play in a game. We try not to play a guy who is not eady. But if a guy can contribute, we try to get him in the game. If we can get him in the game, he is going to prepare better. If he prepares better, we are going to perform better. If he can make a play, he will push the guy playing ahead of him.

Our last goal is to 
win a National Championship. 
We have done that one time in 13 years. Coach Bowden has had two undefeated seasons and has lost in the bowl game for the National Championship. You are not going to win every time you get there. But if your guys keep fighting and getting in that situation, sometime it will fall your way. It has happened to us one time. The way our kids have worked in the off-season program, we will be back. It may not be next year, but we will be back. We'll play for it again, and if we don't screw them up, we'll win it.

We tell our kids that if they want to win the National Championship, they have to be 
dependable. 
They have to do what the coaches tell them. If they are supposed to play in the A gap, they better play it. There are no excuses for them not doing it. This applies to the offseason also. We can't make our guys go to the weight room or come to the off-season program. We don't try to. All we do when they miss is give them the opportunity to make up for what they missed, because we think their mama would want them to do that, and we don't want to let mama down. We want them to be on time and do their job the very best they can on every play. Bobby Knight made the final statement on this when he said you have to do it time after time after time. That is called 
consistency. 
You can't be great without consistency. You have to be 
productive. 
It doesn't matter whether you are on the field, in the weight room, in class, or in study hall. Jimmy Johnson probably gets as much out of this as any coach in the business. If they don't produce for him, they are someone else's problem. We can't do that. We have to find another way to motivate these guys. That is when coaching becomes difficult and the biggest challenge.

I've got a kid who started at strong safety for me this past year. He was a walk-on who started playing a little last year but broke his arm in the fifth game. He couldn't do any upper-body work. He worked on his lower body in the off-season program. The doctors

 





Page 8

cleared him for spring practice the day before we started. I really wanted to see whether he was going to play as if he had a broken arm or whether he would play like a football player. He played as if he wanted to be a football player. He was the most productive man I had in the secondary this season. I called him the "Trash Man." He scored three or four times on interception, fumbles, and blocked kicks. He was always there to pick the ball up and get in the end zone. He had tremendous vision. I remember visiting with the Dolphins' line coach when he was giving a lecture at Clemson. I asked him what he thought was the most important thing about a defensive player. He said 
vision. 
A lot of people talk about a linebacker having a nose for the ball. He doesn't have a nose for the ballhe has great vision. I got to thinking about it. I had never been around a great defensive player who didn't have good vision. An intangible that a player may have can allow him to be productive if he is strong enough at being dependable. We tell our guys that there is no place for excuses. George Washington Carver made the statement that 99 percent of the failures come from people who have learned the habit of making excuses. That is a strong statement, but it is true. We don't want excuses. We want results.

Attitude is 
a thing that can make a difference. Attitude controls how far you go. It controls you performance, preparation, and success. You have to have 
discipline. 
The problem we have is that the kids we get don't have a whole lot of self-discipline. They have to lean on us as coaches. We have to discipline them until they can do it for themselves. That is a difficult thing for most people. The last quality we look for is 
persistence. 
If you are lucky enough to get those three qualities in a person, he is going to be hard to beat, regardless of his speed or size. If you have enough people with those qualities, you are going to be hard to be beat. We are going to try to continue to do things in the off-season to coach those things. Any day we don't scrimmage, we end up on the goal line. Every one of those things comes out on the goal line. We tell our guys, "People don't score on you unless you let them." Every time we scrimmage in situations, we go 3rd and 3 and 4th and 1. Every time someone gets in our end zone, our defense runs a gasser after practice. It is not punishment, because all of them can do that without any effort. It is a matter of 
pride. 
That is the goal-line attitude you are trying to develop.

We have some defensive goals. We want to be the best defense there is in the state, conference, and nation. We have our objectives posted in our locker room, and each player gets a copy of them. This year we ranked number one in rushing, passing, and scoring defense in the state of Florida. In the conference we ranked number one in rushing, but we were number two in the other categories. In the nation we were number one in rushing defense. We were number six in passing defense. We gave up 229 total yards and 11 points a game. This is important for us and our kids to see.

We spend every penny we can to get better as coaches. We travel to other college programs and pro camps to study defenses and schemes. But that doesn't mean we are going to be better coaches next year. Maybe it can prevent us from going out there and doing some things that could prevent our kids from being the best they can be. Every time we recruit a defensive back, I ask him how good he wants to be. If he tells me he wants to be great, I'll try my best to make sure he gets there. Deion told me after his sophomore year that he wanted to be an All American and the best defensive back in America. I told him, all right, I was going to start coaching him like that. I didn't want him to start bitching and moaning at me when I was down his throat and pushing him. If you are a star, you will not be coached gently. When you tell them that and they tell you to do whatever it takes, an agreement has been reached. If they mean what they tell you, they will take the toughest coaching you can give them.

Our base defense is a 4-3. Ours is a little different from most 4-3 defenses. We play our defensive end about a yard wider than most defensive ends. We put them in a three-point stance and cock them in toward the inside. We tell them to get into the backfield as quickly as they can. We had one kid that ran 4.7 in the 40 but played like he ran 4.5. The other kid ran 4.5 and played like a 4.5. They weren't quite as big as most defensive ends. One was very athletic and skilled. The other was a hard-nosed country boy. One of them was a doctor's son, and the other was the son of a blue-collar worker.

 





Page 9

But both of them bought into the scheme that we were going to turn them loose to go to the ball. Their eyes lit up. We've been doing this for a number of years. We tell our two inside people to penetrate. We want them to move the line of scrimmage. Their aiming point is to get a foot behind the line of scrimmage. We want all backs to have to cut laterally. I don't have time to get into our block reaction. Basically, we are trying to take four guys and form a new line of scrimmage. At one time we played our linebackers five yards deep to attack the line of scrimmage. We went for a visit to Nebraska. We changed a lot of our thinking about the 4-3. A lot the things we do we got from Nebraska. Also, we went to Washington and Arizona. We tried to take different things people were doing and incorporate them into what we are doing. Don't take people and try to bend them into what you want to do. One of the best things you can do as a coach is to take your people and adjust your scheme to your people. Offensive people have been doing that for years. If they have a great option quarterback, they run the option. If he can drop-back pass, they do that. Don't put people into situations where they cannot be successful. Take what you have, and put them into the best situation for them.

We now use an "up call" to get our linebackers up to the line of scrimmage. We had three middle linebackers who had to be our linebacking corps last year. We put Bush in the middle and moved the other two to the outside. They were more effective playing up to the line of scrimmage, so we put them up there. I think both of those outside linebackers will make it into pro ball: one because he has great ability, and one because he has a big heart. They'll make it because it means enough to them. The middle linebacker we had this year was one of the smartest guys I've ever been around. I heard someone say that he was so smart that it was like trying to sneak daylight by a rooster. It just doesn't happen. I can tell from that reaction that most of you guys in here were raised in town. Most of you haven't heard that rooster early in the morning.

We started off as a quarter-quarter-half team in the secondary. After the fourth football game in 1993, we changed to quarter-quarter across the board. That was a good coverage for the personnel we had. This past year our personnel did not allow us to play zone coverage as well as we played man coverage. Therefore we played a little more man coverage last year. The main thing we wanted to do was get seven men up on the line of scrimmage and make the offensive linemen account for linebackers. If a linebacker is back deep, the offensive linemen are not worried about him blitzing. If he is up on the feet of the defensive line, they get worried. We tell our linebackers that they can run through anytime they want as long as they can make the play. Most linebackers want to blitz every play. But if he runs through and doesn't make the play, he is going to get his butt chewed out. We can't stand him making bad decisions, but by being on the line of scrimmage, he confuses a lot of blocking schemes.

These are the alignments we used. Our defensive end plays a wide 9 technique and has a 7-gap responsibility. The defensive end to the split end lines up in a wide 5 technique and has a 5-gap responsibility. All four linemen key the ball. We want them to get up as close to the line as they can. We got a bunch of off-sides calls this year. We were the most penalized team in the ACC this year. I'm not bragging because Coach Bowden says penalties are coaching mistakes. You eliminate penalties with good coaching. He's right because he is the boss. I don't believe you can be aggressive on defense and not get penalties. We got a lot of criticism against Florida for hitting late, but we got penalized twice for it. I don't want my defensive ends wondering whether the quarterback still has the ball or not. I want them coming hard, not holding back. If we've got a guy pinned against the sideline and the whistle hasn't blown, hit him. That back should not be running out of bounds. We don't teach dirty ball. Bobby Bowden doesn't, and he would fire anybody who did. But we do teach hard-nosed ball. As long as that guy has our ball, we are going to try to get it. It's all in how you look at it.

The tackle is in a 3 technique to the tight end and has a 3-gap responsibility. The nose guard is in a 2 eye on the guard to the split end side. He has the 1-gap-weak responsibility. Our linebacker aligns tight to the line. The strong linebacker is in a 7 technique on the tight end and has the 7-gap responsibility. The middle

 





Page 10

linebacker is in a 1 technique to the strong side and has the 1-gap-strong responsibility. The weak linebacker is in a 4 technique to the split end side and has the 3-gap responsibility. On the snap of the ball, if the linebacker is not running through, he takes a step back. He does that to let the linemen clear, and he can take a better angle to his gap. When we first looked at it, I didn't think we could do it. I didn't think I could coach a linebacker to back up when the ball was snapped. But it worked.

In our quarter-quarter coverage we are going to read the inside guys. If we get a vertical pattern from the inside people, we are in quarter zones deep. If we get anything else, the safeties become involved with underneath coverage. The bad thing about playing this coverage is that your linebackers end up being curl to flat players and they can get bogged down with fakes. Any coverage you put up is like a front. You are playing that coverage to gain a strength. The reason we like the quarter-quarter scheme is that we can get an eight-man front from a seven-man alignment. We can use one of our defensive backs as a hidden linebacker. We align our corners five yards deep and put the number-one receiver inside to his side. The free safety and rover are eight yards deep, keying the two man to their side.

Let me show you our support to the strong side. If the tight end blocks down or releases, the 9-technique end tightens down and swaps gaps with the strong linebacker. His responsibility is the 5 gap instead of the 7 gap. The Sam linebacker ends up outside the 9-technique end in his gap. If the tight end blocks out, we maintain our gaps. The middle and weak linebackers flow and leave the cutback to the rover.

You may not think that looks right, but it works. Our first objective is to stop the run. Unless you can whip everybody at the line of scrimmage, the best way to stop the run is to get an extra man that the offense can't block. That is why I don't like to play the quarter-quarter-half scheme. If your rover is a half player, he can't help on the run. The linebackers have to slow down, and the whole defense is in a bind. Plus one guy split out blocks two people: your corner and rover. In quarter-quarter our corners basically end up in man coverage. The underneath coverage is a route read by the free safety and rover. On the isolation weak we attack with the weak-side linebacker. He wrong-shoulders the blocker, and the rover flies up and makes the hit on the line of scrimmage. He may not keep him from gaining a yard, but he can make the hit on the line.

There are some problems you can get into when you are playing zone defense. We declare to the tight end. If an offense bring two receivers to the same side, we bring our corner over. We don't take our linebackers out of the box if we can help it. We want to match a corner on a wide out. There is very little adjustment. If the wide out motions, the corner goes with him. If he aligns over, the corner aligns over with him. It gives us cover guys on receivers. We play a lot of situation

 





Page 11

defenses. We play a 1st and 10 as a rundown and run our defense to fit the situation. We keep our linebackers committed to the box as long as we can. We like to play the quarter-quarter coverage because it allows us flexibility to match up talent with talent and still stop the run.

Gentlemen, it has been a pleasure to be here. If you are going to be in Tallahassee, drop by and see us. Just call ahead to make sure we are there. Thank you again.

 





Page 12

John Blake

The Oklahoma 4-3 Front Defense

University Of Oklahoma

Thank you, gentlemen. I would like to thank you for inviting me here. I know that a lot of time when we go to clinics we talk X's and O's. Football is much more than that. In this game you are dealing with kids. The first thing I'm going to talk to you about is philosophy. I'm going to talk about my philosophy and what I feel it's going to take to bring the University of Oklahoma back.

The fact is that there is no discipline in our schools any more. I give credit for the course of my life to my mom and dad. They instilled values in me. They led me into my beliefs and religion. Jesus Christ is the foundation of my success. The only one I have to please is God. I think we should thank God for being here today and for our opportunities. I am a young coach, and I know what it takes. I think the most important things that got me where I am today are my respect for people around me, my parents, and Jesus Christ.

I've got a wife and a nine-month-old baby boy that I thank God for every day. A great deal of my guidance came from my high school coach. Every coach in here had that time when they left mom and dad's influence. That was a big time in my life, because I turned to my high school coach, and he turned me toward God. All of you sitting out there can do the same thing. I thank God for the high school coaches. I know you don't make a lot of money, but it takes a special person to help the kids of today. I know what you go through. I thank you for the effort you show toward the kids and the patience you have with them. It's tough.

I came back to Oklahoma for one reason. I liked it in the NFL and could have stayed there. But I wanted to coach the young men at Oklahoma. They were struggling and needed somebody. I told the president that 90 percent of my time would be spent with the kids, not the X's and O's of football. These kids were under all kinds of pressure from their peers to take the wrong path. There is so much in our society that can lead our kids down the wrong road. Drugs, alcohol, stealing, and all different social ills are out there to prey on these kids. I want to get into the kids' hearts and minds.

There is not much difference between a good player and a great one. If you take a good player and discipline him, get him to lift weights, and get him to do what you say and if he is coachable, he can become a great player. A great player may be able to make the plays, but if you can't control him, he'll hurt you in the long run.

You can develop outstanding players. I've seen it in the NFL. The Dallas Cowboys are a tremendously talented team. But all it takes is one or two plays to decide whether you win or lose a football game. We blew out some people, but so many other times if Troy had not made a great pass or someone didn't make a block, we would have gotten beat.

I know the commitment it takes to be a professional football player. It takes discipline to play 60 minutes of football. On this level the players are paid to do a job. That is not the case in college or high school. I understand that. That is why my one goal is to take care of my players. Half of the meetings I have with my team are not about football. I talk to them about drugs and alcohol. I talk about being out late at night. I talk about who they hang out with and peer pressure. Those are the things I talk about with my players.

I love being at Oklahoma. I played there. I know what it takes, and I left the NFL for that reason. I came back to lead and protect these kids, and I hope Oklahoma gets back on top. I need your help. I know how important you are. I want to recognize you and help you in any way I can. Getting to college coaching is not about who knows the most. It is about opportunity.

 





Page 13

Anyone in this room could coach on the college level if given the opportunity. However, your commitment to high school coaching is a blessing for these kids.

At Oklahoma I had to put together a staff and start to build a program from one that has been down. Oklahoma has great tradition. To build back that tradition, you have to start with staff development. When you put together your staff, you have to have a number of things present within the people you hire. They must be committed to the things you are. They must be loyal to you and the school. They have to be accountable for their work. The players are looking to the coaches for examples. How can we tell a kid not to smoke when we smoke? Those kids are looking at us and mimicking us. What do you want to be remembered for? Do you want to be remembered as someone who influenced the lives of their kids or someone who just won games? I want to be remembered for making a difference in these kids' lives.

Today in college football we have to emphasize academics. Academics will be the number-one priority at Oklahoma. We have an academic support system which sets up study halls, monitors class attendance, and grades. If a kid assigned to that study hall misses, he reports to the stadium at 5:30 in the morning. He has to run. We are going to focus on the direction these kids go.

I put together an outstanding staff with a lot of high morals and values. I got one of John Cooper's coordinators, Bill Young, who had been with him 18 years. He took the same job at Oklahoma. He saw the direction our program was going.

You must have loyalty within your staff. If you don't, it will turn your program inside out. Most programs and jobs are lost because someone from inside the program destroyed it. If you have a situation where one of your assistants has a friend who doesn't like the way you un your program, that is not disloyalty. But if that friend starts to talk about the program and his boss and the assistant won't defend the program, that is disloyalty.

I'm going to talk about the 4-3 defense in just a minute. We'll talk about the alignments, linebacker play, and coverages. That is the type of football you want to hear. Just remember guys, it is not what you run, but who is running it on the field. It is the talent and not the scheme that wins. It is developing the talent with the proper thinking that wins games. Discipline and commitment are tremendously important. Kids have to believe that you care about them and love them. We had a team this year that wasn't that good, but they never gave up. We played 60 minutes of football even when the game was out of reach. That was because our kids knew that we cared about them. These kids were loyal and would fight to get the job done for the coaches. They all know how to line up and play football. That is why you have to reach them to get them to play to the highest level they can. That goes for pros as well as college.

We played a 50 defense at Oklahoma for 12 years. When we went to the Cowboys, we went to the 4-3 defense. At Oklahoma when I was playing, we played the 4-3 in a nickel situation. But for the most part, teams in the Big Eight ran the football. They did not throw the ball all that much. But today the 4-3 defense allows you to balance across the front and secondary. The 4-3 defense is an attacking defense. We teach our guys to attack and react. We get as close to the ball as we can and come off the ball. In our alignment we want the inside hand down. The inside hand is the one closest to the ball. We want our inside leg back. The reason for that is to squeeze the play and not get turned out. That is the hardest technique to play. As the defensive lineman steps with his inside foot, he is creating power in his punch hand where he can squeeze the slip block. The guard is trying to butt up and slip to the backside linebacker. By using that type of charge, the defensive tackle can defeat the reach, squeeze, and slip and maintain control of the B gap.

In a 4-3 defense if you bring a defensive tackle into a 2 technique on the guard, make sure he has A gap responsibility. That way you keep your linebacker clean.

Let's talk about alignment. The end to the 5 technique side is inside ear hole to outside ear hole of the

 





Page 14

offensive tackle. That is generally the short side of the field. If he gets a weak set by the back, he widens to keep from getting cut off. The 3 technique is lined up ear hole to ear hole on the guard no matter what happens. He does not worry about getting reached. A 2 technique is outside ear hole to inside ear hole of the guard. The 4 technique is the same thing except on the tackle.

We play a 7, 6, and 9 technique on the tight end. On all of these techniques we are playing ear hole to ear hole. We do not ever want to be cut off and turned out. Players like Russell Maryland, Leon Lett, and Charles Haley never get reached. A player who gets reached is late coming off the ball. He is reading and not attacking. When he comes off the ball and attacks the blocker, he is in an attacking and power position. I want to lock up and drive the offensive blocker back off the line of scrimmage. After he starts to drive the offensive blocker back, he starts to maintain his gap responsibility. The mistake a lot of guys make is jumping outside when they see the reach drive block. When they do that, the blocker seats his block and drives the defense outside. The key to this technique is to get off the ball.

A type of attacking technique can get the defensive tackle upfield too far on some occasions. That makes him vulnerable to the draw-and-trap play. We work hard on down and distance to give our people a chance to think about what is coming.

We work on recognizing blocking schemes. Make your people complete football players. Make them think about down and distance so they are ready for the draw and trap. As our defensive linemen come off the line of scrimmage, they recognize the set and hand position of the offensive linemen. When they read the draw set, they start to squeeze to the inside. We don't want them to stop their charge, because if it is a playaction pass, it is hard to restart the charge in the pass rush. If the offensive lineman pops straight up and stutter-steps, it is a run.

Our defensive linemen run games and stunts, and we never tip them off. If defensive linemen look at where they are going, they tip the stunt to the offense. Offensive linemen look for the tip-offs. Teach your defensive fronts not to look where they are going. Teach them not to stagger their feet the way they are going.

One stunt we like to run is called ''ET.'' The E stands for end, and the T for tackle. The end goes first, and the tackle comes around. It is generally a weak-side stunt. The end comes down and works to the backfield. He has to get his feet moving and keep them moving. He keeps going until he is deep into the backfield. If he stops his feet from moving, he can lose his balance and fall. The defensive end has to make the tackle take him. If he stops working his feet, the tackle will read the stunt and come off on the defensive tackle coming around. The tackle comes off on the guard and bull-rushes the guard. He doesn't start his move to the outside until he sees the end come down. Again, this stunt requires the defensive linemen to come off the ball hard.

In the 4-3 defense, never let the 3 technique play in just one spot. Move him around. Put him on the inside pinch, or formation game. Use linebacker and defensive-line stunts to free up their playing area. If the inside tackles don't make things happen on defense, the defense is in trouble. If they don't make plays, your Mike linebacker will not be effective. Move the down 2s and 3s around as much as you can. The easiest block for the offensive tackle is when he knows the defensive end will never come inside of him.

In the pass rush we teach our linemen about the 3-, 5-, and 7-steps drop. We have to know what the offense likes to do. If they are a 3-step-drop passing team, our stunts have to be quick and short so that we can get our hands up in the quarterback's face. This goes back to down and distance. That helps your players to execute the plays you want from them. It lets your

 





Page 15

linebackers make the proper drops. If the quarterback is running a 5-step drop, the defensive linemen can run a delayed stunt. Teach down and distance and pass drops in your philosophy.

If the offensive goes to two tight ends versus the 4-3 defense, most coverages are some kind of quarter coverage. Generally, we are in quarters all the way across. The corner has the wide receiver mostly in man coverage in the zone. If the receiver runs past 10 yards, the corner has him man to man. If the receiver climbs to 14 yards, he still is man. After that he is going to trade him to the safety. If he takes off, he lets him go. He is then reading flow and could be support on a run.

In the 4-3 defense versus a two-tight-end set, we will declare to the side we think you are going to run. That means we will play a 36 technique to the strong side and a 27 to the weak side. We declare our strong-side tackle to a 3 technique, which is outside eye of the guard, and the end in a 6 technique, which is head-up the tight end.

To the other side we play the tackle in a 2 technique, which is head-up the guard, and a 7 technique, which is inside eye of the other tight end. In the 4-3 defense there are a lot of holes or bubbles in the alignment. The inside tackles have to be studs. They have to be able to draw the double team. If the offense can handle your tackle with one guy, your defense is going to be weak. The tackles have to make the belly play bounce flat to the line and outside. For this type of offense to be successful, there have to be tight ends who like to block. In the 4-3 defense the safeties have to stay on top of the number-two receiver, who is the tight end.

The two-tight-end formation is a good formation to run against a 4-3 defense, if you have two good tight ends. The offense can make all kinds of adjustments with their personnel and cause the defense some problems with alignment. I like the 4-3 defense because it allows you at all times to run "run blitzes." That gives you the chance to get minus yards in pass situations or definite run situations. The 2nd and 2 is a waste down for the offense. The 3rd and 2 play is a run situation for the first down. These are situations where we like a "run blitz."

A lot of defensive coordinators would like to find one key that will put them in the right front. I'm going to give you some ideas about how to find those keys. When you scout a football team, find out from each formation what play they like to run. We like to run the "tight 5." That puts us in an overshift defense to the tight end. This gives us a 1 and a 5 technique to the split-end side. We are in a 3 and a 9 technique to the tight-end side. We move and jump or stem into those sets. The offense likes to come to the line and call their play. You want to get into a defense and then stem to the "tight 5."

If the offense comes out with a back strong, we can take the Sam linebacker, walk him up on the line outside the defensive end, and bring him. The defensive end takes an inside charge and covers the Sam linebacker's gap and the Sam linebacker takes the defensive end's responsibility. If the offense sets a back weak, the Will linebacker and 5-technique end can do the same thing.

 





Page 16

If the offense stays in an I formation or strong set, we can call "Mike scrape." That allows the Mike linebacker to run a stunt with the 3-technique tackle. The 3-technique tackle slants back into the 1 gap, and the Mike linebacker runs through the 3 gap.

The Mike linebacker makes the call off the offensive set. How many times have you broken the huddle and wished you had called another defense? Let Mike call the defense, and you have a chance to stop the play.

Last year we had a hard time stopping the run. We had to commit to an eight-man front and play cover one, man coverage, in the secondary. We call our eight-man adjustments "one-free." This means that at the last minute the Mike and Will linebackers are going to slide a little bit to the weak side. The end to the weak side would normally, on a running play, bounce the zone play. Since the free safety is gone and cannot support, the end has to box and contain to his side. He plays outside in and keeps the ball inside. When you check to man coverage, the end has a different responsibility.

You can kick the defense to the weak side and get the same eight-man front. We overshift the front to a 3-and-5 technique to the weak side and a 1-and-5 technique to the strong side. The Sam linebacker shifts up into a 9 technique. Mike and Will linebackers stack in the 3 and 1 gaps, and the strong safety walks up to the 7 technique on the tight end.

Defense is an attitude. But you have to practice that. In your philosophy you have to simplify the calls you are going to make. We want kids to be aggressive, not cautious. There are three phases of football. We have offense, defense, and the special teams. Set individual goals with your kids, especially on special teams. If you win in your special-teams play, you have a chance to win the football game. Special teams are a major factor in winning a football game. You have to emphasize that phase of the game.

I measure defense by no points on the board. I understand that teams kick field goals. But they have to drive to get into position to kick a field goal. Never give in to a situation. Try to make your guys play harder when they meet adverse situations and sudden changes. Defense wins championships. Offense wins games. Make sure you build your defense first.

We run the "pursuit drill" to improve our defensive team. The defensive coach gets in the middle of the field, calls the defense, and then watches his team react. We film this and make sure every kid gets the proper angle going in his pursuit responsibility. The kids are conditioning as well as learning. With everything that you do in football, try to make your guys think. They are sprinting, but there is a purpose to what they are doing. They have to think. It is a good drill because they are reacting to the takeoff, sprinting to the ball, and sprinting off the field.

Another drill we use with our defensive team is the "intercept drill." We call the defense and let the defense react with pass rush and coverage. We throw the ball downfield where a defensive back will intercept. He returns the ball, and everyone has to react to that side of the field, get in front of the ball, and escort it up the sideline.

We do the same things with our offense. We like upand-run goal-line drives. We huddle them, snap the

 





Page 17

ball, and run the plays. The offensive linemen sprint down field and escort the ball. That is conditioning with a purpose.

Gentlemen, we are going to do everything we can to help you. We have our football camp. If you come down, our coaches will take you into a room, sit down, and give you some football time. I don't mean just lecture. We want to help you become a good football coach.

Again, your main focus has to be your kids. You have to spend time with them and direct them. You can talk X's and O's all you want or draw plays until you are blue in the face, but if you don't have the kids' minds in the right direction, it won't work. Your focus should be spending time with the kids and making sure they are doing what is right. That is what we are about at Oklahoma.

Gentlemen, if you don't have any questions, I'll close. Just remember how important you are to your players. You are a big influence on what they do. Teach them to do the right things, and I'll promise you everything else will take care of itself. I appreciate your inviting me. Thank you.

 





Page 18

Wayne Bolt

Setting the Offensive Practice Schedule

Troy State University

When Ray Lamb called me to speak, I told him I had four types of lectures. I told him I had a one hundred dollar, two hundred dollar, five hundred dollar, and one thousand dollar lecture that I could give. I asked him which one he want me to use. He said, "Wayne, I want you to lecture 
free." 
I said, "No way." But I am glad to be here.

For those who do not know much about Troy State, let me bring you up to date. We are located in south Alabama. We are a Division I AA football team. We are a member of the Southland Football League. We are about one hour below Auburn. We are about two hours from the beach. We have been pretty successful for the last six years. I work for Larry Blakeney, who was an assistant at Auburn for 13 years. We have put together a good program. Not only is it a good place to go to school and to get an education, but it is a place where we feel we can win some football games.

Today I want to go over what we do with our offense. First, I want to talk about our practice schedule. At a lot of the clinics you go to, they talk a lot of X's and O's. Everyone has a different idea. When I go to speak at a clinic, I try to give the coaches something that will give them some different ideas and things to think about.

The thing we try to do is to get in as many reps as we can in the time we have allotted for practice. A lot of coaches will talk about quality reps. Yes, you do want quality reps. However, getting kids lined up and getting them moving is very important. I go to a lot of high school and college practices, and I see five players working and 50 players standing around watching. What we try to do is to get everyone moving at a rapid pace. We want to create in practice the situation the players face in the game. I am sure everyone wants to do this.

We start off with running fundamentals. We do those 10 to 15 minutes per day. Next, we go to the chute-and-sled drills. We do a board drill as well. I have all of our fundamental drills on a tape. If you want to see it, I can get it to you.

We do half-line runs for 10 to 15 minutes per day. Most of you have only one offensive line coach. If you have two line coaches, that is a good situation. We work on the plays that we need to work on for that day as we go through the drill. We go "no-huddle rapid fire" on the left side, and then we do it on the right side. Then we swap sides between the 1s and 2s on offense and defense. This is with a quarterback and no backs. I find myself spending more time coaching the scout team than I do at times on our kids. It is important to get the look you want from the scout team. We are in the "no huddle with rapid fire" and then "no backs."

Everything is scripted for the drill. I have another man running the defense, and I have the 1s and 2s. Everything is filmed. If I see something wrong, I will correct it. We run a play and then bring the second group up on the line and run the play. This is rapid fire. We want as many reps as we can get. We are teaching on film rather than spending the time on the field. At the same time there are a few little things that you have to do, but you want to get as many reps as possible.

Next, we go full-line runs with our backs against the scout team. We go best against best early in the year. We do not do this much. We do not want anyone to get hurt. We want to be able to use them on Saturday. We do just enough early in the year to get their pencils sharpened. We will do four reps with the first group and four reps with the second group. Then we repeat the four reps with each group. After that we go two reps with each group. They end up getting 10 snaps each, best against the best and seconds against the seconds.

 





Page 19

Next, we go to our "blitz pick-up with backs" drill. This is not a high-intensity period. Again, it is scripted; it is geared to what we want that day. I am standing behind the line calling the plays: "Rapid-right 72." We do not have any quarterbacks or receivers in the drill. We get the defense lined up and work against the blitz we expect that week. You may make your guard and center interchangeable. This is the time to work with them. This is not a "get them tired" type drill. It is blitz pickup working on assignments. Again, we are in the no-huddle set. It is a 10-minute scripted drill.

Pass-protection fundamentals are the toughest thing to teach offensive linemen. We work on that 10 to 15 minutes per day. We have the bags, we do the steps, and we do the whole shooting match. We do that 10 to 15 minutes per day.

Then we do our half-line pass protection. Now we go back-to-back. The reason we go half line is because we do not want the big piles at the quarterback. We do not want anyone to get hurt. At the same time we want the quarterback to feel pressure and be able to react.

Last, we do team work for 30 minutes. We do two huddles at once. We go two huddles, and we run the same play. The second group gets to see the play against the defense. That is basically our practice schedule.

We are an I-formation football team. We run the option. If it were not for the option, we would not be where we are today. We have been ranked in the top 10 in our division for the last several years. We believe in hard-nosed football. A hard-nosed offense makes for a hard-nosed defense. There is no question, you win championships with defense. We believe that, and I am an offensive coach and have been for years. How do you get good defense? You do that by recruiting good players and by being hard-nosed on offense.

We run the toss sweep. We run it strong and weak. We believe in the toss sweep. We have good backs that can run, and we have fullbacks that can block. We do a little scheming, but not very much. We run the isolation series. We will run the ball right dead at you. It is hard-nosed "iso" downhill. We come off the ball and hit them in the mouth.

We have gone to a one-back zone play. It has been good for us. We run the wide, tight, and cutback. We call them wide, tight, and cutback. We do not get real fancy with it.

We have been fortunate to have had good fullbacks. We run four plays with the fullback. We run the belly, trap, dive, and cutback. We fake-sweep and give it to the fullback on the cutback.

We do run the option. We believe in the option. I have heard a lot of coaches say they did not want to run the option because they did not want to get the quarterback hit. We believe in the option, so we run it. We run the option with the fullback and quarterback, and we pitch the football.

We believe in the triple option. We work on the triple option every day during the two-a-days for 10 minutes. We go half line for 10 minutes. We have two quarterbacks running the drills. The most important thing is coaching the scout team. During the season we work twice a week on the option, going half line. On Tuesday and Wednesday we have the pads on, so we go 10 minutes per day on half-line option drills. We work very little on the double and speed options.

We run the belly option. Basically, it is a quarterback sweep play. We pull the guard and log or hook the end. We can run the play to the strong or weak side. The fullback is on the linebacker. The quarterback reverses out and hauls ass. He wants to get wide and run. The quarterback option is big against the blitz. We believe that this play keeps the blitz down in the two-back set. We can get some big plays off the belly option.

We run the misdirection game. We run the counter gap and pull the guard and tackle on the back side. That is basically our running game.

In our passing game we have basic protections. The toughest one is the drop-back pass. That is the one we spend most of our time on.

 





Page 20

Let me go over these very quickly. Our three-step game is gap protection. It is the passing game with the hitch routes. In maximum protection, if the defense gives you a bunch of blitz, you keep the backs in and have them check on the release. Sprint-draw protection is off our isolation series. Split-draw protection is just what it indicates, with split backs. Shotgun and drop-back protection is the toughest protection we have. People ask us what kind of offense we run. We tell them this: "We are an I-option, power-sweep, 'iso,' no-back, shotgun, and one-back offense." They say, "What? You are kidding me!" No! If we are in long-yardage situations, we get in the shotgun. We also use the option-pass protection. If you are going to run the option, you have to have the option pass. We run the ball down the line and make it look like the run. We throw to the wideouts in the perimeter game. We run the bootleg, waggle, and naked protections. We do not run them much, but we have them.

You have to have a plan if you are overmatched. If we are overmatched at the two tackles and they are getting upfield, we have a way to double them. We also have a way to sprint away from them. We have our sprint-out protection. We also have the ability to dash away from them. By 
dash 
we mean to pull the backside guard and get the quarterback on the edge if we are having trouble with the pass rush. We will let our offensive linemen use the cut block as a changeup block on our pass protection.

Next, I want to talk about our half-line option drill. We have the coaches stationed where they can watch the drill. The quarterback coach watches the quarterback and fullback. I am in between the two groups. We have our wide-receiver coach with one group and another coach with the other group. We are looking for reps.

Again, we run two groups at a time. We have a coach outside with the wideouts. Both groups go at one time. We rotate quarterbacks and fullbacks every play. We rotate the line every three plays. If you do not have many people, you would run one group at a time. This drill is to the boundary. We work two groups on offense, and the third team is our scout team. We work against all of the different looks to the boundary.

HALF-LINE OPTION DRILL TO THE BOUNDARY

When we face an eight-man front, we run our option to the field. If the defense has an eight-man front with a three-deep, we do not have enough men to run the option to the weak side. We run the option to the field. We run back-to-back half-line drills with two teams going at one time.

HALF-LINE OPTION DRILL TO FIELD, BACK-TO-BACK

 





Page 21

Again, the coaches are spread out so that they can see what is going on. We go back and forth and try to get as much done as possible. Again, we rotate the line every three plays and the backs every play. The quarterbacks rotate every other play. Again, we film it all.

We work on half-line blocking schemes. These are onside blocks only. We work the backside blocking during fundamentals. We go two groups side by side. I stand in the middle, and we go rapid fire. We work on the particular block we need that day. We do not have backs in the drill. We have a quarterback, and we use our snap count. We go on a quick count. We want a lot of reps here. We work all of the different individuals in the drill. We run to both sides. Then the second group goes. While one group is going, the other one is getting ready. We do not want to waste time. We work on the plays we need to go over for that day. Again, you have to work your center both ways. On one play he goes to the right, and the next play he goes to the left.

We do not flip-flop. We are right side, left side. Most people say flip-flopping is the best thing. The one thing I would be afraid on flip-flopping would be pass protection. That is the reason we do not use the flip-flop.

Next, we go to half-line pass. We want as many reps as we can get. We want to stay away from the big piles. We do not want anyone to get hurt. This is an all-pass drill. Everyone knows it is all-pass. It is a tough situation up front, and it is tough for the quarterback. It is the best drill we do as far as pass protection. We go with the right side at the top. The defense can do what they want, including using the blitz. We do not touch the quarterback on the drill. We tag the quarterback. We script all of the plays we are going to work on.

One group runs the play while the other group is getting their play in the huddle, one yard from the ball. In the game we huddle five yards from the ball. We do not waste time in practice getting from the huddle to the line. We are trying to get as many reps as possible and save as much time as we can. We are not concerned that the defense knows what we are calling. We feel that is overrated. We run the drill from the right hash, right middle, middle, left middle, and left hash. This way the quarterback gets a look at all the reads. We do not do a lot of coaching in this drill. We want reps. Our extra linemen are on a knee off to the side. We have a lot of players working here. Let's see the film.

HALF-LINE PASS DRILL: TWO GROUPS BACK TO BACK

 





Page 22

Chris Brown

Building and Maintaining a Program 

West Grand High School, Colorado

I have come to this clinic for a long time. This clinic started in 1982 in Denver. Eddie Kintz was the first clinic director. I have made all of the clinics held here. This is a special time for me. It is a good time to talk things over and to get some new ideas. The cost of this clinic is only $60. They give you a nice travel bag when you register. That takes the cost down to $40. They give you all the beer you can drink, and now the cost is down to $20. They have 20 speakers here, so if this lecture is worth $1, we are OK. A lot of you got out of school on Friday, and that has to be worth $60. It is to me this time of the year. I am thinking about passing around a collection.

In 19761 started coaching. At that time the philosophy was "We are with you, win or tie." In 1986 the philosophy was this: "Coach, we are with you win or win." By 1996 the philosophy was this: They do not care if you win or not, as long as their son gets a full scholarship.

I would like to thank John Burke for having me on the clinic. I coached with John in the All-State game. I have worked with John on the Coaches Board of Directors. I even have ties to the person who started these clinics, Duffy Daugherty. I do not know how many of you even knew who Duffy was. I grew up in Michigan just north of Lansing. I got to caddie for Duffy in a golf tournament for three days. I can't remember what he paid me or what his score was, but I do wish I could remember some of the jokes he told. He made the caddie stay close to him so he could tell his jokes. Of course, I was a Michigan State fan, and they were good during that time. That was a real treat for me.

One person asked me if I was going to talk about our weight program. I am not. What I want to do today is to share some opinions and ideas on how to build and maintain a good solid program. We have been successful, and I will tell you what we did in our program. I say "opinion" and "ideas" because I do not have any answers. There are many ways to achieve success. There are many ways to run an offense, defense, and other phases of the game. In 21 years as a head coach, I have realized that I do not have many answers, but I do have some strong opinions.

First, I want to talk about what coaching means to me. Our philosophy is simple. You need something to hang your hat on. It is simple for me:

(1) Have West Grand High School football be a positive experience for every player. Just think about that one statement. Sometimes that is tough to do. That is our goal.

(2) Have each player be a better person for having been in the program.

(3) Treat each player as I would want my son to be treated. I try to do that with each kid. I have a son who I am fortunate enough to coach now. He is a junior. I watched him grow up being the ball boy and now a player. That has really been important to me through the years.

(4) Take the talent we have and maximize it to the fullest. We were 13-0. In 1985 we were 4-6, and that was probably the best coaching we ever did. Sometimes when you have a losing record, it does not mean you did a poor coaching job. Sometimes a winning record does not mean you did a great job with the talent you have.

Next, I want to go over the things we want to accomplish:

(1) We want to teach and lead with class and integrity. For us that is important: how we treat our opponents, our kids, officials, teachers, and everyone we come in contact with. We get letters from restaurants, old ladies, and others on how our players act and dress when they are out. That gets back to the school board, and that is important to them. If we ever go 0-10, we hope

 





Page 23

we can drag those letters out and have the board read them.

(2) We want to communicate to our players at all times. (a) They should know what is expected of them on and off the field. (b) They should know their status. (c) They should know they fit into the program.

(3) Our team should be fundamentally sound and well disciplined. The highest compliment I have gotten is when another coach told me our team was fundamentally sound and well disciplined.

(4) We should improve daily and weekly. We want to learn from the best. You can learn from anyone, but learn from the best. I followed Lloyd Gasket. He won 10 state championships. I do not know how many second places he finished. One thing he taught me was this. He said, "Chris, don't try to change everything." He said, "Bite off a little at a time. Get it done, and then go to the next thing you want to change." Lloyd gave me a lot of ideas.

I was in a coffee club that met at 7:30. Bob Smith was a principal, and he was a three-sports star in high school and college. I think he had 12 letters in college. He was a catcher for the New York Yankee organization. He was at the AAA level. The Yankee catcher hurt his leg, and so Bob was to move up, but he broke his leg. The next guy to move up was Elston Howard.

Don Kimble was our band director. This man never took worse than first in the state. How many of you can say that? That was for 31 years.

Another person I learned from was Warren Mitchell. He won more state championships than anyone else. He won the state in several sports, including football, basketball, and track. We would sit around and talk. I would ask him questions. It is amazing how much you can learn from these people. What I am trying to get across to you is this. You have to find those guys. You can always find someone to talk with.

Another way to learn is by reading. Here is a book on Bear Bryant. It came out in 1974. I still read this book each year before we start football. I think I know it by heart, but I read it anyhow. I am not an Alabama fan, but I am a Bear Bryant fan after reading his book.

You can learn a lot at these clinics. If you ever buy anything, it is this manual. I'm talking about the 
Coach of the Year Clinics Football Manual. 
Here is the first one I got, and it was in 1980. Woody Hayes is on the front cover. If you want to know about history and war, he was the man that knew about it. But he also knew something about football.

I went to see Ohio State when they played Michigan State. Woody was out there in his short-sleeved shirt and black hat. He is the one who started soccer-style kicking. Woody would throw his hat down, and he would kick the sideline markers. Then things would go flying out on the field. I think that is where the soccer-style kicking got started. You can watch games and learn what colleges and other teams are doing. You can scout other teams and learn from them.

Someone asked me what is tradition. We all have tradition. Every school and every coach that has been coaching a while has tradition. To me tradition is how others view your program. It is also how your players, parents, community, and related others view your program. For better or worse, we all have tradition. It does not matter whether you have a winning or a losing program.

The next question is how to get a tradition going. How do you establish your own team tradition? I will give you a few ideas on what we do to establish some of our traditions. One of the things we do is to wear a shirt and tie to games. This includes the coaches as well as the players. We coach in shirts and ties, both home and away games.

Another thing we do is this. The moms make us treats every day during the play-offs. The kids love this. Our kids are like zoo animals. You feed them, and they expect it all of the time.

Another tradition we have relates to the dropkick. Two years ago they were going to take the dropkick out of the rule books and eliminate it from football. I wrote a

 





Page 24

letter to the rules committee, and we kept it in. I like to think my letter saved the dropkick. We may be the only team that still does the dropkick anymore. My dad taught me the technique to drop-kick. It has been important to me to keep the dropkick in the books. My dad is still living, and he played when they used the dropkick.

We started using the dropkick, and it became a tradition. In 1978 we did it in a game finally. We were ahead at the time, and the kid tried the dropkick and made it. The referee come over and gave me a hug. He said that he had been officiating for 40 years and when he played football that is what they used to do. He was retiring at the end of that game, and he had not seen the dropkick used in all of his years of officiating. What we do is to have a senior dropkick contest. Some of the kids who do not play football go out and practice drop-kicking. This year we had a lot of good kickers. We even had a soccer-style kicker drop-kick the ball. That was a first for us. In one game he made five or six kicks drop-kicking. We even drop-kicked a couple of kickoffs. It is a fun thing to do, and when we are way ahead or way behind, we use it. Establish your own tradition.

Let me talk about assistant coaches. When I first came out of college in 1976, I tried to coach it all. I was the head coach of everything. The assistants got to coach their positions, and that was it. We started out and were 1-3. After we got beat in our homecoming game, I decided to turn over the defense to one of the assistants. That was the best thing that happened to me. I learned to let assistants coach. It is important to let them have responsibility. It gives them ownership in the team. When we get an award for the season, it is for all of the coaches and not just the head coach. It takes a whole staff to be successful. Our coaches go scouting together and watch films together.

Next, I want to talk about recruiting in your school. Football numbers are important. We all know that. Track and football are the only two sports where you can have a lot of kids out there. It would be good to have five football programs if we had enough players out. What we do is this. We make a list of 8th, 9th, and 10th graders not out for football. It is not so important for the older kids. We have 45 to 50 kids out for football. We have kids walking the halls that should be playing. I am sure it is the same at your school. I am not talking about the butt heads. I am talking about good kids. They may not be good athletes, but they are good kids. They get good grades, and they are dependable. They could be helping your program, and you could be helping them. We get some kids who stick with it and some who quit. But we really make an effort to get more players out for the team. Another thing we have done is to write a letter to each class. We send the team a letter on the subject, and then I send individuals letters encouraging them to give football a try.

On the other side of the issue is weight lifting versus other sports. I would rather have players in another sport than lifting. I teach weight-lifting classes. I get most of the team lifting. We have really seen some progress, but I would much rather have them playing basketball and hockey.

You must realize that attitude is the key. I always start the season with this point. I tell our team that our season will depend on our attitude and our offensive line. I put the pressure on the offensive line right away. I would always rather take an average player with a great attitude than a great player with a poor attitude. I seem to do the best coaching job when I have a great player with a great attitude. I can do a good job of coaching that type kid. If I had more of them, I could really do a good job. An average player with a great attitude can play for you.

Potential will get you beat. We play performers and producers. Also along these lines, we play and practice with enthusiasm. We have fun during the game. A lot of teams will look at us before the game, and we do not say a word. We got out-yelled 13 times this year before the game. We do not waste our energy yelling during the pregame.

We probably do our best job in placement of our personnel. We do an off-season scouting report. Basically, we take a look at the personnel, see whether we need to switch players to other positions, and see what we have to do to be up to date.

 





Page 25

The next area is what we list as playing time and our JV system. We guarantee our freshmen, sophomores, and juniors that they will start somewhere on the JV team. We keep the players and parents happy that way. Also, we are building up the team for next year. This increases enthusiasm and helps the players develop a good attitude about the game. We are a Class A school, but we play Class AA and AAA teams on the JV level. We do not guarantee the seniors anything.

On the varsity level we use all of our one-way starters and our best JVs on our special teams. Now, this ties more players to the varsity level. As far as counting quarters, the kicking game does not count, so we can get kids in the game who are not starters.

Let me talk about the seniors. Next year we will have about 18 seniors. We do not have captains. All of our seniors are captains. Each year I have the seniors over to my house for steaks. We talk about leadership, tradition, and our expectations. We do everything we can to play seniors. We cannot guarantee that they will start. Our seniors expect their best year next year because we had a good year this past year.

The next area relates to penalties, turnovers, execution, and fundamentals. We teach the rules. You would be surprised to find out what your players do not know about the rules, especially in the kicking game.

We really stress turnovers just as you do. We stress taking care of the football on offense and creating opportunities on defense. This past year we were plus28 in turnovers. We teach fundamentals and stress them all of the time. One way we do this is with the perfect play drill. Instead of running sprints, one day we will tell the team they must run five perfect plays before they can go in. As the coach, you can decide how perfect the plays are. It depends on how hard you want to work them at the end of practice.

This next area may be something that a lot of you do not believe in, but we do not hit much in practice. Once the season starts, we hit for only five minutes for the week, and that is it. We play most of our games on Saturday. I think we have two games on Friday night. We have very few injuries. We may go down on the goal line, put our best 22 kids against each other, and go live for 10 plays.

We have most of our JV games on Monday. Our varsity will watch film and go out and stretch. We may not even go out on Monday. We practice Tuesday, Wednesday, and Thursday. Some days we do not practice on Friday because we go to scout other teams. Some of the teams we play are four hours away, so we go scout them and do not practice on Friday before a Saturday game.

Let me list some results of not hitting so much after the season starts. (1) We have very few injuries. We want our best players playing on Friday night or Saturday. (2) It keeps the moms happy. (3) It keeps the players fresh. They are mentally and physically fresh. (4) The game becomes special to the players. It is more than just someone new to hit and another chance to wear the game uniform. On Wednesday or Thursday after the JVs have played on Monday, we may take them at the end of practice and hit for 15 to 20 minutes. (5) It allows us to gear up for 13 games and a longer season.

There is one drawback to this hitting rule. We do not block or tackle well early in the year. We know this going into the year.

We do not let our freshman kids go live against the varsity players. We want to protect the freshmen at all times. We do not want to run off any players. You do not know the type of player a freshman will make. We want to keep as many players as we can, not run them off.

We use an off-season scouting report. We use last year's films. First, we scout ourselves. We look at our personnel. We look to see what we did right and what we need to improve on. We take a look at the plays to see whether we need to change or add or drop plays for the next year.

We scout our opponents. We look at their personnel and their offense, defense, and kicking game. We do

 





Page 26

this on every team we play. We do this in the offseason. We get them back out in the summer and update them.

OFF-SEASON SCOUTING REPORT

Team________________________________________

Coach_______________________________________

Last year's results__________________________

Starters returning - 0 =___________________

Starters returning - D =___________________

Seniors lost____________________________________

Major strengths_________________________

Major weaknesses_______________________

Outlook_______________________________________

Top players back________________________

Offense_______________________________________

Defense_______________________________________

We do an evaluation of the drills we do. The criteria we use for our drills is this: They must adapt to game situations. When I played, we used a drill called the monkey roll. I am sure some of you have done the drill. Do you ever see anyone use the monkey roll in a game? We want the drill to adapt to a game situation.

Now I will talk about our offense. The best advice I can give young coaches is this: ''Be known for something.'' For us, we are known for the option and the play-action pass. We run out of the I formation. I see a lot of teams that change their offense because of the personnel they have coming back. Some years we will have the leading rusher in the state. Some years we will have the leading passer or the leading receiver. Some years we are a ball-control team. Some years we are a big-play team. But, we are always in the I set. We do something different each year, but 85 to 90 percent of our offense is the same every year.

The other thing we do is to limit the number of plays we run. When I first started coaching, we had six running plays and four passing plays, and we won the state championship. When I look back, that told me something. Bobby Bowden said if they add a play, they have to take one out of their offense first. If you are going to add one, take one out.

We really try to balance our run and pass plays. You usually are good at one or the other. If you are really good at one or the other, then you had better work on getting the worst part better. This year we had 30 TDs running and 30 TDs passing. That is balance, but it does not always work out that way.

When you look at your offense, you should evaluate your practice time spent versus how often it is used in a game. We spend a lot of time working on plays, but when we get in the game we never run them. We may run them once or twice. I am talking about your base offense.

It is important to package your plays to get the most out of them. We start out with a base play. Then we have an extension and a misdirection out of the play. Next, we have play-action passes. Finally, we have our special plays.

We work hard on our execution and situations. When I said we go against the bags, it does not mean we are not working. We used to go out and scrimmage in the middle of the field. Not any more. Now we work on situations such as goal line, coming out, 3rd and 10, and two-minute offense.

On defense it is the same thing as offense. Once you know what you are doing, you can adjust. You need to be known for something on defense. This year we saw several different formations. We saw the spread, fullhouse, run-and-shoot, pro, trips, single-wing, shotgun, and wing-T formations. Take your defense, and figure out how to adjust your defense to meet different formations. We are a 6-2 team and could adjust to those sets.

I think there are four things you need to have a great defense. It does not matter what level you are on; these still apply:

(1) Assignments and alignments. Make sure the kids know what they are doing before you play a team.

 





Page 27

(2) Get off blocks. I do not care what position you play, you must be able to get off the blocks. We use a drill we call "get off the block." We do the drill with bags.

(3) You need to learn to run to the football to have a great defense. We use a burst drill, a pursuit drill, and a pride drill to teach players to get to the ball.

(4) Good tackling is a must to have a good defense. We use a lot of drills on this. We run these drills with them: hit and fit, one man, Burma, open field, scramble, and eye opener.

Let me go over one or two more points before we get out of here. First is punt for points. We have had the All-State punter for the last two years. We have had the All-State punter more than our share.
