We do this drill at the end of practice. We may have enough players for four or five teams. You divide the players into teams. We are looking for three things on the punt-for-points drill. We want to work on punt coverage, communication, and conditioning. We line up in punt formation and go down and cover the punt. You can put the ball on the 50-yard line or whatever yard line you feel your punter can reach. The punt is made, and the team covers the kick. If the ball lands between the 15- and 10-yard lines, you get one point. If it lands between the 5- and 10-yard lines, you get two points. If it lands between the goal line and 5-yard line, you get three points. If the ball goes in the end zone, you do not get a point. If you want, you can double the points for kicking the ball out of bounds near the end zone. You set a goal for the number of points you want them to reach. If the goal is seven points, when a team gets seven points, they can drop out and watch the other groups. There are a lot of variations you can use with the drill.

The last point is what we call extra-point reduction. We may tell the team we are going to run 30- to 40-yard sprints after practice, but we will go for the extra-point reduction. We have two kickers and a guest kicker. We line up with our two regular kickers. We line up with no referees and no defense, and we are kicking the PATs. We let them kick, and if they make them, we reduce the number of sprints they have to run. We keep moving them back after they make a kick. We are putting the kickers under pressure. We will do some bag rushing, but we are going against air here. Some days we may not run many sprints, and some days we may run a lot of them. This encourages team spirit. They really cheer for those kickers. We may have to adjust the number of sprints they run, but it is fun.

I hope I have given you some ideas. Thank you.

 





Page 28

John Cooper

The OSU Running Game

Ohio State University

Thank you very much. It's good to be with you today. It is an exciting time of year for me. It is an exciting time of year for anyone in the coaching profession. I used to come to these clinics all the time. In my opinion probably the best coach who has ever coached the game of football was Paul "Bear" Bryant. Coach Bryant used to get up and tell coaches, "If you can live without it, don't coach." I have thought of that over the years. My son played college football for me, and I used to tell him that I didn't want him to go into coaching. I wanted him to get another job. Looking back, I think that was a mistake. I wish he had got into coaching.

I love coaching. I tell people all the time that if I ever get out of coaching, I'll have to get a job. It is a great way to make a living. When I get up in the morning, I can't wait to get to the office. I hope most of you feel that same way. Right now we are looking at tapes of last year's games. We are looking at the good and bad things we did last year. We get in a workout every day. I'm a strong believer in being in coaching condition. It keeps you young. It gives you a chance to work out with your players. We work out in the weight room with our players. When they see us in there, they want to get in there and work out too. It is a fun job, and we all make good money. We are making more money than we ever dreamed we would be making. You get a chance to work with these young players. I don't know what the hell I'd be doing if I were not coaching. It is a great profession. And to think you have any influence over these young guys is a very gratifying feeling.

Every time I go visit somewhere, I ask the question, "What is the best thing about your program?" At Ohio State obviously we have good players. We have some great football players at Ohio State. But the best thing about our program is the chemistry and the work effort of our players. The best thing we do at Ohio State is our off-season program. You win in the winter conditioning program. Our players love to come in and work out. I think we have the best strength coach in the country. A lot of our former players who are playing pro ball work out with us. If you come up to our place at 8 or 9 o'clock in the morning, you will see Eddie George, Terry Glenn, Ricky Dudley, Joey Galloway, Orlando Pace, and a lot of others in there working out. Our players love to have those pro players around there working out.

Our football players spend 12 months a year in Columbus, Ohio. I tell them they don't have to be there in the summer, but if they're not, they are not going to play. They are all there during the summertime. That is why we have a good football team. They are there, and they work their butts off.

We have a saying at our place: "What you see is what you coach." When you look at films of your games, what you see is a reflection of you. There are a lot of things you can control. You may not be able to control the ability of your players, but you can coach them to line up properly. You can coach them to get in a good stance and not make mistakes and things of that nature. Teams that jump offside and get all kinds of penalties are a reflection of how you coach them during the week. When you run a defensive drill, you should move on the ball. Move on the ball, don't go on a cadence. Everything we do in the winter conditioning program with our defense is movement on the ball. Little things like that you can control.

I believe that your players will play as well as you expect them to play. If you talk about next year being a rebuilding year or "we're not very fast," then you are setting your team up. You will not be very good. At our place we lose more underclassmen than anybody. We have lost about 10 underclassmen to the pros. This year Shawn Springs and Orlando Pace are leaving early. I don't worry about guys I don't have. Don't worry about things you don't have control over. When one leaves

 





Page 29

early, that gives another one a chance to play. Those young players will play about as well as you expect them to play.

In our league we think that you have to be able to run the football and defensively you have to stop the other team from running the ball against you. There are a lot of good coaches in the Big Ten. If you can only run the ball, everyone will play you in a nine-man front. If all you can do is throw the ball, they will play you in nothing but a nickel package and take away what you can do. I think the game of football is being coached better now than at any other time. Now you have to be multiple. You better be able to run the ball and throw it. You have to do both. At our place we want to be about 60 percent to 40 percent run-to-pass. We have good balance in our offense.

You have to play great defense. Two of my very best defensive coaches are lecturing here later on this afternoon. I would advise you to go listen to them if you want to know what we are doing defensively. Last year we had a great defensive football team. We gave up an average of 10 points a game, and we play a great schedule. You win with defense. To have a good defensive football team, you have to commit good personnel to that side of the ball. You don't suddenly decide that you are going to play good defense. At our place our best players play on the defensive side of the ball.

We recruit linemen who can run. If they can run at all, we are going to give them a chance to play defense. If they can't play defense, we move them over to the offense. We take the good athletes and put them on defense. Shawn Springs could have been a great wide receiver or running back. But he was a great corner. What you are going to see in our league is the corners playing man-to-man and everyone else stopping the run. That is why you must run the ball and throw it. You have to use a lot of different formations and utilize the strengths of your football team.

At our place you have to win. I found that out at the first Buckeye booster meeting I went to. They assured me that they were 100 percent behind me, win or tie. 3ut they said, "Don't tie too many." I went down to buy a house. I talked to the real estate lady. She asked me what I did for a living. I told her I was the new football coach at Ohio State. She said I should rent this first year. We had an awful football team my first year. I made a mistake. I went in and talked about rebuilding and that we do not have any talent. Well, that is the way we played.

I don't brag a lot or lure players on the field. But I spend a lot of time in the locker room and weight room telling our young players how good I think they are going to be next year. We expect to have a hell of a football team next year, although we lost eight defensive players. We lost six offensive starters, but we expect to have a hell of a team. The Buckeyes are going to be good again next year. I tell that to our players all the time. Nobody is going to outwork us. Nobody is going to coach better than we coach. Nobody is going to work harder in the winter program.

We have a sign in our locker room and in our big meeting room. This is what we hang our hats on: 
Nothing keeps us from winning. 
I don't want to hear any reason why in the hell we don't win. I don't want to hear about lack of speed, size, or talent; inexperience; or anything else. I don't want to hear about injuries or the pregame meal. Don't talk about the officials or bad calls. In my opinion all you do when you talk about those things is to give yourself some reason why you got your butt beat. We don't do that. I don't care what it is. I don't want to hear about it. Just get the job done. I heard Joe Paterno one year, and he said he didn't believe in signs, but if he did, it would be, "Don't bitch about your players, because they are the only ones you've got." You can't trade them or draft some more. You better take the players you have coming back and make them into winners. That is the attitude we have.

Quickly let me show you some things that we hang our hat on. These are not original. They are called the 10 commandments. Tennessee uses them now, and Alabama used to use them.

(1) The team that makes the fewest mistakes usually wins the game. We don't make many mistakes. We don't turn the ball over much. We are not going to make a lot of stupid penalties.

 





Page 30

(2) You play for and make breaks. When one comes your way, you have to score.

(3) If at first the game of breaks goes against you, don't slow down. Put on more steam.

(4) Stress the kicking game, because this is where the breaks are made.

(5) Protect your passer and kicker, and rush their passer and kicker.

(6) Be mentally alert at all times, and leave nothing to chance.

(7) Carry out your assignment.

(8) Intercept the football.

(9) Carry the fight to your opponents. Hit them hard and fast.

(10) Win the surest way.

When you talk about the running game at OSU, we have had some great talents. We had a great back in Robert Smith, who left early. Raymont Harris stepped in. After Raymont graduated, Eddie George stepped in. After Eddie came Peppy Pearson. After him will come someone else. That is the attitude we have.

Robert Smith gained 1100 yards as a true freshmen. After Robert left, Raymont stepped in and gained 1344 rushing in the first year he played for us. He plays for the Chicago Bears now. Then came Eddie George. The first year Eddie played for us he gained 1500 yards. His second year he gained 1900 yards and won the Heisman Trophy. After that came Pepe Pearson. His first year he gained 1484 yards rushing. That's not bad for a first-year guy. He will be a senior next year. Guys, mark it down. Pepe Pearson will probably lead the nation in rushing next year. That is what I'm telling him now. I'm telling him I expect him to be the best running back in the conference next year. I told him he gained more yards in his first year than Eddie George did.

Next year I expect him to gain 2000 yards. I'm telling him that right now, almost every day. He is working his butt off. He is 6' 0" and about 205 pounds. He runs a 4.3 forty. He is a tough kid and ain't going to get hurt. He can catch the ball coming out of the backfield. He will be All Big Ten and All American and might win the Heisman. He is going to be my running back, and I want him to believe that.

We talk about balance in our offense. Last year we rushed for 2700 yards and threw for 2500 yards. You better be able to run and pass in the Big Ten. When we line up against Penn State, if we can't throw the ball, we can't beat them. Michigan and Notre Dame are the same way. If we can line up and run the ball, we are going to pound on you all day. If you are smart enough to take our running game away, we better be able to throw the ball. You have to have balance in your offense.

In the winter conditioning program your team should get bigger and stronger. We want to build confidence and a physical attitude. We want to win games up front. At Ohio State we run the ball to set up the pass. When you play us, you better buckle your chin strap on tight. You have to get down in the trenches, because we are coming after you. If you stop us, we will do something else. If you have nine people committed to the line of scrimmage, we'll run a play-action pass at you and burn you for a big play.

We run the football to make our defense physical. How are you going to be a good defensive team if you don't face a good running football team? We face each other in the spring and, more important, in two days in the fall. One thing is important at all levels of football. On the high school, college, and pro level, you have to learn how to practice. We go against each other every day. But we don't get people hurt. We tell them the first day that they can't get hurt. We had 11 defensive football players who started 13 straight football games last season. They don't get hurt because they know how to practice. We are always in headgear. Most of the time we are in headgear and shoulder pads. Everything we do in practice, we always stay on our feet. We pound on each other every day, but we stay up. We tackle every day, but we don't put the ball carriers on the ground. That way we don't get hurt. Our players get hit in practice but don't get beat up.

We run our inside drill with our best offense against our best defense every day. During the season our pad

 





Page 31

days are Tuesday and Wednesday, and we go seven on nine during those days. We spend 16 minutes with seven on offense against nine defensive players. We bring our safeties into the drill. We go best on best, full speed. Everything is full speed, except you don't tackle the ball carrier. You butt the hell out of him, but you don't put him on the ground. That makes us a physical team. We are going to stop the run and run the football.

Our defensive back and wide receiver work against each other every day. We work best on best in a manto-man scheme. We work that way for at least two periods every day. That makes us better offensively and defensively. We had Shawn Springs on one side and Ty Howard on the other. They played man to man, which allowed the safeties to crowd the line of scrimmage and run the alleys. We played a nine-man front almost all year long.

There are some things you need in your offense. You have to have power inside and speed on the perimeter. You also have to have some finesse, because there are some teams on your schedule that you are not going to be able to just run north and south on. You need some draws, counters, and screens. I'm also going to add an option next year. We are going to run more option because of the styles of defense we are facing. We are facing a lot of man coverage, with the safeties running the alley. They are playing five under and two deep. We are going to mix more option in our offense to control what the defense is doing.

When we are on offense, we are in charge. We know the snap count. We are going to get out of the huddle, sprint to the line, and go. But we are also going to have a freeze call to draw the defense offside. We want to control the tempo of the game. We will give you a lot of different sets. We want the defense to have to think. If the defense is thinking, they are less aggressive and will make mistakes. We run basic plays from a lot of different sets and formations.

We use three types of blocking schemes. We use a man, gap, and zone scheme. The man scheme is exactly W`hat it says. The gap is a double-down and kick-out type of scheme. And the zone scheme is exactly what it says. We work on the board with our best on best, just like the seven on nine. We want to take advantage of our best players. We have had some great offensive linemen at Ohio State. The best one we've ever had was Orlando Pace. He is 6' 7", weighs 330 pounds, runs a 4.9 forty, benches 500 pounds, and goes bear hunting with a switch. He is a 
footballplayer. 
The first day he walked on campus, he was our starting left tackle. We played 13 true freshmen last year and started two. Our middle linebacker was a freshman. He will probably be the next great linebacker to come out of Ohio State. We had a freshman wide receiver who caught 13 passes against Indiana and two touchdown passes in the Rose Bowl game. We are not afraid to play freshmen. We don't guarantee freshmen that they will play. We tell them the best players play. If a freshman comes in and is better than an upperclassman, he plays. In your game plan you should take advantage of your best players, attack the defense with personnel and alignment, and make them defend the entire field.

Our backs read the defense pre-snap. They know whether they are going to cut back or stay straight ahead. We try to predetermine before the ball is snapped whether to cut it back or bounce out. When we are using our gap scheme, which is our doubledown and kick-out scheme, we want to run downhill toward the line of scrimmage. We do an awful lot of "check with me" calls at the line of scrimmage. We call a run or pass depending on what the defense is doing. We count the people in the box. If you have six in the box, we run the ball. If you have seven in the box, we throw the ball. We can run the play both ways with the "check with me" call. In the huddle we call "24-25 check with me.'' When we come to the line of scrimmage, the quarterback decides which side to run. We use our quick-passing game in the "check with me" scheme.

We use movement to try to create mismatches with personnel. We want to be flexible. We run from a lot of different personnel groupings. We are going to run in all situations. Going in, coming out, long yardage, and short yardage are situations where we are going to run the ball. That sets up our play-action passes. We think we are a great play-action-passing team. In the Rose Bowl, we lined up in two tight ends and one flanker.

 





Page 32

We protected our throw well and hit our wide receiver for a 79-yard touchdown. It was from a running formation and a "check with me" call.

Here are some of the things we are doing offensively. We do this if you let us. We line up in the I formation and run power football. We run the isolation both ways. If our fullback can block your linebacker, you are in trouble. We will run our 22-23 isolation play 15 to 20 times a game, if our fullback can block your linebacker. We try to predetermine which one of your linebackers is not really physical. If you have two good linebackers inside who are better than our fullback, we are not going inside.

Coaching is not asking your players to do things they can't do. If our fullback can't handle your inside linebackers, we'll go to two tight ends. That makes the defense balance and puts them in a situation where they can't hide anyone. Most people are like us. Our Will linebacker is about half and half. He is about half strong safety and half linebacker. He is a 205pound linebacker. If we play a team that is trying to hide a guy like that, we go to our two tight ends. We double-down and kick out on him. That gives us a mismatch between our fullback and your Will linebacker.

In the Rose Bowl Arizona State had a defensive end who was all Pac 10. He was 6' 2", and 222 pounds. He was a great pass rusher, and they played him on the split-end side. We lined up and traded our tight end over to the other side. We had a 6'7", 265-pound tight end blocking on this 222-pound pass rusher. It was a mismatch. To make up for the mismatch, they went to an eight-man front. That forced them into man coverage in the secondary. We run both our tight ends on short patterns to the left.

Their corner and free safety were playing man to man on them. Our freshman wide receiver, Darrell Boston, was man to man against the other corner with half the field to work into. He ran a post cut, and we hit him for 79 yards off a play-action pass. When we had Joey Galloway two years ago, people were afraid to play eight-man fronts on us. If they did, they had to play Galloway man to man. There is not one guy in the world who can cover Joey Galloway man to man. There is too much speed out there to defend with one man.

You guys have a wrong impression about Ohio State if you think that all we do is run the ball. We have more guys in the NFL playing wide receiver than anyone. They played at Ohio State, where we run off tackle all day. The point I am trying to make is that we do both: run and pass.

The first play we are going to try to run against you is our bread-and-butter play. It is the 22-23 isolation. The coaching points are pretty simple. We use a man blocking scheme. The onside tackle lead-steps the defensive tackle to the outside and tries to stretch him as wide as possible. The tight ends base up on the defensive end. The onside guard and center double the nose guard to the backside linebacker. The backside guard and tackle turn out on the reduced front to the weak side. The fullback comes off the double team and isolates the inside backer. That is the first play in our offense that we try to run. We work hard on this play in practice. It is a physical play. This is how we toughen our linebackers in the spring. They have to step forward and meet that fullback at the line.

The next play we run is our 24-25 sprint draw. This is the same kind of blocking scheme, except it is run off a play-action passing action. Our tailback takes a little hop and crossover step and gets the ball deeper. The blocking is the same, except the back runs to daylight. We had Orlando Pace playing our left tackle. Because he was such a good run blocker and we ran at him so much, defenses would align four defensive men to his side. It is hard to run the sprint draw toward him when you can't account for all the defenders.

To help us, we ran motion with our flanker to get an extra blocker to that side to account for the fourth

 





Page 33

defender. We could go to two tight ends and get the same results. We are going to use motion, alignment, or formation to handle the fourth defender. We are not going to let the defense dictate to us what we have to do. If we didn't do any of that, we would run the other way.

Last year we didn't have the big, blocking fullback we've had in the past. But you don't have to pancake a linebacker to have an effective block. If the fullback can get a stand off, stay on his feet, and keep them moving, we can live with that block. If he can keep his man from making the tackle, that is fine with us.

The other power play we are going to run is the old double-down and kick-out. If you have a defensive end who is thinking about stopping the quarterback by rushing upfield, we will run a lot of this play. If our fullback can block your end, you can bet on seeing this one. We can get in two tight ends and run it both ways. We have two big, physical tight ends at Ohio State. They are 6'6" and 265 and 250 pounds. We are going to make the small linebacker defend the running game. They had better be able to get off a block. We run our gap scheme on this play. We block down with everyone on the play side. The backside guard pulls into the hole, and the tailback runs off tackle. This play is our 4647.

We coach our defensive ends to cross-arm or wrong-shoulder the kick-out block and make the ball bounce. That is the way just about everyone is playing this type of play. We are getting our ends into 6 and sometimes 7 techniques to keep from getting vertical holes in our defense. That is why this play is hard to run. We run it in passing situations, because we think your defensive end may be thinking about getting upfield to sack the quarterback. If you stop the play, we get the punting team on the field, kick the ball, and play great defense.

Too many times we have too many guys coaching the point of attack. I want guys blocking away from the point of attack. To me those are the important blocks. We want to work on those backside blocks so that plays can break for long gainers. We don't want the single or double. We want a home run.

OK, guys, I think that's it. Does anybody have any questions? We have spring practice in April. We don't have any secrets. Our salvation is you, the high school coaches. Any time we can help you in any way, let us know. Come on up and see us any time. Thanks a lot. 

 





Page 34

George Curry

Developing High School Quarterbacks

Berwick High School, Pennsylvania

Men, it is good to be back in the state of Kentucky. I was in Kentucky exactly 30 years ago. I was down in Lexington. The weather is a little different this time. Thirty years ago it was hotter than hell in Lexington. It is good to be here, and I want to congratulate those of you who are here for coming out to this clinic. I am an administrator in our high school. There is something about football coaches. I am the assistant superintendent in our school system. I have always made other teachers mad in our system. I tell them this: "If you people would work at your job as hard as the football coaches work at their job, no one would be able to touch us as far as education goes." I am sure they do not want to hear that. Men, I mean that. It is a pleasure to be here. I congratulate those of you who have come here to try to learn one or two things to help you become a better football coach.

My topic today is developing high school quarterbacks. Before I talk about the X's and O's, I want you to know that we are not a big high school, but we do play the big high schools. Some of you may say that we get a lot of talent at our school. That is true. But we handpick our quarterback. We have 350 boys in our school. We played the 4A schools. Our whole schedule was against 4A schools. I am not going to bullcrap with you. I will tell you how we approach quarterbacks and how we teach them, and I will take you through the things we do with our quarterbacks. I have four grad assistants who will assist me with the demonstrations.

I have had 13 Division I quarterbacks since 1971.1 think it all has something to do with the way we approach the quarterback position. It has to do with the way we pick the quarterback and how we work with the quarterback.

There are two things that I feel are important in developing the quarterback. Number one is mechanics. You can coach the hell out of mechanics, which we do. The second phase of it is decision making. We have 210 players in our program from 7th grade to 12th grade. Every kid in Berwick plays football unless you are handicapped or an oddball. We tell them we will teach them the quarterback mechanics, and we also do a segment on decision making. I have had some great quarterbacks. I had Powlus, Kelchner, and Orlando, who is now with the Cincinnati Bengals. Those quarterbacks were decision makers. I do not give a darn how far a kid can throw the football. I do not want to hear that. There are kids that look good in the punt, pass, and kick contest. I want the player who can make decisions. A quarterback is a decision maker under duress. When the pressure is on the quarterback, he must make a quick decision. That is the kind of kid we want. We have a way of training those players.

We start with the mechanics, men. We break the mechanics down into two areas: passing mechanics and running mechanics. First, I want to go over the passing mechanics. I will not be able to get very far in the time I have today. I will go until they run me off the stage.

There are three things you must look at when you are trying to develop a quarterback as far as passing mechanics. First is developing the hands. Next is developing from the waist up. Third is developing from the waist down. We take the body and break it down into three areas. We spend a lot of time on the last two-from the waist up and from the waist down.

Some of you may wonder what I am talking about in regard to the hands. I have been in coaching 30 years, and I can tell you that you do not want a kid in your program who has bad hands. We want a player with good hands to handle the ball on every down. That includes games when the field is wet and muddy. How many of you ever looked at the hands of your quarterbacks when you picked them? Have you ever looked at their hands? I have 21 quarterback

 





Page 35

candidates in my junior high program. I look at everything about them. I check their feet and their hands. I check everything except their penis. I check everything about them. I want to know what my quarterback is all about. I want a kid with hands that are big enough so he can handle and throw the football.

We start off with a couple of hand drills. I have three grad assistants here to demonstrate. First, we do the palm drill. We just take the ball and hold it out palm down. Then we do the around-the-waist drill. We just pass the ball around our waist. Next, we do the figure 8 drill.

I got a great drill from Paul Hackett. It simulates the center snap. We want the quarterback to be aware of the snap at all times. When you do these hand drills, you are strengthening your fingers and hands as you grasp the ball. The kids get down in position, bounce the ball off the ground, and grasp it like a snap. This develops the hands. We do a series of these drills every day. We do those drills every day in the weight room. I tell my quarterback and centers that they will always have three balls as long as they are at Berwick High School. They have the two God gave them, and they will have a football in their hand. When our players get through lifting weights, they are going to do those hand drills.

Next, we do a seat drill. Next, we do a one-hand snap and have the quarterback take the snap and try to curl the football. [Demo] Everything is done with the hands. I want to make the kids aware of the hands. If we are playing a game in front of 12,000 people, I do not want the ball on the ground.

In the hand drills we are trying to simulate the snap, and we are trying to strengthen the fingers and hands. We want to make him aware that they are the most critical part of his body.... I better watch what I say because we have a lady in the audience.

Next, we will discuss the area above the waist. The most important thing about passing the football is the release. I will ask those of you in the audience this question: Did anyone ever show you how to throw the football? Raise your hand if someone taught you how to throw the ball. You were lucky! I run quarterback camps. I see over 1000 quarterbacks. I am going to tell you something, men. Those quarterbacks had no idea what I was talking about. Most coaches teach their quarterbacks the X's and O's, and they teach them how to read defenses, but they never show them how to play quarterback.

I want to talk to you about five major points involved in the release of the football. We start on this when the quarterbacks are in the fourth grade. I do not want them to get screwed up when they get to the high school level. It is too late to change their release at that age. If you get them early, you can teach this.

First, we get them in a pre-throwing position. Step one: We want the ball in a trigger position. The ball is between the earlobe and the armpit. I do not want it away from his body; I want it here along and parallel to his chest. We call step one the 
triggerposition.

Step two is to release the ball at the top of his throwing circle. If we took a hula hoop and placed it in front of the player, it would form his throwing circle. We want the player to release the ball at the top of that circle. This is how you develop overhand releases. We do not want a sidearm quarterback. We want him to release it at the top of the throwing circle. You have to have a slight bend in the elbow because you cannot throw the ball with a stiff arm. We do not want a javelin thrower for a quarterback. We want to keep the elbow at a right angle or perhaps at a little less than a right angle. If you raise your arm up and bend your elbow, you will be at the top of your throwing circle.

Step three and step four go together. Step three is transferring the weight from the back foot to the front foot. It is a short step. We do not want to overstride. It is a transfer-of-the-weight step. We want you to get your hips into the step. As you transfer your weight, the ball is going on the top of the throwing circle. You are stepping and hitting that circle all together. As you transfer the weight, you follow through. You should aim your belly button at your target. I want to bring the off arm down and back. Let me have the GAs demonstrate. Give me two guys over here and two over here. Just play pitch and catch with the ball. Not bad!

 





Page 36

Step five is this. As you throw the football, you bring the ball up to the top of the circle, bring the opposite arm down to break the plane of the back, and transfer the weight. As you bring the off arm down, your index finger is the last finger off the football. That should put your palm out of the way. If I throw the football, my palm should rotate inside and point away from my body. If the palm is turning over to the side where you can see the palm, you are sidearming the ball. That means you are turning your wrist. I promise you, if the index finger is the last finger off the ball, you will not break your wrist. As you come over the top of that circle, it is a perfect overhand throw.

We need to talk about a couple of side points here. Let me review the five major points of throwing a football: trigger position, top of the circle, transferring the weight, elbow breaking the plane of the back, palm turned away with the index finger off the ball.

Now consider a couple of other points. We emphasize this to our quarterbacks: ''We do not want you to patty-cake the football." A lot of quarterbacks pattycake the ball as they are holding it. I see them on TV. They patty-cake from the throwing hand to the offside hand-back and forth. Do you know what you are telling the defense when you patty-cake the football? Here it comes! If you do that against a good defensive secondary, they will pick you to death. You do not pat the football! If I see my quarterback pat the ball, he has to run a 40-yard sprint.

When you are standing up and have the football in the trigger position, it is a short release. Have you ever heard a coach talk about shortening the release of a quarterback? If the football is in the trigger position and you come to the top of the circle, you are shortening the release. One thing I take pride in with our quarterbacks is that they have a short release. They are not going to separate and have a big windup. One of the best releases in football is by Dan Marino.

I videotape all of our quarterbacks on their release. I tape other quarterbacks in college and the NFL as well. I am fascinated with the release of the football. I have the kids sit down and look at their release. I show the quarterbacks on the video in slow motion. Some of my players think they are movie stars. I go over the release with each of them, and then I tell them what they need to work on.

Question: What drills do you use to help with the release? We have a couple of drills that we use that I want to discuss with you. We have a drill that we call the rope drill. [Demo] We have the player bring the ball to the top of his circle and have him stop at that point. We take a rope and tie it over his wrist and have another player hold the rope. We make the quarterback throw the ball over the top of the rope. We want them to pretend the rope is not there. Some kids get obsessed with that rope. We tell them not to worry about it. We want them to throw through the rope. It is not going to hurt anyone. We have taken care of sidearm quarterbacks with this drill. We get some kids who want to come under the rope on their release. The rope drill will help them get the ball to the top of the circle. I have seen other coaches who use a volleyball net to force the quarterback to release the ball over. Some coaches use a goal post for the drill. I use the rope because you get 100 feet of rope for two or three bucks. You can have them work on it in the offseason. This is especially good for your young kids. I love working with those young kids. They will listen to you. They are all eyes. If you can get them going in peewee football, you can teach them good habits.

We put the players in a circle and have them throw the ball over the rope to each other. [Demo] This is a coaching clinic, so we are going to correct the demonstrators. We want the throwing motion to be natural. What we do at our quarterback camp is to tape every kid when he first comes in. I see all kinds of techniques. Then I show them at the end of the camp, so they can see the progress they have made.

Next drill-we need two quarterbacks here and two over on the other side. Now we do the kneel-down drill. We start with one knee up. Everything is going to be the same. The ball is between the earlobe and the armpit. We still stress all of the points in the release. He still has the circle, he still releases, he brings the left or nonthrowing arm down, and he comes off with the index finger last. They play pitch and catch with each other. Next we go to two knees. It is the same

 





Page 37

thing. This time they pick the ball up and follow through. Everything is from the waist up.

Next, we stand up and put the throwing foot forward. If you are right-handed, we want to put the right foot forward. We have them pick the ball up and do the same as before. It is still from the waist up. We do not want them to use their legs yet. We want them to come to the top of the circle and stop. We want them to come over the top.

Let me bring you up to date at this point. There are five points in the release, and I gave you four drills to improve the release. There are other drills you can do. I have a lot of drills to use on this technique. I give our kids only five or six and have them work on those.

Next is 
footwork. 
Now we talk about the quarterback from the waist down. We are assuming your kids will soak all of that in so it becomes a habit with them. They must be able to do the drills naturally. Now footwork. We do what we call 
quarterback agilities. I 
have talked to the best quarterback coaches in the country. When I go to clinics, I look for different ideas. I pick the minds of the people I come in contact with. I want to know everything I can about quarterbacks. I share this with our kids, and I am sure it helps our kids. Paul Hackett will tell you that no matter what, footwork is the most important thing in being a quarterback. If your feet are not in the right position, you are not going to throw the ball properly. He is correct. Some kids have strong arms and throw the ball improperly, but they get away with it on the high school level. When you get up against a good team, they are not going to get away with it. It is just like a boxer. He can throw a good punch if he has a good stance. If you are a quarterback, you can throw a good pass if you are in a good stance and your feet are in the proper position. When quarterbacks are under pressure, they never get in the right throwing position. This is where footwork comes into play.

Here are some of the things we teach our kids. We work hard on this. Let me have our GAs come down here to demonstrate again. The first thing I want our quarterbacks to do is to learn how to run. A lot of kids do not know how to run as quarterbacks. We talk about an armpit-to-armpit motion. We run down the field in about three-quarters speed. As we come down the field, I want the ball to come from one armpit across to the other armpit. We want them to come out nice and easy and transfer the ball back and forth. We want the ball up to the chin. Some players will get a little stiff when they run this drill. We do not want too much sway in the motion. It should just come naturally. It is a natural motion armpit to armpit.

The next thing we teach them is run backward, looking over the nonthrowing shoulder. If they are right handed, they should look over the left shoulder. They are just going to run back, taking a crossover step. As they do this, their eyes are focusing down the field. We want them to run as fast as they can. We do not do the carioca. We do that later. We just want to run backward. Why run that drill? That is a quarterback agility. Some kids cannot turn their head around and move their legs at the same time. It is side vision. This is how I work on the drill. I want the quarterback to see from sideline to sideline. We have them put the chin down so they can see to both sides. Some players cannot put their chin down and run backward. You are coordinating all things together. If you start with the kids in the 7th and 8th grade, you can develop them to do this drill. If you want to develop a quarterback, work on this drill. Don't just tell the kid he is the quarterback and let him go on his own. Work with him. You have to work with them.

After he learns to run backward with his chin down, we have him turn the chin over his shoulder and run backwards. Then we have him turn the chin to the opposite shoulder. Next we work on going back and switching the chin from one side to the other side. As they come backward, I will yell, "switch." They turn from the right side to the left side. They turn the chin and switch. As they are doing this, I want the ball in the trigger position. I want the chin tucked and the head on a swivel. The head stays the same, men. The head stays the same. Now, we do not have a lot of room here, so I will have the GAs switch very fast. [Demo] They must be able to move. They are developing hip flexibility as they switch from one side to the other side. You cannot play football with your butt hole puckered up. You have to be cool and calm.

 





Page 38

We want the ball up high on the drop. If you have the ball down low, you tend to fumble the ball. If the ball is up high, you can tuck it away and run. If you do fumble, you have a chance to recover it.

Next, we go to the carioca drill. Now we cross the front foot over the back foot as we back up. It is front-back, front-back, front-back. This is how we work this drill. We work the drill across the field or along the sidelines. You want the kid to do the drill on a straight line. We want him to come back straight on the line. Do it on the sideline or on a line across the field. We may start out on a walk-through. Then we gradually speed it up. We may go to half speed and then to three-quarters speed. We want it nice and smooth. Your kids will develop agility to do this drill. In a week you would be surprised what the kids can do in this drill. This helps develop the feet.

Next is the backpedal drill. We want them on the front half of their shoes. It is just like a defensive back on the backpedal. Instead of pumping the arms, the quarterback has a football in his arms. We want the weight distributed over the front of the shoes on the toes. We are looking downfield as we backpedal.

We can take a rolled up towel, take the quarterback, and do the side-to-side drill for footwork. We want him to step 1-2-3, 1-2-3, 1-2-3, throw. Next we have the quarterback step forward and then backward. We have him step over the towel and then back over the towel. We have him working his feet over the towel.

I am running short on time, and I want to teach the drop step. We have covered the agilities to improve the quarterback's footwork. We have covered how to improve the upper body. Now we want to go to the drop step. I have not gone into details, and I have not covered the stance and snap. They only gave me one hour to cover this material. Damn, Earl, this is a threeday lecture, and you only gave me one hour. I am going fast because I assume most of you know about the stance and how to take a snap.

Now I want to teach you the proper steps in the drop back. We use a power step and two shuffle steps. We always talk about the power step. When you take the football from the center, the power step is a good drive step. On the first step we want to turn the toes on the lead foot at an angle. We want to turn the toes of the back foot toward the sideline. By pointing the second step toward the sideline, it will help the quarterback stop. I got that from Dave Roberts when he was at Notre Dame. He is now the head coach at Baylor University. The second and third steps should stop the quarterback. The feet are the width of the shoulders. The chin is down so they can see the field. We want to take the power step, then two shuffle steps, and then stop.

I am going to give you two more terms. Once we get back on our drop, we have two kinds of steps. First is the hit, and the second is the hitch. A hit step is one step and throw. When we three- and five-step drop, we want a hit step. That is a 1-2-3 step. That is a hit step. Step and throw. We have the ball up ready to throw. We can move the feet 1, 2, 3, and throw. That is a hit step. If we drop back seven steps, we can use what we call a hitch step. The quarterback can come back, set up, hitch step, step up, and make the throw. Some of the young quarterbacks use the hitch step. Why? Because they are too weak to throw the ball from the deep position. They have to step up to get anything on the ball.

Now, we make all of our quarterbacks lift weights. All of our quarterbacks can bench-press at least 300 pounds. If you are getting hit, you had better have a body that can take it. The stronger the quarterback, the quicker he is. If a kid is quick, he can control his body. You can control your body when you are stronger. You can go one step and two shuffle steps, and that would be the three-step drop. You can go three power steps and two shuffle steps, and that is the five-step drop. The seven-step drop is five power steps and two shuffle steps. We still want to keep in mind trigger position and five major points in the drop.

We run an evasion drill. After we teach them to drop back at five steps, we do what we call a cross drill. They go back five steps and stop, slide to the right, slide all the way back to the left, come back to the middle, then a three-step drop, and throw the ball. [Demo] When do we teach this drill? If the rush comes,

 





Page 39

we want them to have some agility to avoid the rush. We are teaching them to keep their eyes open. When they move, the ball stays in the trigger position. When they take the five-step drop and they see the rush coming up the middle, they step to the side and throw the ball. You must be able to do that. If the rush comes from the other side, they must be able to avoid them and make the play. You can improvise on the drills. We have all kind of drills we use to teach them to evade the rush.

There is a lot to playing quarterback. I have 10 decision-making drills. We use these drills to help them make their decisions. I have four camps in Pennsylvania. We work the hell out of the kids that come to our camp. We charge them $35 for an eight-hour day. I saw in the newspaper where Kerry Collins paid Bill Walsh $40,000 to straighten out his release. Damn, I could have saved him a lot of money. I could have done it for 35 bucks. Let me tell you, there is a lot to being a quarterback. Thank you.

 





Page 40

Gerry Di Nardo

The Role of the Head Coach

Louisiana State University

Thank you, gentlemen. It is great to be back here. We have been traveling, and we have been gone since Sunday morning. I coached at Colorado for nine years, and it was great. We won some football games, and I thought we had a great run. Our staff at Colorado was structured a little differently from my staff at LSU. One of the differences was that not everyone on the staff at CU had a piece of the state to recruit. The recruiting at CU was something special to me. I like to travel the state and meet people. It had an impact on me. When I went to LSU, I divided the state up into nine areas. The year doesn't go by when at least one of our coaches will be in every high school in the state of Louisiana. I have been to every high school in the state. I go every spring. One spring I drove 9000 miles and never left the state. Actually I had a GA driving me, but I was in the car. My experience at CU had a lot to do with that.

I have an appreciation about what you do and how you do it. I don't think it changes much from state to state. I admire what you coaches do. I think the state university has an obligation to the high school coaches in their state. Really, it is an obligation to the high school athletes. That goes for all athletes, not just the ones we recruit.

When I left Colorado, I was the offensive coordinator. We ran what everyone called the "I-bone." I was very much involved with the offense and called the plays. When I went to Vanderbilt, it was my first head coaching experience. It was a lot like a military school. We did things the way the Air Force did. I felt the option offense was the way to go. I felt as if I was an expert with the option offense. We implemented the "I-bone" at Vandy, and I coached it. In fact, the last year I was at Vandy, which was 1994,1 coached the quarterbacks, called the plays, and was the head coach.

When I got to LSU, I didn't want to do that. I thought it would hurt us in recruiting, and it wasn't the way we could do our best. Our personnel didn't fit the option, so I got away from it. The reason I mention that is because of what I am going to talk about today. I am going to talk about what I do. It is not the X's and O's part. I am pretty far removed from that part of the game. I've gone from one extreme to the other. Two years ago I was coaching the quarterbacks and calling the plays. Now my job is much different. What I want to present to you today is how I've spent my year.

I could stand up here and be an expert on LSU's offense. I couldn't stand up here and tell you about our defense. I could tell you what we are doing. But if I had to talk on the offense or defense, I would have brought along our two coordinators. What I want to share with you is what I think I am an expert on. I want to tell you how I motivate my staff and players. I'm glad my topic in the program is Tiger football. I spoke at the Nike Clinic in Dallas, and my topic was defensive football. I never intended to speak on that subject. That was a joke. We gave up a bunch of points against Florida the week they called to get my topic. I was joking when I said defense. But, what I'm going to talk about today is what I do.

I'm going to take you through the yearly calendar. I'm going to start in July when the staff come back and start to get together in preparation for the season. I believe that as a head coach I have an obligation and responsibility to set the pace, tempo, and motivate the rest of the coaches. They all generally go on vacation in July. When they come back, I give them a book. The book has everything we are going to do between the first meeting and when our players return. That is a 10- to 14-day period. I always try to pick out some kind of theme that applies to the upcoming season.

When we took over at LSU, they had lost for six straight years. We took over in 1995 and won seven games and went to a bowl game. Everyone thought the program was turning around. This was not the coaches

 





Page 41

and players, but the fans. But the program really wasn't. My chief concern was how to keep the momentum going. That was the theme I brought to the coaching staff. If you are trying to turn a losing program around, your real problems come the second year, after having some success the first year.

That first year when you take over as a coach, everyone really buys into what you are preaching. You sell the team and that the team is the most important thing. In the second year, I believe that human nature takes over, and the team starts to get more individualistic. Some guys began to think that ''the team is winning, but I'm not playing." Those selfish attitudes can crop up and start to wear on a team. Going into the 1996 season, I studied a lot of second-year programs. I got a lot of media guides of all the major colleges through the years. I looked at the coaching changes. I looked at the first year where they had some success, but the second year was worse than the first. I was really concerned about that.

I think that there is a psychology about being involved in a second-year program. I want to share some of my staff agenda with you. I'm not going to cover all of it, but there are some highlights. I think these things are critical to our program. The first thing on the agenda is a senior barbecue. I'll come back to this a little later, because I think there is so much to be said about senior leadership, how you lead them, and how you teach them to lead. The first year that I was head coach at LSU, I found myself constantly talking about leadership. While you are traveling, you have a lot of time to think. I wondered how much these kids knew about leadership. I assumed that someone had taught them how to be a leader. You can't do that.

I have a player council. I started this when I was at Vanderbilt. I have two players from each class. I have the classes vote for them privately; I'm not there when they vote. I meet with the council periodically. Sometimes I take them to breakfast, and sometimes we meet in my office. They have a voice, but they don't have authority. They don't tell me who to play, what to run, or how to run the team. They are going to tell me things that the head coach would have no way of knowing. I have no idea what it is like being a student at LSU. I have no way of knowing what is served at the bars or how many drugs are around. I make a decision to learn about it or not to learn about it.

Every other week we meet as a council. I always go with an agenda. There are certain things I want to know about, so I ask them. They tell me. After that I turn the floor over to them. They have come up with some good things. If you let them talk, they will make your program better. I always ask our strength coach who the five best and five worst players are in the weight room. I like to go into camp knowing who has had a good summer and who has had a bad one. As it worked out, the five best in August in weights had outstanding years for us. Of the five worst, one left the team before camp, one didn't finish the season, one I threw off the team, and two didn't play at all. I was alerted before the season started that these guys weren't going to come into camp and all of a sudden have great work ethics.

After we go through personnel as a staff, we go to organization. I think it is tremendously important that you go through organization with your coaches. I think you have an obligation to them to discuss it. I'm going into my seventh year as a head coach. I've got five guys on my staff who have been with me for those seven years. There are certain things that shouldn't take the amount of time that it took seven years ago. I come from the Michigan school, because I was with Coach Bill McCartney for nine years. If you didn't get there at 4:30 in the morning and leave at midnight, you were a lousy coach. There is something to seeing how you are using your time. It is something for a new staff member, his wife, and his family not to be surprised by how much he is going to work or how little he is going to work. I think you owe that to your staff. I've always reviewed with them our work week and our work format.

I schedule situation scrimmages, such as goal line, short yardage, our pass protection, and other problems we are having. I took the things that hurt us the most during the season in 1995 as the guidelines for the situation scrimmages. We were very average in short yardage, goal line, throwing the ball when we had pressure on us, and the two-minute drill. Rather than get into a big philosophical discussion with the

 





Page 42

offensive and defensive coordinators, I went into the meeting and told them we were going to situation-scrimmage those thing early in camp. Now, the coordinators know these things are coming the first or second day we are in camp, and they can prepare their practice schedules accordingly. It is not up for discussion. This is what we are going to do.

The next thing is the "iso-camera." This is something we use for motivation. It is a little off the wall. At Vanderbilt, the first year we played Alabama, we played absolutely awfully. When the captain of the team came to see me, I asked him why we played so awfully. Alabama may have been a better team, but we played just terribly. He told me that most of the team was scared. I couldn't believe that. I said, "Most of our guys were afraid?" He said most of the people in our program were afraid of Alabama. I didn't know what to do. We played a couple of other games, and then came Auburn. That year Auburn was as physical and as tough as any team I've ever seen. They would knock you sideways. I said to myself, ''If we were afraid of Alabama, what would we do against Auburn? This could be awful." When I was in the hotel before the game, I had an idea. We were taking a wide-angle shot, an end-zone shot, and a tight sideline shot. I got to thinking about this iso-camera. I got in front of the team and talked about being intimidated. I told them I had written out a script from 1 through 75. Every play we are out there, that camera is going to be focused on one person. If you chicken out at all, I'm going to show this video and show everybody that you are a chicken. We played like hell. We got beat 24-22 and missed a field goal on the last play of the game for the win. That gave our kids great confidence. From that time on, it became part of our program.

What we do now in practice is this. I've got a walkie-talkie on my belt. I can talk to the cameraman in the tower. I can tell him who to zoom in on. It doesn't matter whether we shot anybody or not. All they have to do is to believe the camera is on them.

We have drug testing. I don't know how you feel about it, but I think it is something we have to address. We address drug abuse a lot and test a lot. We want to help our kids to stay clean. It may remind you of police-state tactics, but I think it is the best thing for them. When they get older, they understand why we do it. I think that as educators and coaches we have to get involved in this. How you would do something like this is beyond me, but we all have to get involved.

We always study our first four opponents in the summer. It wouldn't matter to me what level I was coaching on. I would assign one assistant coach on each side of the ball to study one of your first four opponents. That would become his team. He would be responsible for putting together a book on that team. We open up the season with Texas, El Paso, at home. We've never played them before. The second game is Mississippi State at State. We play them every year. Then we play Auburn at home. We play them every year. We have a coach on each side of the ball to study those opponents. It gives one guy a lot of responsibility. He can spend a lot of time on that team so that everyone doesn't have to. If you have Mississippi State or Auburn, who we play year in and year out, they become experts on them. You will know much more about your first two or three opponents if you have studied them all summer. I think this gives you an edge at any level. You don't have that time to do it once your regular season starts.

Every coach in our program has to have a position training tape. When the defensive line coach talks to his players about a 1 or 3 technique, he has some game video cut-ups. On these videos are the things he has been talking about. They show our players using the proper techniques in a game situation. We meet with our kids for an hour. If you are on our staff, you better be a good teacher. You have to be entertaining and well prepared. I don't want them lecturing to a group for an hour. I want to see excellent tapes. I don't want to see some two-year-old videos. Position tapes are ways that coaches can study the game. I want my coaches to be students of the game.

I want our coordinators to put any gadget play in early. I tell our offensive coordinator that I want a reverse or throw-back in the first two or three days of camp. I want to do it first to the defense and second so our offense can execute it.

 





Page 43

One neglected area of offensive football is 
cadence. I 
don't think any of us change up enough. I have charted it. I have had a GA in charge of it. I think that as offensive coaches we get into a rut. I am willing to bet that 95 percent of the people in this room, myself included, do not use the cadence to our advantage. The thing in college football today is pressure defense. With all the pressure defenses we are seeing, for our offensive football team not to change the cadence is not smart. The problem is when you decide to try to change your cadence. You go out to practice, and your kids are jumping offside every time you try to change. You yell at the kids, get frustrated, and stop trying to do it. You have to make a commitment to it. We try to commit to it every year, get frustrated, and stop. Each year the point keeps getting pushed back further.

Our coaches are visiting other programs this week. We have coaches going to Florida State. We have some in Indianapolis to visit the Colts organization. There are some at North Carolina. All our coaches at LSU are out visiting this weekend. They will all come back with a million ideas. They all will want to put something in. I won't let them put anything in until they take something out. You can't keep adding on to football. It is not fair to the kids. We don't have them all day long. If you follow the rule, you only have 17 hours a week. You have to count three hours for the game. It all has to do with time. If you go to a pro camp, they all have the great big packages. They also have the kids all day long. We don't have that. I give our coaches a free rein. If they want to add something, they have to take something out.

In our staff agenda, which we started in July, one coach is responsible for the opponent's cadence. I assign a coachand it doesn't have to be a defensive coachto come up with our opponent's cadence. We are not allowed to live-scout any more, but I still want to know that cadence. I have a list of our opponents up on the wall. I put the name of one of our assistants next to each team. I don't care how they get it. I know the rules, but come that assistant's week, he better have the cadence, or he better make something up and hope I don't know it. When you play the same teams year in and year out, you make notes on the cadence. That is part of the way to get prepared on defense. The next thing is tackling drills. We practice tackling every day. I remember listening to Bo Schembechler when I was an assistant coach. He told the story about this defensive coordinator who was delivering a lecture on defense. He had the more up-to-date coverages, knew all the buzzwords about defense, and really looked the role. At the end of his presentation, a guy in the back asked a question. He said that he was impressed by the presentation but noticed that his team was last in the conference on defense. His comment was that they just couldn't tackle. Well that is defense. Defense is about tackling. If we don't have time to practice tackling, we have too big a game plan.

Let me cover the kicking game very quickly. Your approach to the kicking game is very important. There is a principle in leadership called 
ownership. 
I think that head coaches are the owners, and assistant coaches are the renters. I remember when I rented an apartment when I couldn't afford a house. If I put a dent in the wall, all I did was pay a $10 damage fee. When I bought my first house, if someone brushed against the wall, I went bananas because I owned the house. Who owns and who cares about the kicking game? High-profile, well-paid assistant coaches put their names on offenses and defenses. But who the hell owns the kicking game? I don't believe you just go out there and coach the kicker. Someone has to own the team. They have to be responsible for that team. It could be a special-teams coach. Ownership brings more accountability.

In the fall I give every assistant a sheet that details his total responsibility to the team. Every guy on the staff knows every responsibility he has. If a coach is responsible for the field equipment, I don't want any surprises come practice time.

I am in charge of squad letters. If you don't write letters to your squad, you're missing a chance to motivate. I write a letter a week to our team, and I write a personal note on the bottom of that letter. I have our coaches write once a week to our kids. I get a copy of that letter before it goes out. When our players get home, they have a letter from me and one from an assistant coach. I give my coaches the right

 





Page 44

to be creative and talk about what they want to. It is a great motivational tool.

In August every coach on the staff will give a clinic to the rest of the staff in our first meeting. I don't care how long we have been together as a staff. This is an old Michigan tradition. Hal Hunter is our offensive line coach. He will deliver a clinic to the staff as if he were talking to his players. He has 60 minutes to show everyone on that staff what he is going to show his position group. During that 60 minutes he will show us his training video. Every teacher has a different teaching method. That is all coaching is. Our coaches expose the methods they use to the rest of the staff. They can learn from each other different methods of teaching. When a guy joins your staff, it sets a standard for him. If I have a new guy on my staff, I never let him go first. We usually make him go last. These are grown men, professionals. After eight of those assistant coaches have gone and it becomes the new guy's time, he is puckered. He wants to do a good job. He wants to be a head coach, just like those other guys. There is pressure, but it is a great experience. When was the last time you hired a coach and didn't hear him teach? We do it all the time. We have no idea what he is telling those players. I get around now since I don't coach a position. I can tell whether a guy is doing a good job or not. I can tell if he is honest with them, whether they are listening, and if he is going too long.

I have a senior barbecue at my house for our seniors. This is a leadership clinic for them. I want to talk to them about how they see their leadership roles on the team.

The last game I coached at Vandy we lost 55-0. The reason I got the LSU job was no one else would take it. It got late, and they had to give it to somebody. Six days before we played Texas A&M in 1995, we were going to practice on Sunday. Outside our locker room I saw this kid standing on the back of a pickup truck, and everyone else was standing around listening to him. I knew I had a problem. I called a team meeting and put up the practice schedule, which included a goal-line scrimmage because we weren't very good at it. They didn't think a scrimmage six days before a game was a good idea. They hadn't got to know me. At the end of the meeting, I asked if there were any questions. The captain stood up and told me they were not going to practice. They said I was working them too hard. I told them that this was LSU property. If they didn't want to practice, they could get the hell out of my room. They stayed, and we went out to practice. We had a very average practice. That night the captain was in my office. He is a great kid who is playing pro ball now. He told me he didn't agree with the demands, but he thought it was his job to represent their wishes. I told him that was not leadership. Leadership is not counting hands to see who wants to practice easy.

I teach a leadership class. I have 11 guys in it. I bring in other students, legislators, pro coaches, and others into the class to explain their definition of leadership. I don't keep them much longer than 40 minutes. The one thing I stress is, however you start out to lead, that is the way you finish. If you start out as a rah-rah guy on the first day, I better hear from you at midseason. Don't think you can change. Don't think when things go bad you can disappear. Give a guy a chance to not be a holler guy if he doesn't want to holler. Tell the rah-rah guy that if that is his style, that will be his style all year.

I give my kids a handout with typical problems. These are the things I want to discuss. Night activity is a dangerous time for athletes. I don't know where you went to college, but where I went, Thursday night was "get drunk night." If you get drunk two nights before you play Alabama, you will get your ass kicked. That is pretty simple. I'm not going to the bars to throw you out. Who is going to keep everyone out of the bars? Seniors have to make a decision about whether we are going out or not. They need to hold the rest of the team accountable. I make them tell me what they decided.

I don't believe in hazing of the freshmen. We are all one team, and there is no place for that type of thinking. The hazing has a tendency to separate and divide the team concept. I don't allow it to go on in our program.

I never liked the idea of game captains. That is the way it was done before. We have a lot of Louisiana guys on our squad. It has been an incredible honor to play in Tiger Stadium. But it was a bigger honor to serve as

 





Page 45

game captain in Tiger Stadium. I recognized that was another motivating tool, so I went along with that idea.

Always evaluate senior leadership on specific criteria. I started to develop a small questionnaire to evaluate leadership. Is the kid a starter? Is he well liked? Does he practice well? How did he report to camp? Did he pass the conditioning test? I think he should have a personal record on his conditioning test. Why wouldn't a senior in his last year with a chance to play for his last SEC championship have a personal record? The 1997 season will be a challenge if most of our seniors who are coming back and going to play come back in worse condition than when they were juniors. I have a problem if that happens. I better address that right away.

Let me cover these thoughts quickly before I run out of time: (1) Always play someone if you promised to. At Colorado, one of our coaches promised a kid that he would play in the game regardless of what the score was. We said he was going to play, and we didn't play him. (2) Always be yourself. (3) Always teach team. (4) Try to be the most physical team. (5) Work on blocking and tackling. (6) Be able to run the football. (7) Practice situations. (8) Always videotape practices and games.

The last thing I have is "always script practice." Thursday was the toughest day for me as offensive coordinator when I was at Colorado. That was the short practice. I couldn't practice every situation. I made up a form that has every possible situation. It has 100 plays on it. It has everything that could possibly happen to you during the course of a gameor for a season, for that matter. Most of the plays didn't have to be rehearsed on Thursday. All it did was to organize my thinking. That is so that if I need a certain situation to go over on Thursday, I have it. The other type of script was the game rehearsal we had before the first game. It had a kickoff after a safety and overtime games. We used that every preseason, right before the opening game.

I believe that when we play flat, that is my fault. I take that responsibility. I hold the offensive coordinator responsible for the offense and the defensive coordinator responsible for the defense. I hold myself responsible for the emotion and motivation of our team.

On Sunday I meet with the team and review their play in the game. I meet with each unit and tell them what they did badly first and what they did well second. I meet with the offense, defense, and kicking team. I know everything that is going on. I just met with their coaches, and they told me what was going on. Having the presence of the head coach and the knowledge gives them confidence.

Monday is an off day for us. Tuesday is our scouting-report day. We try to be as honest as we can. We don't run guys down who our people will be playing against. We don't overrate the team. This is a motivating atmosphere. We are trying to get our people ready to play.

On Wednesday I meet with them as a team. We come together after practice, and I'll say something to them. On Thursday I have a prepractice meeting to go over the practice plan. There is no screwing around on Thursday. We are not a loosey-goosey outfit. We want no one who is not going to play grab-assing around. You can have as much fun as you want on Tuesday or Wednesday, but Thursday is a very serious day. After practice I meet with the team without any assistants there. It is strictly motivation about the game. On Friday we are very unemotional and technical. I want every coach to go through the game plan. I want it to be really focused, with absolutely no screwing around. I didn't let two kids play this year because they were a minute late. We won both games. Saturday is game day. We generally play at night. I get my last chance at the team in the cafeteria before we eat the pregame meal.

Gentlemen, I got about half way through, but I have to catch a flight. I enjoyed saying hello to some of you guys who came by. I wish I could stick around. It was great being here, and I hope everyone has a great season. Thanks.

 





Page 46

Jim Donnan

The Passing Attack In The Red Zone

University Of Georgia

When you go to a clinic, it is good to talk to other people and share ideas. It is good to talk about your program and their program as far as what is going on. Most of you have a good idea of what you want to do on both sides of the ball, or you would not be coaching. It is good to have a good sense of direction.

It is also important to know that the game changes, and you have to keep up with what happens in football. You have to keep up with the rule changes and use them to your advantage. I think it is important as a coach, regardless of the level you are on, to be objective and coachable, to branch out and get new ideas.

I am not the type of coach who would get up here and preach to you. There are a lot of things we can do better. Last year we were not a very good football team. We had flashes of being a good team, but when you make changes in football, it is difficult to get things across. Hopefully, we will be able to do a better job next year. You must be able to articulate what you are doing to the players. The bottom line is, How are they going to do when the band plays on Friday night at 7:30? Basically, it gets down to what they can handle. As college coaches, we spend a lot of time watching other schools. We have two of the top teams in the country in our division of our league. We have some lofty goals to reach just to be competitive. I am sure it is the same for you.

The point I want to make is this. We want to analyze our past season. That is what we are doing now. We know what won for us last season. We also know what got us beat. As situations develop during the course of the season, the quicker your team learns how to adjust to those situations that won for you and got you beat, the better a team you will be next year. It does not matter if you have a lot of players back or not. You can reflect to them and point out what happened and how the team reacted to those situations.

For instance, our team handled overtime situations very well. They showed a lot of poise and did a great job of handling what we needed to do to win the game. Ten of the 11 games we played last year were decided in the last five minutes. This includes the Tennessee game, where we were inside their 20-yard line and were behind 10 points. We have a lot of games that are decided by certain situations and how the team reacts to them. I want to make sure that I emphasize to you, as teachers of the game and coaches, that we are dealing with kids a lot younger than we are.

One thing I learned when I was coaching at Oklahoma with Barry Switzer was the mind-set of the players. No matter what happened in those situations, we expected to win. We counted on that. When I took over at Marshall University, it was the same. I am not sure that our kids have that mind-set yet. A lot of that is based on success or the lack of success. Some of the other teams we played, we felt, had an idea that they were going to lose to us. When it starts getting tough, teams are going to lose games more than they are going to win games. Teams start making mistakes, and it causes them to lose.

From a skilled level, it does not matter if you want to run a three-back set, a two-back set, or a one-back set. It is immaterial to me today. Formations do not win for you. What wins for you are the players. Some of you are really good coaches one year, and the next year you are not quite as good because you do not have those good players. Sometimes egos get in the way.

You need to develop something good, regardless of what you do. You have to be able to take advantage of what the defense gives you. In the collegiate game today, with all of the multiple defenses and fronts and defensive coverages, it becomes a real guessing game for us. I do not like to guess. On any decision I have to make, I want to eliminate as many risks as I can in trying to be successful. If you are spending a lot of time

 





Page 47

trying to outguess your opponent, it can get in your way a lot of the time.

I know this: "Coaching will not win for you, but it will get you beat." That is a profound statement. What I am saying is this. If we are playing against Tom Osborne or Jimmy Johnson or whoever, we better be ready to play. I will assure you that their team will be ready. We are not going to outcoach them. Their players will be just as well prepared as our players. But if you do not do everything they do, then that will get you beat.

Some coaches think they can come up with magic on Friday night at 7:30 that is going to make them win. I do not believe that is going to happen too often. Basically, you will have to make a few adjustments along the way. The teams that are constantly changing and trying to get a sense of direction and do not have a feel for what they are doing have players who do not have a lot of confidence about what is going on.
