If you change what you are doing every Monday, it becomes hard to believe in. Have a system and believe in it and make it work.

I have been involved with some good quarterbacks, and I have been involved with staff members who helped make them good quarterbacks. I really believe that one of the real key issues, beside the fact that they had good arm strength, is their decision-making ability. It is not just having the ability to do the right thing, but making sure they do not do the wrong thing. A lot of times, players with pressure on them cannot react properly. A lot of times in high school, the best athlete in the school is the quarterback. You need a player who the players have a lot of confidence in.

I do want to cover a couple of other things about handling your quarterback. I think it is critical that you coach all of them, regardless of age. The reason I say that is because one of these days that freshman is going to be a senior. You have to find time to coach that player as much as you do the other players. That is hard to do, I will promise you. Too many times coaches get involved with play polishing, and they are only involved with one group. I would rather have four groups alternating and coach them on the run than have three of the teams just standing and watching everyone else. When they do not know what to do, it is hard to get much accomplished. I think it is important to coach them all and to meet with them all. Have them in your meetings. Learn what it takes to make them tick.

You cannot find out what a player can do by talking to him on Friday night if you do not talk to him all week. We have a rule that if we are in a staff meeting and a player comes over to see a coach, we are going to leave the meeting and talk with him. It does not matter. I know what I can do. You had better make sure the quarterback can accomplish what you want. Over the years, the thing that has been successful for us has been the fact that we have given a lot of meeting time and practice time during the individual period to everyone. Now, you get into the team situation, and it is different. You are not going to be able to work three and four groups. But it is important for players to understand your system and to come along. You would be surprised at how some of these men can play when they are given the chance. My philosophy is to coach them all. Give them all a chance to learn what is going on with the individuals.

What we are going to talk about today is "attacking the red zone." We are referring to the area from the 20-yard line to the goal line going in. When you get the ball inside the 20-yard line by running the ball, it is hard to get away from what you are doing, and they start throwing it. The same is true if you pass the ball down to the 20-yard line and then start running the ball. Our basic philosophy is this. Whatever got us down there is probably what we are going to continue with. If we are playing a team that has been knocking the crap out of us and then we get down to the 20-yard line, we are not going to spend a lot of time trying to figure out which running plays will go. We know that all of a sudden we are not going to become Ben-Hur. Do you know what I am saying? By the same token, if we get down there by throwing the ball, we are not going to change just to be changing.

Regardless of the system you use, there are a few obvious things you need to consider inside the 20-yard ine. The other team is going to start taking more chances. They have not stopped you in 80 yards; what

 





Page 48

makes you think they are going to stop you now? The defense is going to take more risk than before. You must be prepared for these risks. The better you can take care of those risks, the better it is for your offense. We sell our receivers, just like everyone else, that we play all year to get a one-on-one situation. "Now you are going to have it for sure."

The difference in college football now and, say, five years ago is the fact that teams now are playing a lot more press coverage. They are playing more of a sic 'em type defense than it used to be. An example of that is Nebraska. Two of the last three years, they have won the national championship. They went from the completely zone, high-tech read, straight-up eagle defense to now a 4-3 defense with the Mike and Will linebackers walked up tight. They move them in and out. They bump and run on the corners. It is like everything in football. When you win as Nebraska has, everyone starts copying them. A lot of other teams are using what Nebraska does. You can run that defense if you have corners outside that can cover one on one on the perimeter.

We feel that we know what the defense is going to do once we get to the red zone now. Conversely, some teams have gotten to the point where they play more zone coverage in the red zone. They just make you beat them. If you have not scored a lot of points on them, they may play seven across on 3rd and 5 on the 5-yard line and rush four men. All of that is hypothetical that we could get into. You could say what you run, and I could tell you what I run. I am not into those aspects. I am not going to get into that today. All I am going to talk about is some philosophy of using some routes that are multifaceted that will take care of some different things.

We want to make sure our quarterback and receivers are on the same page. Our quarterback coach and receivers coach work a lot of time together against air. We are going to have to read what the defense is doing. You cannot come in after playing in front of 87,000 fans and have the receiver say, "I thought the free safety was playing cover two." The quarterback says, "I thought he was playing bump and run, man." That is when everyone wants to know who is the damn coach? That is not going to get the job done. You are looking for results. The way you do that is to coach them up hard, and basically it gets down to this: Estimate the defense's intent. What can he play from the alignment he is in? You hear a lot about teams disguising their coverage. Let me tell you something. When the band is playing on Friday night and the offense is knocking on the goal line, they are not going to be disguising anything. The are going to be covering as best as they can. If they can do a lot of fancy things on the goal line, that is great for them. To me this is just a lot of clinic talk. ''I want you to line up in an outside shade, then jump inside, and on the snap I want you to come up, then make sure you play cover two." Hey, do you think that kid is thinking about all of that? He is thinking about that war daddy running right past his butt. You have to be careful about giving the other team too much credit. The question we want to ask is this: What can they do from the way they line up? We try to react from that point on.

What we would like to do, to keep from adjusting any more than we have to, is to eliminate a possible bad play. It is like I said about taking a risk. We want to make sure we are on the same page.

The bottom line would be for you to come to our practices this spring, and we could show you what we do. We run what we call a "man-zone read." If they are playing man coverage, we are going to do 
this. 
If they are playing zone coverage, we are going to do 
that. 
This is particular with the inside receiver, whether it is the tight end in the two-wide-receiver offense or the slot in a three-wide-receiver set. We are going to run some type of read route based on the coverage. If the defense is in a three-deep coverage and we have a man coming down the field, he will continue to try to split the middle to work on that area. If it is against a three-deep and the safety is deep, the receiver will hook up. We do not want to run into a three-deep.

If the receiver is running what we call a shallow route, which is a short crossing route, and he sees man coverage, he drives up the field one more step, he gets

 





Page 49

the defender on his hip pocket, and he keeps coming on across. If he sees the zone, he will just hook up. We do not want to leave an area where we are open just to find another open spot. This is very easy to do, as long as you do not have a lot of people doing it. You do not need two or three players adjusting the routes all of the time. We feel that with combination coverage that we get with all of the different reads we see, we need someone working some type of man-zone read all of the time. It may be the receiver in the middle; it may be the running back or a lot of other people.

I do not think it makes any difference what we call these plays, because I do not have time to go over our numbering system except to tell you this. Our first digit is to the single-receiver side on the weak side. Our second digit is the middle receiver, and the third digit is for the strong-side receiver. We add a word for flare control to both sides. The first thing we say in our system is the protection. The reason we do thisregardless of the formation we are in or the grouping we have, as we could have two, three, or four wide receiversis because it gives us a multiple offense. All the receiver needs to know is whether he is the first, second, or third number and the flare control word.

I am going to cover a basic route that anyone here could run, regardless of the type of formation you base out of. What we are doing here is giving the outside receivers the ability to convert their routes. If we face some type of soft zone coverage, we are going to run a five-step hitch-type route. We use three-step protection with the quarterback. He is going to know that he has the inside people taken care of if they have underneath coverage on the outside men. It is obvious that if the corner comes up and plays bump and run on the outside receiver, he will convert his route to the fade route. I know you got to see Florida play a lot this year. Danny Wuerffel was the best I have seen at throwing the fade route. They had great receivers and a large number of completions on that play. Everyone played the receivers, hoping that Danny was going to have a bad day. He only had one bad day, and that was when Florida State sacked him so much.

The fade route is tough to complete. All things must go well for the play to work. You can have the perfect pass and have the defensive back knock it down. As our quarterback comes out of the huddle, the first thing he does is to figure out to which side he has the least amount of coverage.

FLEX RIGHTQUICK 181 STOP/BLOCK

The quarterback reads and works the strong side primarily. If you get straight man, the "80" will have a run-through, and the outside routes will convert to fade routes.

We would like to throw the hitch route here if we can. If we feel we have a mismatch on the outside and they are playing bump and run, then we will throw the fade route. The idea is this: If we are in the plus-20 area, we have a chance to have a completion, avoid the sack, and make some yardage.

Usually when we run this play, it will be packaged with some type of run play. Most of you are familiar with the outside zone play. Some of you may call it the stretch play. Because we are in the one-back set, we may call "check slant or quick pass." If the defense walks up tight on the receivers, plays tight, and plays cover two, we may check off and run the slant play. We may just run the draw play. If they take the linebacker outside, we may run the draw. It depends on the basic philosophy, front-wise.

Most of the coaches here are knowledgeable. It does not make any difference if you are knowledgeable or not, the bottom line is win or lose. This is what you have to do. Coaches will say that they can only run one snap count, and we can't check plays off. Basically, that is what you do not want to do. If you

 





Page 50

want to do it, it is very simple to do it. I teach this to the quarterbacks in our camps. It is just a matter of telling the quarterback whether we are going to run the sweep to the right side; if the defense has more people on that side than we do, we want to run the play to the left side. If the defense has more people on the left side, we want to run it to the right side. That is basically the way we do it. We may call "check 48-49." We come up to the line and run it either way we want to.

Another thing I think you can help your quarterback with is this: Most quarterbacks do not have a good system of coming up to the line of scrimmage. They get in the huddle and call the play: "46 on 2; ready, break." The quarterback comes out of the huddle with his head down and walks up to the line that way. They come up to the line and do not get their head up. What does that do for the other team? That quarterback does not show much confidence. We want the quarterback to get a definite process of looking weak to strong, strong to weak, or left to right. We see some quarterbacks break the huddle and start looking toward the area where they are going to throw the ball on each play. We want a definite process of coming to the line.

This is a simple play, but if you can package it with a running play, it not only gives you a chance to be an aggressive football team, but it is a high-percentage throw for the quarterback. It is easy to complete if the defense gives you some zone-type look. If it is two-deep or bump and run, we convert the outside receivers to the fade route.

A lot of teams that you see playing two-deep coverage are going to sink their corners pretty well. They know they have the safeties behind them. They will let you throw the ball in front of them and then break on the football. Teams used to try to jam the wide receivers inside. If the defense gives us a cushion, we will still try to throw the hitch route inside.

I want to talk about another route that we got from the pros a couple of years ago. The reason we like this play is this: On the same routes that we run off our five steps, we wanted to be able to protect the quarterback against a good defensive front and still be able to run our best routes. We take the double-in; some people call it a dig route, but we are running it off the three-step drop. You could run this off any formation. We are going to slide our protection, so we could take care of anything on the weak side. As long as the defense rushes seven men, we are OK. We have everyone assigned to someone.

TWINS RIGHTQUICK-SLIDE ROUTE 166 BLACK/SEAM

On the weak side we are going to run the same route that we ran on 181. We run the hitch on the back side. If they play two-deep, we are going to convert it to a fade route. Otherwise, we have a cinch hitch route that we can throw if the quarterback comes out and sees three-deep and does not like what he sees to the field side. That is not tough. He is going to run his three-step drop.

One thing we try to do with our quarterbacks when we are throwing to the left is this: We come out and put a lot of weight on the left instep. The reason for that is because we want the knees in a little. When you turn your body, you have a good pivot on the first step. When we take the second step, we start the pivot right now and then we throw the ball. We do not like to go back, 1, 2, 3, and then turn. We like to turn on the second step when we are throwing to the left.

We want the quarterback to find the throwing lane. He throws to the backside number. The bigger target you aim at, the more you are going to miss. We like to aim at a small target. If we are throwing an inside route, we like to throw at the receiver's teeth. If we miss his teeth, we hope to hit his head. If you can hit him in the head, you have a lot that can happen. Pick small targets. Don't just say, "Hit him in the numbers."

 





Page 51

One thing that irritates me is to have a player drop the pass and have a coach yell, ''Catch the ball." Why tell him that? 
Why 
did he drop the pass? Not every good athlete in your program has good hands to start out with. You have to work on their hands. Work on their depth perception. One of the first things you do is to take them to the optometrist. Many kids have poor eyes and have not had their vision checked. Work with them on depth perception. One thing you can do is to work with them on their hands. Work with them individually. A lot of kids lack confidence so much that they get nervous when they are in a group setting and drop a lot of passes. If you have a really good athlete who needs to work on his hands, then get him out on the field and work with him individually. Move the ball around, and make him catch the ball. Teach him to use his hands in relation to his eyes.

On the strong side against the trips look, very seldom do we get a team that will bring a linebacker outside and up close and play us with two deep. If they do, we are going to run the stretch play. If they stay in a conventional look, they will probably be in a threedeep look. This is a simple read.

We have more trouble throwing a slant route than we do the in route. If you just think about it, you will see that it makes sense. If you look at the man coming inside and squaring his body up as compared to a receiver coming in on the slant at a sharp angle, it is easier to throw the inroute. What are we looking for? The high-percentage completions! Teams that throw the ball had better be able to complete 60 percent of them. If you throw the ball a lot, you had better be able to complete 65 percent of the passes. There is not anyone in here, even if you have an average team, who cannot average 4 yards per snap.

The point here is this: If you have a strong-side cover three, you need to send the tight end down the middle. If they are playing cover two, we want the tight end to keep the safeties back deep. Also, we want to keep the linebacker inside where we can do something outside.

Let me talk about cover three. We always look at the defenders' shoulders. Their shoulders will basically tell you what they are going to do. The more they open the shoulders, or the more they close their shoulders, you know where they are going. Look to see what the shoulders are telling you. We are going to take a threestep drop, and we are going to read the strong safety and see what he does. If he hangs back, we have a good split between the two wide receivers. They are running 6 yards apart, and they are running the in route. We throw the ball to the outside receiver. If he opens toward the field, we have a good throwing lane between the strong safety and the inside linebacker, unless they happen to drop the defensive end. Usually when they are playing cover three, the end on the other side is dropping. There is always something they can do differently, I promise you.

This is a good route for you if you are a team that throws a lot of square-ins to your outside receiver down the field and you are having problems with your protection. You can throw this as a three-step drop. You could run this out of three wide receivers and two backs. The inside receiver runs a hitch, and the outside man runs the square-in.

I want to move on to another route that we have had a lot of success with. The reason we have had success is this: What happens when you go up against a threedeep team and you are running three wide receivers? We would like to get four vertical on them. If they are in a three-deep and we send four men vertical, we end up with a two-on-one at one point. If we are running all four on what we call nine routes, we will be sending our receivers all the way down the field. If the free safety cheats to the tight-end side, we hit the split end. If he cheats to the split-end side, we hit the tight end.

FLEX RIGHTQUICK SLIDE LEFT 292 SEAM/BLOCK

 





Page 52

The quarterback reads the strong side first. He reads the defender over number 2. We run the slant or seam routes. What happens is that everyone will show you cover three and then rotate to cover two. You have four men going vertical, and the defense is stacked back there against you. This route was put in to use against teams that like to play cover two against four vertical. The second routes were routes that we like to run against teams that rushed the passer. That is where we like to run the double-in routes.

This route is used because we can't run four vertical because the defense is going to cover two. We are going to run the two men on the outside and make it look like they are going vertical. What did I say a minute ago about teams that play cover two now as compared to in past years? That is right. They are sinking back. If they are sinking back, we want to throw the ball in front of them. We are going to make them think we are running four vertical. It is OK if the two inside get in collisions. If the man on him collides with him, it will open up the throwing lane outside.

This is how our quarterback looks at the play. He gets his pre-snap read. It looks like the defense is going to be in cover two. He wants to see which defender has the best chance on the inside jam. We would prefer that we not get the collision on the inside men. We would like to stick the ball in deep if the safety is backing up. If the inside is covered, we look at the defender's shoulders. We look at the defender's shoulders and read him. If he jams the inside receiver, we throw the ball to the outside man on the 2 route. If he does not jam the man, we stick the ball to him. It is a 7- to 8-yard route. Sometimes they run for days on this route. This is one of the best evolutions of the three-step passing game. The reason is this: Before, we only had two or three routes we could run from the three-step drop passing game. Before, we could run the quick out, hitch, and slant routes. That was about it. Now we have two more plays that we can run here. The 292 pass is a great route for us. We can run the 166 play as well.

I am going to coach you up. See if you are with me. We are in a three-deep situation. We may try to go four vertical or throw the hitch to the outside. If the defense is in a two-deep, we are going to run some type of double-in or the 292 seam. The whole point is that we have all three of these routes that we can run at the quarterback's command.

I am going to coach you up on the next situation. We call, "Flex right, check draw, slant or quick pass." The quarterback comes up to the line, and it has a three-deep look. It is a balanced front. We are going to go 181. If we come up and the defense has everyone walked off and has a balanced front, we are going to run the stretch play to the tight end side. If they have everyone walked off, an unbalanced front, and the linebacker walked outside, we run the draw play strong. This is not hard. It is not a matter of me having the chalk. Those are three things we could do to run or pass the ball.

Now we come up, and the corners are playing off the wide receivers, and they are playing cover two. We do not want to run a desperate route; we will just run 292. It sounds good, doesn't it? It would sound a lot better if you could win some games with it. Do you understand? We have been used to doing this. We just have to get it across to our players. We got a lot better at moving the ball. The point is that you have to have a system in place.

In the early 1970s, I was coaching at the University of North Carolina. Part of my learning process as a coach was to go watch Dean Smith practice basketball. Ijust sat in the stands and watched him coach. Everyone in the building was 100 percent behind the system. It did not matter if it was Michael Jordan, a sub on the team, a manager, or whateverthey were 100 percent behind the system. There was not any doubt about it. They believed in it. To be a player or an assistant coach involved in part of the change of command, you have to believe in your leader and make sure you back him up to the hilt. When Dean Smith blew the whistle, everything stopped. When players were supposed to be some place, they were there. Sure Dean Smith recruits, but he coaches their butts off, men. He even coaches them when they get out of school. In all of the time he has been there, he has only had two players who did not graduate from college. 
Two 
of them! Both of them are making a lot more money than

 





Page 53

he does. The point I am making is this: Everything he does the kids are really sold on. If you expect your team to be good, you are going to do better.

Now we are going to the five-step principle. Now, this situation for the inside receivers is similar to what we had for the outside receivers. Earlier I talked to you about man-zone read. What this boils down to is this: We have a good idea of the type of coverage you will be in, based on the pre-snap estimate. But again, we are not sure. Normally, we call this route when we know the defense is getting ready to get after us hard. We have a man-zone read with the split end.

The first thing we do is to come up and check the wide receiver to the split-end side. We have a hitch route for him. If we get cover three, we run the weak-side hitch. We are thinking inside on the play. The reason we have the hitch set up is in case they have any kind of zone coverage. If the defense is playing man coverage, we have what we want inside with the second route.

The inside receiver runs what we call a shallow route. It is a shallow crossing route. Generally speaking, we should have a weaker cover man on us on this side. It could be the weak safety or the weak-side linebacker. He may not be as good as a player on the outside.

It is built into our system that we can change the split end and the wideout to that side. If we want to put another receiver into the game and put our best receiver in the split-end side, we can. We can interchange those two men.

DOUBLE RIGHTSLIDE LEFT 168 SHALLOW/WIDE

What is the split end looking for? A man-zone read. If he has a man on him, he gets the man in his hip pocket and continues across the field. He tries to outrun the man underneath him.

The tight end runs a deeper six route than he did on 166. He goes deeper because the quarterback takes a five-step drop. We would not run this play out of the shotgun, because we are going to hit the split end on the crossing route quickly. Some shotgun plays are better in the red zone than others.

The tight end comes down the field and reads the defense. If it is zone, he sits down inside. If it is man, he keeps coming deep. If we get the zone coverage, we end up with a high-low between the two ends. If it is man, we look to the weakest cover man, and most of the time it would be on the split end. If we feel that we have a good shot, we may go over the top and throw to the wide receiver on the post. If the free safety starts jumping the crossing route or they are doubling a receiver, we take a shot at the post route over the top. That would be based on the game plan and situations.

We can take the formation and reverse the patterns. We can make the tight-end side the weak side of your formation and have them run the same routes as the other side did. They reverse patterns. The tight end would run the crossing route. The split end runs the six route. The pro back runs the hitch, and the wideout on the split-end side runs the post route. The quarterback reads the set. If the hitch is available, we take it. If the defense is up tight, we run the 60-shallow combinations.

This has been a good route for us, particularly on third down. It was good on 3rd and 5 or 3rd and 6. You need to work on those situations in practice. As I was saying earlier, you know what won for you, and you know what lost for you. We do a lot of team passing drills because we are going against good people every week. We go one against one every day except Thursday. Now, they can't tackle the quarterback. It makes the line work hard on protection, and it give your defensive front work against good people. Another thing, it

 





Page 54

makes your secondary go against your best players in stressful situations. When we go scout team, the defense is on top of the quarterback before he can throw the ball.

We run another route very similar to this. We like to get into the trips look because we like to run the speed option, strong or weak. We like to look at soft run support. You cannot throw the ball if you cannot make the defense worry about stopping the run. You must make the defense support the area on the outside. We like to run the speed option inside the 20 because we do not have to block as many people

We run a wide- drag combination that is very similar to the shallow route. Now we have both ends running across the field. We do not teach them to pick. If the defense runs into us, that is part of the game. We do not teach the pick. If the defense is in man, we have our best receiver running a full-speed crossing route. We can run this play from a lot of formations. The idea is that one of the two ends will be open.

We have an 80 route for the outside receivers. We have the outside man coming over the top on the post route. If the defense is in man and the safety gets hung up, we will try to get the ball deep. If the defense is in a zone, we usually hit the other wideout on a curl route.

One of the best plays in the game today is the stretch or zone play. Now teams are running the naked boot off the stretch play. The question for us on that play is what kind of route the inside man will run. We do not want the quarterback to rush the play. If the defense is not fooled and comes for the quarterback, he must be ready to throw the ball to the tight end coming across. If the defense is fooled, he can look to the flat area.

After the quarterback comes out of the fake, he always looks deep. We call it the Ray Charles play. Very seldom is he going to throw the deep ball, but as he comes out of the fake, we want him to look for the deep man. If the deep man is not open, the quarterback looks for the 30 route on the outside or the tight end coming across. The man on the boundary needs to know where he is on the field. If he is on the hash mark, he can come across quickly. If he is in the middle of the field, he will have to settle down. He does not want to get over too far.

We like to put the backs in motion inside the 20-yard line. We like to run the stretch play where we can secure the edge. We are not talking about the running game, but we want to secure the edge so we can get the ball to the next level.

Another thing we may do is to call toss or quick screen. The only difference between the stretch and toss is this: We are going to use zone blocking with our line, but our quarterback is going to turn and toss the ball to our tailback and let him run with it a little more quickly.

What happens when you call the quick screen? Half of the time, you end up checking out of the thing. We always package the screen with a running play. If the defense plays us two on two, we will throw the screen. If they play us in base coverage, we run it up and down the field.

One more point before I quit. As we get closer to the goal line, one of our favorite plays is the flood route. Again, we like to package it with a sprint-out pass action. Even if our quarterback is not really agile, we will run that action. We make it look just like the flood route, but we end up blocking people. Now, we run the play, and they are playing pass. We come out making the play look like the pass, and the quarterback runs the ball. You would be surprised, even if you have a limited athlete as quarterback, how well that play works. He can walk into the end zone with the ball. Now, the better the quarterback, the better the play will work. If we send a man in motion and the defensive man runs with him, they are playing man. If he does not go with motion, we have to make an adjustment and hit another receiver. Men, it was great being here.

 





Page 55

Roger DuPaix

A Simple Approach to the Passing Game

Skyline High School, Utah

It is a real privilege for me to speak to you. I have been to Denver several times. I enjoy coming to this clinic.

I will start off with a video of a game with Plano High School, Texas, when they needed four onside kicks at the end of the game to win. They were successful on all four onside kicks. They were down 41-17 in the last minutes of the game, made a great comeback, but lost the game at the end after they went ahead. They made a regular kick on the kickoff, and the other team returned it for a touchdown. [Video]

I could do a lecture just about the things that happened in that film. We show this tape to our kids to convince them to never give up. You always have a chance. I recall going to a clinic at the University of Colorado when Bill Mallory was the head coach. He brought in a high school coach from Cincinnati by the name of Bron Bacevich. Bron was a legend in Ohio. Bron talked about the fact that when you are down by only two touchdowns, you always have a chance to get back in the game. We try to emphasize this with our kids each week. It does not matter; as long as we are only down by two touchdowns, we have a chance to win the game. I really believe in this. It has happened to us several times. Our kids have been prepared and programmed to believe that they can come back and win.

We have some great coaches in the state of Utah. One is LaVell Edwards of BYU. Some of the best coaches in America are right here in Colorado. The Air Force Academy is on the cutting edge of what they do. The University of Colorado and Colorado State do a great job. The new staff at Wyoming will do a great job. You have a national champion in Northern Colorado. You can go to them and get information to help your program. You can attend clinics such as this. You are lucky to be here. I think I am lucky to be a head football coach. I hope you feel the same. A football coach has a big influence in the lives of young men. I have enjoyed this clinic and have gotten some good ideas that I think will help our program. I hope the material that I share with you today will help you in your program. I will tell you what we do at Skyline High School in Salt Lake City. Drop me a note if you are interested in additional materials.

You cannot have a good program without a great coaching staff. It does not matter whether your coaching staff is big or small or whether your school is big or small. I have found that most coaches are good teachers. I found that out at this clinic. You can tell if a coach is a good teacher because you just learn from him. You can pick up good things from good coaches.

People who have influenced me in my career include Coach LaVell Edwards. When I was a senior playing at BYU, it was his first year as an assistant coach at BYU. I did not get to know him that well. He was coaching a position different from what I was playing. I remember after we played at Arizona and got our butts kicked, he came up to me and said, "Roger, you did a good job." I felt bad that we had lost the game, but just that one comment from him stayed with me. I will remember that the rest of my life. I am sure you have remembered the good and the bad that your coaches told you when you were playing. If we can be positive with our players, it will pay off in the end. We are the dream makers for our players. Twenty years after they are away from football, they are not going to look back and say, "Boy, we had a great English teacher." They are going to say, "Do you remember what that dumb coach did that one day?'' They will talk about the coaches and remember the good and the bad things.

When Bill Walsh was speaking, I would try to go to listen to him. I first heard him speak in Reno when he spoke at a clinic just after he took the Stanford job.

Another name that I will mention is Tony Mason. He has passed away, but he was great. He was an

 





Page 56

assistant at Michigan before he went to Arizona as head coach. If you can get any of his motivational tapes, you would get a lot out of them. He was a real coach. In Ohio he won something like 55 straight games.

Fisher DeBerry is another great name. We run an offense similar to the Air Force in some aspects. He has been so helpful to me in everything that I know about the option offense. I was an assistant coach for seven years and then the head coach at Highlands before going to Skyline High School.

The year 1982 was the turning point in my career, when I saw Air Force come in to play BYU. They beat BYU. Ken Hatfield was the head coach at Air Force at the time. Coach DeBerry was an assistant. They got me to start thinking about the problems with our passing game. Our quarterback had messed up throwing the ball, and we had some real problems. Our players did not seem to be as big as the other team. I decided to study the Air Force offense. That winter, I took an assistant coach with me, and we went to visit the Air Force Academy. Coach DeBerry was very nice to me, helping me with the offense. We came back and put it in our offense, and I have loved it ever since. It is a perfect offense for high school. You can always find someone that can run the ball. It is so team oriented. We still go back to the Air Force Academy and visit. They have some of the best teachers in the county.

At Skyline High we have mottoes. In 1993 our motto was "one heartbeat." Our captain would get in the huddle and yell "one." The entire team would respond "heartbeat." This was a great motivational tool.

The next year I wanted to keep the same motto. In 1993 we went 13-0 and won the state championship. Why change? The captains wanted to change it. We got to the semifinals but did not win the state.

In 19951 decided that we did need a new motto. I saw Coach Lombardi when he was coaching in northern California. He talked about his motto. He would yell out, "You gotta." The team would respond with "believe." I really liked that. We used that motto and felt we would keep it forever. We won the state championship again.

In 1996 I decided we would get a new team motto, but we would also keep the other one. We did the double logo that Texas Tech has. Our motto was "Teamtogether." When they were in the huddle, the captain would say, "Team." The team would say, "Together." As the season went on and we approached the playoff, another motto developed. We had a couple of team members who just could not focus. We adopted the motto ''Focus and finish." Marty Schottenheimer uses this in some of his talks, so we stressed to the team that they had to "focus and finish.''

We elect two new captains in December. I like doing this because that helps share the responsibility of getting the team ready to go next year.

In one of the 
Coach of the Year Clinic Manuals 
I read that a team selected team leaders, so we have done that recently. We just elected seven team leaders. We have two captains and seven team leaders. We are going to have an NFL draft-type program.

We are going to have those nine players select the rest of the squad and put them on their squad. Now if we need to contact the team or give something to the kids, we can go to the captains and leaders, and it goes right down the line to the rest of the squad. Now we have nine players that can monitor the program to see how everyone is doing. In the summer we will select one of the seven leaders as a third captain. We will have three captains and six leaders during the season. During the season we have one honorary captain, so for each game we really end up with four captains. That captain can be anyone on the team. It has been really helpful for us.

When we first came to Skyline, we had to put together a staff. We have been together for 11 years now. We have outstanding coaches. They set a good example, and they are leaders. They are good teachers and good coaches.

 





Page 57

I studied the teams that we have had a hard time beating over the years. I found that the teams that give us the most trouble are the teams that play a tough, hard-nosed defense. They are playing man to man in the secondary. They blitz about half the time against you. They do enough to really confuse the offense. As a result of this, I encouraged our coaches at Skyline High to develop this type of defense. We are an attack man-to-man defense. We try to cause confusion for the offense by running a lot of stunts. We want to put pressure on the offense to keep it from getting momentum. It took a couple of years to sell this defense. Since we have been running the pressure defense, we have won three state championships in four years. Actually, we have won four championships in seven years. We have been in the state finals six of the last ten years.

I am not going to talk about our defense, but I think the reason we have had so much success is because we play an aggressive defense that keeps the offense off balance. If the offense can deal with the pressure, you may be in trouble, but most offenses do not deal with pressure very well.

We line up and stem our line and linebackers. We do not stem them together. The tackle or noseguard can do his own stemming. We have the line moving individually. Our linebackers do not stem a lot, but they are getting better at it. That has really helped us on defense.

One of the things that we stress to our kids is to try to determine whether the offense is going to run the ball or whether they are going to pass the ball from the stances of the offense. We spend a lot of time looking at film. We want to see if they give away the play when they are running a pass play, running play, trap play, or any special play. Sometimes you can tell when those big tackles rock back on their heels to pass block. You can tell when the weight is on his hand and his butt is up in the air and he is ready to go. We spend time with our kids trying to show them what to look for on this. We do not guarantee this, but we are about 90 percent accurate. When we think we have the play figured out, we make a call that alerts our linebackers and secondary and out line. If we think it is a run, we call our tank. If they are in a pass stance, we call out an airline, such as Delta, American, or whatever. If they are in a Delta stance, we let our secondary know. If one man is in a Delta stance and everyone else is in a tank stance, we look for the counter tray or trap play. This does not help you with every team. Some teams do a good job of disguising their offense. However, some teams tip their plays a great deal of the time.

We have evolved into a two-platoon system. We have only been doing this the last two years. You may give up some players on one side of the ball, but in the long run it will make you stronger. We get twice as much time to work with each group by running the twoplatoon system.

We do use a lot of different formations. We used to line people up exactly the same all of the time. Then we started moving them around within the formations and got some great results. We used to run the ace formation just as the Air Force Academy ran it. Now we have what we call a new ace formation that allows the two wide receivers to move to adjust their splits.

ACE FORMATION

After we ran the new ace formation, we decided to adjust some of the other formations. We started splitting people out and putting the backs on the hash marks. This changed things a lot.

NEW SPLIT FORMATION

Then we went to our trips set. We could run it to either side.

 





Page 58

TRIPS

We run the rip-twins look with the inside man on the hash mark.

RIP TWINS

Now, there are so many things you can do. You do not want to confuse your kids. You do not want to do all of this on offense and beat yourself. You still need to keep it simple, but you can keep the offense so that you do not get confused and the kids do not get confused. I think that is the trend today. Also, a lot of coaches are using motion with the different sets. Some coaches go to it too much at the high school level, and the kids get confused. When that happens, they do not execute well. If our kids do not execute the new plays that we put in very well, then I put them in the recycle bin. If a play is in there, I will not use it for the game. If it will confuse anyone on the team, we are not going to use the play. That is my rule. I do not want to beat myself. Most teams beat themselves. We won a lot of games because the other team beat themselves and not because we were a better football team. If you can cut down on your mistakes, then you have a chance of winning the game. What I am saying is this: On motion, formations, and new plays, you need to be careful to make sure you know what you are doing.

I would like a balance between the run and pass at 50-50. That is never going to happen. It would be nice if it were at 60-40. It would be good if it were 70-30. We are about 80-20. The parents of our wide receivers think we run 95 percent of the time and pass 5 percent of the time. But look, if we run the option and the defense can't stop us, what do you do? Do you stop running the option and throw the ball? No! I feel that if you work on the pass, you will be able to throw the ball. If you are behind in a game, you have the chance to come back and win that game if you have a decent passing attack.

I get tired of hearing TV commentators say that teams that run the wishbone cannot come back. They get in the fourth quarter, and that is when they start telling everyone that the team is in trouble if they are behind. Sometimes they are right. Those wishbone teams just have to grind out the yardage. I keep asking this question when I see teams that have trouble coming from behind: Why can't they put in some simple pass plays with a really strong running attack, so if you do get behind, you have a chance to come back?

To me there is nothing better than the play-action pass. We all score from those plays. I think you need some type of bootleg action; you may have a run-out action. We do not sprint out, but we have a semi-sprint. Where we may be different from other people is this. We put in a three-step, five-step, and seven-step package. This is so that if we do need a certain type of pass in a given situation, we can do that play.

From the three-step drop we have our quick-out, slant, and fade routes. From the five-step drop we throw the square-in route and a streak route. Bill Walsh said that if you can complete two or three streak routes per season, that it is a successful play for you. That is about what we do for the season. On the seven-step drop we run the deep out, curl route, and a play that we call scissors. It has been a good play for us. When we are behind, we run this play. It is my favorite play. It is good against the zone or man. BYU was using this play in the 1980s. They do not do this much anymore, but they have variations of the play.

This play is out of our ace formation. The way we teach the seven-step drop is three big steps and four quick steps. It is really good against man coverage.

 





Page 59

SCISSORS

This is when crossing patterns work best. We do not run the pick with two ends. Once the end gets past the linebacker, he should be able to outrun him. Get him the ball when he clears. Against the zone, the split end underneath is open, unless the free safety comes up, and he never comes up. When we get in trouble, we run this play.

What should we put in our passing attack? What type of option should we run? In the spring we start working with our quarterbacks. We do not interfere with the other sports. We want other sports to be successful at Skyline. We want all sports to be successful at Skyline. If the quarterback is involved in one of the other sports, it is hard to work with him. We have most of our football team in our weight-training class. We have them most of the year. We do start working with the quarterback in the summer. We start working seven-on-seven drills. Some schools go out of state to do the seven-on-seven camps. We will go to one or two tournaments in Utah. By the end of the summer, I usually know how much passing we can do and how much we can't pass. I have an idea who can throw the ball and who can't throw the ball. I know who the good receivers are.

We do not start working on the option until our conditioning drills and our two-a-day practices. We spend most of our time trying to see whether we can do something passing the football. We need to be able to execute and look good on the play. If you win a state championship in Utah, then you get the privilege of coaching the All-Star Game. What a headache that can be. The All-Star Game is just before two-a-days. You do not get a lot of time before you get to the two-a-days, and you do not go in fresh, but the All-Star Game is a lot of fun. You get good players to coach. Energy-wise and time-wise, it kind of puts you behind. We got the all-stars for one week, and we were able to put in all of our offense. We had a simple option attack and a simple passing attack. We have coached three All-Star Games, and we are averaging 40 points for the three games. If you keep it simple and do not confuse the players, it will help. If I can understand it, they can understand it. How much coaching do you have to do with an all-star to run a streak?

The other part of our passing game involves the screen passes. I heard Coach Don Read of the University of Montana talk at a lecture. They were national champs in 1995 and runners-up in 1996 in Division II. He retired after winning the championship. He was a smart coach. Quit while you are on top. He said they worked 20 minutes a day on screen passes. I have never worked that much on screen passes. This past year we worked on it twice a week for 20 minutes each of the two days. It really helped us. The screen can be one heck of a play. Montana throws 11 or 12 screens per game. If you break that down, it comes out to two or three per quarter. They run a lot of different screens.

We threw two or three screens per game. It was a lot of fun, and they really worked. I will cover our wide receiver middle screen that we call "Millie." I like to give names to our pass plays. I give names to a lot of them. Our screen left is called "Sally." Our screen right is "Sara." Our middle screen is ''Millie." If it is coming from the opposite direction, we just call it "Millie Left.'' We do have a fullback screen, but we never used it in a game. It was fun to practice. The fullback had trouble catching the ball. We ran it in practice, but we did not use it in a game.

"MILLIE"

 





Page 60

It really helped our attack this year to spend some extra time on this play. Let me show you the play, and then I will show you the play on video.

We bring the slot man outside to block the corner. The inside linemen release on the inside linebackers to the safety. Our rule on the option is this: We go block the linebacker or safety. If the linebacker is knocked down, go knock the safety down. If the safety is not there, escort the runner for a touchdown. It is simple for our linemen, and they do a really good job on it.

We have the line block for one half of a second. That is not long. They show pass, and then they go. The quarterback is on his own. He is an athlete. When the defense is coming in, the quarterback must find a way to get the ball to the wide receiver. We want the wide receiver to run straight at the quarterback. We find that we get a better angle that way. He takes one step up and turns and comes back toward the quarterback who is dropping back.

We have run this play out of a three-step drop and the five-step drop. The right tackle must release faster than the other linemen. He only sets for about one fourth of a second. He releases more quickly.

This play is a good way to get the ball to a shifty back and let him do his thing. You can put a real good running back out there and throw him the ball. Let me show the video.

 





Page 61

Spike Dykes

Basic Defensive Alignment and Adjustments

Texas Tech University

I am proud to be here. Defense is sort of fun to talk about. Offense is not fun to talk about. If you coach offensive football, you have to be a genius. You have to remember all those rules. You have to learn what to do in every situation. That is assignment football. Assignment football is fine. You have to have it. But I'm glad we have someone else to coach it. Defense is reaction. You react to the ball and situation, and that is a whole lot more fun to coach.

I coached at a little school in Texas called Midland Lee High School. At Midland we had a good little old team. I had coached in a lot of high schools, and I got fired at a bunch of them. I was coaching at Midland and then had a chance to go to Texas Tech. I got a chance to be the defensive coordinator. I don't know why I did it. We had just lost in the state championships. After you lose a game, you are sort of crazy anyway. The dog doesn't like you. Your wife doesn't like you. Nobody likes you.

The guy from Texas Tech called me and asked me to take the job. I told him I would. I told him, "A lot has happened since last week when I turned down the job." I took the job and went up there. I was the defensive coordinator. I could bring any of my coaches with me that I wanted. "It's your defensedo anything you want to." I told him it was going to be a high school defense. He said he didn't care, just come.

I went up there, and we did pretty good. We had some pretty good defensive players. We did good enough that the head coach got fired. I was getting ready to go to the next school when they hired David McWilliams. He and I had coached together for five years at the University of Texas. I stayed on and later became the head coach after David left. He went to the University of Texas, and they hired me.

The moral of the story was that I really liked what we were doing on defense. We ran a 4-3 defense. It was the old Bum Phillips defense that we had run before. It was amusing; we ran a high school defense the first year I was the defensive coordinator at Tech, and we led the nation in pass defense. That just proves statistics don't mean much. Nobody had to pass. They just ran the football up and down the field on us.

I became the head coach at Texas Tech and my secondary coach, Coach Carlos Slaytor, became my defensive coordinator. We hired a couple of pro guys who were out of a job, and, boy, were they smart. They had all the answers. They started talking to old Carlos, and the next thing I know, we are running a 3-4 defense. We started doing things I had never heard of. We had guys in the press box watching everything. The defense was so complicated, it was a mystery. People roared up and down the field on us. The players weren't any good either. That had a lot to do with it. They were terrible.

The Chicago Bears called and wanted old Carlos to come up there and coach. He went. I started looking around for a defensive coordinator. I came to the conclusion that I would be the defensive coordinator. I used to have more fun doing that than anything else. I hadn't been having any fun being the head coach. As the head coach, all I did was walk around with a clip board. Being a head coach is overrated. There ain't much to do. All they want you to do is get out of the way. I tried to look like I knew what I was doing.

Well, I took the defense, and we put in a new scheme in the spring. We had more fun that spring than we had in years.

The second year that I coached, I coached at Ballinger High School. That is my hometown. There were 3112 people who lived there. There's not that many there anymore. We were a AA school with two coaches. Bob Wright and I were the only coaches. Bob loved offense. He coached the offense, and I was the line coach. When we went to defense, he told me that he was

 





Page 62

going to the coffee shop to get a cup of coffee. We worked on defense until he came back. Sometimes we worked on defense for two hours. He didn't know who our defensive players were or what we were running. That was great. When you get rid of the head coach, you do all the things you would do if you were the head coach.

The last year that I was in college, Bum Phillips came up with his numbers defense. He was coaching for Bear Bryant at Texas A&M. We ran the numbers defense at Ballinger. The even techniques are head-up positions. The odd techniques are shoulder techniques. From the center out both ways are the numbers. Head up the center was a 0 technique. Head up the guard was 2, the tackle 4, and the tight end 6. The inside shoulder of the guard was 1, and the outside shoulder was 3 technique. The outside shoulder of the tackle was 5. The inside shoulder of the tight end was a 9 technique. The inside shoulder of the tight end was a 7 technique.

The first thing we did was to know how to line up. We could change those defenses as quick as you could shake a stick. We could call 37 on one side and a 59 on the other. That was a good defense. We had an eagle on one side and a 50 defense on the other. We were good and had one heck of a football team. When Carlos left, I thought I would go back and run that Ballinger defense. I had three assistant coaches tell me I couldn't do that. This was in the 1990s, and I was talking about running a defense we had run in the 60s. We did that. I think it is still pretty good.

At our level you have to stop the run. You can do that with an eight-man front defense. If you can't stop the run, just tell old Grandma you're going to be there for Christmas. You are not going to any bowl games if you can't stop the run. I'm not very smart, but through arithmetic I know that if you've got seven guys up there, they can't do as well as eight. We are going to have eight guys up toward the line, and sometimes we are going to cheat and have nine. We are going to play the safety as the middle linebacker. That was part of what we set out to do. Also, we said we were going to keep it simple.

I learned a great lesson four or five years ago. Bobby Ross went to San Diego as head coach. He hired Bill Arnsparger as his defensive coordinator. Well, Bill is 150 years old. He hadn't coached football in 25 years. He went in there and told them they were going to line up correctly. The year before, the San Diego Chargers lined up by alignment wrong over 50 percent of the time before the ball was snapped. Their defense was the worst in the NFL. When they went to camp, they didn't work on technique. They worked on getting lined up correctly. I believe that the next year they ended up number 4 in the NFL, and the following year they were number 1 on defense. That was all due to lining up correctly.

I didn't think lining up was so hard. It is hard if you are not careful. We said that we were going to start over. We had a notebook burning. We had a notebook that weighed 80 pounds. We had a couple guys on our staff who were "notebook nuts." I said, "Get rid of the notebook." I told them, if they wanted to change jobs, to do it. We are going to change this defense. If you want to be part of it, that was fine. If you don't, then leave. We are going to be simple. We told our players if you don't like it, don't bitch. Just take off. We were going to find somebody who does want to play. Just leave, and good luck to you.

We started off with four simple objectives. They were simple. (1) Stop the run. (2) Be simple. (3) Create some turnovers. This next one I really think is important in football. I think the difference between winning and losing is momentum. (4) Have the ability to change the momentum of a game. If you are not careful and do not have a way to change the momentum of a game, you are in trouble. Sometimes you don't have any control over that. We are willing to roll the dice and change the momentum of the game.

We stress turnovers. We have a drill which takes us nine minutes to run. It has five stations in it, and we

 





Page 63

practice causing turnovers every single, solitary day. For the last three years we have been fourth, seventh, and third in turnover ratio in the nation. That gives you a chance to play football and be successful. The number one objective of the offensive team is, "Do not turn the ball over." If that is the number one objective on offense, then the number one objective on defense should be, "Make them turn the ball over." That is what we are going to try to do. We had a board with 31 defensive objectives on it. We had things like "Stop teams 20 percent of the time inside the 20-yard line." You had to figure what yard line it was and use a calculator, and by that time they were snapping the ball. We couldn't keep up with all our objectives.

I told the coaches, "No more objectives." My assistants said, "We have to have defensive objectives." I said, "Okay, we're going to have five." Here they are. (1) Execute the defense called in the huddle. That took the place of four of the previous objectives on that board. (2) Be physical. The defense doesn't have to worry about assignments, so be physical. (3) Sell out every play. That means give 110 percent on every play. I shouldn't say this and don't take this wrong. We've got players, and then we've got eaters and crappers. We put 60 guys on an airplane to go play. You have about 40 that play their butts off. You have 20 guys standing around giggling and farting. They eat like hell; they eat everything. You just have a lot of eaters and crappers around. You need to get rid of them and get some players. We are going to have a bunch of guys play for us. But you can bet that if they don't bust their butts when the ball is snapped, they're not going to play for us. (4) No gifts. Make the offense earn everything they get. It is hard to take the ball and move it for a long period of time. (5) Tackle. That is what defense is all about.

We have some guys who are not very smart around our place. It starts with me. I'm probably the dumbest guy, but I can remember those five objectives. We quiz our players on these all the time. They better know them.

The thing we want to do is match up well. If you have a great back, we are going to make you throw the football. If we play a team that has a great receiver, we will do everything in our power to take him out of the game. We want to make the offense do something out of the ordinary to beat us. We don't let you do what you have always done. This is how we set up our defense. I hope this is not boring you to tears; at the same time, I hope it is not too elementary. This is the best I have ever seen.

We are going to base our defense off the numbers defense. There are eight gaps, and we have gap responsibility. We have gap control and will have a guy in each one of those gaps. We tell our tackles that we are going to call 1, 2, or 3. That tells them where to line up. We have a strong and weak side to the defense. The first number is the strong-side tackle or tight-end side, and the second number is the weak-side tackle or split-end side. A 31 defensive alignment puts the strong-side tackle in a 3 technique, and he has B gap responsibility. The backside tackle, in a 1 technique, has A gap responsibility. The Mike linebacker plays to the strong side and has the strong-side A or B gap. The Will linebacker plays to the weak side and has A or B gap. In the 31 defense, since the strong tackle has the B gap, the Mike linebacker has A gap. On the weak side the weak tackle has A gap, so the Will linebacker has the B gap. That is how we start the defense and where the tackles and inside linebackers line up.

On the outside we do four things. We play regular, solid, hammer, and wide. If we play a regular alignment, the defensive end lines up in a 7 technique. If we call a "33," that is a regular defense for us.

 





Page 64

If we call a "31 solid," the defensive end would be in a 6 technique, and the Sam linebacker is in a 9 technique. If we called an "11 hammer," the defensive end is in a 9 technique, and the Sam linebacker is in a 6 technique off the ball. If we called a "22 wide," the Sam linebacker lines up in a 7 technique off the ball, and the defensive end lines up in a true 8 position.

On the split-end side we are playing an outside linebacker 3 yards outside and 3 yards deep. The weakside defensive end is playing a loose 5 technique on the offensive tackle.

Our Mike linebacker is the A or B gap player. He is the toughest linebacker we have. He has common sense. He has to cover the fullback and tight end sometimes. You can get by with an average guy with good intelligence who understands the game. He is an old-fashioned inside linebacker.

Last year, the leading tackler on our team was the left end. He only weighed 212 pounds. The reason he was playing there was that we didn't have anyone who weighed any more who was any better. He ran a 4.5 forty, which helped him. You can play a guy here who is small but can run. This next year we can move him to linebacker because we have two bigger ends that can run.

Our Sam linebacker was a 193-pound high school tailback. He started 43 football games at Texas Tech playing in front of tight ends. He is one heck of a football player. He has to play pass defense and rush the passer. But the big thing is that he has to play against the run.

The Will linebacker has to be a pretty good football player. He has to run pretty well also. He has to cover the tailback on passes and a fourth receiver vertical in some cases. He has to play the run some. He fills the lanes on the cutbacks. Ours is about 210 pounds.

The outside linebacker is called the "Raider linebacker." Since we are the Red Raiders, our rover is called a Raider. This guy here is a good football player. He is a secondary player who is tough enough to play against the run. We can get guys for these positions. If they are big enough but don't have enough speed to play the corner, they can play here. We can get personnel to fill these positions. That is how we pick the front eight people in our defense. Our tackles are just leftovers. That's where we play the principal's son. If nothing else, just put them in a gap, and tell them to submarine and tie up two players.

Everything we do on defense is based on two or three things. We are a gap-control defense, so let's start out in a regular "22." We want all 11 guys to key the football. That is the first key. The next key is whether the ball is on or off the line of scrimmage. The third thing is whether the ball is toward or away from our position. The fourth thing is the speed the ball is moving. That is called the tempo of the ball. Everything we do on defense is based on those things.

One hundred years ago when I played football, the middle linebacker had the first threat coming into the line of scrimmage. Our Mike linebacker has A gap on flow to him. If the ball goes away from him, he pursues and takes the first threat off the line of scrimmage. On flow away from the Will linebacker, he has the first cutback threat. If the ball is tossed to the tailback on a sweep, the tempo of the play is fast. If the ball is handed off, the tempo is a little slower. That is how we decide on what speed to pursue the ball. If the ball is away from me on the line, I chase it down the line. If the ball is away from me off the line, I chase it off the line. If it is fast, I go fast. If it is slow, I slow down. That is what tempo is all about. You can't coach defensive football if your team doesn't understand tempo. We work on this all the time. That is the reason we run to the ball so well.

If you knew whether they were going to run or pass, it would make your job easier. Somebody on the offensive line is going to tell you. We think we know

 





Page 65

whether it is pass or run every time before the ball is snapped. The quarterback may lick his fingers when he is going to throw. The left guard may tip when he is going to pull, pass block, or run block. Somebody on that line will tell us, and we communicate it to the others.

This is really a big word in our scheme, but it is important. If the offense runs a sweep to the outside, we tell the safety, who is lined up in the middle of the formation, to attack the line of scrimmage where he fits. That means don't run into some responsibility blind. If the contain gets knocked down and the ball is not being turned inside, don't fill inside. Go to where you fit. Have enough sense to know what the defense is all about. Make your pursuit fit the play.

Our cover one is our man free coverage. If you want to cut your teaching down, go to this numbering system. I heard Wade Phillips talk about this about 10 years ago. We number the receiver from the strong side to the weak side. The wide receiver to the strong side is 1, the tight end is 2, the fullback is 3, the tailback is 4, and the wide receiver to the weak side is 5. If 3 and 4 are in the I formation, whichever receiver comes weak is 4 and the one that goes strong is 3. In our man free coverage, the strong corner has 1, the Sam linebacker has 2, the Mike linebacker has 3, the Raider has 4, and the weak corner has 5. The Will linebacker and safety are free. We can blitz him or leave him as a hole player. He could take the coverage on 3 and let the Mike linebacker blitz.

We can call "1-key weak bracket." That means we double both wide receivers. The weak corner and Raider take 5 in some kind of combination man. They play him in and out or short and deep. The strong corner and safety take 1 in the same scheme. The Sam linebacker takes 2, and the Mike and Will linebacker take 3 and 4.

We can call "1 cloud." The corners roll up and beat the hell out of the wide receivers. The Raider and safety play behind the corners and protect deep. The linebackers pick up their people as they did in the double. If the receiver tries to come out and cross, the linebackers wall them off and combo them in and out. The Will linebacker takes the back who runs outside, and the Mike linebacker takes the back who comes inside.

We also play cover three. Cover three is our three-deep zone. The Sam linebacker and Raider are the curl to flat players. The Mike and Will linebackers are hook to curl players.

The corners and safety play thirds. The funny thing about this is we play cover three about 15 percent of

 





Page 66

the time, but we made 50 percent of our interceptions while we were in this coverage. We don't like to play this coverage because it doesn't fit the style of defense we run.

We line up the same way every time in the secondary. That is the way we disguise our coverage. Our tempo changer is the blitz. We blitz the hell out of people. The best blitz we have is when we bring these guys. We also run a "double dog." That has been good to us. We also like to bring the Raider and Will linebacker off the weak side. When you bring four rushers weak, it presents a problem for the offense. When we bring both of these guys, the Mike linebacker picks up 4, the Sam linebacker takes 3, the safety takes 2, and the corners have 1 and 5.

I've covered a lot of ground in a short period of time, but I believe that is a pretty simple method. That is not what wins for you. I understand that. You have to be able to block and tackle. I don't think we have any problem lining up correctly. We are never outflanked.

We don't have any problems with motion or flipflopping or any gimmick. When we play a twins team, we put both our corners to the same side. We match up wide receivers on corners. We can still play hard corners and have the Raider backing them up.

We have a circuit we do in practice. We have five stations with 10 guys at each station. The first station works on recovering fumbles. It is not a picking-themup drill, it is recovering them. We do all kinds of fumble drills. We do that for a minute and 30 seconds.

The second station is a one-man strip drill: one ball carrier and one tackler. The tackler is working on stripping the arms away from the ball.

The third station is working on catching the football. They are running tip drills and every ball-handling drill you can think of. We work on intercepting the ball. Everyone does it, from the tackles up.

The fourth station is a two-man strip drill. The first man tackles and strips. The second man comes in and clubs the crap out of the ball. If the ball comes loose, the first guy who made the tackle picks it up and scores. These types of things happen every Saturday.

The last station is helmet on the ball. We stick the ball with our helmet. If the ball comes out, we pick it up and run with it.

Coaches are the luckiest guys in the world. We are the last frontier. If you don't enjoy and love kids and really love coaching, you need to quit. You can make more money driving a Greyhound bus. We have the greatest profession in the world. I coached a long time, and the greatest thrill I have is to see the guys I've coached go into the coaching profession. That makes you feel good. Today in the United States of America people won't try to be a doctor because they are afraid they will fail. They are scared to become a coach because they don't think they can do it.

 





Page 67

I learned this years ago. I went to Alabama to watch spring practice. Bear Bryant was the coach. I stayed there three days. It didn't take long to figure out why they were national champions. We got ready to leave and went out on the field to tell Coach Bryant thanks for the hospitality. He told us before we left to go to his office and read "that deal on the wall." He said, "That is what it is all about." I did not know what he was talking about. "Yes, sir." I thought that was what you were supposed to say. We went to his office and waited for him. When he came in he showed us up on the wall a framed poem. He said, ''Read that, boy, that's the real deal. That is what it is all about." The poem was "The Man in the Arena.''

THE MAN IN THE ARENA 
It is not the critic who counts, 

Nor the man who points out 

How the strong man stumbled, 

Or where the doer of deeds 

Could have done them better. 

The credit belongs to the man 

Who is actually in the arena; 

Whose fate is marred 

By dust and sweat and blood; 

Who errs and comes short again; 

Who knows the great enthusiasms, the great 

devotions; and spends himself in a worthy cause; 

Who at best knows in the end 

The triumph of high achievement; 

And who at the worst, 

If he fails, at least fails 

While daring greatly; 

So that his place shall never be with those cold and 

timid souls 

Who know neither 

Victory nor defeat.

Thank you, men; I have enjoyed this opportunity to be here.

 





Page 68

LaVell Edwards

The BYU Passing Game

Brigham Young University

Thank you. It is good to be here in Hawaii with you guys. I was a high school coach for eight years. They buried my record so deep that nobody has ever found it. I never had any winning seasons. The only reason I got into college coaching is because I was the only Mormon in the world running the single-wing offense. Hal Mitchell took the job at BYU and wanted to bring back the single-wing to college. He hired me. It obviously didn't work. A year or so later Hal was relieved.

I became the head coach there in 1972. The only reason I mention this is because a lot of you coaches are faced with the problems I had. When I was coaching high school football, for the first five years I didn't have an assistant who had played football, let alone played in college or had any experience. I finally got one who had some experience, and that helped considerably.

When I went to BYU as an assistant, they had averaged about three victories a year for the first 47 years they had played major college football. They never had a winning season or won any type of championship. They were thinking about giving up football. After we got things turned, it has gone on from that to be a very successful program. We have had a great deal of success over the past 20 years.

In this business there are the "haves" and the "have nots." If you are a ''have," no matter what you do, you will be successful. If you are a "have not" at a place like ours, you have to be creative. You have to do some things a little differently. You really have to work at it. BYU is definitely a "have not." We try to create a different approach.

I had never thrown the football. For the 10 years that I was an assistant at BYU, I was a defensive coach. I worked for two coaches over that 10-year period. When I was appointed head coach, it was not a matter of 
if 
was going to get fired, it was 
when. 
That was the history of the way things had been going at BYU. There are about four or five key words that I stress a lot with our team and staff. Particularly with my staff, since I don't do a lot of coaching myself anymore. We have a veteran staff. I try to set the tone for what we want to accomplish.

Attitude is everything. It is an elusive thing, but to create an attitude is so important. One of the greatest stories I've every heard comes from a guy named Bob Blackman. He coached at Dartmouth and Illinois. I heard him speak one time at a clinic. He told this story. This great big tackle got married to this tiny girl. On their wedding night after all the activities were over, they were in the room together alone. He took off his pants, handed them to her, and told her to put them on. She told him that was crazy; they were too big. She said, "I can't wear these pants." He said, "That's right. Let's establish that fact right now; I'm going to wear the pants in this family." She said, "Fine." A little while later in the evening, she took her panties off. She tossed them to him and told him to put them on. He said, "Are you crazy? I can't get in those panties." She said, "That's right. And there is no way you're going to unless you change your attitude.'' She got to the heart of what attitude is all about.

I am not here to convert anyone to do what we do. Hopefully, you can gain an idea that will fit into what you are doing. That is my sole purpose for being here. That is the way it is with motivation. That motivation has to come from within. I don't believe in fear motivation. I don't coach that way. I work our guys hard, but I'm always talking to them about creating that attitude, that intensive desire to be successful.

Our guys believe that when we go into a game, we are going to win. No matter how bad or ugly things are, they believe we can pull out the win. It hasn't always been that way. I don't know all the keys to motivation. All I know is you have to have that attitude.

 





Page 69

There are two things that I absolutely insist on in practice. One is concentration, and the other is intensity. Your players cannot improve or get better unless they concentrate. The thing that gets to me quicker than anything is a guy going through the motions and not paying attention. We are not on a clock in our practice, but we demand concentration. We have coaches who will not let anything go by.

We want our players to be intense and play hard every play. There is nothing new about that. But you are not going to get it done without it. We play one down at a time and play it hard.

Our whole approach has been one of consistency and execution. It doesn't matter whether we look great or terrible on Saturday. I do the same thing on Monday. We have done it that way for years. I will be a little more upset if we have lost. But I don't bring guys back and scrimmage them or bench them. I try not to do anything differently. I don't know if that is all that important, but that is the way I do it.
