What is important is execution. We don't do a whole lot of things differently today than we have been doing for the last 15 or 20 years. I'm going to take a couple of plays and show you what we have done with them. One of our lead plays is the same pass route we have been running for the last 20 years. The very first day we go out for spring practice, we will start out with "69, 66, and 62." We have been doing that for years. If we have had any key to what we do, it is in the area of execution. By the time a player gets to play for us, he knows what is expected of him.

It is amazing to me that we still see teams that almost change from week to week. If they continue to change too often, you won't see them around too long. If they continue to change, that staff will be gone, and someone else will be in there.

We have some basic ideas. Our pass protection is the key to everything we do. If the front is odd, we are in a man-blocking scheme. If it is an even front, we zone-block. There are always changes to the rules, but that is pretty basic for us. We always want to block "big on big." By that, I mean linemen blocking linemen and backs blocking on linebackers whenever possible. We always block weak on a 4-4 front.

We have a lot of different sets. We go from two backs, I backs, one back, and a lot of different things. What the defense does and what their tendencies have been are how we determine how to utilize our backs. Basically, we release the flanker, split end, and tight end all the time. At times, we may leave our tight end in to block if we flood the weak side. We have had such great tight ends over the last few years that we have gotten away from leaving him in.

When we come to the line, the center will block weak when he is not covered. That way, we may be able to get the back out to the weak side. I'm not going to get into the protection too much, but if the inside linebacker comes, we base-block. If the outside linebacker comes, we slide. If we don't know what they are doing, we double-read it. Some of this I may try to get back to, depending on the time.

In the individual technique of pass blocking, there are some important things. When the lineman comes out of his stance, he jab-steps to the inside. We always protect the inside first. We honor an inside fake. We never honor an outside fake. The offensive line coach studies the moves of the opponents we play and works with our linemen.

It is important to anticipate but not be overanxious. One of the hardest things to teach an offensive lineman is not to fire out, lunge, or get overanxious. The hands come up, not out. The knees are bent, and the buttocks are lowered. That is hard for the offensive lineman. An offensive lineman has to slide with his feet, never cross over, and never take big steps. They hit and recoil. Their shoulders are square at all times to the line of scrimmage. They deliver a blow and get separation. The defense wants to get a hold of the offensive lineman and get his shoulders turned. Once that happens, he can do anything he wants.

In training and developing our quarterback, we don't fool around with his throwing motion. If you have a young guy and are starting out with him, you can have

 





Page 70

an influence on his throwing motion. I've heard a lot of people talk about quarterback mechanics. They talk about pointing the knees and other things. That is confusing to me. I really believe there are only five things important in throwing the ball.

When you are teaching a kid how to throw, make sure he gets his elbow shoulder high. The best explanation I've ever heard in throwing the ball is as the quarterback starts to throw, he should expose his number to the target. When he throws, he should pronate the hand so that the thumb snaps down and inside.

Make sure he does not overstride. The final thing is to never throw across your body. If he exposes his numbers, it takes care of throwing across the body. He should never let the ball cross his face. That is a way of saying "Don't throw across your body."

The first play we work on is called "69." The set I'll put up here is a pro set with split backs. The split end does a takeoff. The tight end releases upfield to a depth of 7 yards and crosses to a depth of 15 yards. The flanker runs an 18-yard square-in and comes across the field. He can also run a post and then square it in or run a post-corner if he sees the corner is jumping the in route. If we want to run that option, we call "69 Z post-corner."

The linemen block big on big. The center blocks weak. The tailback and fullback have the outside backers. If they come, they block them. If the linebackers drop, they release into the flat.

We have man and zone routes. If you are playing against a zone team, make sure you create a horizontal and vertical stretch. If it is a man route, we use crossing routes or individual cuts.

If you tell your receivers to run a 12-yard curl, they will run it at 9 to 10 yards. We have to stay on our receivers all the time to get the proper depth from our patterns. The defense playing a zone wants to force the offense in. They want to compress the zone and force the receivers to run their routes short or too tight. That is our job, to keep that zone stretched.

For years, the key on this play was the strong safety. If the strong safety went with the tight end, the quarterback read man coverage. He went to the flanker running the 18-yard in. That is a good read, but this is a play we would call against a zone team. The progression for throwing is flanker, tight end, and running back.

From this we call "69 H delay." If the linebackers don't get a good drop, there is a big hole in the middle to hit the tight end. When the linebackers get their drops under the tight end, we delay the running back and bring him out late and under the linebackers. The tailback steps up and waits for two counts. If nobody comes, he trickles out under the linebackers. We do this a lot on possession downs. When we have to have the first down, this is the play we run. Most of our delay patterns are backs coming out of the backfield. If the outside linebacker comes, the back blocks him first, then trickles off.

People began to play zone deep and man to man underneath. We went to "69 H option." On the H option, we look for the halfback all the way. The halfback has no blocking responsibility on this pattern. As soon as the ball is snapped, the halfback goes hard upfield for

 





Page 71

6 yards. If it was a zone defense, he turned and spotted up. As much as we drill and run this play, our backs still want to break the pattern off at 3 and 4 yards. He has to get to 6 yards so the quarterback knows where he is.

The quarterback takes a five-step drop. He gets his depth in his first two steps. The next three steps are gathering steps. When he hits his fifth step, he delivers the ball to the halfback. He can't move around so the quarterback can find him. The halfback spots up between the middle and weak-side linebackers.

The defense knows about the H option. They study our tendencies and have a feeling for when we run the play. The outside linebacker, instead of dropping so deep, drops shallow and tries to squeeze the halfback. When that happens, the tight end becomes the number one receiver.

If the defense showed man coverage, we still run the same patterns. If the outside linebacker blitzes, the halfback knows that probably the middle linebacker has coverage on him. The halfback has a free release. The fullback comes over to take the blocking responsibility of the halfback. The halfback run his 6yard pattern, leans in on the linebacker, and breaks out. He does not spot up on a man coverage. He runs an out cut.

From this play came the most successful running play we have in our offense. It is called "draw trap." This play looks like the H option. The guard, if he is covered, tries to get his man to rush inside. The tackle gives his pass set then comes off for the inside linebacker. The center block backs for the cutoff. The backside guard pulls and traps the first thing to show past the guard. The halfback drives hard as in the H option and blocks the weak linebacker. On the snap of the ball, the fullback takes a step toward the trap and waits. The quarterback brings the ball back, slides it to the fullback, and breaks inside the trap block. This first example is against an even 4-3 front.

Let me show you different ways of blocking it against different fronts. Early on, if the defense got into a Bear front, we had a tendency to automatically drop out of the play. We don't now. Let's look at the odd-50 front. The left tackle takes a hard inside step and invites the tackle to rush outside. After he does that, he comes up on the inside linebacker. The left guard takes his inside step. He and the center have to block the noseguard and backside linebacker. They do it with an area scheme. If the noseguard slants into the strong gap or plays the center head up, the center takes him. If he goes weak, the left guard takes him. The right guard pulls and traps. The right tackle seals inside. The back blocks the weak outside backer. The quarterback and fullback do the same thing.

 





Page 72

We see a lot of 4-4 looks. Here is how we block against it. The left guard and tackle have to block the man on the guard and the offside linebacker. The guard and tackle take their inside steps. If the defensive tackle comes hard inside the guard, the guard blocks him, and the tackle goes for the offside linebacker. If he plays the guard, the tackle blocks him, and the guard comes off on the linebacker. When we first started to run this against this defense, we had the halfback going in to block the onside linebacker. But all that did was muck up the play. We have found the inside linebacker follows the halfback out. Instead of going in on the inside linebacker, the linebacker comes out to the halfback.

What we follow that up with is a "draw trap pass." On this play, everything is blocked the same way. The split end runs a curl pattern. The halfback has a free release and runs to the flat. If the linebacker runs back under the curl, the quarterback hits the halfback. If the linebacker jumps the halfback, the quarterback hits the curl pattern. We key the inside linebacker. He can't be right. Where he goes, we throw opposite.

We run a weak-side sweep. To be frank, this play has not been as consistent as we need it to be. We are going to work harder on this play. We get a lot of the 4-3 look. We played Penn State a few years ago in Provo. We ran the ball 50 times and threw it 20. We didn't have any idea the run would work out that way. Of the 50 runs, 25 were weak-side sweep. The split end cracks on the outside linebacker. The tackle downblocks the man over the guard. The guard pulled for the corner. The halfback chops the defensive end. The center blocks the onside gap and the backside scoops.

We are big on option-type routes for our receivers. We like to run this off the right hash mark. We bring the flanker in motion back to the quarterback. We could line up in the wide slot to the field. The reason we like to motion is so the defense has to tip their coverage. If the corner goes with the motion, we feel that is man coverage, and that is what we want. The fullback has a free release. He runs a 5-and-5 route, that is, 5 yards downfield and 5 yards from the sideline. Once the fullback finds out it is man, he keeps going up the sideline. The tight end takes an inside release and works upfield.

As the quarterback drops, he keys the outside linebacker. If he comes, we have no one to block him. The back is looking at the outside linebacker. If the linebacker blitzes, he gets the ball right now on the hot route. The tight end finds who is picking him up. He carries him downfield 10 yards, leans in, and pressreleases to the outside.

If we read zone coverage, we run zone routes. The fullback has a free release and runs his 5-by-5 route. The flanker reads zone, runs a 6-yard curl, and sets

 





Page 73

down right in the middle of the set. The tight end takes his inside release. He reads zone. He runs his pattern up to 6 yards and hooks outside between the two linebackers. The tight end is the primary receiver on this play. If they jump the tight end, the fullback will be wide open. This basically is the same play as the H option. We call it F option because the flanker is running the choice route.

This next play is called "62 X, Y cross." This Is What I Want we want to do against man coverage. We narrow the split of the split end to 6 or 7 yards from his tackle. The flanker takes a post move. The tight end takes an inside release and comes across at a depth of 5 to 7 yards. The split end comes underneath at a depth of 3 to 5 yards. We want the mesh or cross of our receiver in the middle of the formation. The quarterback reads the free safety. People know that is the first pattern we run against man coverage. They try to hang the weak safety in the cross area and rob the ball. They are taking a gamble by putting their other corner on an island with the flanker. The weak safety hanging in the middle gives the flanker the entire field to work with man coverage. When the quarterback sees the free safety hang in on the cross, he goes downtown to the flanker. Most of the time we win that battle. That is where we work our option route to the flanker. We can give him a post-corner.

There are a number of different ways to run the same play. One way I like to do it is bring the flanker in motion. The split end runs the post route, and the flanker and tight end run the cross. The read is the same. We can set the halfback in the weak slot and run the flanker on the go route. The split end does the post, and the halfback and tight end run the cross. The keys are the same, and the pattern is the same.

Basically, the guy who comes underneath catches the ball as soon as the cross takes place. The underneath receiver starts to float upfield. He doesn't continue his pattern across the field. We run this pattern with all different kinds of combinations. We can take X, Y, and Z on go routes and bring both back out and cross them in the middle.

One final thing here, and then I'll answer any questions you may have. One of the best plays against a zone defense is "all curls." From that we can get into the "Y choice." That is the option route for the tight end. Our outside receivers run a 12- to 14-yard curl route. This is not a hook pattern. As the receiver starts to make his move, he looks at the quarterback.

If there is someone between him and the quarterback, he takes it further inside. That is why it is important to align wide. A zone team will eat you up if you don't get the stretch. He doesn't come down and stop. He works back to the quarterback. We look at the middle

 





Page 74

linebacker in the 4-3, weak-side inside linebacker in the odd front, and weak linebacker in the stack.

The tight end's pattern is 10 to 12 yards deep. If the middle linebacker goes weak, the quarterback goes immediately to the tight end. If the middle linebacker goes to the strong side, the quarterback immediately goes to the weak side and keys the weak-side linebacker. If he hangs on the curl, the quarterback hits the back. If the linebacker jumps the back, he hits the curl of the split end.

Men, it has been a real pleasure to be here. I'll stick around for a bit if any of you would like to come up and ask any questions. Good luck to you. Thanks very much.

 





Page 75

Joe Glenn

Our Best Pass Plays

University of Northern Colorado

Thank you very much. I have to pinch myself every morning to believe that we won the national championship. First, I want to thank the high school coaches from the state of Colorado. Fellows, we won the championship with a bunch of Colorado boys. Thank you very much, Colorado coaches. Out of 100 players on our football team, 87 of them were from the great state of Colorado. Again, thanks. We just recruited 33 more players from the state of Colorado. "Dance with the one that brung you."

I grew up one of 12 children in Lincoln, Nebraska. People ask me why I got into coaching. I have to tell them it was because of the competitiveness that ran in my family. I will tell you one story to illustrate. I will tell you the story about my brothers Jimmy and Johnny. We were playing on a Little League baseball team. We were playing for the city championship. My brother Jimmy was one of those kids who, anytime anything happened that he did not like, would get into a fist fight. If the other team would bean one of our batters, he would get after them. If the other team gave him any reason to get into it, Jimmy would be in the middle of it. That was his style. I prided myself on having a nice, clean uniform after the game. I had my uniform washed before the next game for each game. Jimmy did not have his uniform washed all summer. He was a grub. Our team and coach loved him, and he was a prince of a player to coach. He was everything you would want in a Little League player. However, we got to the city championships, where we got to play under the lights for the first time. The first time we came to bat, naturally they hit Jimmy with a pitch. Jimmy ran out to the mound and lit into the pitcher. Both benches cleared, but they threw Jimmy out of the game. The coach loved Jimmy so much, he let him go down and coach third base the rest of the game.

The game came down to the last inning, and I had my chance to win the game. The bases were loaded, and the score was tied. There was no one out. I looked down to first base but saw no signal. I looked down to third base and again saw no signal. I was just waiting to make contact to win the game. The pitcher started his windup, and I looked down and saw a runner breaking from third base. My first thought was, "Did I miss the signal? Was a squeeze play on?" The pitcher speeded up his windup and threw the ball home. The catcher grabbed the ball and put the tag on the runner. The umpire yelled, "You're out!" My brother Jimmy got up and yelled, ''You can't throw me out. I am the third base coach." Competitor! They made him go sit with my mom after that.

I do have one story to tell you before we get into X's and O's. There were four old men who were golfing partners. The four golfers had a pact. Whoever died first would find some way to get back to the others and let them know how the hereafter was. Winter came, and they quit playing golf. That winter, Ernie passed away. Spring came, and the three old men went out to play golf for the first time. After they teed off, they heard a strange voice: "Hey, fellows!" They recognized the voice. "Is that you, Ernie?" The voice replied, "Yes, it is Ernie. Did you forget about our deal?'' The guys said, "No, we did make a deal. Tell us, Ernie, what is it all about?" This was his reply: "You can't believe it. You get up in the morning, and the first thing you do is have sex. Then you go for a walk. When you come back, you have a drink and then eat. Then you have sex again and then relax awhile. Then you have a drink, then eat, and then have sex again. All day long all you do is eat, drink, have sex, and take a walk. It goes on day after day." The other golfers said, "Wow! Ernie, we knew heaven would be great, but we had no idea it would be that great." Ernie replied, "Heaven? Hell, I am in Billings, Montana." Reincarnation! Come on, guys.

Now that I am getting a little older, I try to sneak away at noon to take a little nap during our fall practice two-a-day practices. I wanted to stay fresh so I could stay

 





Page 76

in our staff meeting late into the night. One day I went home and had no more laid down when the phone rang. I have a little dog named Patty Murphy. The phone call was from my neighbor Mrs. Scott. She said, "Coach, your dog Patty Murphy is over here on top of my little dog Pepi. "Pepi was her show dog. She had the dog registered and had all of the papers to prove her pedigree. She did not want my dog having sex with her dog. I said, "Mrs. Scott, just grab Patty Murphy by his tail and pull him off of Pepi. You know Patty Murphy. He will not hurt you." She replied, "I just tried that, and it does not work." I said, "Why don't you grab a bucket of water and throw it on the dogs. That will work.'' She said, "I just tried that, and it doesn't work.'' I said, "OK, I know what to do. Mrs. Scott, take the telephone over and set it next to the dogs. Hang up the phone, and I will call you back. Let the phone ring three or four times." She said, "Will that work?" I replied, "I don't know for sure, but it just worked at my house."

When you win, you get a lot of things going for you. They raised our recruiting budget after we won the championship. Now we have the third highest budget in the state. The University of Colorado is first, Mullen High School is the second, and now we are the third highest. I am hoping we will pass Mullen High up soon. Mullen did a good job this year, so I can joke with them.

I want to share a couple of things with you. Most everyone knows where we are and what we are doing. We have about 10,000 students. The best-known person from our school is James Michener, the Pulitzer Prize author. He is still alive. Those of you who have been on campus know that Michener Library is named in his honor. He wrote 
Centennial.

We have a great school. This is my 10th year at the University of Northern Colorado. I flat love the school. Greeley, Colorado, is a great town. We are the summer home of the Denver Broncos. If you have not done so, you should get up there and check them out. They have been very gracious toward coaches. It is a lot of fun to visit with them. We built a new stadium two years ago. We are 13-0 in our new facility.

I am not going to tell you that we have reinvented the wheel. The best thing I have done is to hire good people. I learned this at a young age. This is true if you are hanging out, playing games, or coaching. Get yourself good people. I have a great staff. They are getting the job done for us. We have about 14 other coaches on our staff. I have not coached a position in the last several years. I have been fortunate to move around to each group. I have delegated a lot of things to my staff. I have been very fortunate that the guys have carried it out. It has allowed me to do a lot of other things. I focus a lot of my time on recruiting and fund-raising, and I get very involved in the motivational part of it. I love working with our coaching staff.

Talking about our offense, philosophically we want balance. A lot of you remember the old UNC that could win the league passing every year. Those teams took a lot of pride in the passing game. I think that is a great way to move the football. Now, the passing game only does not wash in our conference. In the WAC that philosophy works. They have a track meet in about every game. In our league it is just about a fistfight every week. Even though we were national champions, we still lost three games in our conference. There are 10 teams in our conference. I like to think of our conference as the Big Ten of Division II. If you can power your way through the conference and make the final 16 tournament, you should have an excellent chance to still be standing when the tournament is over. Our league definitely prepared us for the national championship. We had a lot of injuries, but we made it through the year.

We really feel we must have balance in our offense. We work really hard at running the football. We are still a two-back attack. This has been my philosophy ever since I played back in 1959. I am a big-time fan of Bob Devaney of Nebraska. He was the coach who came to Lincoln when I was about 12 years old. He got Nebraska off the ground, and he did it by first playing good defense and then by running the football.

By running the football, you learn to be tough. Your defense learns how to stop the run by working against your offense each day. I am not going to say that we hammer the eight-man front all day. We feel we work harder than most teams. We keep two backs in the game most of the time. We are a two-back attack

 





Page 77

about 85 percent of the time. We want a good hardnosed fullback. The hard-nosed fullbacks are hard to find today. Some people prefer another wideout instead of the hard-nosed fullback. We like the hardnosed type of players. To me, it is what football is all about. As Buck Nystrom would say, "It is a tough game for tough people." I believe that way myself.

In our offense I would like to think we could complete 50 percent of our passes. I would like to average 4 yards per carry. Those may be lofty figures. In our season this past year, we rushed for 2776 yards, and had 48 touchdowns. We did play 15 games. We were able to pass for 2491 yards. That is good balance, as far as I am concerned. We scored 20 touchdowns passing. Those may not be lofty figures, but if you are playing good defense as we did this year, it isn't bad. Our defense gave up 88 yards per game rushing.

In having a balanced attack, you can protect your defense. By that, I mean that we really work hard on the turnover category. We cover the end of the football with our middle finger. We pull our elbow down on the football. When we are in traffic, we put two hands on the football. We work with our quarterbacks in making good decisions. We will punt most of the time when in doubt about what to do. We try to make our opponents look at 70 to 80 yards every time they touch the football. We are playing a good layer defense, and by that I mean a four-man front, with three or five underneath with a two-deep secondary. We have three layers that you have to make it through before you hit a big play on us. We do not want to give up big plays on defense. We do not play a lot of man-to-man coverage. We just want to belly up and keep everything in front of us. Again, that is the philosophy that I believe in, and I have coached that way ever since day one. We try to make it work in Greeley, and so far it is working. Again, the better the athletes you have, the better it works. That is where we are coming from, philosophically.

I want to talk about the defensive problems that we are seeing now in our league. Teams that we are playing are going to an eight-man front. I think Buddy Ryan used this type defense and made it popular. The defense has their ears laid back, and they are lined up in a two-point stance. You do not know who is coming and who isn't. You have to anticipate an eight-man rush a lot of the time. You will get a lot of eight-man rush. Some of you may call it the full-court press, or max blitz, or whatever.

Last spring, our staff discussed our problems in handling the eight-man front. Our offensive coordinator, Kay Dalton, suggested that we go to the option. He knew we were not handling the eight-man front totally with the passing game. We needed to find a way to slow down the eight-man front.

We went to Lincoln and spent two days with an old friend of mine by the name of Coach Milt Tenopir of Nebraska. I knew Milt when he was a GA at Nebraska. I actually knew Coach Osborne when he was a GA, believe it or not. I spent some time with the Nebraska coaches and came back and put in some option plays in our offense. It is not our bread-and-butter offense. You would not believe how much it helped us this past year. We were not very good at hitting slant or fade routes, getting the ball to our backs out of the backfield, or tight end in pressure situations. If we did hit the back or the tight end, we were not making enough yards to hurt the blitz.

Now when we see the eight-man front or the blitz package, we kind of spit into our hands and say, "Oh goody, here we go." The option helped us win the national championship. We block the play just as we do on our outside zone play. That leaves one man left, and we pitch or keep on the option off that last man. If you have a quarterback who can run the option, it puts a hell of a bind on the defense. The small amount of time that we did run the option, it helped us a lot.

I guess it is true that the more we change, the more we remain the same. When I came into the game, power football was popular. Woody Hayes was at Ohio State; Bob Devaney was at Wyoming and later Nebraska. He was having success with a full-house set, and Duffy Daugherty at Michigan State was having success with the unbalanced line and power football. Then we developed the I formation. To me, the I formation is power football. Then we started seeing the option. Other teams adopted the option

 





Page 78

until it was the thing to do. Then the option faded away, and the trend of "pass, pass, pass" came into being. Now we see great corners who can cover one on one. The defense can bring unbelievable pressure on your quarterback. They try to knock the hell out of your quarterback every time he throws the ball. I have a feeling that the option is coming back into football. It was real good to us last year.

We run the inside zone, which is a power play to us. We double-team at the point of attack. One of the two will try to come off on a linebacker. We run our outside zone, and we run our option plays almost the same way as the outside zone plays. We run the lead draw. To me, it is perhaps our most successful play. It is right up the middle. I believe the reason that we are having success is because most teams have gone to a 4-3 defense. The lead draw has been really good. We attack the 3 technique and get a lead back up inside. If you have a good blocking fullback who can get on the linebacker, you turn people the way they want to go. The defense gets in the rush lanes and comes very hard. The lead draw has been good against that defense.

We will run the trap play. With everyone rushing so hard, we will run the trap as a "check with me" type play. We run the inside trap and the G trap. Both of these plays have been good to us. These are the type of plays that are coming back that were not very popular for a while. We have found that the zone plays and the trap plays are good package plays against the pressure defense or the eight-man front defense.

We do run some counter plays as well. We run some play-action passes, and we do run some three-, five-, and seven-step drop passing game. We have a lot of offense.

I want to take you through four passes that were really good for us this past year. When we run the zone to the outside, we have a hard time with the strong safety filling so fast. We run the play just as we would the zone play and fake the ball to the tailback. We run a 17-yard post route to our Z back. We call the play "pass 38 Z post." The 38 is for our running play. We add the Z post for the pass play. Everyone blocks down. If the strong safety bites and comes down, the quarterback reads him first. If the strong safety bites, we will take a shot at it and see if we can't hit the Z back on the post route. The Z back has to get a depth of 17 yards to keep the free safety from getting to the ball. If he runs a shorter route, the free safety can get it.

PASS 38Z POST

Now give the corner credit, and say he hangs back deep. Our fullback usually blocks the strong safety on the 38 play. Now he runs what we call a swing route down the sideline. We have seven-man protection up front. Now the tailback will help us with the strong safety blitz. The quarterback checks number one first. If the free safety bites, we will throw the post route. If the free safety hangs and the corner hangs, which they normally do, we will hit our fullback on the swing route to the flat. That swing route has been really good for us. We have made a lot of big plays off that one play.

We have made a lot of yardage out of a simple pass play we call our angle play. We simply dump the ball off to our back coming out of the backfield. We like to run the play in the middle of the field. Our protection is a six-man scheme. We run the play when we face a two-deep secondary. Most of the time we want to throw underneath. We like this play against dropping linebackers. BYU has made a living with this play. It is a five-step drop for the quarterback. As the quarterback reads the linebacker, he tries to dump the ball to the back swinging out of the backfield. The back releases to a point 3 yards outside the tight end,

 





Page 79

plants, and then works on a 45-degree angle back toward the ball. Most of the time, the linebacker drops out of that area. We drop the ball in the area where the linebacker was at the snap of the ball.

ANGLE PASS

We do not want the quarterback to stand and hold that football forever if the back is covered. It is hard to pass-protect, so we know we have to get rid of the ball. If we get man to man, we still have had luck with the angle. The reason is because we run so many arrow routes outside. Our tight end breaks down the middle. If it is a two-deep, the tight end goes deep. Against a three-deep, he will sit down in front of the safety. Most of the time we have passed the ball to the back against zone defenses. We did get a touchdown throwing the ball to our Z back out of this play.

This next play was taken from the Denver Broncos. I do not know whether they have run it recently. We call it fire boot pass. It takes advantage of the quarterback coming outside. It has been a good play for us. It is a bootleg-type play. We block down flush on the strong side and pull the off guard. The secondary gets a run read on the strong side from the tight end. We run a 12-yard out route on that side. The tailback runs an inside route and makes a fake inside. He picks up anything coming through. The quarterback turns and looks at the back. There is not much of a fake. We just front out, sprint five steps, and set up behind the tackle. It is a pre-snap read. If it is a three-deep secondary, we run the out route to the Z back at 12 yards.

FIRE BOOT PASS

If we get a cover five or a two-deep, the quarterback knows he will not go to the Z back. He will take his five-step drop and look for the X end on the sit route inside. The quarterback knows from where the free safety lines up what he is going to do with the ball before it is even snapped.

Now, we have another phase of the play. We called this play "continental" when I was growing up. Now, we run the same play, but this time we bring the left halfback out of the backfield on the swing route behind the X end. He comes out and up, getting depth as he widens. That play has been so good to us over the years. We used this pass in the playoffs against Clarion State in Pennsylvania. We did not score on it because our quarterback wanted to make sure we completed the play, so he did not lead the receiver far enough, but we scored on the very next play. It is blocked just like the bootleg.

FIRE BOOT CONTINENTAL PASS

We do run some two-tight end plays. This is our four vertical route. We call the play F delay. Our back steps up in the protection, but he will continue through the ine and sit down over the center. If we are getting a 

 





Page 80

soft zone, the play goes to the tailback. This is not a "Hail Mary" play. The wideouts run 20-yard go routes, but we are not trying to throw the bomb. We are trying to get a stretch against a three-deep scheme. If it is against man-to-man, we are looking for a mismatch. As I said, if it is against a really soft zone, the best bet is to dump the ball to the tailback on the F delay. If it is man-to-man, the tailback has to break one way or the other. He has a choice to go right or left on the break.

F DELAY

We would designate one of the two tight ends or the H back, if he is in the slot, to break to the middle of the field. He would break to the open spot against a two-deep scheme. If it is man, it becomes a footrace. If we have swinger called, the wideouts run a comeback route.

I am going to add my favorite pass play of all times. This will be my 25th year of coaching. I have always liked the curl-flat route. As a quarterback, when I played, I felt it was the easiest pass to read for the quarterback. You get a stretch to the flat, and you have someone in the middle who can work back to the quarterback. It is an easy throw. You do not have to have a strong arm to throw the ball. It is not a big timing route. I have always been a big fan of the curl-flat route. Kay Dalton put in a little different wrinkle on the play. I am sure some of you have used this, but I had never used it. We call the play "Hank." Our tight end hooks up over the ball at 8 yards. The wideouts run a curl at 16 yards. We get our fullback on the stretch route to the flat. The tailback sets to block and then runs a scat route. It is six-man protection.

Hank Pass Route

Our quarterback comes back on a five-step drop, and our tight end runs the 8-yard curl right in front of the quarterback. He sits in between the linebackers. If it is against a 4-3, he will try to get away from the middle linebacker. The quarterback takes a five-step drop, and if the end is open, it shows right now. If the tight end is open, we get him the ball. It is an easy throw.

The defense will sit on the tight end. Now we go to our curl-flat read. Most quarterbacks want to throw the ball down the field. Most defenses will play the deep route and give you the short or flat area. You have to throw to the flat area until the defense takes it away.

The only way that the defense can take all three receivers away on the strong side is to come hard with the blitz with tight coverage. If we see the Mike coming hard, we will work the back side of Hank. The defense will be short on the back side if they are playing zone. I like the play because it shows early, and it is a relatively easy pass to throw.

Now I want to show you these five plays on tape.

 





Page 81

Jim Heacock

Defensive Line Techniques

Ohio State University

It is a privilege for me to be here. I want to talk to you people about defensive line play. I have had a chance to work in some great programs for some great coaches. I had a chance to work with Dennis Stoltz at Bowling Green, Don James at the University of Washington, and John Cooper at Ohio State. I've been at Ohio State only one year, but it is a great learning experience working with a coach like John Cooper. The defensive coordinator is Fred Pagac. He has helped me a lot, and I have learned a lot of football from Fred.

Fred is a little different. Anyone who has followed Ohio State knows they have been pretty much a read-type defense. They have been a "bend but don't break" type of defense over the years. When Fred took over this last year, he kind of rolled the dice a little. He is a get-after-them type of guy. If you make a mistake, you make a mistake.

Let me start by telling you that we had some great football players. I think everyone in this room probably knows that. We had an outstanding defensive football team this year at Ohio State. I was fortunate to walk into that situation. We had four guys up front, and three of them will probably be drafted. One of them will be a first-round pick. We had three linebackers that were outstanding. Two were seniors who had started for three years. The middle linebacker was a freshman and probably as good as any linebacker I've ever seen. Our corners were outstanding, and we had a couple of safeties that could get it done. We had a great football team and could do a lot of things.

As we went through the year, our philosophy changed a little. We started out the year as a slide defense with a reduction toward the tight-end side. Throughout the year we changed up and played a lot of "Ohio defense." The Ohio defense is a Bear defense where we are flatout after you. We also played a lot of weak reduction, which we didn't think we were going to play at all. Things happened during the year that really helped us out. We found out that we had two corners who could play man to man. We had a great corner who was probably as good as anybody in the country. The guy on the other side was just about as good. When we found out that we had corners who could cover people, it made our life easier. We became a blitzing, attacking football team. We also had a free safety who developed the nickname of "Wacko." He was a blitz guy. I've found out over the years that if you find a guy who likes to blitz, send him. Every time we sent this guy, he made something happen.

I'm going to talk about defensive line play. I'm kind of the brunt of everyone's jokes at Ohio State. They say that all I have to do is take care of A and B gaps and they'll do everything else. There are four areas I'm going to hit on really quickly.

I think the front four is the most critical part of any defensive unit. There are two things they have to do. They have to control the run and get pressure on the quarterback. I've been in this situation before. If you have to blitz to get pressure or stop the run, you will get burnt eventually. This year we had a front four that could get pressure. When we brought linebackers or the free safety, they created havoc in the backfield.

I think the next point is the most important thing. This is the thing I say most to the defensive line: Beat the blocker. That is the name of the game. The first thing a young defensive lineman wants to do is make a play. As coaches, we have to make them understand that if they want to make a play, they have to beat the blocker. That is the first thing I tell them. Someone is going to block you. Then I go through the offensive play book. I show them in that book that they are not going to leave any linemen unblocked. That is where I start the progression.

 





Page 82

There are three things that can happen to defensive linemen. They are going to use a base block, reach block, or cutoff block against him. If the defensive linemen can defeat the block, fight pressure, and get across pressure, that will take you to the ball, where you can make plays. The first thing that young lineman wants to do is to rise up to find the ball. He wants to make a play. The first thing he knows, the offensive lineman is blocking his butt off. The example is two boxers in the ring. If we are equal, and I'm not watching the other boxer, who is going to win the fight? The defensive lineman is going to get killed if his focus is on the quarterback or running back. The offensive lineman's focus is on the defender. I have to get them to focus on the blocker.

If the guy across from you leaves, there is someone else coming to block you. He will get a down, fold, or trap block. Once the guy across from you leaves, the next guy coming is the guy the defender has to defeat. If it is a trap, he has to wrong-arm it and spill. If it is a down block, he has to get upfield or across the face. If he can beat the blocker, he can make plays.

Should a defender attack upfield or sit on the line of scrimmage and read? When I went to Bowling Green, we were strictly read defenders. We were in a parallel stance and were not going to get reached. When I went to Washington, I got more into getting upfield. Now we want to be able to do both. I'm convinced we have to do both. We have to be able to get up the field and come backdoor on all down blocks. We also have to be able to come cross-face on the line of scrimmage.

If I am a tackle over a guard who pulls, what do I do when the down block comes? In our game plan, we are going to have two ways of playing it. We are either going up the field and coming backdoor, or we are going across the face. If the offensive tackle knows the defensive tackle is attacking upfield, he takes a flat step to the line every time to get the defense walled off. But on the other hand, if the defensive tackle is always sitting on the line of scrimmage, the offensive tackle gets more depth on his charge.

What we teach is two calls. We have a red-and-green call. The green call is go. We are going upfield as hard as we can. If we get the down block, we are going backdoor. We grab cloth, swim, and come down the line of scrimmage behind the blocker.

If we have a red call, we sit on the line of scrimmage. If we get the down block, we strike and fight across face to get inside-out on the ball. Against Arizona State in the Rose Bowl, we always played red unless we were in a nickel defense. The reason for that was their counter play. They ran that play better than anyone I've seen. We wanted to stay on the line of scrimmage. Our 3 techniques made more plays than anyone because they thought he was coming upfield.

At Ohio State we basically have three fronts. We play the Bear front and the reduction to both sides. We call them slide, tight, and Ohio defense. Off each one of those defenses we have a front-line movement. That lets us change a gap for any defensive lineman. We have games to stunt the interior four defensive linemen. We also have blitzes off each one of those. This gives us a multiple look.

I know you guys don't want to listen to my philosophy. So I'll cover it quickly. It is not too different from most people's. But I do think this is important. I spend as much time with the players with this as I do with technique. One of the things we did this year was blitz a lot. Every time we blitzed, we twisted. I've never done that as much as we did this year. Our philosophy was to twist two down guys and tie up three guys trying to block them. Let me give you an example.

Let's take the noseguard and the 3-technique tackle. If we were coming with a Mike or Buck linebacker blitz, we twisted these two guys. We felt that if it took the two guards and the center to block the nose and 3 technique, no one was coming free. The only problem was the defensive linemen were not getting any sacks. That goes back to our first philosophical thought: The team comes first. We may not make the sacks, but the team does.

 





Page 83

If you are going to play defensive line at Ohio State, you have to get after it. Activity is important for us. From when the ball is snapped until the whistle blows, these guys better be busting it. We do two things to help our activity. We are going to be in great condition, and we are going to keep fresh people in the game. If we have guys of equal ability, we will substitute more. We tell them to go three plays all out, and then we bring in the next guy, who does his three plays. This year we had four great players before there was a drop-off in talent. We didn't play our 2s very much. We went with the 1s. They had to be in great physical shape and very disciplined to the ball. The game that won us the Big Ten championship came at Indiana. We had a defensive end get up off the ground, pursue to the other side of the field, pick up a fumble, and return it for a touchdown. He actually picked the fumble up off an Indiana player's back and returned it.

The evaluation of a coach is whether his players improve. I always got a little nervous if I had a player who wasn't better the second game than he was the first. If he doesn't improve as the season goes on, that comes back to the coach. If you are working that kid every day with reps and techniques, he should get better. I emphasize that we have to get better every game and our last game better be our best.

Repetition is the name of the game. The more you can rep it, the better you are going to be. It is monotonous and boring. We try to use different drills to break it up. Our guys must see a reach block a thousand times. They see the down block a thousand times. We do it a million times if they need it.

We feel that we have to do a great job with adjustment. We have to adjust during a game. I can remember two games in my coaching career where we didn't do a good job of adjusting, and we lost the game. After we saw the film, it was too late. We have to adjust in advance in the game.

We were fortunate this year. We had guys who were students of the game. If they were not in class, they were watching tape of the opponents or their drill work. There is no excuse for missed assignments. I've been places where we had to prod those guys to get them to watch tape. They have to execute. They have to know what their assignment is and execute it. There is zero tolerance for making a mental mistake. I've never heard Coach Pagac yell at a guy for making a physical mistake, as long as it was aggressive.

Football is not going to be all good. You are going to have some adversity. Some things are going to go wrong during the course of a game. The last thing I need are guys coming off the field complaining. Against Purdue the first two times they had the ball, they threw two bombs. It was 0-14 before we knew it. I've been around some teams when things started to get hairy and panic set in. That is where I give Coach Pagac and Coach Cooper credit. They didn't panic. We had three quarters to play.

One of the biggest distractions you can have is players playing for you who are always in trouble. When I get together with my players, I tell them, "Here is the policy. I don't set them. They are Coach Cooper's policies. I work for him, these are his policies, and you are going to follow them. If you don't want to follow them, you don't play." You get that ironed out at the beginning. If not you can have a coach-player problem when you have a game coming up. You don't need those distractions.

The last thing is this. I expect you to win and be excellent. If you want the same thing, then we will always be on the same page. You always have players who think that you are on them all the time. Just have them look at this philosophy. If they don't want the same thing, I'll get off them, but they won't play.

The next thing I want to do is go through what I call "10 vital techniques." If you are going to be a good defensive lineman, you better be able to do these 10 things. Number one, and probably the toughest thing to do, is stance. Stance is number one in my opinion. You have to get in a good football stance. We start

 





Page 84

out with the flat back, toes upfield, and good balance. As the season goes on, players get lazy. A good stance will make you a better football player.

In our base stance, or red stance, we are heel to toe in stagger. We want 60 percent of our weight on our hands. I want their backs flat enough that I could eat dinner off them. However, I want the butt up a little.

If I get into the green stance, I want the stagger elongated. The hand is a little more in front. The feet are a little closer together. We are in a sprinter's stance somewhat. That lets us explode off the ball and get upfield.

The next thing is alignment. This is something we do a lot of. Basically, I have four alignments. I have what I call head, split, shade, and foot. The head is aligned head on head. The split is splitting the man right down the middle. That means inside foot splits the crotch of the offensive man. The shade is my foot on his inside foot. I am not head up, but cheating a little to the outside. The last one is foot, which puts my inside foot on his outside foot. That is as wide as we get. This is a way to adjust the players' technique. If a player comes off and tells me he is getting reached, all I have to tell him is go to a foot alignment.

The distance off the line depends on the skill of a defensive lineman. The better he is, the closer he gets to the line. Our younger players start out back off the ball. That gives them time to read and keeps them from getting reached so easily.

The third thing is assignment. We call our two inside rushers power rushers. The two ends are speed rushers. We paint on our field our rush lanes. We run stunts and twists on these lines to make sure we stay in lanes as we rush the passer. I've seen a lot of great pass rushers who don't get too many sacks, but they get upfield in their lanes. That is what we emphasize. I start off with nothing but the bull rush. The pass-rush lane is their assignment.

The noseguard is the guy who gets beat up by the center and two guards in the pass rush. This year the noseguard could make a "me-you" call to the 3-technique tackle. That exchanged gaps and lanes in the pass rush. On the "you" call, the noseguard would step into the center-guard gap and then rush outside the guard. The tackle starts outside the guard, throws, and comes back inside in the face of the quarterback.

The fourth thing is get-off. This probably is the most important thing we do. We want the player's hand moving when the ball is moving. We have to get off on the football. That is the first thing we do in practice. We try to teach them to go on the movement of the ball, not sound. If you saw the Rose Bowl, we jumped on sound about three times, and I almost got fired. We watch the football.

We do a drill to work on this. A lot of the pro teams do this. We align our defensive linemen 5 yards away from the offensive linemen. All the offensive linemen do is back-paddle. The defensive linemen sprint to them as fast as they can. They just tap them. We make a little game of it. The defense has to tap the offense before he gets 10 yards. The loser does up-downs. The whole idea is the get-off.

The strike-and-blow delivery defeats the offensive blocker. If the blocker weighs 320 pounds and the defensive player weighs 250 pounds, if you take him on square, he will beat your ass. We have to change the direction of the offensive blocker. That is where the bend in the knees and strike up through the target come into play. When we hit the sled, I do a jab-dip-rip drill. They take two steps and jab the bag. I say dip. They have to bend their knees down as far as they can go. I let them sit there for about five seconds. That gives them the feel of bending their knees. Then I say rip. They explode up through the sled.

We want to put our eyes in the V of the neck. That is our aiming point. We want shoulder pad under shoulder pad. We try to bring the hands under both armpits, bend the knees, explode up through the neck, and lock up. We have to stop him on the line.

After the blow delivery comes extension of the arms. That leads to number 6, separation from the block. The

 





Page 85

defensive lineman has to lock out his arm. It has gotten so bad with the holding calls. The referee calls holding once a game, and that is it. In our league we are getting held every play. When we run scout teams in practice, I tell them to hold our defense.

The seventh vital technique is disengagement. That is throwing the linemen away to get off the block. We work really hard on throwing the man. If you try to move before you disengage, the blocker will be right on you. We run a simple drill to practice this. I have a blocker in front of me. The defensive man has his hand under the armpits and locked out. All I do is step one way or the other. The defensive man throws the offense off the other way. It's a simple drill.

The eighth thing is pursuit. This year we did a cut-up of every play that gained 10 yards or more against our defense. There were 27 plays in 11 games where the opponent gained 10 yards or more. Out of that 27, 6 were reverses, 5 of them were against our second team or others. That brings it down to maybe 17 plays in 11 games for 10 yards or more. I think that is pretty good. Our guys run to the football. We talk about relentless pursuit. We run a lot of pursuit drills. We constantly harp on it. The hardest thing I have to do is to get men on the line to run after a pass. To do this, the two most important things are attitude and effort.

The ninth thing is tackling. I'm not going to talk a lot about that. The one thing I will say is about fine-tune drills. We want to tackle a guy high from behind. That prevents a quarterback from throwing the ball, or it may cause a fumble. I do a drill at least once a week on this technique. Maybe it will happen in a game because I worked on it.

Another drill that is a fine-tuning drill is called the "bend drill." How many times have you seen a defensive end get a great pass rush, beat the blocker, but end up running by the quarterback because his momentum carried him wide? That is centrifugal force. We run a drill and time our ends on running around a cone and bending end. They have to dip their shoulder, touch grass, and run all the way around. We do it from both directions. That may be the skill that strips the ball from the quarterback.

One tackling drill I do every day is work on a quarterback breaking the pocket. I put a manager back behind a pass-rush drill. I use the quickest man I can find. We run our pass-rush drill, using our techniques to get free. Once we break free, the manager scrambles outside. I want them adjusting their paths and making an effort to get him. I don't want them to break free, break down, and sneak up on him. I want them coming hard. I call this the "brain drill" because as that defensive man is breaking free, I want him thinking that the quarterback is going to run somewhere. I don't want him to just rush upfield like a big lug. He has to think after he beats the block to get the quarterback.

These fine-tune drills are realistic. They are things that players are going to see in the games. I want them to have worked on them so they can handle them in the game.

The last thing is react to schemes. Every team that you play is going to be good at two or three schemes. We are going to identity the three or four things they do best. We are going to work like crazy on them that week in practice. Those are the 10 vital techniques we have to get across to our kids. When you put that all together, you have yourself a football player.

The most important part of the game to me right now is pass rush. You have to get pressure on the quarterback with your front four. I work four areas of pass rush. I work the get-off. That is the green stance. Next is the setup. This is the one I've tried to work on over the years. The next area is the move. You can use the rip, over, or bull rush. The last thing is the countermove. That is how I break down the pass rush.

Let me talk briefly about the setup. What we are trying to do is get you off balance. Every offensive coach in the country tries to keep his offensive linemen in a balanced frame all the time. When they are in that stance, they are good. It is hard to get around him when

 





Page 86

he is balanced up. We can't let him do that. For the first three series of every game, we are going to bull rush. The first thing I want that offensive lineman to do is to start to lean. If you hit that guy enough under his chin, he starts to lean into you. When he does, your pass-rush techniques are going to work.

When we bull-rush, we have to feel the offensive linemen. I saw this in a 49er camp. They blindfold their pass rushers. The offensive blocker retreats in the bull rush. At some point the offensive lineman has to make a stand to keep from being pushed back into the quarterback. When he locks back and leans into the defense is when we pull him forward and go around him. We don't use the blindfold, but we do close our eyes. It is the feel they have to recognize. The setup is the bull rush, which is designed to get the guy leaning forward.

With our 3 technique and noseguard, we use a head fake. All they do is jab to the outside, club to the inside, and rip underneath. We are trying to move the offensive linemen's weight to one side or the other. In the power rush, we work the offensive linemen to the outside of our pass lane, then rip and come inside. We do these things within the confines of our lanes.

Pass rush is attitude, effort, and toughness. Football is a physical game. We always look for those types of guys.

In the drill is where you have to be creative. This is where football gets boring. I have never had a player who liked individual drills. They want to scrimmage. The first drill that I like to use is a pass-rush drill. This is a toughness drill. Sometimes I have trouble with the head coach over this drill because it gets out of hand at times. I want to find out how tough he is, whether he wants to get to the quarterback, and whether he won't give up. It is also a good conditioning drill.

The drill has two offensive blockers on one pass rusher. The first lineman engages the defensive rusher at the line of scrimmage. The second blocker waits in reserve. If the rusher gets past the first blocker, he has to take on the second blocker. The first man now drops in back to take on the rusher again. The drill continues until the rusher gets to the quarterback or the whistle blows. If the rusher gets there, the offense has to do 20 up-downs. You can tell a lot about your players from this drill.

In this next drill I have an offensive man on a defensive man. I use two dummies and two backs on each. The ball carrier is in the tailback position. All blocks are base blocks. I point to the back who I want to carry the ball. On the snap of the ball, the offensive man tries to block the defensive man's butt off. The back runs through the bags. The defensive man has to use his vital techniques to get to the ball carrier. The first thing he has to do is neutralize the blocker. In the beginning, most of the defenders are trying to see which back is coming. They are not focused on the blocker and end up in my lap.

The first thing you are going to find out is where that defender is looking. I tell the offense that holding is legal, so he grabs the defender. If the offense can hold him, he didn't lock out. The young guy finally gets the lockout completed and goes for the tackle. The offensive man will bury him, because he did disengage. You can see all the mistakes from the results that occur. This drill teaches the progression to the tackle. We don't put the backs on the ground. We form tackle.

I can stand behind the drill and see all the vital techniques. We videotape this drill. The biggest problem they all have is trying to watch the ball carrier. This probably is the best drill we do. It covers everything.

I adjust the depth of the backs and the width of the dummies to the skill of the players. For the younger players, I tighten the dummies and move the ball carrier back. With our older players, I challenge them. I put the dummies wider and the backs closer. I determine that by how happy I am or how much I want them to do well or not so well. With the young guys, the ball carriers are about 8 yards deep. The great defensive linemen that I've had fight pressure naturally. They are instinctive. Wrestlers are good at that. I love to coach wrestlers on the defensive line because they have a knack for fighting pressure.

 





Page 87

The next drill I use is a repetition drill, working on schemes. I line up the guys across the field with offense on one side and defense on the other. I stand behind the defense and give them commands. I tell them they have their left arm free. They take two steps, getting the pad under pad. I check each of them as they go. It is rapid fire. In the course of five minutes I can get through base, reach, and cutoff blocks.

After that I work on low blocks. I work on everything that might happen to them in a game. Then we go to stunts. I tell them they have their left arm free, slant right. I coach the offense to do certain things to the defense. I tell them to scoop on their steps and watch the reaction of the defense. Then I line them up and we pass-rush. So in 5 to 10 minutes, I can get in all the situations that they see in a game.

This drill goes along with the bend drill. I put them on the line. They have to sprint upfield, bend around the cone, and dive on the dummy. I time this to see how fast they can get it done. There is nothing wrong with diving. They come around the horn and dive in on the dummy. That forces them to angle in.

When we play the low block, I want the eyes of the defensive linemen down on the blocker. What they want to do is hit them on the back. That is being lazy and not wanting to bend their knees. I want the hands in front of them, keeping him away from their feet. If the defensive guy is focused on the ball carrier, he'll get cut. If the blocker goes down, the defender goes down with him.

We don't see the double-team block much any more. The block we are seeing is the chip off to the linebacker. If the defender drops to the ground on a chip block, that is what the offense wants him to do. We stay on our feet until we feel pressure coming.

We don't break down when we tackle. If we break down on a good back, it won't make any difference. We probably aren't going to make the tackle anyway. I tell them to go get them. If we miss, make them readjust their movement. We are going to attack everyone. The only thing different is that the second guy in strips the ball. Our approach is to attack and go make it happen.

Let me show you the three fronts we run. This first one is the reduced front weak side. We shade the noseguard to the weak side. The ends are in a 5 and a 7 technique. The tackle plays a 3 technique to the tight-end side. We play with the Will linebacker to the weak side, the Mike linebacker in the middle, and the Buck linebacker to the tight-end side.

Our best run defense was what we called ''tight.'' We played the nose in a strong shade to the tight end. The tackle played a 2 technique to the split-end side. Our strong-side defensive end was in a 4 technique, and the weak-side was in a 5 technique. The Will linebacker played on the tight end. The Mike linebacker played a 3 technique strong. The Buck linebacker played a 2 stack to the weak side. The tackle slanted

 





Page 88

into the A gap weak. Mike and Buck linebackers fastflowed to the strong side, and the free safety played the weak-side B gap for the cutback. That was our 1st and 10 defense.

The Bear defense is probably our best defense. We played this a ton in the bowl game. As far as making things happen, this is our best defense. We have a good front five who got off on the ball. We twist our inside people and blitz like crazy from this. We played manfree in the secondary. The other thing we did off this was to show the Bear, drop out, and play zone. We slanted back to the strong side, and the defense ended up being the tight front. The Will linebacker would drop, and we played zone out of it. In the bowl game we played this with our nickel defense.

Gentlemen, thank you for your attention.

 





Page 89

Ralph Hensley

Multiple Defenses and Stunts

East Bank High School, West Virginia

It is a pleasure to be here with you. I hope we can make a few suggestions that may help you and your program. I have my staff with me today, and I want to introduce them. Neil Hopkins is our defensive coordinator. John Hopkins is our offensive and defensive end coach. Tommy Canterbury is our offensive and defensive backs coach. Jon Carpenter works with offensive ends, and Brian Massey is our quarterback coach.

We have been very fortunate at East Bank High School in that we have won four state AA championships in the last eight years. We are located in the Kanawha Valley, close to Charleston, West Virginia. We have about 450 students in the top three grades. East Bank may be better noted as the home of the famous basketball star Jerry West. Jerry is now general manager of the Los Angeles Lakers.

Before we start on our clinic topic, I would like to let you know that we know that there are other qualities that make you a winner. After our season is over, we sit down and have each player who is returning next year write down our team goals and his personal goals for the next football season. We feel this helps the players to understand their role on the team the next year. When we write down these goals, we always leave the "I" out. You do not find the letter "I" in team goals. When we talk with them as a group, we tell them how important it is to be better than they were last year. "You must be willing to put forth a lot of extra effort, and you must be coachable with discipline to win.'' Discipline is the key word. Discipline is important in our program if we want to have a chance to win. We feel the off-season is the best time to install discipline and create a feeling to treat everyone equally.

We believe in stressing the academics all year at East Bank High School. You cannot win if your players are in the stands and not on the field. I am sure most of you have heard the statement that athletes win games and coaches lose games. Let me tell you, if you are to win, then athletes must make the grade and be on that football field. It is your responsibility to get the players on the field. Our athletes are also recognized for their success in the classroom. We have a special page with the players' pictures in the football program for students who maintain a 3.0 or better GPA. They are also given special recognition at our assemblies. Our ultimate goal is someday to have a complete team picture on that 3.0 GPA page!

We also believe in our weight program. I am not an expert in the weight program, but I do know if you are bigger and stronger, you have a better chance to win. One has to understand that football is played from the chin up. If they have confidence in their strength, they are going to win for you. Athletes must have a good image of themselves and believe in what you are doing in your program. If you have any questions about our weight program, let me know after the lecture, and I will be glad to visit with you. We also weigh and measure our athletes at the start of the weight program. We weigh and compare them two times during the off-season to see if they are getting bigger and stronger. All of the athletes like to see their names on the weight charts, and they like to follow their progress. Again, recognition is a big part.

We have a few signs we like to stress. "UnityWinning is a weekly affair." We feel you must have a group effort. Painted on our locker room wall is this sign: "Bad boys of AA." Another sign we stress is this: "Pain is temporarypride is forever.'' We tell our players that we love them. We let them know that they mean a lot to our coaching staff.

Now, getting into the defensive game plan, we have what we call our nine commandments of defense. Let me cover them for you quickly. These points are based on our philosophy and our perception of the weaknesses and strengths of our personnel, and they are based on our players' ability to learn each week.

 





Page 90

Nine Commandments

1. Play your best players on defense. Defense is what wins championships. A good pitcher can shut down a good hitting team.

2. Gamble only for a purpose. This applies to gap control and reading defenses with man or zone coverages.

3. Defend the field. We may force the offense to go back to the short side of the field where we can use the boundary for help. We use man-to-man coverage a great deal of the time. This was especially true this past year because of the great speed we had in the secondary. We had three players who could run a 4.5, so we played a lot of man coverage.

4. Never get beat by our alignment. We try to keep our adjustments as simple as possible. We cover the types of blocks each player will see in the game and how we want them to defend against these blocks.

5. Go to automatic fronts on certain calls. Some offensive sets will predict what front we want to play. If you have better personnel, it will not make a lot of difference, but if you do not have better personnel, you may need to play certain fronts against some offensive sets.

6. Defend the obvious. We believe certain situations have a high probability for special type of plays. We must play the odds against these special plays.

7. Defend dangerous personnel. We like to take away the most dangerous threat while maintaining a general defense. Control is the key word. Never weaken your other areas in an effort to stop an outstanding player.

8. Defend what the offense does best. Tendencies are the key. Focus on what your opponents do best and their best players. By disrupting the offensive game plan, we can enhance our chances for success.

9. Keep the adjustments simple and easy. We must be able to adjust to unexpected formations. We simply call time out. We must be ready for shifts and motion. Big adjustments during a game are a gamble. You must make them for a definite reason. One key for us is to only use what we have had the opportunity to practice on and to perfect during practice.

As you know, you can only play with the players you have. This fact is very important in developing the defensive game plan.

Next, I want to get to our defensive front. I went to Marshall University and got a lot of help from Coach Tim Billings. want to cover the points Coach Billings went over with me.

1. The first thing to stress is position. It is important to stress stance and alignment.

2. You must learn to step to the gap. You need to learn to go from either hand.

3. The toe should be pointed straight ahead. Knees and feet should be straight. The shoulders should be pointed straight upfield and with a toe-to-heel relationship with the feet.

4. Foot back should be the foot to the shade man.

5. Start with two steps: Back foot first, right foot, step into man. Thumbs up, elbow straight, as if you are taking a picture.

6. Make a line six inches away. Don't step over the line. The foot replaces the hand. The hand comes with the foot. Hand above eyes, sit on heels, lift man up and out. Hips down, thumbs up, elbows in. Lead with the eyes and hands. Face into target, look for ball through gap, and feel pressure. Remember to have a comfortable stance, short step pick, a target area; punch press; slide and escape; don't turn your shoulders. Always teach hands first, feet second.

7. Three ways to control the gap are (a) my body, (b) his body (if he reaches me, knock him back), (c) my eyes (locate ball, pursuit drill).

 





Page 91

8. Hands go to target point; play side number. Hands above eyes; press and then slide; escape. Get off the block.

9. Target drill: Shuffle, right, left, step, hands, slide. Eyes are even or below the hands.

10. You may use the sled, but it is best body on body.

11. Use offensive lineman against defensive man. Don't use a snap count. Go on movement. You may have to go half-line if you do not have enough personnel.

12. Work against reach block: drive offensive man back; punch and press.

13. Work on press and squeeze: Get hands on them, punch-press, attack, then read. Don't turn shoulders. Anytime you get a down block, squeeze it. Don't get upfield; trap; use opposite arm.

14. On center fills to backside guard, punch center; drive him back; don't turn; keep shoulders square. If a person ever reaches you, get down the line of scrimmage.

15. On double team, step and punch; get shoulder down; turn to double team. Split double team.

16. In a 4 defense, you have to read the guard.

Now I want to get into the fronts. This is how we number our fronts. We line up in a 1 shade, 2 shade, or a 3 shade. We line up eye to eye on the offensive man. We number the offensive linemen from his right to his left. In a 1 shade, you are right eye to right eye. In a 3 shade, you are left eye to left eye.

We will always stunt from a 2 call unless it is a red call. The 
red
 means
that we will be in a certain type of defense for the offensive set that you are using.

The first number tells us our alignment as far as the type of defense. The next number is always for the line. The third number is for the secondary. If we end the call in a 1, it is man-to-man coverage. If we end the call in 2, we are going to play halves. If we end the call in 3, we are going to play zone coverage. We have tried to keep this simple for our defense. We do not number all of the offensive linemen with techniques as some people do on defense. We use the KISS principle. This is a 533 call. We can call 533, 532, or 531. We all play the 3 technique on the line.

This is a 523 call. All down linemen play the 2 alignment. We can call 523, 522, or 521.

This is our 513 call. All the down linemen play the 1 alignment. We can call 513, 512, or 511.

We call 523 slant right. Now all 3 down linemen line up in a 3 alignment and slant to our right.

The line should back off about six to eight inches to help on slants. We try to avoid contact from the offensive linemen. We want to break the line of scrimmage and find the ball. We try to rip the opposite arm under to the slant side.

We like to slant to the short side of formations. Anytime we are doing any stunting, we like to line up n a 2 technique. We do not want to get whipped. We want to make sure we have an equal chance to get to

 





Page 92

the inside or to the outside. We do number the gaps as most of you do: A, B, and C gaps.

One of our best calls has been our Y call. This is a loop technique for the tackle. The end is going to come down inside, and the tackle is going to take the quarterback.

We have some techniques where we always step with the foot that is to the shade side and follow with a short jab step with the trail foot. This is a six-inch punch step. With our hands under his pads, we roll the hips, thumbs up, head up, and tail down. This would be our 531 call.

We want to step to the offensive man, play gap control, and stay square with the line of scrimmage. You may use a three- or four-point stance. A three-point stance allows more movement laterally. A fourpoint is good against short yardage or pass rush. A four-point stance is good against teams that like to double-team a lot.

For a pass rush, change stance to sprinter stance. You are faster off the ball in a sprinter stance. We use the rip, hook-under, fake-and-go, and pull-and-tear techniques.

Against the trap, we must not get trapped. Anytime you do not feel pressure, we step down. Don't penetrate upfield. Wrong-arm trap if you can; if not, make a pile. Try to turn all traps into a sweep play. We want to make the other team run east and west, never north and south.

At times we call our red call, which is our auto-

alignment. We could call "red 1." This means we are going to go with a 2 technique to the formation side. We are going to shade the tight end and tackle in a 2 technique. We stack the linebacker.

We feel that we can protect the formation side to the strong side of the formation. We want to protect the linebacker by moving the down linemen. We can play a red 1, 2, or 3.

We can use a 40 front against multiple sets. This is our base alignment on our 40 base front. We still run the 40 front, and we still use the same type of calls.

We can use our calls from our 50 front while we are in our 40 defense. The call would be a 413. The 40 sets the defense. The 13 sets the rest of the defense. The 1 s the technique for the line, and 3 is our secondary coverage.

Against two tight ends we will go to our 60 alignment. We
may change some of our personnel, particularly

 





Page 93

inside over the guards, to help us inside. We may bring in our big people to help us inside on this defense. This would be our 63 look.

We can run our 523 alignment against two tight ends. We play our ends on the outside eye of the tight ends.

Also, we can play our 40 defense. This is how our 423

would look against two tight ends.

Here are our basic drills that we use to teach our techniques. We really stress these drills during the preseason. We do not run them as much in the regular season, but we do use them in warm-up. First is the shuffle drill. We go through the bags.

SHUFFLE
