Next, we step over the bags. We do not cross over; we keep the shoulders square, the head up, and the tail down.

STEP OVER

The third drill is what we call fill and search. We shuffle and then step up and over, looking for the hole between the bags.

Next is the mirror or follow-the-man drill. One man tries to mirror the other as they go over the bags. We are working on footwork and agility on this drill.

Next we do a front-to-front or back-to-back find-the-man and form-tackle drill. We do not want anyone to get hurt in the drill. We have both men lie down on their backs and then have them get up on a signal. They get up and move down the line of bags.

Next is our work-up-the-field drill. We do not want them to cross over with the feet. We work upfield.

We do a lot of rope jumping during the off-season. This also helps us with our agility.

We use bags to teach our defensive linemen different techniques. We prefer to use man-on-man instead of

 





Page 94

the bags, but we do use the bags. We start out on our knees and work from that position, so we can go from a upright stance. We teach the punch, rip, swim, and underhook techniques. If we use a live man, we can get more out of the drill after we have the techniques down.

Neil Hopkins, Our Defensive Coordinator, Will Go Over Alignments and Stunts. Coach Neil Hopkins

It is a great honor to speak at one of the best coaching clinics in the United States. I have attended this clinic for the last several years and have always come away with something that helped me become a better coach. It has helped us become a better football team. We feel that if we do not attend clinics, we are hurting ourselves and our team.

We have been very successful at East Bank High School because we have good athletes and they believe in us and what we are trying to do. Hopefully, I will be able to give you one new idea that each of you can use in your program.

We use a multiple defensive scheme to try to balance out against the many offensive sets we see. We try to cause as much confusion to the offense as we can. As you know, you must defend the whole field as well as you can. You must try to balance your defense to take away as many weaknesses as you can.

We had always been a 50 defensive team until 1993. We went to a 40 scheme but still liked the 50 because it has always been very good to us. When we started looking at other schemes, we tried to come up with a sound system that would allow us to play both defenses, without a lot of confusion or personnel changes. We depend on our Will backer to be able to play down in our 50 scheme or up in our 40 scheme. We line him up at nose when he has to be able to play the run and also be able to defend the pass as a backer in our 40 scheme.

We are a gap-control defense in both the 40 and 50 schemes. We try to attack the offense and force them into situations they do not like to be in. We feel that if you have the ball, you have stolen the royal jewels, and we want it back. We want every offense that we face to feel like a virgin in a house of ill repute. They know they are going to get it, but they do not know when or how bad.

I want to go over how we call our defenses and change our alignments. Coach Hensley talked about our alignment for our down linemen and how they move and stunt with our calls. I will cover the base 40 and some alignments and stunts that we use.

We play three basic coverages in our secondary. Our 1 call is man to man. The 2 call is two deep. The 3 call is our three-deep zone.

Our Mike, Will, and Sam backers get their alignment by our line call. If we are in a 40, the call will be 413, 423, or 433. The first number is what defense we will be in: 4 means 40. The second number sets our linemen: 1 shade, 2 head-up, 3 shade. The last number-1, 2, or 3-tells our secondary what they are playing. Here are some examples.

CALL 413

CALL 433

 





Page 95

CALL 423

CALL 513

CALL 533

CALL 523

We can change our secondary calls at any time. We may show three-deep and adjust into a man or a two-deep call.

Now I will show you a few of our best stunts from the alignments I have covered. Everyone here knows that when you stunt, you take a chance. We try to cover ourselves every way possible. We stunt for two basic reasons. We are trying to make something positive happen, or we cannot match up and we are trying to give ourselves a chance to win.

Our red-hot call in both the 40 and 50 schemes is an all-out blitz where we are bringing at least seven people. First is our 40red hot1.

40RED HOT1

Next we have a film to show on some of alignments and stunts. If you have any questions, we can talk later. Thank you.

Darren Hernandez

Philosophy and Organization

Campbell High School, Hawaii

I have a lot of notes that I will be referring to today. It is an honor to be here. I want to thank Rick Sakata for giving me the opportunity to speak. When I was asked to speak, I was apprehensive about speaking before such a large group. My wife told me to suck it up and to quit complaining. She said, "It will be a piece of cake." Some of you know my wife. She can talk. She has a bachelor's degree from Harvard and a master's from Yale. She is getting a doctorate from the University of Hawaii. Next month she will be speaking before 500 physicians at the National Cancer Institute in Washington, D.C. This is her topic: "The Role of Gap-Junctional Communication on Human Papilloma Virus-Induced Cervical Dysplasia." Wow! It makes my topic seem a little lame by comparison. Here we go.

In 19941 became coach at Campbell High School, which is my alma mater. I played football for Campbell High. Many of you know about our poor record when I was a player. We did not win one game. We were terrible. Our futility on the gridiron is well documented. We have been playing football since 1964. From 1964 to 1993 we only had four winning seasons. We had never won a play-off game.

People ask me how we turned the program around since 1994. We have had three winning seasons in a row. In 1994 we went 7-3, in 1995 we were 7-3, and in 1996 we were 9-3. The big question I get is why we have to lose three games each year. I do not know. A lot of it has to do with our schedule and our big rival Waianae High School.

To change the program, there were several things we had to do. The first thing we had to do was to set a vigorous weight-training program. We did this by making it mandatory for all football players to enroll in a seventh period weight-training class for credit. They come to class at 2:20 P.M., and they lift until 3:30.

The second thing we did was to demand discipline through changes in their behavior both on the field and off the field. To change any program quickly, you must have a foundation and a philosophy that you believe in. It must be a philosophy that will serve and guide you throughout your coaching career.

There are three elements of the Campbell High School program: philosophy, off-field activities, and on-field activities. I will hit practice organization later, but first I want to talk about our philosophy.

First, treat kids fairly. By this we mean what 
we 
think is fair. It could be unfair to someone else. Be honest and consistent. We deal straight up and tell it like it is. Kids can tell if you do not tell the truth.

Second, we must coach with enthusiasm. If you are not excited, don't expect the players to be. Is football fun? Winning is fun. If you are losing, it is not fun; however, you cannot guarantee winning. It is no fun if you are in a fight and someone is beating the crap out of you.

Third, we want to play the best 11. That means both ways if needed. You want to get the kids to a position where they have a chance to win. As coaches, we must do our utmost to prepare them for the chance to win. We tell our players to play hard. We do not buy the excuse that they are tired. This is not basketball or some other sport where you can raise your hand and come out of the game. We do not care how tired you are; you are going to play. You are going to do it for the team. We have found that no football player has ever missed a school dance or a chance to party after the game. After the game they find a lot of energy. If they can play two ways, they do.

This is our philosophy:

1. We want to physically dominate our opponent. We want to do it quickly. We go for it in the first quarter. We want to plant the seed of doubt in them that we are better and more physical than they are.

 





Page 98

2. We want to outcondition our opponents. We try to beat teams in the second half or fourth quarter. We always want to be in better condition than the other team. It is not only physical condition, it is mental condition. We define mental toughness as the ability to maintain physical toughness for a long period of time. If you are only physical for one quarter, you will not win. If you are physical for four quarters, then you are mentally tough.

3. We want to generate emotion. These are kids. Get them to love each other. Sell them on being a family. We tell them to back their football family.

4. We expect total commitment from everyone associated with the program. We do not want people on the side talking crap about us. If someone has a problem, they need to come and talk to us.

We do not tolerate any excuses from anyone. We tell the kids not to listen to parents, friends, or others that don't know crap about football. We run the team. Listen only to the coaches.

At Campbell High School we develop athletes three ways:

1. Academically. We preach self-discipline, responsibility, self-motivation, and accountability. We tell kids to do the right things. They must go to class, make good grades, etc. However, we are not going to hold their hands to class. We provide guidance and study halls and any help they need, but they have to ask for it. We cannot bail them out for the rest of their life. We do not treat them any differently from the rest of the student body.

2. Socially. We stress being a good person. Be polite and respectful of others. Respect your family and teachers. Don't cheat or lie or do anything to embarrass the school, your family, the coaches, or your teammates. Pride is doing the right thing when nobody is watching.

3. Athletically. We stress being the best you can be. Not everyone has natural talent, but if you work hard and build upon what you have, you will be successful. A strong work ethic will carry over into other areas of life. Go all out, or get the hell out! The three stripes of a Campbell Saber are pride, poise, and courage.

You need money to buy a lot of different things for the football team. There are a lot of hidden costs involved with a football team. Our A.D. buys us jerseys and other equipment, but we still have to raise money. At Campbell we have a booster organization called the Sabers Football Club. The head coach has to be ultimately responsible for everything. The buck stops with you. You can have booster members, including parents, friends, or wives, but you do not turn over the program and let the parents run things. They will turn it into a monster. Make it clear that the are working under the head coach. There are a lot of areas in which booster clubs can help. This is how they help us.

1. Football banquet 

2. Spring football game 

3. Souvenir and T-shirt sale 

4. Kickoff night 

5. Newsletters 

6. Homecoming 

7. Passing league 

8. Helmet awards 

9. Highlight video 

10. Power-lifting meet 

11. Finances 

12. Fund-raising

The spring game has been dropped because of the High School Athletic Association. We used to make $2000 just from the spring game. We are thinking about having a passing league spring game. We may just ask them to donate a dollar at the game.

We have the kickoff night just before we start the three-a-day practices. We invite all of the parents, and we feed them. We show the highlight video from the year before. We talk about all of the forms they have to fill out. We try to get them pumped up by talking

 





Page 99

about the upcoming season. We try to get them excited. I bring out the equipment and tell them how much it cost.

We send them two newsletters, one in the summer before practice starts and one at the end of the season. In the first letter we tell them what they need to do to get ready for the season. I tell them about camp and what we have planned for the year.

Let me talk about practice organization. Many of you have asked me about our practice schedule that coordinates both the JV and varsity together into one practice.

1. JV and varsity practice together. They do the same drills and use the same terminology, same plays, and the same coaching staff for the last four years.

2. The JVs exist for the sole purpose of developing players for the varsity. If a player develops rapidly while on the JVs, he may be called up anytime during the season or postseason. We are not trying to win the JV championship. That is not our goal, but if we do, it is a bonus.

3. Our practice is split up. We designate one side of the field as offensive and the other side as the defensive side.

4. Everything in practice has to be a progression. Everything should logically build on the preceding principle or concept that you were teaching before. At Campbell, we feel that practice is one of the most important aspects of our program.

5. Our practices come down to these four principles:

a. Organization. Based on scouting information, every adjustment and contingency imaginable will be covered. Every minute of practice will be used wisely. All coaches are responsible for completing the teaching within the required time. If it is a 10-minute period, then it is the coach's responsibility to teach the material in that time frame. Be overprepared. Team morale is achieved when your players truly feel they are prepared.

b. Performance. Simulate playing conditions. Don't do nonsense drills that just take up time. Be sure that they have a specific purpose and that each player is given the chance to perform his fundamentals, techniques, and assignments as a football player. Be sure the things that are important to you in August are still important to you in November. Always stress fundamentals and techniques.

c. Precision. Again, everything that we will be doing in the game will be practiced precisely. If we want to add three plays on a given day and we are incapable of doing it precisely, then we practice just the one or two plays that we were able to get in, and we will do those precisely. When we beat Kailua with 19 seconds remaining on homecoming, it was not a desperation pass from Kaleopa to Acidera that won it, but it was a play that had been rehearsed and practiced time and time again. Everyone knew exactly where they had to be. If you are not performing your plays precisely during practice, then you will not perform them precisely during the game.

d. Evaluation. If you do not evaluate and constantly upgrade your practices, you will never know what your problems are or how to fix them. Analyze and rate your practices. Film them if possible. Coaches need to give input about what they feel is working and not working. Every coach needs to know exactly what his role is and what he has to do next. Coaches should meet every day after practice to go over what has happened at practice. Are we doing too much of this? Not enough of that? Are we too negative? Are we too soft? What can we do better? This process may be the most important aspect of practice.

6. To us, enthusiasm is a prerequisite. Our coaches are very vocal, but not in the negative sense. They are constantly encouraging the players and are on top of things. We will not tolerate anything less than the players' best efforts and are always ready to encourage or pick them up with encouraging words.

 





Page 100

7. One final note about how you approach an opponent on the week of practice and the game. Be careful when prejudging an opponent. When Lou Holtz talked about an opponent prior to the game, he would pump them up and make them greater than they actually were. You have to be careful with high school kids when you do that. While we never want to underestimate an opponent, it can be equally wrong to overestimate them. By doing so, we concede certain things that they have not earned. We tell them we are going to prepare for the game.

We tell them that we must believe that we are going to win the game. It is not how badly you are going to win, but we are confident, we have prepared, and we have tried our best in practice. We are united as a team, we came here to win, and that is what we want to do. If you lose, you know that you tried your best. When you leave, you never hang your head.

Let me cover our coaches organization and how we divide the responsibilities in our program.

JV HEAD COACH

4. Contingency game plan

5. Defensive play book

2. Organize position drills

3. Special teams game plan

4. Contingency game plan

5. Special teams play book

Here is our three-a-day practice schedule. We do not give them time to do crap. It is a warrior mentality. If you come to our three-a-days, it means you have given up the summer and the good times to work on football. We do not have sleep-overs. We do not have the facilities to stay overnight. They have to get there early. This is how our day goes.

THREE-A-DAY PRACTICE SCHEDULE

7:30-8:00-Breakfast/clean-up

8:00-8:30-Prepractice: Bring out cones, bags, balls, etc.

8:30-11:30-Practice no. 1

11:30-12:00-Change/get ready for lunch

12:00-1:00-Lunch/clean-up

1:00-1:30-Free time (coaches meet)

1:30-2:15-Practice no. 2: Offense lift; defense on field

2:15-3:00-Practice no. 2: Defense lift; offense on field

3:00-3:20-Free time (coaches meet)

3:20-3:30-Prepractice: Bring out cones, bags, balls, etc.

3:30-6:30-Practice no. 3

When our guys go home, they are dead tired. They go home and go to bed. They know they have to be back early. That is a typical three-a-day practice schedule.

 





Page 101

Let me set the football schedule after school starts. I will talk about individual activities as we go through the schedule. Our first period is the seventh period at 2:30 P.M. when we have study hall or lift weights. That is where we have our lectures and films and weights. Then at 3:15 we tape and get dressed. Each schedule is set up like this.

DATE
DRESS 
OPPONENT
TIME
VARSITY
ACTIVITY          JV

As you can see, we have the varsity listed on the left side and the JVs on the right side. The next session is the special period. We have all of the punters, snappers, kickers, and return men. They report 15 minutes before practice starts. We have 30 minutes for our special period. A lot of people think that is a long time. It is important to us. We do not just give it lip service. It is important to us, so we work on it. We also use this period as an individual time for the non-kicking people.

Next is our Saber period. That session is for 15 minutes. Then we have our block A session for 20 minutes. We have three sessions on the A block. Then we switch blocks and work the same amount of time on block B. Next we have 15 minutes of conditioning, and it is pure hell. Then we finish with our postpractice. We also open the weight room for those who want to lift. Then the coaches have our meeting.

At the bottom of our practice schedule, we have several items that are helpful to our staff. We list the players that might be ineligible, injuries, players who did not dress that day or were absent or tardy, and other comments. Then we have opportunities listed. That is our punishment list, but we call it an opportunity list.

On the next sheet we have each activity listed in the practice. For example, we have everything listed that we plan to cover in our special teams session. Everyone is involved. Special team players are on one side of the field, and all others on the other side. They are working on individual work while the special team does their thing.

We do not do all of the kicking game in one day. We try to punt every day, at least one or two. We work on normal punts, pressure punts, kicking the ball into the end zone, and all other situations that we need to work on. We work on some phase of the kicking game every day, four days per week.

Our Saber period is this. It is my favorite period. I carry a horn in my pocket. I do not use a whistle. When I blow the horn, they know it is time for the next period. The Saber period is a walk-and-talk session with the entire offense on one side of the field and the defense on the other side. We use this on Saturday mornings and on Mondays. We go over mistakes and other things we are working on. It is a walk-and-talk session.

Next, we go to form tackling and block stations. We all talk about the game being a game of fundamentals, or blocking and tackling. We practice it every single day. We do blocking and tackling stations. We have forced them to do it. It may be hitting a sled or driving the sled, but we are going to do it.

The question is asked, How do we incorporate a player who plays both offense and defense?

In the block A period, we have the varsity working on offense and the JVs working on defense on the other side of the field. All of the varsity players are on the offensive field-all of them. The defensive starters are playing the scout team. We do not practice against a bunch of scrubs. We are running against our first defense. They are the scout team. They are getting some things done, but it may not be the exact things they would be doing on our defense. We will change it up and alternate players in and out.

We run nine-on-seven drills. Some of you may call it skeleton drills or combo drills. Only the first and second teams get reps, with 70 percent of the reps going to the first team and 30 percent to the second team. Everyone else must play on the scout team. We work our first receivers against our first DBs. The second-team quarterback will throw to his receivers against air.

 





Page 102

We have 20 to 25 plays written down on a script sheet. It is a nine-on-seven drill script sheet, and we run our plays in sequence. We list 20 to 25 plays to work on that day. We may not get all of them off. Each coach has the list of plays we are working on. On our sevenon-seven drill, we do the same.

TEAM OFFENSIVE SCRIPT

Team                             Date

No. Hash/Down & Distance

Formation/Play/Offense

1. Across the page

2. Down the page to 25

During this period the 0 line and D line will be working on run blocking for the first 10 minutes. For the second 10 minutes they do pass blocking. It is a drill for the offense. This is where the defensive coach works on new things with his players. Everything is offense in this period.

Then we go to period 3. This is our team period. Now we work against the defense that we expect to see. We do everything by situations. We have the areas of the field covered. We work on being backed up, in the danger zone, and on the one-yard line. We have to be prepared to come out of our end zone.

We practice as if we are going in to score, and when we are in the red zone, we work on short yardage, say, 3rd and 2. We work on ball control and controlling the clock. We practice motion and any special play. Also, we work on our two-minute offense.

We have a team offense script. All of our offensive coaches have this list. We work on all of the situations. We list the number of the play, the hash, down and distance, formation, play, and against what defense. We list special situations and goal-line plays. We work against all situations that we feel we need to cover for the game that week. We try to get them all into that time frame of 20 minutes.

Now we honk the horn and go to block B. Now all of the JV players will come together and run the offense. All of the varsity will play offense against the JVs. If you have a player who wants to play both ways and he has been working on offense, now he can go and work with the defense. He will get the same amount of time. If he is a first-team offensive player, instead of playing scout team during the first defensive emphasis, he is with the defense working on plays.

We switch after 20 minutes and work JVs on defense and the varsity on offense. We do the drill all over again. The coaches never leave. They work all of the JVs, and they coach all of the varsity. They stay on one side of the field, and the players switch.

Next we go to seven-on-seven again, but now we are working on defense. We work one-on-one and go over our coverages.

The last part of the defense sessions is our team defense. This is where we work on our stunts and variations. Again, we have the plays scripted that we want our defense to work on that particular day.

The conditioning drills are individual. We break up with individual coaches by positions. Each group works on conditioning.

Next, we have a postpractice session. An opportunity is not a good thing for our players. If they are one second late, they get one opportunity. If they are more than 15 minutes late, they get two opportunities. At the conclusion of practice, we line everyone up on the sideline. We call the roll of opportunity victims. They come out and go on the goal line. The team is on the sideline, and they are not going to say a word. It is punishment with the whole team watching. Everyone knows they must pay the price equally, no matter who they are. The team will encourage them during the punishment. One opportunity consists of a 200-yard sprint followed by a 200-yard jog and drop, followed by a 200-yard bear crawl, followed by a 200-yard roll. That is 
one 
opportunity. After one, they never want to do one again. They make a real effort to be on time.

If you want, I will send you a copy of our practice schedule. Drop me a line. Thanks. Again, I want to thank Rick Sakata.

Glynn Jones

Defensive End Techniques

University of Louisville

It is a pleasure to be here. I feel fortunate to be at a school such as the University of Louisville. It is an honor to coach for Ron Cooper. Ron is a good coach and a good person. I think he has a tremendous staff and support system. We have a training room that is great. I can't say enough about the job Ray Ganong does with our players in the weight program. I think we had a good season last year, and a lot of credit goes to what they were doing in the weight room with our players. If you get a chance to stop by our weight room, make sure you visit with Ray Ganong and his staff. They really do a good job with our off-season program.

First, I want to go over our defensive staff with you. Our defensive coordinator is Everett Withers, and he coaches the secondary. Rock Roggeman coaches the defensive tackles. Sal Sunseri coaches the linebackers. I coach the defensive ends. Together we have all been able to put our thoughts and ideas into the defense. We all bring something different to the table. I think that has helped us overall as a defense.

I have a few stats on how our defense did this past season. The Cardinal defense has been ranked among the top 25 nationally in scoring defense in each of the past two seasons, including a number seven finish in 1995. In the last 22 games, the defense has allowed an average of 16.8 points per game. This past season, our defense held opponents to an average of 235.8 yards per game, good for fifth among NCAA Division I teams. Actually, after the bowl games we were fourth. First in defense was Florida State, then North Carolina, Virginia, and then Louisville.

We held opponents to 96.7 yards per game in passefficiency defense to finish 13th overall on defense. We do stress stopping the run at Louisville. We talk each day about how to stop the run. We stress this to our kids, and we have been successful in doing that. We allowed opponents an average of 81.1 yards per game on the ground to finish fourth nationally. Senior Sam Madison, our defensive back, received first team All-American honors. He has a great chance to be drafted as a number one pick in the NFL. He had six interceptions in 1996. Another defensive leader for us was linebacker Tyrus McCloud. He had 144 tackles in 1996, and he too has a great shot at the NFL.

Our defense had a school-record 45 sacks in 1996. Senior defensive end Carl Powell had 11. Seven of the sacks came in a two-week period against Memphis and North Carolina. I will talk about some of the defensive techniques he used to get those sacks.

Next I want to share with you our defensive goals for the year. What do you want to do to be successful? We keep the goals on a chart and keep them updated after each game. This is what we wanted to achieve in 1996.

1996 Defensive Team Goals

Each of these headings is listed at the top of the chart. What we feel we need to do to win is listed under the heading. We have our schedule down the side of the chart, and we fill the columns in as the season progresses.

WIN

·
Points allowed.

·
Hold opponent to 14 points per game.

RUSH DEFENSE

·
Hold opponent to 150 yards or less per game.

·
Hold opponent to 3.3 yards average per game.

PASS DEFENSE

·
Hold opponent to 150 yards or less per game.

·
Hold opponent to 6.0 yards average per attempt.

·
Hold opponent to under 60 percent completion per game.

 





Page 104

TURNOVERS

*
Cause three or more fumbles per game.

*
Turn the ball over at least two times per game.

*
Turn the ball over to the offense at least one time on the plus-50-yard line.

*
Intercept one out of every seven pass completions.

TOTAL DEFENSE

*
Hold opponents to 300 yards total offense per game.

*
Hold opponent to 33 percent or less 3rd down efficiency.

*
No long runs over 15 yards.

*
No long passes over 25 yards.

You can see the areas where we did well and the areas where we did not do well. We use these goals to set up our defense for the season. We stress to our defense that we must get to the football. We ask them this question. What must we do to win? This is what we came up with.

How we will win on defense? Do not allow a long pass completion for a touchdown. Do not allow a long run for a touchdown. Intercept passes and return them for yardage and touchdowns. Force the opponent to fumble.

We stress the fact we must play great goal-line defense. We feel that if we can do that, we have a chance to win. We stress fundamentals on defense. This is what we stress with our players: Shoulders square; proper arm free; pads over our toes; keep a base; don't run around blocker; do not give them anything but pads, bones, and elbows; stay on your feet.

We start with our general techniques. We start every thing from a base. This is what we stress: stance, alignment, key ball to man, responsibility.

This past year we played three different defensive fronts. The first defense we teach is what we call our load 53 defense. We have a tackle call to the tightend side. The tackle and end to the tight end are going to line up in a 3 technique and a 7 technique. On the split-end side we have a tackle in a shade technique and the end in a 5 technique. Our Sam linebacker goes to the tight-end side. The Mike linebacker is on the outside shoulder of the center. The Will linebacker is stacked inside the offensive tackle and behind our 5technique end. As you can see, we are based out of the 4-3 alignment. That is our base defense. We do a lot of other things out of this defense.

LOAD 53

The next defense we look at is the over 53 look. Our call-side end is in a 9 technique, and the backside end is in a 5 technique.

OVER 53

Between the load and over calls, only two players changed their techniques. The only two people to change are the call-side end and the Sam linebacker. The end steps out to a 9 technique, and the Sam linebacker steps inside. If we call 
load, 
the call-side end knows he is in the 7 technique. If we call 
over, 
the end is in a 9 technique.

We are a one-gap defense up front. The tackles are going to work together. The tackles know that if we are in load, the offside tackle is responsible for the B gap and the backside tackle is responsible for the A

 





Page 105

gap. The ends know that if we call load, the call-side end is responsible for the C gap. He is a C-gap player.

Our linebackers are responsible for two gaps. They have a primary gap and a secondary gap. In our load, the Mike linebacker is an A-gap player. The Sam linebacker is a D-gap player, and the Will linebacker is a B-gap player. We are a four-spoke secondary. We have two safeties and two corners. We will always give you that look. If we go to our over 53, the look will stay the same for our secondary. What we are doing is nothing hard. We try to keep everything simple. Those are the two fronts we ran against pro action. If the offense came out in two backs, one tight end, and two receivers, we call that look 
regular personnel 
When we see that set, that is what you are going to get from our defensive front. If you come out in what we call H formation, which is two tight ends with one back and two wide receivers, we get into more of a base look.

When we call load G, this tells the backside tackle he is going to move from a shade to a 2 technique. Our backside end is in a 5 technique. This is good against the bootleg reverse. I will cover that later.

On the isolation play, the offside tackle is going to get a drive block. The first block that the end has to learn to defeat is the drive block. He has to step up and knock the tackle upfield. He keeps his outside leg and arm free and forces the ball inside.

5 TECHNIQUE VS. DRIVE BLOCK

The fullback is going to lead on the Will linebacker. He steps up and takes on the fullback with his inside arm and leg. He forces the ball back inside for the Mike linebacker. The Mike linebacker is playing two gaps. He is responsible for the strong-side A gap, and he is also responsible for the backside B gap on flow away. I want to talk about two drills that I do that help the ends take care of the men in front of them. [Demo] The first drill I will talk about is our explosion drill. We have to be able to attack that man in front of us. He needs to know what to do if that offensive tackle knocks him off the ball. He has to be able to step back and strike again.

We start off in a two-point stance with a slight bend in our ankles, knees, and hips. The head is up. As the man steps toward me, all I want to do is explode and put my face mask inside with my head up and get my hands inside on the breast plate. If you have a two-man sled, you can use it to work on the drill. If you do not have a sled, take a player and have him hold a hand dummy. Teach him to explode into the dummy. Teach him to strike a blow in a good football position. We call this pads-out drill.

We want to teach them to come up with the ankles, knees, and hips and explode into the man and push him off of me. We want to teach him to play with pads up. He has to learn to step up and hit the blocker and knock him back off the ball. All you need is three or four reps a day working on pads-out. It is best to start the drill with a stationary machine. He does not need to move his feet. You are looking for explosion and the power move.

We teach the drill a little differently than most people. Most people start from a three-point stance. We start out teaching this technique from a two-point stance. Once he learns to hit the sled and to explode and get his knees and hips into the drill, we teach him to step up and strike from a three-point stance. We call this our pads-out drill.

We have a defensive checklist. There are a lot of things to teach the defensive ends. We do not make this list very difficult. This is very simple. It is a good thing for me to review. Each day I go over certain drills. I do not do everything on the checklist. We do some of these drills every day. We talk about these drills from the two-, three-, four-, and six-point stance.

Next, I want to talk about the skate drill. In this drill we do basically three things. I will demonstrate these

 





Page 106

three things. [Demo: lock out and separate, lock out and rip, and lock out and beat the reach block]

I start the defensive end in a good football position. His hands should be on the outside to start out. We always talk about hand and eye placement. We want the hands inside on the breast plate. His palm is up, and we are in the V of the neck. If we have inside gap, we only work on one thing. We pull with the inside arm and lock out with the outside arm. We just turn the shoulders.

After that, we need to go upfield with the man. We pull on the outside and lock out. As we do that, we are putting a step into the move. It is a quick step. We are trying to get the players to understand leverage on the blocking. After doing some research, I found out that our kids need to know and understand pressure points and leverage to play good football. [Demo]

The next phase of the drill is lock out and rip. We do the same as before, but now we rip and come upfield three yards. We want to keep the shoulders square. We run the drill so each player gets one or two shots on it.

The next drill is against the reach block. Now we want him to lock and reach. He is in a good football position, and he is inside. The offensive man has already reached the defensive man. The football is going to the outside. Now we want to pull the man back inside. We want to use his weight and leverage. We want to throw him back inside and get off the block. If you are playing the 5 technique, you do not know where the offensive man is going. You must react to what he is doing.

5 TECHNIQUE VS. REACH BLOCK

Let me go back to the basic drive block again. As the offensive man comes at our end, he is in a three-point stance. My inside foot is going to be on his outside foot. I have to step up on him. We hit him in the V of the neck, keep the hands inside, and drive him back upfield. If you get movement and knock him back upfield, then the offense does not have a chance. You are forcing the ball inside. We do not want them going inside too quickly. We know that tailback can squirt back outside. We know all linebackers are going to scrape to the ball.

On the toss sweep, the 5-technique end must make his first step upfield. He has to know how to react against that blocker. That blocker is trying to get to his outside number. We want to string the play out wide. We do not want the ball going north and south. We have the safety who is going to fill inside. Also, we have a Will linebacker who is going to fill. We want our end to get outside and make the play.

The next block we are going to see against our 5 technique is the down block. This is the block we see on the inside zone play. I want the end to step upfield with that first step. He sets his feet with the second step. This gets him going. He wants to keep his shoulders square. The offensive tackle blocks down inside. I tell our ends to choke down and to find out what is going on. I want him to drag that back leg two steps. If the ball is inside, the end reads the mesh point for that quarterback. His eyes should be on that quarterback. The quarterback is going to give the ball to the fullback, or he is going to keep the ball on the bootleg, or it is going to be a reverse play. Most teams like to run the zone play and cut back into the C gap. We want to take that away from the man with the ball. As the blocker steps down, we want to step up. We want to punch the tackle inside. Now we are going to drag our feet and keep our shoulders square to the line of scrimmage. They can see everything from this position. I tell them to train their eyes to keep the shoulders square. The first thing he should expect is the fullback on the inside zone play.

5 TECHNIQUE VS. DOWN BLOCK

 





Page 107

The first person we tell the end to look for is the pulling lineman coming down the line. If the offensive lineman is off the line, it will probably be some kind of bootleg or waggle play. If he sees that, he heads upfield to try to contain the quarterback. If the man is pulling down the line, we want our end to occupy the hole and squeeze the play down. We want to attack the inside shoulder of the blocker and push him back upfield. This will allow our Will linebacker to scrape over the top and make the stop. If our defensive end does not get under the pulling guard, we have trouble.

DOWN BLOCK/KICK-OUT/COUNTER

That allows the tackle to get down on the Mike linebacker. They have a chance to get the play away. Will is going to scrape off outside. The Mike linebacker is filling inside and plays the football.

The next play that the 5 technique looks for is the bootleg reverse. If the fullback does not have the ball, he looks for the reverse. We ask our end to keep his shoulders square to the goalpost. We want him to make a straight line and get up the field. We do not want him to try to get outside. We want him to go straight upfield. We want to make the football bounce wide. We want to make the football bounce deeper and wider. Hopefully, we will have some help, and they will make the play. We want to make the ball bounce deep and wide and let our pursuit catch the ball. If we can do that, we will be in good shape.

Those are the reads we do every day in practice for the 5 technique. This is the bread and butter of playing defensive end. We do not switch our ends. We keep them on the same side. We play the techniques the same.

5 TECHNIQUEBOOTLEG REVERSE

The next thing I want to talk about is our 7 technique. I feel that we play the 7 technique pretty well. Again, the inside hand is down, and the outside foot is back. He lines up on the inside neck of the tight end. Everything we talk about starts with that first step. The tight end comes off to drive-block the 7 technique. We do the same things that we did against the offensive tackle when we were in the 5 technique. We want to step up and use our hands to get inside the V of his neck and drive him back up the field. We do key inside. Some teams key the offensive tackle. We feel that the tight end will tell us what is going on. There are only three or four things he can do. He can reach inside to try to cut us off, he can drive-block us, he can release outside on a pass route, and he can sit back and block for some type of play like the draw. If our end can understand the progression and know what is coming off, he will be in good shape.

Again, it is the same as the 5 technique against the tackle on the drive block. We want to step up, punch, get our hands inside, and lock the tight end out. We want to find out what is going on with the ball. If the football is inside, we want to close off the C gap. He is a C-gap player.

7 TECHNIQUE VS. COUNTER

 





Page 108

Let's go on to the zone play. The next big read he is going to get is the counter off tackle. This was a big read for us this year. The tight end would step up and get our end into a drive block. He would punch to the inside. As he was doing that, the offensive tackle was taking a zone step to get outside of our end so he could reach him. That is a tough block for the tackle, especially if the end is a player. We want our end to explode and make a straight line up the field. We want to attack the end as he releases. We do not want him to release to get to our Sam linebacker. We try to hold him up from going inside. If we can do that, it will take the tackle and end to block our end. Our Sam linebacker can make the play on a scrape to the outside. Also, our Mike linebacker is scraping to that side. The zone play may go five yards outside. If we can make the play go back inside, we are happy. If the play stretches outside, the C gap also stretches. The same is true for the B and A gaps as they stretch. Everyone has to get into their gaps and stretch the play. As the play cuts back, the pursuit will make the play.

7 TECHNIQUE VS. OUTSIDE ZONE

The next thing I want to talk about is playing the draw and screen plays. Every day we talk to our ends about these plays. If that offensive tackle gives our end a high hat and the tackle tries to punch him upfield and opens his hips, he knows something is wrong. We need to learn how to play the draw. As the end comes off the ball, most tackles are going to hunch back and try to knock the end upfield. I tell the end that he has to stop in his tracks. If he sees the tackle and sees the draw, he has to retrace his steps and come back down inside and make the play. If our end does get upfield and then he sees the draw, we want him to retrace his steps straight back down the field. If he can do that, he probably will make the tackle at the line of scrimmage, or the play may bounce outside. Next, I want to talk about what I call all of the unusual plays that the defensive end is going to see from a 7 technique. This is something you have to put into your practices and work on. We work on the screen with our 5 and 7 technique. If the tackle tries to chop us as we get upfield and the quarterback continues to give ground, then something is going on. The end has to develop some instincts. If he feels that the screen is developing, he must get downfield. We want to plant and move our eyes to the outside. We are looking for a receiver coming back down inside. He may be able to break the play up if he can get there in time, or he may be able to make the tackle as soon as the receiver catches the ball.

We work on the draw and screen every day. We take a football and have them in their positions. We simulate the quarterback on a drop. If the quarterback drops the ball down, we have them step and plant the inside foot and open back to the inside. He retraces his steps, looking back inside. We want him in position to make the play. If the quarterback drops deeper, the defender should know it is a screen. One of the first plays we are going to check for is the screen. If the outside screen is not there, we look for the middle screen. We work this drill every day. In the course of the game, when they run the screens, you have to be able to react to them. Because we have covered what to do, we feel we have a chance to be successful.

Next, I want to talk about the pass rush. The thing we want to do is to key the football and movement. We are in a three-point stance. When we are in the pass rush mode, we want to close our base stance. We use more of a sprinter's stance. We tell him to get into the most comfortable stance. We want him to be able to rush upfield. We want him to be able to rush the quarterback. We want our rush people to know the down and distance on each play. We want them to attack half the man as they get upfield. We always want to have a game play. I tell our players that we have a first move and then we have a countermove. I am going to go over three types of pass rush. I want to cover the rip, swim, and spin techniques.

The first technique I will cover is the speed rush. It is 3rd and 10, and we have to get to the quarterback. I

 





Page 109

want the end out wide in a sprinter's stance. I ask our end to get to three yards right behind the offensive tackle. We can run by the tackle for that four-yard stretch. We can beat the tackle to that point. If we can beat them on the speed rush, we can get to the quarterback. That is the speed rush. Use your speed to beat the blocker.

Next, we talk about attacking one half of the man. If the tackle gets to the spot and we get tied up with him, we want to be able to beat him. Once the blocker gets his shoulders turned, you have something you can work with. We work on the outside half of the man. What I am talking about is basically a rip move. [Demo] We step with the outside foot, rip with the inside arm, step through the man, and go get the quarterback.

The next move we talk about is the swim move. Now, I bring the arm up and bring the elbow up inside. This will clear you, and you can get to the quarterback. [Demo]

We have the tackle so concerned because we have beaten him to the outside. Next, we want to get to the bull-rush move. We get our face mask into the man and drive him back into the quarterback. The feet never stop moving. I want them to perfect their moves and be able to use their best move in a game.

The next move is the spin move. As we make contact, we want to take the inside foot and step up and then spin. As we spin, we want to make sure that we are by the man. If you don't, he can step back inside and pick you up. The spin move and countermoves have been good for us.

I will go over three pass-rush stunts with you. We do not do a lot of different stunts on the pass rush. First is what we call the Texas stunt. The tackles penetrate first, stepping inside and then slanting outside into the face of the offensive man. The ends are going to come down inside. They come off the inside hip of the tackles. This should free him up to the quarterback.

PASS RUSHTEXAS

On the Ex stunt the ends go first, and the tackles come outside off the hips of the ends.

PASS RUSHEX

The stunt that was very effective for us was what we called Taco. We have a Texas on the call side, and we have an Ex on the split side. This is good against man protection. We have an end in the A gap and one in the B gap.

PASS RUSHTACO

That is all of our pass-rush stunts. Most of our sacks came off the base rush. I want to put on our game film so you can see what I have talked about. Thanks.

 





Page 110

Dean Kreps

Zone Blocking Against Stunting Defenses

Hope College

I appreciate the opportunity to speak with you this morning and for your coming to hear me speak. I am honored to be here. I have been very fortunate as a person and especially as a coach. I've had great support from my family my entire life. I am the seventh of eight children, and one of my brothers is here with us today. Steve is a high school coach down in Illinois. That is where the real coaching takes place. I love my job, but I admire and respect all of the high school coaches. I was hired at Hope College when I was only 24 years old. I became the head coach when I was 32. What I want to do now is tell you a little about what we are doing with our zone running game. I believe we have started having success again because we are able to teach our kids how to block in this system. I believe that I can teach our kids how to block the man across from them. It's an attitude, and I have to instill that in our linemen. I truly believe that the offensive line has to lead the team. They have to set the tempo and set morale. When things go badly, everyone looks to the offensive line to get it going again.

When I became the head coach two years ago, I felt there were two things that had to happen right away. One, we had to install a legitimate off-season program. We started four sections of a conditioning and weight-training class that our kids can take for credit. I really felt I had to be the key player in the offseason program if it was going to work, so I teach three of the four sections myself. We concentrate on quickness and change of direction before we ever step foot in the weight room. I would be glad to talk to any of you later about our off-season program, because we really like what we are doing and it works.

The second thing I changed right away was our offensive line coach. George Kraft had coached the offensive line at Hope for 28 years and had done a great job. I had no qualms with his coaching, but I felt it was imperative that I take over the offensive line myself. Again, it goes back to the attitude that I wanted our players to have. As far as I'm concerned, a team's attitude starts with the offensive line. Who better than the head coach to instill the attitude I wanted with the most important position on the field? I would like to say that I have learned and picked things up over the years from a number of people, and what I know is a compilation of their coaching. I've spent time with Tony Wise from the Chicago Bears, Mike DeBord at Michigan, Joe Moore at Notre Dame, Mike Jacobs from Ohio State, Mike Barry from Southern Cal, and George Kraft from Hope. The guy who has probably had the biggest impact on my coaching of the offensive line is Bill Callahan, who is the line coach for the Philadelphia Eagles. I was Billy's graduate assistant at the University of Illinois back in the mid-1980s.

Base Philosophy

I think it is important that I share some of my base philosophy with you so you know where I am coming from. When I was hired to be the head coach two years ago, the president of Hope College asked me what my goals for the program would be. I told him that I wanted to win and make sure that our players graduate. He said that we would both be happy if I could do that. The graduation part was easy. We have only had three players in 27 years who have not graduated from Hope. t is important for me and my staff to be in this game for the players. All of our coaches at Hope also teach. Even in our league, this is unusual now. There are many facets of our players' lives that I want our coaches to be concerned with. I want our coaches to be concerned not only with a young man's physical development, but also with his academic, social, and spiritual development. I don't believe that you have to preach to the kids, but they will act like their coach after a while. That is why it is important that we model ourselves in such a way that they can pick up on it. Discipline becomes more important to me as I get older. For me and our team, I stress it for three reasons: (1) if it will help us win, (2) if it will help our players graduate, and (3) if it will help our players become

 





Page 111

better people. Like all coaches, I want athletes who are disciplined in all phases of their life. For me, this includes physical discipline (lifting, running, staying in shape), mental discipline (this precedes all else), emotional discipline (staying calm under pressure), social discipline (this includes drinking, drugs, friendships, etc.), and spiritual discipline.

I look for leadership at all levels. We expect more from our players the older they get. I do not discipline all of our players the same way. I used to think that you had to treat everyone the same as a coach. I don't believe that anymore. We have more invested in our upperclassmen, so we expect more from them. If we are going to be good, our seniors must be in it with us. It really hasn't been much of a problem for us. I like to use the mind-over-matter principle: I don't mind, and they don't matter.

Our offensive philosophy is based on the premise that we will establish the run first. Running the ball breeds toughness on offense. My quarterback coach tells me all the time that ''the quickest way from here to there is through the air,'' but I tell him that running the ball breeds toughness. Our tailback rushed for over 1100 yards as a sophomore and over 1500 yards this year as a junior. Again, we are committed to running the football.

Second, we are going to use different sets to run the same plays. A good way for our coaches to get a play into our offense is to show me how many different ways we can run the same play. We use motion across the formation, we use short motion, we use tight-end motion, and I would like to start using backfield motion. It has been interesting for me to coach offense after being on the defensive side for nine years. I want to make opposing defensive coordinators work on all kinds of shifts and motions. If they don't, I want to be able to use motions and shifts to give us an advantage. We won't use all the various shifts and motions in every game, but it sure makes our opponents work on things. The basic set that we use is the I formation. We will be in a two-back set about 95 percent of the time.

What It Takes To Block

A. EffortBlocking is hard work. I have to have guys up front who will work hard.

B. SkillsWe do most of our line drills every day. I tell that to our offensive line the first day of camp so they will know what to expect. They know I never get tired of working on the fundamentals of line play, so they better get used to it.

C. KnowledgeWe are fortunate at Hope because our kids are smart. Our average GPA in this year's freshman class is a 3.5, with an average ACT score of 26.

D. StrengthOur kids are getting stronger, but we still have work to do in this area. We test our kids three times a year in the squat, military, bench press x 225, pro shuttle run, body fat, and the 40yard dash. We also do an 8 x 110 test that we picked up from the Bears. We run eight 110-yard sprints with 45 seconds' rest between each one. We found with a timed half mile or mile that our best kids could bust their butt and make the time without being in shape. That is less likely with the 110 test. If they are not in shape, they will not make their time.

E. ToughnessI want to coach scrappy, hard-nosed players.

F. AnticipationI am a big believer in the first step on the offensive line. We win a lot of battles up front with quickness and anticipation off the ball.

G. ConditioningWe probably do less true conditioning during practice time than most high schools. We expect our players to report to camp in shape. We try to drill a lot at full speed to make up for this.

PersonnelWhat We Look For In Offensive Linemen

A. ToughnessPractice it; preach it; accept nothing less.

B. QuicknessQuickness is more important to me than speed. I have seen too many good linemen who have poor 40 times to think that it is vitally important to our success.

 





Page 112

C. Athletic abilityI want athletic ability more than size. If I can have both, of course, that is what we want. Height is not a major concern to be able to adjust on the move.

D. AggressivenessNot a passive mentality. We want the defensive mentality on offense.

What I Want From My Coaches

I think too many times we look at only the players for improvement, when, in fact, improvement from your coaching staff could help you a great deal.

A. Coaches must be a good teachers. This is easy for us, because all of our coaches still teach, just like most of the high school coaches here.

B. They must be demanding. We must tell our players exactly what we want from them and then make sure that it gets done.

C. I want our kids to trust us. You hear a lot of coaches give this lip service, but it really is important. This takes time. I had recruited a good number of our offensive players, but when I switched from defense to the offensive side of the ball two years ago, I had to earn the trust of the offensive players.

D. The fourth thing I want from my coaches is to remember that most individuals still learn far better from positive reinforcement than they do from criticism. I should pay closer attention to this myself. Too often, we are too critical of our players. I started off our preseason meetings a year ago by telling my coaches that unless they had a way to recruit players into the program during the season, they had better complain less about them and concentrate on making the ones we had better.

E. The last thing is to be up front with our players with criticism and praise. If he does something well, make sure you tell him. If he does something poorly, make sure he knows about it. Most of all, we must remember to be positive.

We take a lot of pride in what our team has done and especially in our offensive line. I want my offensive line to be tough, play as a unit, and be as athletic as I can find. We have to have a system, whether it be the zone concept that we are using, or the wing T that many of you use. I coached defense for nine years at Hope. Seven of those years were spent as the defensive line coach. The problem with being an assistant on the opposite side of the ball from the head coach is that you always get players dumped on you from the other side that they don't want. A kid might be third or fourth string, and those other coaches would always try to convince me that he could really help us. It is nice to be the head coach and be able to refuse those switches now.

Why The Zone Blocking Concept?

We switched to the zone offense two years ago after running the wing T for seven years. When I became the head coach, I knew what I wanted to be known for. I wanted our offense to be balanced, and I wanted to have a dominant running game. We have done that. We are now known for our zone running game. Our zone blocking helped our tailback to average 170 yards per game this year. I wanted a blocking conceptnot a schemethat we could use on a variety of running plays. It just seemed to me that with most other offenses, so much time was being spent on scheming and diagramming plays on a weekly basis. I wanted to spend more time on teaching fundamentals and techniques, and this offense allows me to do that.

We didn't rush into the change either. This is the offense that I became familiar with 12 years ago at Illinois. We listened to Tom Osborne and Pete Schmidt talk about this offense in Dallas two years ago, and I met with Bob Nielsen from Wartburg College. We had seen it run effectively at all levels and became more and more convinced that this was what we wanted to do. All of these coaches cautioned us to keep it simple and stick with the base plays, especially in the beginning. I should have listened more closely. We installed too much in 1995. In 1996, we scaled the number of plays back considerably. We decided to do exactly what everyone had cautioned us to do: Keep it simple, and do a few things well.

Now we run three plays from the inside zone: the dive off the option look, the play we call "inside" (the play

 





Page 113

that Tim Biakabuttuko ran so well at Michigan), and the lead draw. From the outside zone, we run the toss sweep (pitch), the option (lead option), and our off-tackle play that we call the slant. We also run some misdirection like the inside trap, the counter, and play action. We basically zone everything, or in the case of the lead draw, we use a man scheme. We really feel that we have to stick to our base plays and not invent new plays each week. This helps so much with our players' confidence, and we can see them improve each week. Since we have very little new learning from week to week, it helps our players to play with more aggressiveness and reckless abandon. We will go through the play book again later this spring. I want every play in there to serve a specific purpose. If it doesn't, then it will have to go. We still practice plays that are not run in the games enough. I have to either call them more or get rid of them. It is hard to let plays go. There are so many good plays that could be run. A lot of teams that run the zone scheme use a handoff sweep. We had it in and got rid of it. It's a great play but didn't give us anything that our toss sweep didn't already give us. We do not change our offense when we get near the goal line. We have to keep it simple. Our kids are not going to the NFL after Hope. They are going to be doctors and lawyers and teachers. We have very little meeting time outside of practice, and I hate to use on-the-field time to talk. I want on-the-field time used for reps as much as possible, so we have to keep it simple.

Our players know that the plays that get us into the red zone will be the plays used in the red zone. There is no reason to change. I like to use the plays that our players have confidence in.

Practice

A. Drill at full speed. This teaches games like angles, body position, reaction to defenders, and conditioning/effort.

B. Drills that pertain to the job must be done.

C. Work body on body as much as possible.

D. Repetitionsmust have it; do it again.

Stance

I want the toes out a little bit and not the other way around as many of us have been coached. I want a balanced stance that will allow us to make contact on the second step. I don't want my linemen to chop their feet in place. Don't get me wrongI want them to have quick feet, but chopping them in place before a drill does not help us get what we want. If you look at tape of offensive line play, you'll notice that you still have to plant your feet to move or to strike a player. I want the weight on the balls of the feet. I want the hand under the chin for balance. I want the feet under the body, again, for balance. As far as the hands go, I don't want a big windup. We work all of our drills with the hands out in front of our bodies. Our splits vary according to three things: (1) the alignment of the defense, (2) the play that is called, and (3) the quality of the defensive personnel that we are facing.

There are two kinds of leverage: inside and underneath. I want the elbows in. It works on the same principle as many of the martial arts. Stay compact on impact for a more direct blow. Tony Wise of the Chicago Bears gave me a great idea on how to work on this. He said to take an old car inner tube, cut it into 1- to 2-inch strips, and use it like a big rubber band. We do a lot of drills with these bands around our wrists to keep our hands and elbows in. I cut these up last summer. Don't make the same mistake I did. I went to the tire shop in town and had the man give me about 15 old inner tubes. I found out that you can make about 500 bands out of 15 inner tubes. One or two will give you all the bands you need.

Blocking is not the same as it was in the old days. We don't grab our jerseys and shoulder-block anymore. It is important to keep the hands inside the cylinder of the body. This helps cut down on holding penalties too. The second kind of leverage is underneath leverage. The low man will win most battles in the line. We work to keep our pad level lower than our opponent's. On many of the drills we do, I tell my linemen that they should be looking up at the chin of the man they are blocking.

 





Page 114

Our first step really just gets us into position. We must stay square on blocks and stay on balance. Many times, we can't tell who is on offense and who is on defense at the line. We are both working for the same thing. Our first step will vary with alignment. Steps 2 and 3 will make or break our blocks. We have to make contact and then accelerate. I want our linemen to make contact with the shoulder and then bench-press the defender to gain separation. A couple of my guys like to make contact with the head. We don't coach this because (1) it is dangerous and (2) it gets us overextended. We don't want defenders getting into our body, and that is exactly what happens when you make helmet contact. Defensively, that is great to do. We must keep separation on offense. I want the helmet on the play side, and we teach our guys to squeeze back with the head and neck. The best rule that we have is this: When in doubt, step play-side and drive. This is the first lesson that I tried to instill in my offensive line. In talking with the Bears, I know they like to use very little apparatus like dummies or shields, and I agree to a point. I don't like for our guys to block dummies, but I will use a Rogers Lev sled fairly often. I just feel that blocking a dummy forces our linemen to put their head down and bend at the waist. I've been asked about the type of steps we take on a base reach block. It varies, because the best way to describe it is to take what you can get. Alignment will affect this, but mostly it will be affected by the type of player on the other side of the line. As far as the centers go, I want them to snap and step at the same time. This would be ideal but not always realistic. Sometimes our center snaps the ball and steps back just to keep from getting rolled. I want our centers to work on this snap-and-step action every day.

We have very few blocks to make in a game. Other than trapping for the guards (which really is not part of the zone offense), we base block, base reach, and combo. In essence, what we are trying to do is create as many initial double teams as possible. The inside hand should hit the sternum, and the outside hand should go to the shoulder near the armpit. After two years of running this offense, I will tell you this. Zone blocking is easy to learn and tough to master. It is not complicated mentally. We only make two calls on the line, covered or uncovered. The best thing our linemen can do is to help us recruit good backs like we had this year.

We had Brett Kohler from Edwardsburg and Steve Sanders from Grandville sharing time at fullback. We also used a freshman, Scott Langlois from Hopkins. All three of them can block and run like trucks. At tailback we are fortunate to have Brandon Graham from Hudsonville, who rushed for 1525 yards on 270 carries in nine games with 21 touchdowns. My math isn't great, but that is over 5.5 yards per carry. To put it in perspective for our level, Barry Sanders led the NFL with 1553 yards on 307 carries for a 5.1 average and 11 touchdowns in 16 games. Terry Allen led the NFL in touchdowns this year with 21. Our tailback rushed for 21 touchdowns in nine games. Brandon Graham is the best back I have ever coached. I know there are better backs out there at other levels, but he is the best I have ever personally been around. He ran the toss sweep successfully on everyone we played this year.

Keys To Our Zone Blocking

1. We are blocking an area. We must block the line of scrimmage first, but we have to block the second level as well.

2. We must get movement at the point of attack. Backside blocks are more of what I call position blocks, but the we must have movement frontside.

Hope College Zone Package

22/23 Ld. Dr.28/29 Pitch

QBOpen at 4 or 8. Make handoff on second step. Fake play action/bootleg.

FBTake a course at the inside leg of the TE, and block #4 or force.

TBDrop open step, and take a course at the inside leg of the TE. Read TE block.

XStalk FS.

YBase reach DE.

ZStalk SC LT; cut off BSDT.

LGCombo NG.

OCCombo NG to BSLB.

RGCombo PSDT.

RTCombo PSDT to PSLB. When you think you should come off the combo, go two more steps.

28/29 PITCH

QBReverse out and pitch ball. Turn to block backside pursuit.

FBTake a course at the inside leg of the TE, and block #4 or force.

TBOpen at 90 degrees and read FB's block.

XStalk FS.

YBase reach the DE.

ZStalk SC.

LTCut off BSDT.

LGCombo NG.

OCCombo NG to BSLB.

RGCombo PSDT.

RTCombo PSDT to PSLB. When you think you should come off the combo, go two more steps.

22/23 INSIDE

QBOpen pivot at 5 or 7, make handoff deep, and boot.

FBTake course at inside leg of DE, and kick out DE.

TBTake belly course at outside left of the play-side OG.

XStalk FS.

YBase reach the DE.

ZStalk SS.

LTCut off BSDT.

LGCombo NG to BSLB.

OCCombo NG.

RGCombo PSDT to PSLB.

RTCombo PSDT.

22/23 LEAD DRAW

We only want to run this play to the tight-end side. We have to take true pass sets. We did not run this play well during the 1995 season, and that is why. We ran the play like an isolation and got destroyed. This year, we worked on having patience. When I say that, I mean patience by the linemen with their pass sets. I mean patience out of the backs by showing a good

 





Page 116

pass block fake. And I mean patience by our quarterback by selling the play with a good pass drop.

QBOpen pivot at 5 or 7, hand off deep to TB, and fake pass.

FBLead step, cross over, and read the B gap to the PSLB.

TBSlide step, delay, run to daylight.

XRelease and stalk WC.

YChip DE and release to SS.

ZRelease and stalk SC.

LTPass set and cut off BSDT.

LGPass set release to BSLB.

OCPass set on NG.

RGPass set on NG.

RTPass set and cut off PSDT.

30/31 TRAP

QBFake a pitch weak. Outside handoff to the FB.

FBSlide step and take an outside handoff.

TBTake a pitch course away from play.

XStalk FS.

YCut off DE.

ZStalk SS.

LTCut off BST to BSLB.

LGTrap play-side.

OCCombo NG to BSLB.

RTChip PSDT to PSLB.

24/25 DIVIDE PASS

QBOpen at 4 or 8 and fake handoff on second step. Boot and avoid DE.

FBTake a course at outside leg of the OT and block first man to show.

TailbackDrop open step, take course at outside leg of OT. Release into flat if boot is toward you. If away, curl up inside.

XDrag behind LBs.

YClear to middle.

ZClear to flag.

QBOpen at 4 or 8 and fake handoff on second step. Boot and avoid DE.

XDrag to middle if boot is away; clear to flag if boot is toward you.

YOutside release to a drag if boot is away; inside release to an out if boot is toward you.

ZDrag to the middle if boot is away; clear to flag if boot is toward you.

PASS DRILLS

A. Wave drill, right and left.

B. Work on picking up twists by having the man go blow up the slant guy. Drive him back into the hole and catch the looper with his own man.

C. Stand up and punch OL in the face to have him work on keeping his head back.

D. Stand up and bull-rush with pad to work on keeping butt down.

E. Stand up and bull-rush with pad to work on punch.

F. Bull in the ring with pads, right-left, right-left. Time up punch and keep hands up. Lunge and fake.

G. Stand up and have DL knock hands off and refit.

H. Mirror dodge. Jump, spin and roll, but don't touch offense. Hands up at near point.

I. Mirror dodge at 45-degree angle.

J. Quick sets.

Bobby Lamb

Quarterback Drills and the Passing Game

Furman University

I tried to get my wife to type out the information for the overhead, but she insisted that I do it, so here goes. First, I want to talk about seven traits of a great quarterback.

1. He must demonstrate leadership and responsibility. The quarterback must be able to communicate in tough situations. He must be willing to take the blame and the glory. He must be a leader on and off the field.

2. He must be a competitor. He must hate to lose. He must be competitive in practice. He must be ready to compete in all phases of the game.

3. He must be confident. He must have confidence to the borderline of being cocky. He must inspire confidence in his teammates.

4. He must have credibility. We hear the expression, "You're the man." In football, the quarterback must be "The Man." His teammates must believe in him. When it is 3rd and 6 on the last drive and you must stick the ball in the end zone, 
the team must believe in him.

5. He must have an instinctive mental process. He must be able to react on the move. He must know all of the assignments for the entire team.

6. He must have a quick delivery. I know it is great to have a quarterback who is big and can run, but if you are going to throw the ball today, you must have a quarterback who has a quick delivery. He must not wind up to throw the ball. He must be able to get the ball out of his hand.

7. He must be a playmaker. This is the most important of these seven traits. He must make the difference in winning and losing.

A quarterback must believe in himself, and his work must convince his teammates that he can be trusted when all else fails. I think this about sums everything up about the quarterback's position. The quarterback is the man who must step up and make the play. He must get the ball in the hands of the best receiver or the best running back. He must be trusted by his teammates.

The big thing we look at in the passing efficiency rating is this: How many interceptions did the quarterback throw? The next thing we look at is percentage of completions. I will talk about accuracy percentage later.

Next, I want to talk about drills we do in practice. First, we run air routes. We start with wide receivers. Later we bring in the tailback, fullback, and tight ends. We want a lot of air routes thrown during the early practice.

The next route we work on is one of the best for the wide receivers. It is a one-on-one versus the defensive backs. We also use the tight end in the drill. We split the receiver, and the defensive man lines up and plays him man-to-man.

 





Page 119

We have a stopwatch on the quarterback because we do not want him to hold the ball too long. We want the ball gone in less than 2.8 seconds.

Next, we work on a combo route. We have two receivers going against two defenders: a safety and a corner. The defense will play what we call a two-read coverage, or they will be playing quarters. It gives our receivers a chance to see what the defense is doing with their coverage. Myself, I like to get the linebackers involved in the defense. We add them to the drill later on.

The next drill is with our tight end, tailback, and fullback against three linebackers. We call this the Dallas drill. The defense can play their different coverages as we run our different routes against them.

Then we go to our skeleton drill. This is where we bring our skilled people on offense to go against the entire defensive secondary. This is our team passing drill. This is our best drill. We never want our quarterback to get hit in this drill. He must stand in the pocket and throw the football. Then we go to an 11-on-11 drill. This is where we let it all out, and the quarterback must make the play.

We keep an accuracy percentage on our quarterbacks. Also, we keep a completion percentage on them. If the pass is dropped by a receiver, it is counted as a completion for us in this chart.

If the ball is batted-and we see this in the film-I give them the benefit of the doubt and count it as a completion. If the quarterback throws the ball away to prevent a sack, we do not count it as anything. It is not counted as a catch or an attempt. Every day in practice we have a goal for the type of passes we throw.

Air routes
100%
One-on-one
95%
Dallas
95%
Skeleton
85%
11-on-11
70%

Last year, our quarterback had a passing accuracy of 77 percent. He did a good job of getting rid of the ball, although we had several passes dropped.

Next, I want to get into the drills for our quarterbacks. We are going to do four drills every day before practice. A lot of you have been to our camp and know these four drills. I do not want my quarterbacks to warm up unless I am out there with them. I do not want them to get into any bad habits. I want to be with them when they are warming up.

1. Toes-on drillWe want the quarterback to put his toes on the line about shoulders' width apart. The quarterbacks are going to be 10 yards apart. We want the quarterback to rotate his shoulders and use his upper body. His feet should never leave the ground. We want him to set the ball, turn the hips, and work on the shoulder rotation. He must come over the top with the ball. He rotates his shoulders from one sideline to the other sideline. This drill helps them to get loose. We really stress setting the ball. We want the ball up high on the drill.

2. Weight-shift drillThe quarterbacks are still 10 yards apart. They take their back foot and put it on the line. We do not want them to rock all the way back, but we do want them to shift their weight. We talk to them about finishing the throw just as they would if they were a baseball pitcher. We want them to transfer the weight, take a six- to eight-inch stride, and set the foot. Again, we stress the follow-through.

3. Step-n-strideAgain, the quarterbacks are going to put their foot on the back line, take a short stride toward the target, and throw. They are working on a short stride, and they are standing tall. Again, we concentrate on the follow-through. They are just like a baseball pitcher. We want them to come over the top.

4. Crossover plantWe simulate being at the top of our drop. Now we are going to cross over and plant the foot and throw the football. We stress balance and the weight transfer with the shoulders level. The most important step in the drop for the quarterback is the fourth step. He has

 





Page 120
