to break the momentum to cross over and plant. If the fourth step is too long, he will never be able to throw the ball without gathering his feet. We do these four drills every day. We make sure we are on the field to supervise them. We want reps, reps, and more reps.

Next we go to a series of quarterback drop drills. The quarterback is not throwing the football; he is dropping back. We do all of these drills on the lines. We start on the sideline.

First is the distance drop. I blow a whistle, and they start the drill. The quarterbacks drop all the way across the field. It is full speed all the way across the field. We want them to stay on the line and to keep the head up and shoulders level and work the football. This is sideline to sideline. The drop is second nature, and it helps lengthen the drop. This is how we warm up. You can vary the distance if you want. At times in practice, we may have them drop 100 yards. We put them on the goal line and have them drop all the way down the field. This is usually after they have made several mistakes.

Next is the swivel-hips drill. Again, they start out on the sideline. I blow the whistle, and they are going to drop. I blow the whistle again, and they swivel or rotate the hips. We want them to stay on the midline. We do this all the way across the field. I blow the whistle, and they rotate. On every whistle they must rotate the hips. We want them to try to stay on the midline. We want them to keep their feet working.

The third drill is the carioca drop. This drill is used to improve footwork. The upper body remains the same. This drill goes well with our big-five drop, which I will discuss later. Again, we are sideline to sideline. We start out and carioca all the way across the field. We want to stay on the midline. The upper body remains the same. The lower body must do the work in a carioca-type style. It is very good for developing footwork. We like to stress this point with our quarterbacks. Playing quarterback starts from the ground up. Feet, feet! If they have good feet, they have a chance.

Next is the tapioca drop. It is the same as the carioca drop except the quarterback is giving a quick washing-machine action. We want real short choppy steps. We go from the sideline to the hash mark on this drill. We start out by straddling the sideline. Everything is the same as the carioca drill, except we are speeding things up. Again, this drill goes well with our quick-five action. We do not take many seven-step drops anymore. The defensive ends in college are too good. We are a quick-five-action team and a big-five team.

The fifth drill is what we call our alternate drops. Now the mind and body must work together. It is the same thing. They start on the sideline. We may be working on the big five, quick five that day, or we may be working on three quick steps. We may be going three quick and big in the drill. I will blow the whistle, and the quarterbacks go 1, 2, 3, and throw to the right. When I say, "Throw to the right," they get the ball and bring it to the other hand. Then they reset and go again down the line. They throw and reset all the way across the field.

The sixth drill is one that we have been doing a great deal of lately. It is what we call our mental drops. I line the quarterbacks up in front of me, and they get on the hash mark. I will call the formation, the play, and the defensive coverage to them. Then I will say, "Sam runs to the flat." Then I blow the whistle. They take their drop, a quick five or a big five. Then they must throw the ball to the receiver they think will be open. The quarterback drops and goes through his reads mentally. I know who they are throwing to. If they screw up, I help them. This is way they are going to learn to throw the ball in a game. This is something you must do over and over.

Next, I want to go over our throwing drills. The first is what we call the release drill. We have two quarterbacks standing side by side. We have a wide receiver in front of the quarterback, but it could be a manager, another quarterback, or another player. The coach stands in front of the receivers. If we have an injured wide receiver who can take part in the drill, we will use them. The quarterback is in a set position. When the coach throws his hand up, the quarterback must release the ball to the receiver.

 





Page 121

RELEASE DRILL

The quarterback is in a set position. The coach will throw his hands up in the air. The quarterback releases the ball as quickly as possible. Start at 15 yards apart, and vary the depth and angle of throws. Get the ball out of your hands!

Next is our carioca drop-n-throw and our tapioca dropn-throw. We have a quarterback and a wide receiver. First the coach blows the whistle. The quarterback must carioca and set his feet. The whistle blows again and he throws the football to the receiver.

CARIOCA DROP-N-THROW AND TAPIOCA DROP-N-THROW

The coach blows the whistle. The quarterback takes the snap and carioca- or tapioca-drops. The coach blows the whistle again. The quarterback sets his feet and delivers the ball. You can vary the spots for the receiver. The whistle may blow while the quarterback s in the middle of the carioca. If that happens, the quarterback must get set and throw the football as soon as possible. We do not want to run the drills where we are throwing the ball straight ahead all of the time. We like to practice throwing at angles. Throw at a spot. If you run a curl that week, work on the curl route. We like to let the quarterback throw to the spot where he will be throwing to in a game.

In the tapioca drill we take quicker and choppier steps. Now we want to get the ball away much quicker. That is what our quick-five pass drop is. We take the short, choppy steps.

The fourth throwing drill is what we call our avoid drill. You may not want to work on this, but we feel we have to.

AVOID DRILL

The quarterback takes the snap and drops on a quick-five or a big-five drop. He sets on the fifth step. He must avoid the rush to the right or left. Then he must sidestep and throw the ball to the wide receiver. He must reset his feet before throwing the ball. If he gets pressure from one side, he must slide, reset his feet, and then throw. We have the dummies set up deep enough to simulate the depth that we want the quarterback to drop. Most quarterbacks want to get out of the pocket when they get pressure. It is a feeltype thing that the quarterback must experience. The way to work on that is running this drill over and over. You work on throwing from both sides.

Next is our step-up drill. Now the quarterback must step up in the pocket to make the pass.

STEP-UP DRILL

 





Page 122

We take two defenders, who can be other players, quarterbacks, or managers, to rush the quarterback. The quarterback takes his drop and checks the rush. We rush at the angle that the defensive ends will be rushing in the game. The quarterback must step up in the pocket and make the throw.

Next is our pressure drill. Do not let anyone do this but the coach. As soon as the quarterback releases the ball, the coach throws a hand dummy at the quarterback. Do not hit the quarterback anywhere near his throwing hand. Again, do not let anyone throw the dummy except the coach.

PRESSURE DRILL

I like to use this drill on individual routes. I have a hand shield, and I throw it at the quarterback as soon as he releases the football. I do not sling the dummy, I throw it at the quarterback. Again, we do not want to hit the quarterback on his throwing arm. We want to simulate the type of pressure they will see in the game.

The last drill we use in our throwing drills is our clock drill. The coach will point to the wide receiver. The wide receiver flashes his hands. The quarterback must work from his right to his left and from his left to his right. The quarterback must step and turn on each target. You can have as many receivers as you want. We want the quarterback to move his feet and shoulders together.

CLOCK DRILL

If I flash my hands to the number one receiver, the quarterback must throw the ball as soon as possible. If I flash the number two receiver, the quarterback can look at number one and then come off to number two receiver. You can build this in with the different pass routes you are running. We mix it up on the patterns.

We feel it is very important to take the first 15 minutes in practice and work on these drills. It is very important for us to do them, especially this year. What we like to do is to get out to the practice field and warm up, then do our flexes, and then get into our drills. After we get through with the drills, we go to individual routes.

Next we go to our quarterback throwing techniques. These are the things we work on.

Lower bodyStep toward the target, and shift the weight. We want a flex in the left knee. We want the quarterback to allow the hips to come through. We want to take a short stride. We want to follow through toward the target. That is the number one thing that we stress. When the quarterback throws the football, he wants the hips and everything else to follow through straight forward.

Upper bodyWe want to set the ball high. We want to get it up in the air. We want the quarterback to turn the ball away. We like to say that we take the football off the shelf. This is what we teach in our camp in the summer. The elbow must be at 90 degrees. The elbow must be higher than the shoulder. The lead arm must assist with rotation of the shoulders. We want to snap the throw-off.

I am not a big grip person. We want the quarterback to snap off the throw with his finger rotation. If the quarterback is throwing with a normal rotation, I will leave him alone. If the ball is wobbly, we work on the rotation with that quarterback. We want the quarterback to use as little motion as possible in throwing the ball. We want to get the ball out of the hand of the quarterback.

The next area is the passing sequence. This is after the drop. First, we want to stand tall and be balanced.

 





Page 123

We want to set the ball high. Next, we must step toward the target. We want to throw the ball with finger rotation. We want RPMs on the ball. And last but not least, we want to follow through to the target.

The second problem is that they are unbalanced after the drop. The front shoulder should be up.

The third problem is that the lead arm folds. This does not do them any good. We want to make sure that the lead arm pulls them through.

Next I want to talk about the two-man game. This is where we break the routes down. The first thing we do that is different from what most people do is that we put the tailback in the halfback position behind the tight end. Most teams put their fullback in that position. We move our tailback to that position. We have our tailback and tight end working together. Our fullback goes to the split-end side. This is a standard set for us, especially in a passing situation. I want to break it down, take about three individual routes, and go over them.

First is the flat route. The depth of a flat route is 3 yards from the line of scrimmage. If the tailback is deeper than 3 yards, he is wrong. We stress width. We say width is more important than depth. He wants to reach the line of scrimmage 3 yards outside the tight end. After he gets to the 3-yard area, he should bend it outside. As soon as he reaches the line of scrimmage, he must snap the head around and look for the ball. More important than depth is for the tailback to get leverage on the Sam linebacker.

FLAT ROUTE

The choice route is run by the fullback. He goes to a depth of 4 yards versus a zone and 6 yards against man coverage. When he reaches the line of scrimmage, he wants to be 4 yards outside the tackle's alignment. Against the zone, he wants to snap his head to the outside and anchor his feet to the ground. Against man coverage, he wants to get vertical and give the Will linebacker a jab step to the inside. The key to the route is to get vertical. If he does not get vertical, he does not have a chance out there.

CHOICE ROUTE

Next is our option route by our tight end. All of these routes can be run out of all three wideouts. This route is for the inside receiver. The depth is 6 to 8 yards. It is a backyard route. The receiver does just the opposite of what the linebacker does. The receiver has three options. (1) If the linebacker walls the receiver off, he must plant and break to the outside. He wants to lean back to the quarterback. (2) If the linebacker drops over the top of the receiver, he hooks to the inside. (3) If the receiver passes the linebacker, he turns back to the inside.

OPTION ROUTE

If we make a quick-five call, our quarterback is going to take five quick steps and shift his weight to this back foot and throw the football right now. This is our two-man game. His first option is to read the Sam linebacker. If we have Sam outflanked, we want to throw the ball to that receiver. We throw that route until they change the defense. A quick five to us is usually a 5- to 6-yard drop of our quarterback, but we are getting the ball away very quickly.

If Sam has our first receiver covered, the quarterback will reset and throw the option route. If the option

 





Page 124

route is covered, the quarterback resets and throws to the choice receiver. It is a quick 1, 2, 3. It is just like the clock drill we just did.

This is what the routes look like against what we call cover-two float. The Mike linebacker cannot cover the tight end if he is going deep enough. If the safety gets uptight, we send the wideout deep inside behind him.

R-L VS. COVER-TWO FLOAT

What happens if we see cover three? If we get cover three we can work on either side. We work against both sides. We like to work the backside post. We want the wide receiver to break inside at 8 yards and catch the football on the hash mark.

ROWLEE VS. COVER THREE

Let me show you the same route from different sets. These are what we call our Row and Lee sets.

ROWLEE VS. TWO FLOAT

If we call 
move, 
we can set the tailback to the splitend side, bring him back in motion, and run the same play. It is simply 830 flat choice.

ROBLIZMOVE VS. TWO FLOAT

We went with a one-back set and ran the same plays.

DOUBLESONE-BACK SET

Everything we teach on routes develops off this route. This is where we start out teaching. In the no-backs set, notice where the B receiver is lined up. We ran the 839 flat choice route.

839 FLAT CHOICEEMPTY

This gives the defense a different look, but it is the same route. We want to give the quarterback as much of an edge as we can. That is our two-man game. There are a lot of ways you can do it.

 





Page 125

Frank Lenti

Goal-Line Offense

Mt. Carmel High School, Illinois

Thank you. I appreciate all of you being here; believe me, I do. I will tell you, I do not have time for any jokes. I want to get right to teaching about football.

One thing we try to get across to our players is this. We talk to teach; we listen to learn. We teach this to our kids in school, in football, and we should keep this point in mind this weekend as well. How do we learn? There are basically three ways we learn. We learn through the people we meet, we learn through the books we read, and we learn through the tapes we watch or listen to. Those are the three basic ways in which we learn.

This weekend they will have a clinic manual available. Invest in the clinic manual. It is a great manual and will contain articles you can learn from. A magazine I brought with me is excellent. It is the 
American Football Quarterly 
It is put out by a man who played for Bear Bryant at Alabama. I think the magazine runs about $20 per year. It is the best magazine of its kind that I have seen or read. You can call those people up and tell them you heard about it from me. I do not have anything invested in the magazine, but I would like to see it continue. Their number is 1-800-537-4271. Keep in mind the three ways we learn. Go buy books on how to teach, how to learn, business, and leadership. Make yourself a better teacher, and you will automatically make yourself a better coach.

This is what we try to get across to our staff: We are going to achieve whatever we emphasize. I coach our offensive line. We emphasize coming off the ball low, hard, and quick. We emphasize having great splits in our line. We emphasize taking care of the little things. Keep this point in mind. As coaches, we are going to achieve whatever we emphasize. If we make it important to the kids, it will become second nature to them as we go along. Teach your kids to play the game with their feet and their eyes. You do not have to have great speed, but if you have great feet, you can play the game. We tell our players it is just like a car accident: speed kills.

We want to be great technicians. We want our kids to be great technique players. We have had some great athletes. However, we have had some kids who have developed into great technique players. We want to develop leaders in our program.

We have players all over the country who will be team captains next year. We have had some great players come out of our program. Some of you may have heard of Simeon Rice, who was the third player taken in the NFL draft last year. He came to Mt. Carmel as a running back. He did not even want to play defense. We talked him into playing defense, and now he is worth a lot of money. The point I am making is that you can have players with ability, but you still have to coach them and teach them.

I am going to talk about goal-line offense. If you are going to be a good football team, you better be good with your special teams. Think about the amount of time you spend on special teams. We spend four days a week, from 15 to 30 minutes a day, working on our special teams. To win, we know we must play great defense. From 1988 to 1991, we were in four straight state championships. We went 10 consecutive quarters in the state championship play without giving up a point. We finally gave up a touchdown because our offense turned the ball over. The point is that you have to emphasize all of those areas to be a good football team.

The things that I want to talk about today are not just X's and O's. My total topic is goal-line offense: theory, concepts, and practice. It is important to know how to practice on the goal line as well. First, I want to go over our overall offensive philosophy. Our offense is based on execution. We want to do something our athletes can do, as opposed to something they can't

 





Page 126

do. We make sure they do it; if they won't, we find someone else. We do not settle for anything less. When we say they won't, I mean attitude-wise they will not do what we ask. You find some kids that will not take to your coaching. Sometimes we may play a kid with less talent but one who will take coaching. If you have a great athlete and he does not do the things you ask him to do, I will assure you that he will get you beat somewhere down the road. You win with the little things: huddle, steps, alignment. Close games are won by the team that does the little things right. Little things will turn into big things. We insist that our players do the little things right.

We have a term that we use; we call it this: ''Be brilliant on the basics.'' Let me talk about fundamentals. Teaching must be done in the classroom and not on the field. At our school, the players report to the locker rooms immediately after school. Two days a week, the defensive players go into a classroom as soon as they get out of school. The offensive players go get taped or start getting dressed. At the end of 15 minutes, the offense and defense switch. While one group is getting dressed, the other team is in a team meeting. We go over our scouting reports, and we give the players handouts we have prepared. We begin to let them see what the big picture looks like. We do not want to waste time doing that on the field. We want to get after it once we get outside.

We feel it is very important to get a lot of repetitions on the field. The whistle blows; the coach is on the run. We want to coach them on the run. Get after them. Don't make it a laissez-faire attitude. Put a sense of urgency into your coaching. Coach them on the run. Our coaches are on the go! I am on the go. Our kids do not want to be the last one to get to a group drill. I jog to the drills, and the kids run to beat me there. We let them know when they are doing a good job. We let them know that we are interested in them becoming better people, better students, and better athletes.

Mental errors will not be tolerated. Players must know what to do and how to do it! That is part of our job. We have to be able to answer the whys and wherefores. Coach the kids; teach them what to do and how to do it. Offense is a team game. The team is far more important than the individual. We are not interested in setting statistical records. We played all 80 or our players in 11 of our 14 games this past year. That is more kids than we usually carry on the team, but we played them. We are only one of three teams in the history of Illinois that has scored over 600 points in one season. For the 13 years that I have been at Mt. Carmel, we have never had an area leading rusher, a leading passer, a leading receiver, or a leading anything. That is because we believe football is a team game. We spread the ball around, and we give everyone an opportunity on both sides of the football to play the game.

Consistency is a must in order to be successful. You must live and die with your offense, defense, and kicking game. You can't change things around in the middle of the season. I am amazed at some coaches who change their offense from year to year. How do you know how the people you will be playing against will adjust? You have to make a commitment to an offense that you believe in and go with it. You have to work on the offense. You can't be adding play after play week after week. You get down to the end of the season, and you need a roll of toilet paper to write down all the plays you have run for the season. Pick the things you feel are important, emphasize those points, and then have your players become great technicians.

We subscribe to the John Wooden philosophy. Before we worry what the other team is going to do, we want to be concerned with what we do. In August when we first get started, we never worry about working on the first opponent. We work our offense against our defense and our kicking game against our kicking game. We are going to make sure that our kids are good at what we need to do to be successful. We do not look at the scouting report of our first opponent until the week of that first game. We are going to be excellent at what we do first and foremost.

This sign is on our wall in our locker room: "Football should be fun!" Fun comes from doing things well and enjoying success. It is just like a classroom. It is fun to

 





Page 127

do well on a test. We want it to be the same way on the field. You can have fun in practice if you can see that you are getting better. You can coach them on the run and push them to get better. We want to make sure that the players are doing things right. Look for a perfect practice. Are we ever going to get a perfect practice? Probably not, but we are going to push and strive for it. We are not going to accept anything less than the best effort. When the season is over, I do not want a player to come up to me and say, "Coach, we could have been better." Would'a, could'a, should'a. When it is all said and done, we want the kids to know that we did everything that we possibly could have done.

This is something we do at the start of the year. I call all the coaches up around me. I have all of the players out in front. I ask the players to hold up their hands if they want to be coached by a coach that gives them a half-ass effort. "Raise your hand." No one raises their hands, naturally. Then I say this: "Men, our coaches do not want to coach players that only give them a half-ass effort!" Everyone must be in the same boat. Everyone must believe in what we are doing. This may be a little thing, but the light goes on with some of the kids. They know they must put their heart and soul into what we are doing.

Confidence comes from consistency. Goal line must take a top priority. There is no reason to get down there if we can't score. We must study ourselves and go over and over our goal-line package. It is important that you study yourself. I will cover this again later. Everyone must be doing the best he is capable of doing and nothing less. We never walk on the field, coaches and players alike. Coaches must coach on the go!

Goal-Line Offense: Theories And Principles

Here are some theories and principles that we believe in. This is more important than giving you our X's and O's. If we have time, I will show you some of the things we do on the goal line. What I am going to cover with you now are ideas that you can develop and use in your offense, regardless of the offense you use on the goal line.

You attack the defense in two ways: (1) in an 
area 
where the defense is somewhat weaker and (2) a particular 
individual 
whose style of play can be exploited. We concentrate more on the first point, which is to attack an area. When I first started coaching, we would attack the individual first. But what I found out was this. We would pick out an individual who we felt we could exploit, and we worked on plays against that individual. However, that player's coach could see what was happening to that player, and by the time they played us, they had pulled that player out of the lineup. We wasted time working against a player who did not even play against us. Now, that does not mean that we are not going to look at attacking an individual, but first we are going to look at an area to exploit the defense.

We want to present a well-balanced offensive attack inside the 10-yard line. What does balanced mean? It does not necessarily mean 50-50. I can't tell you what well balanced means for you. You must decide what the balance is for your team. We like the balance to be about 60-40 in favor of the run. If we look at our offense and find that we are running the ball 80 percent of the time, we will make an effort to include more passing in our attack to get back to being balanced in our attack.

We want to force the defense to balance up to our offensive formations. We can go from three wide receivers to two tight ends with the same personnel. Do you change your personnel on the goal line, or do you use the same personnel? We run the split-back veer-option offense. We do not have to change personnel. The same offense we are running on the 40-and 50-yard line is the same offense we will be running down on the goal line. I get confused when I see teams that run the I formation in the middle of the field and then get down on the goal line and go to the wishbone formation. To me, that is an entirely different offense that you have to spend time on in practice.

We like to run option football inside the 10-yard line. We continue to attack on a broad front. Coach Lou Holtz use to say this: "Don't flinch." If option football got you down to the 10-yard line, don't be afraid to call

 





Page 128

it again. One point I learned from Bo Schembechler at Michigan is this: You better believe in everything that you do, and you had better be ready to sell it to everyone involved. If you can't do that, you will never be successful. When I first became the defensive coordinator in my first two years at Mt. Carmel High School, we were a Rip-and-Liz, power-I football team. People told me that I would be fired in two years if I tried to run an option offense in our conference. We can knock on wood, because we have had good kids and they have been able to overcome my coaching. We are still at Mt. Carmel after 13 years.

It is important to maintain good line splits on the goal line. How many of you here today tell your players on offense to close their splits down when the defense gets in the gaps? We do not tell our kids to cut their splits down because the defense is in the gaps. We want good line splits because gap defenses give us good angles. That does not mean that you have to run every play in your offense down on the goal line. Run the plays that are going to be conducive to success on the goal line. Don't run plays that you are going to have a hard time being successful on.

Eliminate mistakes on the goal line. This includes fumbles and penalties. Missed assignments mean stops. Everyone in this room knows this, but do we coach our kids in that manner? Do we teach the kids these things? Stop the stoppers. We do not want things that are going to stop us on the goal line. We want to eliminate them in practice.

We want to control the line of scrimmage. If the defense does penetrate, we want to use it to our advantage. Our number one play on the goal line is our outside veer. If the defense tries to pinch the tackle and end down inside to stop the outside veer, we look at the next phase of plays that we want to go to in order to be successful. If the defense takes away A, are we good enough to go to B? If they can take away A and B, do we have C available? We want to sustain our blocks and block lower on the goal line. We want to get the shoulders into the end zone. We want movement at the point of attack. We tell our linemen to finish the play with their shoulders over the end zone. This is particularly true from the 5-yard line on in. Think about it, coach. If I coach the line to finish the play with their shoulders in the end zone, where will the back probably be? Right behind the linemen in the end zone. We tell our linemen that they are successful if they finish the play with their shoulders in the end zone.

We will use all four backs, so we can run our plays to either side with equal speed, deception, finesse, and power. I will show you this in our tape. We run the same plays but block them a little differently. We insist that our backs run low and harder through the end zone. Here are a couple of coaching points. How many times have you seen the backs run into the line at the 5-yard line with their heads down? If your eyes are down and you get hit, your body is going down. The first thing we teach our kids is to get their eyes up and to look for the back of the end zone. It does not matter if you are running 8 yards or 80 yards, our kids are going to run through the back of the end zone. The back has a better chance of staying up if he has his eyes up, looking for the back of the end zone. Most people tell their backs to tuck the ball away and to get the front hand over the ball. We tell our backs to get the ball in front of them on the goal line. We want the pads down, eyes up, and the ball in front on the chest with one or two hands on the ball.

We want to throw the play-action pass off our most effective runs. We use crossing patterns versus man coverage. Because we are an option team, we like to use dump and curl routes. These things fit our scheme. You have to decide what will fit in with your scheme. Throw the play-action pass off your most effective runs. That is a big key.

We want to keep pressure on the defense. Our tempo must be at its best. When we get inside your 20, we want them excited. We want a sense of urgency. We are trying to get the plays in as fast as we can. We want to create a sense of urgency, and we do not want to give the defense a chance to rest. Our kids are going to hustle up to the line and get down, and they are going to go. We are going to get after them as quickly as we possibly can. Again, what we want to do is to keep the pressure on the defense.

 





Page 129

We use sequence football when possible. You only need four or five runs and three or four passes. When we are down inside the 15- to 10-yard line, we do not need a lot of offense. How many patterns do we need? We need something that will work against man coverage and something that will work against zone coverage. I am not saying that you cannot use dropback passing on the goal line, but we find that we have better success with play-action passes.

Effort and execution equal success! Our kids hear our coaches yelling out all of the time: "Think and execute; think and execute. Effort and execution; effort and execution." Those are vital concepts to our kids, and they understand the concept. They live with it, and they believe it. Our weekly goal-line package is simply a limited version of our base package. It allows for consistency. We are not going from one formation to another once we get on the goal line.

Let me talk about some advantages of our goal-line offense. Again, see if these advantages apply to your situation. You should write down what you think the advantages are for you and your goal-line offense. Here are the advantages of the split backs on the goal line. First is continuity. You stay with the same offense that got you there. Next is simplicity. It reduces the teaching time and techniques. When we teach our offense in August, there is very little we have to do after that. We do have to polish it up from time to time. The key is that we want our kids to be great technicians. In the last 11 years, we have continued to play until the last week of the season nine times. We have gone down the road as far as you could go in nine of those 11 years. Some of the drills we teach in August we are still doing the last week of the season as far as fundamentals are concerned. Be brilliant on the basics. Don't shortchange your kids there.

The third advantage of our offense is that it builds confidence. It can be done, and you can prove it with statistics. Develop your own statistics. If you are a varsity coach, don't have the varsity coaches keep track of the stats. If you do, they will make them say whatever they want them to say. Have a coach on the JV or frosh level take care of the stats. If you are a defensive coach, have a coach on offense keep the defensive stats, and vice versa. By doing it this way, the stats mean more to your team and can be helpful.

Next, I want to talk about some prerequisites for any goal-line offense. First, you can't stop yourself. What are you going to emphasize? What are your goals? The ideal goal is to score 100 percent of the time inside the 10-yard line. I do not know of any team that can do that, but it is the ideal goal. You might say that we want to score 70 percent of the time inside the 10-yard line. Then look to see if you are doing the things that will allow you to be successful.

We want to identify our bread-and-butter back and get him the football. Does that mean every down? No! If you are smart, it does not mean every first down. It may mean that you will fake the ball to that back and throw the play-action pass.

You must evaluate your statistics. Do you always run the ball on first down? The opponents have that figured out if you do. Give yourself an opportunity to be successful. Come up with the play-action pass on first down to throw them off balance. We take a look at our goal-line efficiency. We look at the situation and chart the number of times we are successful and the number of times we fail. If we scored a touchdown, we want to know why we were successful. If we had to settle for a field goal, we want to know why we did not score the touchdown. Did we do a poor job, did we make the right call, or did we get a penalty? We want to know why we were successful or why we failed.

Look at the reasons why you failed to score. Fumble! Was it a contact fumble or an exchange fumble? If it was because of contact, you will need to emphasize proper handling of the ball. You have to emphasize this to the ball carrier and motivate him to hold on to the football. If it was on the exchange, we want to know if it was caused by penetration, poor technique, or a missed assignment.

Another reason why we fail to score on the goal line is because of interceptions. Why did you throw? Was

 





Page 130

it play selection? Was it because of poor mechanics on the part of the quarterback? Was it because of fundamentals of throwing, catching, or pass protection?

Penalties will stop you from scoring on the goal line. Concentration mistakes can't be tolerated. Your starting count must not be too complicated. You cannot have too much in the cadence.

You should evaluate your first-down calls. Make sure you self-scout. Know what you like to do on offense. Review your play-selection philosophy. Do not just grab plays that you like. Decide whether you are going to attack alignment or personnel. You need to use the press box for help. Are we getting the expected look? Are we seeing the defense we worked against in practice? We have gotten very good in our goal-line offense over the last several years, so that teams are now playing us with their regular defense inside the 10-yard line. They are using their base defense to try to stop the things that we do best on the goal line.

You need to be aware of the clock when you are on the goal line. Do you have a no-huddle stop-the-clock play? Do you have a two-play sequence that you can run from the line of scrimmage? It could be run-run, run-pass, pass-run, or pass-pass. We give our two-play sequence a name. We call it 
caravan. 
You can call it whatever you want. You must prepare for a situation when you do not have time to call two plays.

Next, I want to get to our goal-line offense. Later we will talk about practice time. Our 36 and 37 outside veer is the best short-yardage and goal-line play we have. This is our version of a power off-tackle play. We are a split-back-veer team, but we usually get a double team at the point of attack if we have a tight end in the home position. Our quarterback has one read and one reaction. We are going to read the end on the line of scrimmage, and we react to the defensive back or linebacker, whoever is the next man to show up. We have one read and one reaction. If you are going to run option football and tell the quarterback that he has two reads, you are whistling in the wind. That is not going to happen. It is too much to ask of a quarterback. We ask him to make one read and one reaction. If we run the play correctly, the outside veer will result in a three-on-two advantage. You do not have to be a great option team to run that play. We are going to combo or double-team at the point of attack. Again, it is our power off-tackle play.

I want to make sure we talk about practice time. Our weekly goal-line package is a limited version of our base offense. This allows us to minimize our goal-line practice, concentrate on potential problem situations, and perfect our execution.

Here is exactly what we do during the week. We play most of our home games Saturday night. When I was a student at Mt. Carmel, our league played all of our games on Sunday afternoon. When the Chicago Catholic High School started playing football in the 1920s, there was no NFL to compete with. Now our kids always have Sunday off, unless we are playing. Monday is a light day for us. We go out in helmets and shorts. Tuesday is our first weekday in pads. We work 10 minutes on our goal-line offense against any unusual defensive fronts you may show us on the goal line in our next game or against what we think you may show us based on your package. On Wednesday we spend 10 minutes on your base goal-line defense. Once we get into the season, we do very little full contact. We do go live on the goal line. We get after it. It is ones versus our ones. There is less chance in getting someone hurt on the goal line.

When we teach tackling in the preseason, we have one rule. You can never tackle anyone on your own team below the waist. That does two things for us. First, it saves on ankle and knee injuries. Second, it makes our defensive players better tacklers. They have to run to be in position to make tackles. They can't just be diving at people. We never allow anyone to tackle below the waist in practice.

On Thursday our goal-line offense works against any defensive stunts and combinations of fronts you may show. We go over all the possible blitz situations. If we play on Saturday, we will go out in light gear on Friday. We have our helmets and thigh boards on. I do

 





Page 131

not make our players wear knee braces on Friday. We are out for about one hour and 15 minutes. It is basically a mix-it-up day. We do a little of everything. We may be on the goal line 10 minutes or 15 minutes, depending on what we feel we need to work on.

Next, I want to show you a couple of sheets that we keep during the week in practice. It will not mean a lot to you, but they are sheets that you may want to develop. At the top of the sheet is "Mt. Carmel Running Game Tally Sheet." Along the top are all the formations that we use. Along the side are the even-numbered running plays and another list of the odd-numbered running plays. A lot of this is the same play but with different blocking schemes. In August and during the season, we have kids that keep a chart and tally the plays that we run. They tally those up so we know when we get into a game how many times we have run a particular play in practice. If we say that 32 and 33 are going to be big plays for us this week, we better make sure we are running enough of those plays in practice. We do the exact same thing with the passing game. You can develop your own chart or tally sheets. This is something I had my wife put together on the computer. We list all of our formations that we will use. Then we list all of our plays down the list. We have one area where we keep special down-and distance plays. We do not use all of our plays every week, but we have them listed. You can develop your own sheet to meet your need. One sheet is for the running game and one for the passing game.

Men, I am going to be here all night. If you want to sit down and talk about the things we do, I will be glad to visit with you. I am going to go very quickly on some of the things we do. Then I will let you see it on a videotape. I will cover the X's and O's with you later. I want to talk about our 36 and 37 outside-veer play. As you can see, we have one read, and we are going to react to the defensive back on the outside. If the linebacker scrapes, he will react to the linebacker. We teach the quarterback to cut up underneath the linebacker if he scrapes outside.

36 OUTSIDE VEER4 CALL

36 OUTSIDE VEER5 CALL

36 OUTSIDE VEER40 CALL

I would like to have someone come up so I can demonstrate how we run the option. I ask the running back to put his bottom hand across the belt. When we want to hand the ball off, we want to pronate the thumb down so the top of the hand is up. Notice how the elbow comes up when we turn the thumb down. It gives us a better pocket. On the outside veer, the quarterback is going to open at 4 o'clock, cross over and shuffle, shuffle. Now, can I tell you to take one long and two short steps? No! You have to work that out depending on the size of your quarterback. It is an individual technique.

The quarterback wants to get the ball into the pocket of the running back. If we want to give the ball to the running back, we simply pull the back hand, press with the front hand, and continue on. If the quarterback wants to keep the ball, he must read the end.

 





Page 132

If the end attacks the mesh, the quarterback snaps the ball off at the elbow of the running back and tucks the ball under the chin. Now the quarterback is going to turn up the alley. He wants to run for a first down.

We used to teach the pitch with the thumbs down. We now teach the basketball pitch. It is a little thing, but if I get hit as I make the pitch in a basketball fashion, the ball goes up in the air. We have a chance to catch the pitch if it is in the air. If the ball is pitched with the thumb down, the ball is likely to go on the ground, and that is difficult to handle.

Those were finesse plays. If we want to run a power play, we can run what we call the same play with zing motion. We want to make sure we secure the linebacker or the B gap. We are still going to read the same man, but this gives us power at the point of attack.

36 OUTSIDE VEERZING MOTION

If we want to hand the ball to the lead back, it becomes an I formation iso play. Now the blocking back is up at the line of scrimmage, and my tailback is only four and one-half yards deep. We can move the Z back in motion, so we can pick up the linebacker in the C or B gap. If we want to give the ball to the wingback we call it 08.

08FAKE DIVE, ZING MOTION

If the defense sees the wideout coming in motion, they think it is the lead play again. This time we fake the dive and give the ball to the wingback. These are old wing-T sweep principles.

We are only pulling one guard instead of two, because the front-side guard has to block down or he is covered on the goal line. We want to go inside or outside. We are going to go where the defense is not. We tell our down defender to read the defense and replace him. Don't run away from him; replace him.

While the video is warming up, I want to leave you with this thought. This is something we teach the kids. Earl Nightingale said, "You are what you think about." I think he got it from Emerson, to tell you the truth. We added this to the quote: "You are what you think about and act upon!" It is important that we tell our kids that if they want to be a good athletes and students, they must do the things that will allow them to be successful in that particular area.

My time is up. I will be glad to spend time with you if you want. Thank you.

 





Page 133

Hal Mumme

An Update on the New UK Offense

University Of Kentucky

Thank you. It is really good to be here today. It is an honor to get to speak at this great clinic. I hope our staff has had a chance to visit with you this week. I apologize that I have not been here for the whole clinic, and I know this is a big clinic for the state of Kentucky. My wife has cancer right now, and these were the only five days she was allowed to leave Valdosta, Georgia, to come to Lexington to look for a house. We have been house hunting the last four or five days. I am sure some of you can identify with that. The good thing is that we have found a house that we can buy, and we will be able to move in very soon. In the future, I intend to be at this clinic each year. Hopefully, our staff has gotten to know some of the coaches here.

I have spent a lot of my career as a high school football coach. That part of the game is really important to me. I hope it is something that carries over into our program at the University of Kentucky.

A few years ago a friend of mine by the name of Spike Dykes of Texas Tech came to play in our golf tournament at Valdosta State. We had a couple of good years at Valdosta State. We were talking with Spike, and he asked me what my ambitions were. I said, "I think I want to stay a head coach." A lot of Division II head coaches move up to become coordinators at a Division I school. I told Spike that I liked being the head coach and that I liked being the boss. I guess that was the basic reason. He asked me what I thought my next step would be in the coaching profession. I said, "Well, my last name is M-U-M-M-E, I coach at V-A-L-D-O-S-T-A State, and we play our games in a high school stadium. I have set my goals for the next job to be in a school where everyone would know where we were talking about without having to spell it out. It is hard enough with a name like Mumme to communicate. You pick up the phone and say, 'This is Hal Mumme from Valdosta State.' You have to spell everything in the sentence for them." So I told Spike Dykes that I wanted to coach someplace where the school owned their own stadium and when you said the school, everyone would know where you were. Then all I would have to do would be to spell my last name. I guess the good Lord took care of us. He fulfilled my dreams with the Kentucky job. It is great to be in the state and in the SEC.

My topic today is supposed to be "An Update on University of Kentucky Football." I have only been here for two and a half months, so I do not have a lot to say about that. I know we have not had a winning season in eight years. I know we have a great quarterback. If he can overcome my coaching the next few years, he probably will be one of the best in the nation. We have a long way to go in terms of the rest of the positions. However, here are some things that may be of interest to you. After that, I will talk about some offense. That is what I like to talk about. Then I will give you a chance to ask questions about what we are going to do. Also, I will show a highlight video at the end of the talk.

I do not think the job at the University of Kentucky is any different from any other jobs that I have had as the head coach. The head coach is in charge of these things. He must have complete vision. What I mean by that is this: You must be able to visualize what you want the program to look like. This carries down to your staff. Your defensive coordinator must visualize what the defense looks like. You must visualize what it looks like in every situation. You must visualize what it looks like when you play an option team, what it looks like when you play a passing team, what it looks like at its inception. You have to visualize what it will look like at the start of the season and in the off-season as you group the players by position and into units. As you go through the spring, you must visualize what the team is supposed to look like at the end of spring. Next year, the high schools of Kentucky will be able to have spring practice, and you will be able to visualize what your players are doing. I think that will help the game in Kentucky. But the head coach must be able to totally visualize the situations.

 





Page 134

As he gets into the season and into game planning, he must have a complete vision of what that defense looks like on 1st and 10, 2nd and short, or 3rd and long. If he can't do that, he is in trouble. If he has to go to a play book, it will be a tough year. If you go to your defensive coordinator and ask him what he is going to do on 3rd and 10 against North Alabama and he can't tell you, then he is not ready to be your defensive coordinator.

The best thing I can tell the young coaches here today is this: You must study the game. You cannot have a good vision of what things should look like until you study the game. It is great that you are here at this clinic. It is great that you go visit colleges. At UK we will be open to the coaches, and you can come anytime you want to. We are going to have a totally open-door policy. I think you should come to see us, but I think you should go other places as well. You need to spend a lot of time educating yourself on your particular aspect of the game. As this carries over to the head coach, he must be able to do all of these things.

The head coach must have a vision of what his offense is going to look like, what his defense is going to look like, and what his special teams are going to look like. That is the game. Then you get into other aspects of the game. How are you going to handle academics? How are you going to handle discipline? What are the boundaries that you are going to draw?

One of the problems we are having to deal with is this: There are no hard lines for our young people. Everything is a fine line or a gray area. There is not much right or wrong anymore. At UK we have some ideas about discipline. We are not going to let our players cross the boundaries. Now, that does not mean that we have to be very regimented. We are not a very regimented staff, and we do not run a very regimented program. We are going to have a lot of discipline. There are certain things they will not be allowed to do. If you steal, you cannot play for the Cats. If you do drugs, you cannot play for the Cats. If you beat up on your wife or girlfriend, you cannot play for the Cats. There are some other items that I deal with concerning our players. The point I want to make is that I have the process all thought out. I can walk through the office door and sit at that desk and wait for the next problem to happen. If I am in the office 150 days, I can promise you there will be at least 149 days when there is a problem ringing on the other end of that phone. That is what head coaches must be able to do-handle problems. If you do not have a concrete plan about your discipline, when the phone rings, you are grasping for straws. You are not sure how to handle the situation. If you have thought all of this out ahead of time, all you have to do is to make the decisions and to solve the problems on the other end of the phone. It is not always discipline. It could be academics, boosters, fund-raising, budgets, and other things. If you are going to be the head coach, you must have an idea what you are going to do about this. What are you going to do in the off-season? Who is going to be in charge of that part of the program? How are you going to handle it, and how are you going to approach it? When are you going to have spring practice? If you want to be a head coach, you must have a clear vision about all of those aspects. When those problems walk through your door every day, you do not have to dream up a new way of dealing with the problems.

The best thing I can tell you about the update of UK football is that we do have those things covered. We do not have a lot of players, so to speak. We do have a tremendous schedule. We do have a lot of enthusiasm about recruiting in Kentucky and every area that we can. We are going to recruit the southwest part of Ohio and the state of Kentucky as one area. One of our assistants will have that area, and he will cover every school in that area. We will know where every player is from the ninth grade up in that area. That is our goal over the next two years.

From there we are going to position-recruit in Texas, Georgia, Florida, New Jersey, and Samoa. Some of you may laugh when we say Samoa. We have had a player from Samoa every year. We were late this year and did not get a player from there. We will go any place we have to go to recruit. We feel we have great ties in these areas. I will send the position coach to those areas to recruit those players. If it is an offensive lineman, I will send Coach Guy Morriss, our offensive line coach. That is my vision of how to recruit. If we can convince the people in the area of southwest Ohio

 





Page 135

and Kentucky so that each year we can get between 7 and 15 players, that will be about 50 percent of our scholarships. If we can get the best players from this area who can play in the SEC, we have a big head start.

The reason I like to recruit this way is because of what I have experienced over the years. A lot of you have had great players who go on to college football and just seem to get lost in the shuffle. Here is what happens. You send a linebacker coach to Texas, and he recruits an offensive lineman. What happens to the kid from Texas? The kid is 17 years old and is a senior in high school. The linebacker coach is 40 years old. The two develop a close relationship over the 11 or 12 months of the recruiting period. The coach convinces the player how good he is. He spends a lot of time either calling him, writing him, or seeing him. He convinces him to come to UK, or Tennessee, or wherever. They develop a close relationship. The player signs. Then comes August 5, and the season comes around. He is an offensive lineman. He comes to the campus, and who does he deal with when he gets there? He deals with the offensive linemen and the offensive line coach. That is who he spends about 17 of the 20 hours per week that we are allowed to work with them. When is he ever going to see the linebacker coach who signed him? Hardly ever. If the player is on the scout team, the coach may see him. When do they ever talk or visit? We do not want that to happen to our players. We are not going to recruit that way. If we can recruit in this area and get to know the players over a period of years at camps, then we will sign them. But we are going to send the position coach to recruit the players.

You see kids all of the time who get caught in staff politics and do not make it. Now, if one of our coaches tells me that he does not like a player, I can tell him it is his fault because he recruited him. I can tell him to get down there and start liking him. They are not excused. This eliminates a lot of problems. This is what I am talking about: having a vision about what you are doing.

You must have a vision of how you are going to accomplish a set of objectives. Our set of objectives at the University Of Kentucky is to win the SEC. I mean in 
football. 
I wanted to say that so that everyone would know what I was saying. I love Rick Pitino. I think he does a great job. I had a great week with him this past week. I went with him to play Alabama. He is one hell of a coach and a good guy. We want to win in football. His program is the one we would like to emulate.

That is my idea of where we are as far as an update on Kentucky football. We have to recruit some more players. We do have a vision, and we do have a plan. We want all of you to get involved in the plan. We have a JV program for our walk-on players. That is the way we are going to handle that situation. Ever since the NCAA started the gender equity plan, they said we could only bring in 105 players to two-a-day practices. Well, 85 of those players are scholarship players. That means you only have 20 walk-ons. In this area, I will promise you, there are more than 20 players who want to walk on at UK. Coach Hatcher is here today. He has an office full of tapes of players who want to walk on. At this point, we have about 100 who have indicated that they want to walk on.

Each year we can invite 20 players to walk on. If we have 70 to 80 players who want to walk on, then we make 50 to 60 players mad. What we are going to do is this. We are not going to bring any of them. If they are walk-ons, they come when school starts. They will play a JV schedule. This may sound old-fashioned, because most teams do not do this anymore except service academies. We are going to play a four-game JV schedule. Those players will not practice with the varsity. We have a staff made up of GAs and student coaches. By doing this, they will get looked at. They will play Hargrave Military Academy, Georgia Military, and Middle Georgia College. Also, Campbellsville College JVs have agreed to play us. We are going to play a four-game schedule with those walk-ons in October. Instead of only bringing 20 in August, we can bring them all out when school starts.

Walk-ons do three things in Division I football. They sit on one knee and eat a lot of ice, then they get unhappy, then they quit. That is about it. They do not really get looked at. It is hard to show what they can do when all they do is run scout-team plays. They get tired of

 





Page 136

being yelled at by a 40-year-old coach holding up a card and telling them to ''run this play.'' That is not much of a tryout or a look. By having a JV program, we feel we can develop some talent. We can bring them in, set them aside, put them on another field, and let them learn our system. They will play the four games in October. At the end of that season, the JV coaches will make recommendations, and we will bring 25 to 30 up to the varsity level.

At that point, we have our 85 players practicing in the fall. Because we have low numbers of players, we have to use a lot of players who are twos to run the cardteam plays. Toward the end of the year, they are not enthusiastic about doing that. At the end of the season, those JVs are excited because they can show the varsity coaches what they can do. It is a good system. We used it for five years at Valdosta State and it helped us win a championship. This past season, our starting strong safety, our starting center, our punt-return man, and one of our key backup receivers were all scholarship players who were walk-ons when they were freshmen and had come up through the junior varsity. It allowed us to fill in those scholarships that we lose when we have to put someone off the team for discipline or a player does not make his grades. This gives us a pool of talent that we can draw from. We need the high school coaches here to help us on this. We can't go out and recruit walk-ons. You can tell your players that they will get a fair look at the University of Kentucky with our JV program if they want to walk on.

I want to show a video of our games last year so that you can see what we are doing on offense. We only have four running plays. The lead has always been the best running play for us. We like corner routes a lot, and we coach them a bunch. Notice that our offensive linemen are in a two-point stance a lot. I think you can see what we are doing. [Video]

Next, I want to talk about our offensive philosophy. We came up with this in 1986, when I was the head coach at Copperas Cove High School in Texas. We had 1500 students in our school. They had won 14 games in 10 years. The next smallest school that we had on our schedule had over 2000 students. We were outmanned. On top of that, all of our kids lived around Ft. Hood. At that time it was the largest military base in the free world. We would get the kids in the ninth grade on up. We came up with this philosophy. We had seen Brigham Young University use this system, and it worked. We felt if we could teach this to high school students, we could teach it to anyone. We did it as an experiment, and it worked out. We have been doing it ever since. Our one basic statement about our philosophy would be this: Throw the short pass as many times as possible to people who score.

In the three years at Copperas Cove, we won 14 games. We beat four schools that the school had never beaten. We made the kids and the community proud of the program. We did not win the championship, basically because we were outmanned. In three years we had one Division I football player to sign a scholarship. By comparison, most of the schools we played against signed four or five Division I players per year.

We told our players that we could not do a lot of things well. We were not very big, and we did not have a lot of numbers. We convinced our kids that we could do this: We could throw the ball better than anyone in the state of Texas. The kids got excited about it. We did everything we could to promote that concept. We did it over and over again in terms of promotion and talking about it. We tried to pick out of our younger grades the skilled people. You can find them if you will look.

When we were at Copperas Cove, we had to coach both sides of the ball. Mike Majors, who is our defensive coordinator, coached our offensive line at Copperas Cove. He helped develop a lot of this offense. We just talked about these skilled people all of the time. We wanted to know who the players were who came along who could score. Who were the guys who were coming along who had a knack for scoring?

We identified the players who we felt could score. Then we put them in a position to score. If you looked at what they were doing at BYU and what we had been doing as an I formation and option coach through the years in Texas, you could see the similarities. All we were ever trying to do was to take the kid who had a

 





Page 137

knack for scoring and put him in the tailback position. Then we want to get him some space to work in.

A lot of this thinking started a long time ago. When John McKay was at USC with O. J. Simpson at tailback, they would pull the linemen and run the student-body right sweep. Basically, what they were doing was to get their best back on the corner and have him stretch the play outside. He may stretch the play all the way to the sideline and go out of bounds. They would run the play over and over. Then suddenly he cut back and ran the ball into the end zone.

If you looked at the wishbone teams that Barry Switzer had at Oklahoma and Darrell Royal had at Texas in the 1960s, their object was to get the ball to one particular spot on the field with the pitch. They would arc-block with the lead back and be able to kick the ball out to the deep back. They knew they could score with the great backs that they had if they could get the back around the corner.

If the defense stretched outside, the fullback up the middle would score on you. Of course, they all had a quarterback who the defense had to take away. They were trying to get the football into the hands of the players who could score. They wanted to put them into space where they did not have to go over a pile in order to gain yardage or score. The people who were innovative on offense were doing that. They were trying to get the football to players who could score.

At West Texas State and at UTEP, we had done it like this. We had a nice little package that Grant Teaff and Bill Young had made popular at Baylor University. In those days, most people ran a 50 look. We ran the isolation with the wingback coming back in motion. We would run it and run it. After we set it up, we would reverse body-block, and the onside guard would pull and cut up on the free safety who was running in the alley.

A lot of people had the ideas of getting guys into space who could score touchdowns. The objective of offensive football is to score. We took the same idea and put it in at Copperas Cove. We decided that we would do all we could to put the ball in the hands of the people who could score and see what they could do. The formation we got into was this. We split both ends and had a wide slot to one side. We went from a split backfield set. Everyone liked to play cover three or cover one in those days. They wanted to leave seven in the box to stop the run. The teams we were playing were not gearing up to play us. They were getting ready to play the good teams. Those teams ran the ball out of two backs and two tight ends. We made them pay for not being ready to defend us. We would usually try to get the split end deep. We would look for a player who could catch the ball over his shoulders and the one who had the best speed on the team.

Mike Majors and I would sit in my little office and write all of the players' names down on a dry-marker board. I would get to make two picks for offense. I got to pick the quarterback and the split end. After that, Mike got 17 picks for defense. That is how we split our players up. I knew for us to have a chance to win, we had to be able to play defense. All of the good players who were average high school players ended up on defense. On offense we ended up with one fast man at the split end or X position. We did end up with a trigger man under center.

Let me show you how we lined up. We had an ex-tuba player at center. At the left guard, we had a player who was big. His name was Chester Jordan. When we got there, he was a ninth grader. He was 6' 4" and weighed 340 pounds. He could run the 40-yard dash in a day. He could bench-press the bar. When you take over a new job, you have some guys left from the previous staff. We would always ask them to tell us

 





Page 138

something positive about each player. At Copperas Cove we had 70 to 80 players that we asked about. No one had anything positive to say about Chester Jordan. He had duck feet, and they stuck out to the side when he walked down the hall. He had a big butt and duck feet. Finally, I asked the track coach about Chester. He said, "The best thing I can say about Chester is this. He was my manager for the track team last year. When we would go to track meets away from home, Chester could eat a whole loaf of bread and a jar of mayonnaise in one meal." We took him and started working with him because he was big. The good thing about Chester was the fact that you could not go around him very fast. For sure, you could not go through him.

At the halfback spots, we would have little players who were good athletes. They would try hard for you. On the outside, I would cheat on Coach Majors. I would not put the two wide receivers on the strong side on the board. I would get two basketball players and put them outside. I put them out wide because I knew they were not going to hit anyone. You could not wad them up and throw them on anyone. I would make a little deal with them. All during the football season they did not have to come to a certain part of the practice. They could go in the gym and work on basketball. I would let them go in the gym. Then during basketball season, two days a week they would come with me, and we worked on throwing and catching the football. We did that all year long, and the two got really good at the skills of the game. We only asked them to do a few things.

We would find a man at the wideout that could run the curl. The inside man would need to know how to run a flat and a wheel route. That is the way we set up our attack. We ended up leading the state of Texas in passing. We won some games that the school had not won before. The kids felt good about the program, and I moved on to a college job.

The thing that carried over was in keeping within the philosophy. At the split end, we had a man who could catch the ball over his shoulder. At the halfbacks, we had players who could catch the ball underneath. The left halfback may not always be the best runner, but he would be able to come out of the backfield and catch the ball. He knew how to get open when you played in the street. I always ask the kids this question: "If you were playing street ball, who would be the first man you would pick as your receiver?" It would always be the halfback. We would take that set and put our best athletes on the other side. We would teach them a couple of things. First, we would teach them what was covered and what was uncovered. In high school football, you can look out on the field a lot of times and find the defender covering the split end, who has his feet 10 yards from the line of scrimmage. To me, that is not coverage. We had a signal when we had that coverage. We would have the quarterback take the ball, even in the shotgun, and he would just rise up, throw the ball outside to the split end, and let him be one on one with that defender at 10 yards. We worked on the play and found out that the break point was about 7 yards deep. If the defender was at 7 yards, we had to start looking at the scouting report. If the defender was at 7 yards and really quick, you do not want to throw the ball to the split end. If the defender was on the slow side, we would do it. We got the quarterback and the split end to understand what was covered and what was not covered.

We were going to force teams to bring the corner up. Since that was our fastest man, if the defense brought the corner up too close, we would send the split end right on by him. We just started working on that play. If the defender came up really tight, we would run by him and throw the ball over the top. If the defender got in the 7-yard area and we did not think we could beat him with the quick pass, we would run him deep.

 





Page 139

If we did not think we could beat him deep, the quarterback would throw the ball behind the receiver. The quarterback would throw the ball a little flatter and over the receiver's shoulder. He did not want to throw the ball over the top of the defender. What we did was to force the defense to cover the split end tightly with the corner and safety. Most defenses wanted to play eight-man fronts. They wanted to play that safety man in the middle. When we got them to move the safety over on the split-end side, we had to be able to attack the slot side. Now they had the same problems on the slot side with the Z end. We were teaching him the same things.

After a few years, teams started working on the uncovered aspects of our offense. Then we started pulling linemen outside to block for the receivers. That got us to our quick-screen game. Every time we came to the line of scrimmage, the defense had to cover the X end, or the quarterback was going to get him the ball. If the defense covered him with two men, we would go to the Z receiver. If you did cover him with two defenders, we would go to Z. Soon we had two receivers covered by four defenders. If you start looking at your running game, you have to feel that is a good deal.

The coaches before we got there ran the I formation. They tried to run the ball between the tackles. The defense played them with an eight-man front. They could not block them because they were outnumbered. We wanted to get the advantage back on our side. If you will look at the thing, you can see what I am talking about. If the defense is covering the X and Z receivers with two men, then we are going to look at the Y man in the slot. If the defense did not put someone close to Y, we did the same thing with him. You could occupy the corner, and Y would have to work on the strong safety.

You can take a player who is not a tight end and play him at the Y. You can take a basketball player and play him there. You can do all kind of things with him. You can put him in motion and do a lot of other things. He can control the man on the running game. By using our philosophy to throw short and all of that, we have controlled five of the defenders.

It does not really matter how you line them up inside on defense. You have a chance to run the ball. You have a hat for their hat. If your players will get after them, you can run the football. We play little games with the defense over and over again. The thing that will happen is that you will get to guessing with them. You do not know when the defense is going to blitz or drop seven or what they will do. What we did was to take it out of the coach's hands and put it in the quarterback's hands. If the defense is guessing with me, he is in bad shape, because the quarterback is calling the plays. If the quarterback is calling the plays, he can see what is happening. As the game goes on, he knows what the defense is doing. Now the quarterback becomes a part of your offensive philosophy. He becomes a part of the rhythm of the game, so to speak. The quarterback can check plays a lot better than the coach. We were teaching the quarterback what we wanted. We wanted to give the quarterback enough freedom to call the plays at the line. We had to give him a system that was simple enough that he can change the plays with one word most of the time.

 





Page 140

This is how we taught the quarterback to do what we wanted. We took our starters and lined them up in the formation. We took all of the other players who thought they were quarterbacks and put them on defense. They were the Will, Mike, and Sam linebackers. We put the extra receivers out wide. All this for us is a walk-through. We put the ball in the quarterback's hands. I stood behind the offense where the quarterback could not see me. I would motion to one of the defenders to move. I would have one defender drop too deep or come up tight. The job of the quarterback was to give the one-word call and throw the ball to the right receiver. The next time, I would have the defense do something different. The quarterback had to react to the defense, make his call, and throw the ball to the right man. By the time we got to the end of the two-a-day practices, all of our quarterbacks knew how to locate the underneath defenders. As we got better at it, we would put a couple of defenders back deep as safeties. We were able to get them to locate the linebacker, corners, and safeties. If the quarterback can locate those seven defenders for you really quickly, it is easy to sort out the other four defenders. This was just a mental drill we did.

The other thing we had to do was this. We did not have fast people. After the receiver caught the pass, we had to do something to make sure we gained yards. If you were a running team and could average 5 yards per carry, I am sure you would feel good about it. This is what we told our wide receiver to do after he caught the short pass. We did not want him to duck and dodge or shake and bake. We wanted him to make 5 yards straight ahead. Then God would tell him where to go after that. The main emphasis was to catch the ball and get upfield. How do you teach this? This sounds easy to do, and everyone says that. But how do you do that day after day, week after week, so that it works? For warm-up, this is what we do. We got rid of the stretching. I wanted to practice two hours or less. I felt that was all we could get out of them. The players and parents loved this. I did not like the stretching drills when I played. Have you ever seen a dog get up off of the front porch and stretch before he chases a car? If he is going to chase a car, he is going to go chase the car. If you are going to play football, you come ready to play football. Football is not a sport where you need to do a lot of different things to train. The way to get ready to play football is to put on your equipment and go out and play football. We came up with this warmup drill. We are going to spend 10 to 15 minutes each day doing this drill. We are going to be warmed up. We are not going to be tight. We have a line just before they get to our practice field. When they cross the line, we let them know that they have come to practice. We are really serious about it. "Men, this is not softball practice." If you are a receiver, you come over and get in the receivers' line. We get the quarterbacks in another line. We give the quarterbacks the "drop of the day." It may be a five- or three-step drop. The quarterback works on his footwork on the lines. He takes the snap and comes back on the line. The receivers jog toward the dummies. They end up closer to one dummy than the other dummy. The quarterback throws the ball at the number opposite the dummy. The quarterback wants to keep the ball below the dummy. The drill represents what happens in a game. The dummy represents the linebackers. After the receiver catches the ball, we want him to turn and head straight upfield. It is a warm-up drill. He can jog straight up the field.

Now, we go back to our philosophy: Throw short as many times as possible to people who can score. We are trying to put X, Y, Z, H, and F in positions to score. We do two drills that we can do every day and not get bored. If you come to watch us practice, you will see that we have only four running plays and five passes, and we have our quick game, which I have described to you today. That is pretty much it, men. We have a zillion sets. We are going to use the one that puts our guys in the right position. We are going to put them in

 





Page 141

space where they can run straight upfield with it. When we get the defense spread out, we can run the football. That is why we have always had a 1000-yard rusher on our team. That is an overview of our offensive philosophy. That is kind of what we are about.

We are more comfortable in a 30-28 game than we are in a game that ends up 7-6. In all of the places that I have been, we have had to develop a mentality and a philosophy with our kids that did not destroy them when we gave up a touchdown.
