On defense we want to have 11 hats on the ball. That is really big for us. We run the pursuit drill endlessly. We will take speed over size. We will always take character over speed. We want 11 players who believe they can stop the opponent. You must have a philosophy for the entire game. In the SEC you are not going to play many 7-6 games. I did not coach in many of them at Iowa Wesleyan or Valdosta State. I was outnumbered at Copperas Cove, and I had to hope for the high-scoring games. We all have been on the sideline when we were involved in a tight game. When the opponents score a touchdown, you can just feel the wind go out of your team. The whole sideline is down. It has an effect on the offense and defense. We do not want that mentality. We want our offense to believe that we are going to score every time we have the football. Because of that, our defense can play as aggressively as they want. We want speed and aggressive football players.

Because we are a man-protection team and because we throw so much, we try to make all of our running game fit our pass protection. We can run the running plays that we use out of the two-point stance. That is where it starts. We want to create running lanes and throwing lanes. We have to be able to pass-block up front. Since we man-protect, we have to win the battle up front. When we have five of them who can win the battle up front, we have an offense. We work on this over and over in practice.

I appreciate the opportunity to speak with you. If you want to come to UK, remember, practice is open and our doors are open. It is a privilege to represent the commonwealth of Kentucky. Thank you very much.

 





Page 142

Fred Pagac

The Eight-Man-Front Secondary Play

Ohio State University

Thank you. If you saw us play this year, you know that we had a great defense. We had some good players, and they were smart. They played hard. We had kids who understood the defense. There were two things that improved us to the status we had this year. We ended up in the top 10 of every defensive category there was. We finished number one in the country against the pass. We ended up fourth or fifth against the run. We had a good defense, and the credit goes to the players.

We had a pressure package. We weren't going to line up and play read defense. In our league, the average offensive lineman weighs about 310 pounds. We played with guys who were a whole lot smaller than that. We didn't want to get in a situation where we played a hit-and-read defense. If we had, we would have gotten mauled off the ball.

Our premise this year was to stop the run. We wanted to put people in passing situations so we could use our talent. We had two outstanding corners. We may have the first corner picked in the NFL draft, and he is only a junior. We basically told our corners to have a nice day on the outside. We were going to use the safeties to help stop the run.

We wanted to get people in a 2nd-and-long situation. To us, that is a 2nd and 8 or more. After we did, we were going to use a "zone dog." I believe in timing in the blitz. You have to be able to bluff and disguise the coverages and blitzes. We have to keep things simple, because of the 20-hour rule. When we go into a game, we are going to have basically two blitzes and two zone dogs for each front we play, plus our bluff scenario. When we get into the bluff game, it is important to understand the automatic system of the team you are playing. We want them to check off and take away what they are checking to.

With our defense, we play basically three fronts. We play a reduction, which we call "tight." We play this defense on 1st down and about 40 percent of the time. We have the ability to play two deep, a quarter scheme, and a weak zone from this defense.

We like to zone the formation in this defense. We call "tight 10 zone." We spoke our safety to wherever they run the ball. If the offense runs to the split end, we run sky support weak. The linebacker communicates that to the safeties. The down linemen play their gaps as they usually do. In this coverage, we pick up the extra player in the run game. We adjust to all sets to keep an extra man in the running scheme. In the Big Ten, the teams were not patient enough to throw the ball to the outside all the time. That is what we were giving up and forcing the offense to throw.

The next thing from this would be our man-free concept. We have the ability to show a sky-support defense and play man principles.

The second front we play about 25 percent of the time. We call it "slide." It is our stack package. There are three linebackers stacked off the ball. I was a linebacker coach for 12 years before I became the coordinator. Nine years ago, our defensive coordinator came in and wanted to play the Miami stack package, but he didn't coach the linebackers. We used to get our ass whipped with the tight zone cutback. We were asking a linebacker or safety to take an inside running gap. He had the ability to fall back into another zone. This year, we went to a 6 technique. We still played flow linebackers and quarter scheme and put the Leo end on the inside gap. That really helped our defense. We forced the ball to run east and west. We used our safeties for run support or robbers in pass coverage.

 





Page 143

The corners played inside out, man to man. That gave us nine people in the box.

The third defense, which we played about 25 percent, was the 46 defense. We have played this defense since 1983. The guy who this defense was named for, Doug Plank, helped us install it. We have pretty good input on what is going on in this defense. It was a double-eagle look. When we played this defense, it was probably our best defense statistically.

The key thing on this is that the linebacker coverage in the three defenses does not change whether you are playing man or zone. The secondary does not change in the three defenses. We are playing different fronts, but we are playing the same coverages with all the fronts. That cuts down your teaching. A lot of people wanted to know how we improved this year. I think we have a great defensive staff. We had a good understanding of what we were trying to accomplish. The thing we improved the most was stopping the big play. We consider a run over 10 yards and a pass over 15 yards a big play. Our opponents had only 23 running plays over 10 yards in an 11-game season. Out of the 23 runs, 6 of them were reverses. Of the other 17, about half of them were missed tackles. The year before, we had 93 runs over 10 yards. The reason for that was we took away one of the bubbles in our stack defense, and we started using our safeties in our run defense. When I talk about the bubble, I'm talking about playing a 6 technique over the tight end instead of a 9 technique.

We studied our films and found that most of the big plays came from lack of tackling. Over the last 10 years, the big fad has been stripping the football. We work the crap out of that. I'm not sure that we took away from our kids the ideas of tackling. From the first day of spring ball, we practice tackling every day that we are in pads. If we met on the practice field, as long as we had head gear on, we practiced tackling. We became a much better tackling team this year.

The third thing we did was work on an attitude principle. Attitude means a lot of different things. The first thing we wanted was to be tough. We wanted to be the "get after 'em" team, the hustle guys, and the hitting team. I think the big thing was the way we handled the mental things on the sideline. If we had a mental mistake or a physical screwup, we didn't jump their asses when they came off the field. We had a "so what" attitude. We felt that the next play was the most important one. We can't do anything about the other play. We said, "Let's go back and play some more defense.'' We gave all of our kids silver bullets. They were empty of course. That was our motto. We wanted silver bullets flying to the ball. The big deal was that the most important play is the next play. Play the play you are playing hard. Play with great physical effort. If you don't make the play, hell with it. Get ready for the next one.

We tried to get our safeties involved in rundowns. When we called our tight front, we checked the coverage to the formation they come out in. We call defenses by the personnel that the offense puts out on the field.

I'm going to show you our tight 10 zone. This is our huddle call and our automatic system. A lot of communication goes on in our huddle system. The Willie linebacker is checking the opposite sideline and trying to call out the different personnel groups that are coming on the field from the opponent's bench. He would call out "snake." To us, that means two tight ends and one back. If he called "viper," that would be three wideouts and one back. Those are examples of our terminology. The linebacker who calls the defense knows who is in the game, and that helps him make the calls.

If the offense comes out in regular personnel, he makes a left call. When he said "left," that aligned our defensive line. The bubble goes to the call side. Our nose guard is in a 1 technique to the tight end. The defensive end is in a 5 technique. To the weak side the tackle is in the 3 technique, and the Leo is in a 5

 





Page 144

technique. The Willie linebacker is in a 9 technique on the tight end. The Mike, or bubble, linebacker is in the 3 technique. The Buck, or stack, linebacker is in a 2 technique to the weak side.

If we are playing a team that is a pro-set, split-end-oriented team, we adjust the secondary. The secondary starts off showing a shell, or disguised defense. We give our secondary people a lot of freedom in moving around. Prior to the snap of the ball, the right safety yells, "Sky, sky, sky," and moves into a sky-support position. He does that for the front people. The backside safety is yelling, "Backer, backer, backer," and is moving into the middle of the formation. That is for the backside linebacker.

The Buck linebacker knows there is a weak zone his way. Any time we call this defense, we play the run and react to the pass. If the Buck linebacker gets any kind of flow to the split-end side, he fills the A gap inside out. If he gets a lead blocker coming in on him, he spills it outside. The right safety will be the unblocked player. He is the fill player. The Mike linebacker is a backside hip-stack player, and the Willie linebacker will fold.

If a pass shows, we play curl-hash marks with our outside people. The Buck and Mike linebackers play the inside hooks. We have a post player in the safety, and the corners are playing a loose man coverage on the wide receivers.

If a team comes out in a twins set, that is an identity in itself, and we go to an automatic cover three. We brought our corners over with the wide receivers. Whether the field or boundary corner aligns inside depends upon the receivers. We had a kid named Shawn Springs. He always gets the best receiver. That is more teaching for the corners, but it is setting up the blitz package.

If the team came out in a weak or tilted set, we go to an automatic "sky weak" call. That is the first adjustment I showed you. It is the zone weak. That is the same defense that is pictured in diagram 4.

If a team came out in a strong set, it is an automatic call. We can go to a "sky strong" call, which is like the weak call except it is to the strong side. We also have the ability to use the term "buzz." That puts the strong safety in a linebacker position. Buzz meant we were going to have support to that side of the field. To the boundary, we have linebacker support. The free safety was the post player. In buzz, the Willie linebacker is the outside dropper in pass and leverages all inside-out blocks. He turns everything back inside to the strong safety, who is unblocked. That gave us two ways to play the defense. Our strong safety against Notre Dame made 19 tackles. We held them to about 40 yards rushing, and they are a big-time rushing team.

If a team plays two tight ends and two backs and puts their flanker to the defenses left, we would make a left call.

 





Page 145

We play 1, 5, and 9 technique to the left. To the right, we play the tackle in a 3 and the Leo in a 6 technique. The bubble linebacker is left, and the stack linebacker is right. The corner takes the flanker to the left side. To the back side, the strong safety moves up into a monster position, and the corner plays back on the second tight end in a safety position. We play a read support on the back side. We are in an eight-man front with everyone gapped up. The linebackers are fast-flowing.

If the offense came out with both the tight ends on the same side in a wing set and a split end on the back side, nothing changed. The call was still "left." We left our corner on that side with the wing. We played a read scheme to the wing and a sky call to the split end.

The next look we got predominant in the Big Ten was what we called "snake." That was two tight ends and one back. When we get this set, we make a stud call. What that meant was to study your formations. When they do this, they generally run to one of those tight ends and throw to the other. It might be the same guy, but normally it isn't. Normally, they have a big-ass blocking tight end and a little pass-receiving tight end. We set our linebacker on the running tight end and the safety aligned on the pass-receiving tight end. If the run tight end was left, our linebacker would make a left call.

The Willie linebacker will align on the blocking tight end and make a stud left call. The safeties balance opposite Willie linebacker. We are back into an eight-man front. We take away the tight zone play and plays of that nature.

Let me get into the man principles from this defense. This is something we did this year that was new for us. I went to visit with the 49ers last year. This is something they did that really helped us in our running-game defense. We could call this defense in all downs and distances. It ended up being a man-free defense with a four-man rush. Also, one of the linebackers would end up being a hole or half player. We got good mileage out of this defense against all situations. We called it "tight scarlet key." It has the same principles as the tight zone. Before, we were going to zone, whatever formation the offense would come out in. Now we are going to man all formations.

If the offense comes out in one tight end, two backs, and two wide receivers, the linemen do not change. We could change the linemen's gaps with line calls. But as a base defense, the line stays the same. We let our kids move around in the defense. But they must be smart enough to know that when the ball is snapped, they have to be in position. The Willie linebacker can move outside, inside, wide, or tight, but when the ball is snapped, he attacks the tight end with an outside-in attitude. He is predominant a pass player. He is playing man coverage on the tight end with an outside-in attitude. It is a very easy read for him. There are only two blocking schemes that he has to defend. The first is the tight end attacking him. If that happens, he punches and gets his hat to the outside in the D-gap area. If the tight end blocks down on a low hat release,

 





Page 146

he closes down and spills all blocks outside. Since he is charging from the outside, the only release that the tight end has is an inside release. If that happens, he jams him, reverses his hips, and gets to an inside-out trail position on the tight end.

In the straight I formation, the Mike and Buck linebackers key the fullback. We are showing a twodeep or quarter-shell coverage. The offense doesn't know which way the safeties are going. The safeties key the tailback. If the fullback takes on the Mike linebacker, Mike takes him on in the B gap with an inside-out charge. The Buck linebacker flows to the inside hip of Mike. If pass comes, he is the hole player on the inside. If run comes, he is the cutback player. The same thing would happen if the fullback attacked the Buck linebacker. Mike now would be the cutback player and inside hole player on the pass.

Both safeties key the tailback. If he goes left, the right safety goes to the post, and the left safety comes to support. The offense didn't know which side we were spooking to, and we always had the extra player on flow. The hardest thing to teach and get accomplished is the linebackers overrunning the ball on the cutback. It is a sound defense against the option. The support safety is the pitch player, and the post safetyafter he checks the postis the alley player.

If the offense comes out in a twins set, it is an identify in itself. We play it just like the last coverage. The linebackers key the fullback. The two safeties key the tailback. The Willie linebacker has the tight end. The problem is whether the Willie linebacker can cover the tight end. We did it this year. The kid we had doing it was not a great athlete but a smart kid and knew the difference between a high-hat release and a block release. He has help from the linebacker in the hole and the free safety.

If a team came out in a pro set with the tailback set in the weak halfback position, we made adjustments to that. We taught that the offset back is the most dangerous pass receiver. Basically, we are in sky support to the right, the difference being that the safety is man on the offset back. The Buck and Mike linebackers are on the remaining back.

If the offense came out in a pro set with the strong set, we would play it exactly like the weak set. The only difference is that we would never buzz in the scarlet key. The right safety takes the strong back, and the left safety is free.

 





Page 147

If the offense comes out with a twin set and breaks the tailback into a wide-receiver position away from the set, the strong safety takes the break. The corners take the wide receivers. The free safety is free in the middle. The Mike and Buck linebackers take the remaining back.

If they came out in what we called a buckeye set, which is a three-wide-receivers set, we made another adjustment. We put both corners on the wide receivers and the strong safety on the third receiver. The free safety was in the middle. We gave them a three-deep look. If we played a team that played a lot of this type of personnel grouping, we would pull our strong safety out of the game and put in another corner. If the third receiver is a tight-end split-out, we would leave the strong safety in the game. We pulled the Willie linebacker off the line and put him in a stack position behind the defensive end. The linebacker makes a trio call. All linebackers are in stack positions. The three linebackers are playing trio coverage on the two remaining backs. The linebackers are fast-flowing. If there is action toward Willie linebacker, he is in an outside-in attitude. The Mike linebacker blows the B gap to the Willie side. The Buck linebacker blows the A gap away from the Willie linebacker. On action the other way, the Buck linebacker is in the stack. The Mike linebacker blows the A gap, and Willie hangs back for the cutback. That particular defense helped us a lot against both run teams and pass teams.

Any time a team took the tight end out of the game, we came after the quarterback. We felt that they had shortened the distance to the quarterback.

Against snake, the two tights and one back, we want our Willie linebacker on the worst pass-receiving tight end. The call linebacker makes the call to the Willie, and Willie makes the stud call. The free safety kicks the other way, and the remaining back is taken by the Mike and Buck linebackers. We are gap-sound against the run, sound against pass, and in an excellent position on all option games.

If the offense comes out in con grouping, two tight ends and two running backs, they are doing it for a reason. Very few teams come out in this set to throw the ball. We make one of three calls. We play tight defense, Bear, or Ohio, or we substitute in the defense. The coverage will be 10 zone or man free. They are going to try to play smash mouth with us. We want to get people in gaps and people running downhill at them. When we played scarlet key coverage against the con, we matched the strong safety up on the second pass-receiving tight end. The free safety and boundary corner keyed the tailback. Mike and Buck took the fullback.

If we played our Ohio or 46 defense against the con package, we would give a left or right call to get our personnel in the proper places. We put the boundary-side corner and the free safety in the safety positions and backed them off the ball. They keyed the tailback. The Mike linebacker had the fullback. The strong safety and Buck had coverage on the two tight ends. The strong safety handled all breaks in the formation, and the Buck linebacker handled all tight-end adjustments.

We have a dog call that we worked with our scarlet key. We game-planned what we were going to do. If the offense was a straight I formation, the dog call tells us that one of our linebackers is blitzing. We don't know which linebacker it is going to be until they show

 





Page 148

us what the formation is. We had to know whether they ran the ball toward the tight end or split end. Everything we did was designed to stop the run. If they were a tight-end-running team, we blitzed the Mike linebacker through the B gap from a depth of about 2.5 to 3 yards. If he got action his way, he spilled everything outside, and the unblocked player to that side was the strong safety. Both safeties in the scarlet key are reading the tailback, so they are on the move with the tailback.

If the team is a split-end-running team, we bring the A gap player. In that case, the Buck linebacker comes through the A gap and spills everything to the safety moving with the tailback. The other linebacker was the cover linebacker, playing behind the hip of the blitzer, never overrunning the ball. Because the safeties were keyed to the tailback, you always had a player to spill the ball to. We could be super-aggressive.

We played the scarlet key on the strong and weak halfback sets with our safeties. The safety opposite the set back is the post player. The safety to the back took him in coverage or was a spill player for the blitz linebacker.

The other blitz came from a one-back set. In this set, since the Mike and Buck linebackers were both on the remaining back in our scarlet key, we called a ''flow dog." That meant that whichever way the back went was the linebacker who came. The other linebacker was the cover linebacker. It didn't matter where the wide receivers were.

Before I close, let me talk about the Ohio, 46, or Bear defense. I won't even put the corners in on this defense. Our free safety was our adjuster. This year when we run this defense, you will see him a little deeper. He is going to be 5 to 7 yards deep. The Willie linebacker and Leo are mirrored positions. They are coming off the edge against a straight I, aiming for the near hip of the fullback. If it is an offset I, they aim for the inside throat of the offset back.

The B gap players penetrate the line of scrimmage. We want them to key the ball, get their asses up in the air, and create some havoc in the backfield. They want to disrupt pulls and fold-blocking in the offensive line.

The noseguard is a backdoor player. He crowds and keys the ball. On the snap, he tries to drive his eyebrows through the center's throat. He tries to maintain inside leverage. If the center's helmet is trying to lead-block him, he tries to press him as deep into the backfield as possible and come off the back end of the center.

If we get all that done, we have two free football players plus a free safety in the hole. We call the adjuster that for a reason. He will handle all one-back breaks. He handles anything different in the formation. In a two-tight-ends and two-back set, he is the guy who walks up on the line of scrimmage. If they come up in a one-back set, the adjuster goes out and plays the coverage on the third receiver. The adjuster is a secondary player.

The Mike linebacker in the one-back set plays on the inside hip of the remaining back. He is a free football player on flow. There is no one who can get to the Mike linebacker, but he has the opposite A gap of the noseguard. If he gets flow, he is filling downhill. If the noseguard makes a mistake and gets across face on the center, the Mike linebacker may have to rock back and take the cutback.

The Willie and Leo coming off the edges have responsibility for bootleg and reverse. We tell them that if the tailback's shoulders are perpendicular to the line, there is no threat of cutback. They should slow down and check quarterback on the bootleg and split end on the reverse. If there is any threat of a cutback, they need to play that.

 





Page 149

Bill Payne

Scout Team Utilization

Buffalo High School, Wyoming

I consider it a privilege to be speaking here today. John Burke suggested that I tell you a little about my background. I have coached high school football for 10 years. I coached the defensive line for John Burke for four years at Douglas County High School in Castle Rock, Colorado. I coached the offensive line for Mike McCall for three years at Douglas County. For the past three years, I have coached at Buffalo High School in Buffalo, Wyoming. My primary responsibility is the scout team and the tight ends. As my résumé indicated, I started coaching a little late in life.

In Wyoming high school football, we have four classifications: 1A to 4A, with the largest being 4A. In 1995, Buffalo was the 2A runner-up and was state champ in 1996. Mike Moon is the head coach and does an outstanding job. This past year was his fourth state championship.

Buffalo High School is located, as you might guess, in Buffalo, Wyoming. It is located 430 miles north on I-25 where it ends. We are about 60 miles from the Montana state line. The population of Buffalo is about 3500, on a good day. As in most small towns, we have great community support for our athletic events. I am sure you have heard the standard joke: "If you want to rob a bank, do it on Friday night, because everyone will be at the football game." Actually, I do not think it would do you any good, because there is no money in the banks.

At Buffalo High School, we have 370 students. We have 60 to 65 kids out for football. We have about 40 on the varsity and 25 freshmen. There is another high school in our county. It is located 40 miles south of Buffalo. It is in Koycee. They have 85 students in their school. The WHSAA allows their kids who want to play football to travel to Buffalo to be a part of our team. We have about four kids who take advantage of this. They travel 80 miles to be on our team. We take 65 players to Black Hills State University in August for our four-a-day football camp. Black Hills State is located in Spearfish, South Dakota, and is about 150 miles east of Buffalo.

We do not platoon at Buffalo. Our average road game is over 200 miles one way. Because of the distance some of our kids have to travel, we have a strict two-hour limit on our practice schedules.

This is what our normal practice looks like. On Monday we talk and walk through our offensive and defensive game plan. We do some heavy conditioning as well. On Tuesday we work on team offense and scout-team defense. On Wednesday we work on team defense and scout-team offense.

On Thursday we do something a little different. Our kids come to practice at 6:30 in the morning. We practice one hour and fifteen minutes until 7:30 A.M. This practice is in helmets and shorts. We run through the offensive and defensive game plans and the kicking game. We feel this gives them extra time before the game. On road trips, we usually have to leave by 11 A.M. or 12 noon on Friday to be there in time for the games. Going in early on Thursday gives us some extra time.

Let's talk about the scout team. It is full of all kinds of surprises. You have to know when to chew them out, and you have to know when to say "nice job."

How many of you have seen the movie 
Rudy? 
Looks like about 25 percent. That is not bad, because the rest of my talk is based on the movie 
Rudy 
Three years ago, we went to Black Hills State for our camp. We stayed in a college dorm that had a TV lounge. One night we were in the TV lounge and the movie 
Rudy 
just happened to be on. Most of our kids watched the movie from start to finish. If you have not seen the movie, you should. It is a great training film, and it is a great

 





Page 150

story. It illustrates the role of the scout team and its players much better than I could explain it. Ever since that night, our scout team has been called the "Rudy team."

The movie is about a scout-team player at Notre Dame. I will try to explain a few points in the movie. This kid on the Notre Dame team was a scout-team player, and he got to dress for the final game of his senior year. In the movie, as the Notre Dame players went out of the locker room to the stadium, they would reach up and touch a sign that hung in the hall leading to the stadium: "Play like a champion today." I think it had the logo of a leprechaun on it. Every player who passed by the sign would reach up and touch it with one hand on the way to the field.

We are the Buffalo High School Bisons. We have a decal on our helmet with a buffalo on it. The day after we got back from camp that year, I noticed one of our decals over the door that leads out of our dressing room to our football field. Now when our kids go out to the field, they reach up with two hands and touch the buffalo on the way out, just as the Notre Dame players did in the movie. Of course, our players are a little smarter. They realized that two hands would be better than one.

Now, all of that may sound silly. But wait until you see our scrimmage vest. [Yellow scrimmage vest with "Rudy" painted on it displayed.]

While the scout team is a tough job, we do not candy-coat it or blow smoke. We try to tell it like it is. We want to have fun, but we want to stay focused. This is what we want to accomplish. (1) Show outstanding effort and attitude in duplicating opponents' plays. (2) Your job is to make the first team better. (3) The better you make them, the more playing time you get. (4) Our secret goal is to score on the first team.

We try to sell our kids on being Rudys. I do not know whether we sold it to them or they bought into it or a little of both, but being Rudy is a big part of our team. Believe it or not, when Coach Moon says that it is time to scrimmageand those of you who have not seen the film 
Rudy 
will not understand thiswe have four or five chants of "Rudy, Rudy, Rudy." They start chanting from four or five places on the field. In the movie, that is what happened. On the last play of the year, the scout-team player named Rudy got into a Notre Dame game.

We do not sugar-coat the scout team. We tell them what our goal is. We want an outstanding effort and attitude in duplicating the opponent's plays. Notice, we added 
attitude 
in there. On Tuesday we get in the huddle, and the first thing I tell them is this: "Your job is to make the first team better. That is your job. That is what you are here for." On Wednesday we tell them again, "The better you make the first team, the more playing time you are going to get." I tell them this in the huddle. Again, our secret goal is to score on the first team.

This Rudy deal has been going on for three years. Our kids know that being a Rudy is being part of the team. That goes with the territory. We tried something new this year that worked out really well. When our freshman team completed its season, we still had three or four varsity games left. This year, the freshman coach picked five players from his team to be Rudys for the rest of the season for the varsity. Freshmen cannot play varsity at our school. However, they can play on scout teams. This is voluntary, because it extends the freshman season. So far no one has refused it; in fact, they consider it an honor.

As the scout team coach, I try to be as positive as I can. In fact, it gets a little sickening at times. But when my 5' 9", 120-pound tackle comes back to the huddle looking through the ear hole of his helmet, it is not going to do me any good to chew him out. My big job is to convince him that we are having fun. Sometimes that gets tough to do.

Generally speaking, Buffalo High School gives no individual awards. We do not have a "Most Valuable Player" award or anything like that. However, two years ago, we changed that. I am sure that all of us have had teams where we had seniors who have been on the team for four years and have never missed a practice and, more than likely, have never been late for practice. When the Rudys huddle up, they are usually

 





Page 151

first in the huddle, but the only time they get to play is when we are way ahead or way behind. But he loves the game, and he loves being part of the team. He is a true Rudy. Now we give a coaches' award to this type of kid. We do not give the award every year. The kid has to be a true Rudy. Attitude of senior leadership is critical to their success.

The movie 
Rudy 
illustrates to the senior leaders the value of Rudys much better than I could. This is what we stress to our seniors. For the program to be a success, they must understand the role of Rudys. I refer to the move as a training film. (1) Attitude of senior leadership is critical to their success. (2) They need to understand the purpose and values of Rudys. (3) They should receive recognition by the first team: "Good job, Rudys." (4) Senior leadership takes part of the Rudys for the second team.

I am always positive. If we gain 1 or 2 yards, we are giving them high fives just as if we scored a touchdown. Now, I am telling them "good job" and how much fun we are having.

How do we accomplish all of this? First, we have computerized our scouting information on each team we play. If you are like most schools, you have a four-drawer file, with one or two of those drawers crammed full of scouting information. What we did was to go into those drawers and copy three years of data on a disk on each team. We copy four categories. It is a very unsophisticated system; it is strictly a storage system. We copied only four areas: our defensive game plan, your offensive tendencies, your defensive tendencies, and 10 to 12 of your offensive plays. When you put that on a computer and print it out, you come out with about 20 pages. Last year, when we played a team, we went through these four categories and put them on the computer. That becomes our current scouting report.

We do all of our scouting from videotape. We are so far from our opponents. When we go through the tapes to scout you, we prepare another game plan. We have a defensive game plan, and we identify your offensive tendencies, defensive tendencies, and your best 10 to 12 offensive plays. We compare them to what we just printed out. We put the latest report on the computer and develop a current game plan. Then everything slides down another year.

The goal is to be able to find this information fast. Before, when we went to the filing cabinet, we could never find what we were looking for. Now we know where everything is located. We print out the four categories, and we are ready to go practice.

I have found that most head coaches do not like to throw away anything. Our plan is to keep three years of data. It does not matter how many years of data we keep, we probably will not refer to it unless someone says something like this: "Didn't they run something special three years ago that we need to be aware of?" Then we can go back to the computer and look at the past scouting reports. We may not print it out, because we most likely will just look at the film of this year's team, print out the scouting report from the past year, and then create a package that we can take out on the field.

The package we take out on the field looks like this. I have each play that I want the scout team to run that day on a printout. All assignments are drawn out. That is what I take into the huddle. It takes me about five minutes to print these plays out. It does not matter whether the plays get wet, torn, dirty, or whatever, because I can duplicate them instantly on the computer.

Here is a sample of one of the scout-team plays. These play sheets are numbered and match an index card created by the defensive coordinator. I look at the films and determine some of your blocking schemes. I make sure to draw out each assignment. You must remember

 





Page 152

that we are dealing with young and, most of the time, inexperienced players. I create the package with blocking schemes on it. I take the plays in the huddle. We try to put as much info on the sheets as we possibly can. Again, we are dealing with young players.

Our defensive coordinator creates his index cards to match these plays included in the package. We have them numbered so they correspond. We have the play number, description of the play, the defense we will use, and special notes on who to watch. He has the list prepared and gives it to me. I start down through the list, running the plays. Occasionally, our defense will not do well on, say, play number 6. He will come back to me and say, "In two plays, run play 6 again." We want to make sure that the defense knows what they are doing.

We play a 50 defense. It is very easy to put in the blocking schemes for the scout team. Our goal is to put as much information on these as possible. I have discovered in dealing with the Rudys that I get a better job if I put the blocking schemes on my play sheets. We want to be as effective as possible. We want to accomplish four things. (1) We do not want to waste time in the huddle. (2) We do not want the first team waiting on the Rudys. (3) We do not break the huddle until the first team is ready. (4) If we wait at the line of scrimmage for longer than 10 seconds, we huddle again. We want to communicate as quickly as possible.

We do some things on the scout-team plays to help communicate with the players. If we pull a man, we draw the path for the pulling man in red. Now there is no excuse for the guard not to know he is pulling. For the ball carrier, we indicate with a heavy black circle and a heavy black line the path he is to run. We try to give our quarterback some key information on the fake or action he will employ. For us, a blue line indicates a pass or pitch. Now, remember, this is not ours; it is our opponent's offense.

We line up the backs to indicate their alignment. I do not have to say anything in the huddle. I just hold up the sheet and then ask if there are any questions. If no questions, we can run the play. We make sure to give the direction that the quarterback comes out. We give the location of the slots and flankers. We make note of any fakes. Also, we indicate where the quarterback would call the snap count on motion plays. If we draw a receiver with a straight line and nothing at the end of the line, he is to run the corner or defender off. If the line has a line forming a T, he blocks. If a pass play is called and the man is covered, we still want the quarterback to throw the ball. Remember, the practice is for the defense, not for the offense. We have to explain that to the quarterbacks.

If we want to run an audible, we can. If we find that the defense is loading up on us, we can call the audible and run the dump pass to the tight end. We would call our live color, call "red 33" on the line, and run the pass play.

We have found that most teams run 60 percent of the same plays each year. We do not have to draw these plays up each year. We just use the same plays the next year.

We try to duplicate everything you do. We try to duplicate the huddle you use. If you are in two lines facing the line of scrimmage or if you huddle in a circle, we do that. The more I ask of the Rudys, the better they perform. They do not execute at 100 percent all of the time, but they try hard.

If you walk or run to the line of scrimmage, we try to duplicate that. We duplicate your line splits. If the linemen come up to the line of scrimmage and get down, we do the same. If they wait for the quarterback to call "down," we will give the same look. We can't always do it, but we try to duplicate the quarterback's cadence. If you like to run an audible in certain situations, we try to do it too. The big thing that this does is to allow us to be able to call a snap count on something other than one. Again, we are trying to make it as realistic as possible. You must remember that the attention span of the Rudys is not too long.

When the scout team goes to defense, our offensive guards make a call on every play. There are four calls for the guards. They are covered, uncovered, inside gap,

 





Page 153

and outside gap. If we are in the 50 defense, both guards would make an uncovered call. If the tackle kicks down and covers the backside guard, he makes a covered call. If the nose man is in the 1 gap, the guard to that side makes an inside-gap call. If the tackle on the back side is in the 3 technique, the guard to that side makes an outside-gap call. If the defense has 11 men on the line of scrimmage, hopefully the quarterback can see it, and we will do something different.

When the scout team is on defense, we try to give them as many combinations as we can on this. Both of our offensive guards make the calls on every play; it does not matter what the play is. The left guard may make a covered call, and the right guard may make an uncovered call. This identifies our blocking schemes on the line of scrimmage. Also, it indicates to the back the blocking scheme and where he should run. This helps us communicate with the players when we talk to them during the game. We want to duplicate as many looks as we can for our guards in practice. This includes the blitzes and stunts you might run. We huddle and call the defense. We try to duplicate everything as much as possible. We give the guards as many combinations as we possibly can. I am convinced that there are three things that a team needs to be successful. (1) You need good players. The more good players, the better. (2) All players need to be schooled in sound techniques and fundamentals that complement your offensive and defensive schemes. (3) You need team chemistry.

I believe that team chemistry exists when the players care about one another. In 1995, when we were state runners-up, we felt we had a better team talent-wise than we did in 1996, when we won the state championship. Our 1996 team drew a line in the sand and said, ''This is it. You are not going to pass our line." They did not let you past that line. I have spent a lot of time trying to figure this out. I have talked to Coach McCall about this a great deal. I think team chemistry is when the team truly cares about one another. Now, if that is a correct definition of team chemistry, what does a coach do to cause that to happen? I am not sure about that point, but I have thought about it a long time. I think that we as coaches must care about our kids if we want our kids to care about each other.

My time is up. I thank you for your attention. I hope your scout team has lots of Rudys.

 





Page 154

Robb Rogers

Speed and Power Development

Baylor University

We have a new head football coach at Baylor. Dave Roberts is the new coach. Any time you have a new coach, you have a lot of things to go over. We are trying to get used to each other. We get there early and stay late. It has been a long winter season for us, and we start testing next week. Before I was at Baylor University, I was at Southern California for four years. Before that, I was an assistant at the University of Missouri for seven years. All of you Michigan fans will get to see us about the third game of the year. I know we will just be a scrimmage game for Michigan. I want to see if I can find Lloyd Carr and shake his hand now, because I know I will not be able to get to him after our game next fall. The players will carry him off the field, and I do not want to get into that.

The thing that I do when I go to clinics is this. I want to learn some things that will provoke me into thinking a little. Also, I want to learn some things that I can take back and put into our off-season program. Hopefully, I will be able to provide you with both of those things tonight.

The things I am going to talk about are things that I have learned from others. Just like you, as a coach I am going to visit with others, watch film, and break down workouts. I am going to learn from coaches around the nation who I think are smarter than I am in one way or the other. The material that I will present is not necessarily anything I have invented. It is material that I have learned from others. At times, I will refer to those coaches.

The first quote is from a book, 
Periodization of Strength 
by
Tudor Bompa. Tudor is one of the guys with an accent. If he is farther away from home than 100 miles and he has slides, that makes him an expert. Perhaps tonight I am an expert. I may sound like I have an accent to some of you. This is what Tudor said: "Flexibility can be improved day to day; strength, week to week; speed, month to month; work capacity, year

to year." Is that true? From what I am finding out by applying some principles, it is true. If you do the right thing flexibility-wise, you can be more flexible today than you were before. That is true the next day and the next day, next day and the next day. Can strength be improved week to week? Yes! It can if you do the right things from week to week. We all have experienced that.

Speed is month to month. That sounds a little tougher. Speed is tougher to improve. We used to start out by getting into an aerobic base. We would lift weights. After so many weeks, we would get into conditioning. Then we would worry about speed training. If speed is month to month, we had better start training on speed right from the get-go.

Michael Johnson is a graduate of Baylor University. He still comes down and trains. He is going to race Donavan Bailey this summer in June. It will be a 150-meter match race for a million dollars. After the Olympics, Michael took some time off. Then he did a lot of commercials, and then he started back in training. He did not start off by getting a base. He started on speed work first. The race is going to be a race to see who can run the 150 meters the fastest the most number of times.

So, speed is month to month, and it needs to be worked on from the very beginning in some way. We will get into that later.

Work capacity is year to year. Work capacity is fitness with the ability to handle the load that you are doing. It is the ability to handle practice. We see that each year with our players. The freshmen and sophomores are going through practice, and it is killing them. They may be going through the off-season drills, and they are crawling around as if they are dying. The seniors are cool. They want to know what is wrong with the

 





Page 155

young players. The seniors have four years of work capacity from the training program, and they know what to expect. The freshmen have never done the program before.

What we have found in our training is this. The foundation of all of our training is our work capacity and our fitness. This is also what the eastern groups believe. The more fit you are, the more work you can do. The more work you can do, the more quality work you can do. The more quality work you can do, the better you can be. This is true, even if it is only 2 percent per day. If it is 2 percent every day, then you are going to be better than the other guy.

Fitness is the basis of our program. It is the cornerstone of our program. That is what we work on first. You must have great fitness. It does not matter if it is weight fitness, conditioning fitness, running fitness, jumping fitness, speed fitness, or anything else. We are going to approach it from a standpoint of fitness first. After fitness comes strength, power, and then speed. Those are the three parts of our program from a weight-lifting type of program.

In our program, there are three more things involved. They are part of our cornerstone. We are going to train hard. We are going to take care of that in the weight room and with agilities. The coaches will take care of that on the football field.

We must eat right. Most of us are getting older. If you are like me, you have a problem buying clothes. Instead of buying clothes a little longer, we have to buy them wider. There are not enough hours in a day, if you do not eat right, to work it off or to work through it. You must eat right.

We must have rest to recover. That is a problem now. When we used to have athletic dorms, we could control what they ate and when they rested, and you could control the breakfast check as to when they got up. You can't do that anymore. Today, kids think that they can train really hard on Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, and Thursday and then go out and go hard on Friday and Saturday nights. That does not work well on your body. The key is train right, eat right, and rest to recover. That is one thing that Michael Johnson does in his program. It makes a difference over the other athletes. When he was in high school, the best he ever finished was third in the state. He got into college 10 years ago, started training, and liked the program. He got into track and really liked what he was doing. Now he will not miss a workout. I bet he has not missed 50 workouts in the last 10 years.

Now, all of this is a nice theory. How do you apply it? Here is how we apply it. When we are training our people, we can do this. When we are lifting light weights, we can lift them very, very fast. It is like a snatch, clean, or jump. It is very fast. It is body weight. Next we go to power. Now there is more resistance, and we have to use more strength and movement. It is like a power clean or a jerk, or it could be a jump and holding a weight of some kind.

Next, we get into strength movements. They are very, very heavy and very slow. You have to train all up and down the continuum. When you first start out training when you are in high school and you start lifting heavy weights, you get better at everything. This includes speed, strength, and power. That is because you are not very strong. But after you have been lifting heavy weights for a few years, you do not learn as fast. You do not jump as fast because you spend too much time training at one end of the continuum.

SpeedPowerStrength
Load
Light
Medium
Heavy
Speed of movement
Fast
Quick
Slow
Amplitude of movement 
Long
Medium
Short

You can see the speed of movement at the one end is very fast. Also, it is very long. It comes with long movement.

The next movement is more medium, and it is quick. It is like a clean. It is heavier, and the movement is not as long. It is a quicker movement.

 





Page 156

Strength movements can be short and slow. If you are maxing on a squat or squatting on a bench, it is very slow movement.

Next we get into some keys and some applications. In the off-season, what day are you the freshest? Monday! They had the whole weekend off. That is the day we are going to do our speed training. We are going to do some type of movement warm-up. We do a lot of different things in our warm-up. After that, we are going to do light, explosive things. We do hang snatches. This is how we teach hangs and snatches. We teach it backwards or from the top down. The first thing we teach is space. Then we teach from the hang, and then we teach it backwards.

Order in a Week
Monday
Tuesday
Thursday
Friday
Speed
Power
Strength
Fitness
Order in a Day
Finish fast
Similar mmt
Sprints
Intervals

What is our base? The easiest way to teach our kids is to jump three times and land in a breakdown position. That is the position they are going to hang-clean. All they are going to do is to jump with weight. Now they are ready to go. That is how they set their feet. That is the first clue: feet.

Our next clue is the knees. What do I mean by knees? The bend of the knees. How far? If there is a wall in front of me and my toes are touching the wall, then my knees touch the wall.

The next clue is the chest. I want to tighten my lats up and get a big chest. Now I have feet, knees, and chest.

The last clue is my wrist. I am going to turn my wrist over. There is a straight line down my wrist. Now I have feet, knees, chest, and wrist. We get our guys standing up tall, holding broomsticks. We are going to do two things, but one at a time. My guys can only do one thing at a time. When I ask them to remember two things at a time, that is when we start having problems. We remember one thing at a time. We are going to go feet, knees, chest, and wrist. Now we want them to bend over. Now jump. They will ask, "Do you mean off the ground?" Yes! That is a jump. It is feet, knees, chest, wrist, bend over, and jump. Everyone can do that because it is a very simple movement. That is great posture. Posture is a key in sports. You can't shoot a free throw without posture, and you can't squat without posture. You can't pitch without posture. You can't swing a bat without posture. You can't power-clean without posture. Feet, knees, chest, wrist, bend over, jump. Very good.

Now, let's do an upright row. They will keep the elbows down. I tell them to get the elbows up. It is elbows up, back under, up, back under. Now we have them put it together. Feet, knees, chest, wrist, bend over, jump. Good, you can clean. Let's go to a bar. It takes about that long. If you have 10 players, it takes a little longer. That is how we teach the clean. They can learn to clean in about one day.

We always go from the hang for about one month. After about two or three weeks of hang-clean, we will do hang-snatch. It is the same thing. The only difference is that we are going to have a wide grip. How wide? We have grooves on the bar, so we say get outside the bar. We adjust them as we go. We tell them to get outside the bar, and we coach them on the run. Again, feet, knees, chest, and wrist. Now, in the bend-over in the clean, I bend to here, and in the snatch I bend to here. [Demo] We just cover the bar more with the shoulders. It is feet, knees, wrist, chest, bend over, and now jump.

Now, it is feet, knees, chest, wrist, and upright rowing. This is hard for them. Football players tend to be weak back here and strong up here. They start to lift that broomstick, and they want to lift with the grip wide. No! A lot of them can't even do it with a wide grip. We want them to come back inside, then drop the elbows, and then push. Upright row, drop the elbows, and push. We always keep pressure on the bar. They think they can reach down and bring it straight up, but they end up on their back. We want them to drop the elbow and

 





Page 157

push. Finally, they will get it. Here we go. Feet, knees, chest, wrist, bend over, jump. Boom! Now you can snatch. It takes about that long to teach them. If we ever get messed up, we go back to our clues.

After three or four weeks of hang-cleaning and hang-snatching, we tell them to go off the floor. We never snatch off the floor. We just clean off the floor. That is how we teach clean and snatch.

Here is an example of a work week. Our warm-up is footwork, core work, pre-hab, and loosening up. Then we go to our workout.

On Monday it is a speed day. We are going to do plyometrics and jumps, but not a lot. We are not going to jump off anything high. We usually set some hurdles up and jump over them. We have a jump floor built in the middle of the weight room. We got this from a flooring company back in Baltimore. We just jump over hurdles that are to one side on the jump floor. We set some cones up and jump with one leg over to the other side. All this is special strength. That is all it is. We are just jumping over some little things.

As they get more confident, we play games. Last week, we had five hurdles set up that they had to jump over. After the last hurdle, we had a box that they had to jump up on or jump over. We had a 350-pound offensive tackle who was not able to jump up on the box. We started teasing him, telling him it was the "box of death" and that he could not do it. He got mad and proved that he could do it. As he got more confidence, he proved that he could do it. It was a fun thing. It was at the end of training. It was jump hurdles 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 and jump on the box. That was at the end of our training.

Back to Monday: plyometrics, wave squats. What are wave squats? We take a load of about 25 percent of what we can squat, and we are going to put it on our shoulders. We are going to do 2 plus 4 plus 2 with it. It may be 1 plus 5 plus 1. It may be 3 plus 6 plus 3. It is something along that pattern. We are going to put the bar on our shoulders, and we are going to squat. The bar is on our shoulders and we are going to squat, 1,2. Next we are going to do four quarter squats with power. The coaching point is this. I want to see a violent change from one direction to the other. It is from down to up. We are not doing squat jumps. I have 30 players in the weight room. I can't watch 30 guys doing squat jumps. If I were coaching one long-jumper, yes, but not 30 football players in the weight room. We are going to do our quarter squats: bam, bam, bam bam. We want an explosive change of direction, down to up. What is the key when you are doing the vertical jump? It is being able to get from the down position to the up position and get up in the air. What is the key when you are changing direction on the field? How quickly you can get from one point to the other. You want to create separation on offense and close separation on defense.

We are doing 2 plus 4 plus 2. It is with a light load. We use 20 to 30 percent. We use the hang in the 20 to 25 percent region. When they first start, they will have trouble with their balance. You have to teach them to sit up, just as you do in a jerk.

On Monday we go out and do our speed skill drills. We will work up to where we are doing some fast sprints. They are 20- to 50-yard sprints. Also, we do bench presses; that lasts about one-half hour. All of the coaches want everyone to bench-press 400 pounds. If a kicker can't bench 400 pounds, he can't kick a field goal! Everyone has to bench-press 400 pounds before they leave. This is not going to do them any good unless they get knocked down and they have to get up really quickly. You do not have to bench 400 pounds to be a good football player, because that is not how the game is played. I have worked with a number of players who have gone on to the NFL who could not bench-press 400 pounds. Most of them were just average on the bench press. Now, they all could clean well over 300 pounds, squat 500 to 600 pounds, and move and change directions. The key to the game is butt, back, hip, and legs. That is our Monday workout.

Tuesday is a power day. We warm up, and then we do some cleans. We are going to do either hang or power cleans. We are going to do some jerks. What are jerks? It is the same movement as the quarter squat. Every

 





Page 158

movement that we do in the weight room with weights is with jumping. A squat is a very slow jumping movement. It is all jumping and running. When you played hopscotch when you were six years old or when you jump rope, it is still jumping. We are just going to get more fancy with it. We are going to clean and jerk, and we are going to do single-leg squats. We do not do front squats. Most of our guys are built for speed, and they are long from the hips to their neck. If we try to put the bar up high, it gives them trouble, because on the clean it puts the bar under their throat. They can't lift with the bar on their throat. They have to hold the bar off their throat. For that reason, we do not do them. You have to spend so much time, we just do not do them. That is the reason we went to the single-leg squat.

We are going to elevate our back leg on something. We have benches beside our squat racks. The key is this: The front leg cannot be back more than the knee or even with the knee. If you do not do that, you will start opening your hips up, and you put a lot of pressure on the SI joints. You will have players with SI joint problems. Just as when you do a lunge, you want the front foot turned in just a little for balance and stability.

Then you go to this position with the knee even with the back knee, or the other knee. The rules are the same as in squatting. It is big breath, sit, and come back up. I am not going to get the top of the thigh parallel, because it is almost impossible. That is all right. I will get to hamstring parallel. That brings the ham into play. Our load is going to be fairly light. We are going to move down slowly and up quickly. Our guys who were squatting 500 pounds had trouble starting with 135 pounds on this drill, but now we have players who will do 180 to 200 on this. We keep the reps low: two to four reps. If you start doing six, seven, or eight reps, they will constantly get sore in their butt. They get sore in the butt, they get tight in their ham, and their back gets tight. We keep the reps low. We keep the weight conservative. We do not want anyone struggling on it. We want good movement. We want to move the weight down slowly and up quickly. That is called compensatory acceleration. You can overload them with more weight, more reps, or more speed. In this lift we use a combination of all three. We always finish fast.

Now we are going to go over and do power step-ups. We are going to go unloaded to a bench. As I stomp on the bench, I drive up in the air. Where is this important? It is the first get-away step in sprinting. It is very important in football. How many steps do the offensive linemen get? One and then contact. He needs to come off the ball harder and faster. It is the first step, the get-away step. It is key, key, key! We want to finish fast.

So, we do single-leg squats, we do power step-ups, we do hang cleans, and we do jerks. After single-leg jerks, I do not feel comfortable taking players out on the field and sprinting them for 40 yards. When you get a lot of single-leg work going or a lot of lateral agilities going, you can irritate their lower back. It is not ''Oh, coach, I hurt my back," but their SI joint gets out of whack. It is just like when you are running straight ahead in the middle of the season and they have had no problems, then one day you hear one of them say, "Coach, I pulled a groin." How can you pull a groin running straight ahead? They have been training for weeks. How can they do it? It is because of the back. The back gets out of whack.

If any of you have ever been to a chiropractor, he will tell you something like this: "Your hips are out of alignment, and you have one leg shorter than the other leg." Well, that really can't happen. If you get your hips out of alignment and have one higher than the other, now you are pulling unevenly on the muscle. You are running straight ahead, and the groin pulls. That is because it has an uneven stress on it. It is a muscle imbalance. We all know that the quad-to-ham ratio causes hamstring pulls and all of that. You can also have an imbalance just because the muscle is being stressed or stretched. It can be stressed from front to back and other ways. For those reasons, I do not feel comfortable sprinting them on Tuesday. We go out and work on stance and starts. We do them for 10 to 15 yards. On Monday we will do 8 to 16 of the 10- to 15yard stance and starts. We run forward and laterally using the crossover step, pulls, and all kinds of stuff.

 





Page 159

Wednesday is kind of a day off from the weight room. We are still going to do strength work, because we are going to go on the field and do some agilities. We have a long warm-up. We do speed skill drills for 15 minutes. Then we are going to loosen up. Then we do a few agilities. They may get 12 to 16 reps in agilities. Compare that to a morning mat drill program: They get 60 reps in 30 minutes; we get 12 to 16 reps in about 15 minutes. Our intensity is higher, but our tempo or our workout is lower. That is because they get plenty of rest. Once they finish that part of the workout, we do some running, but not hard. Then we lie down and stretch for 10 minutes. It is like track practice. We do 20 minutes of work in one hour. We are trying to recover from the day before. The next day will be a squat day.

We come in on Thursday and do our warm-up. Now we go over, get on a platform, and do light hanging shrugs, hanging cleans, front squats, and press. It is just technique work. It is nice and smooth, but it is going to get them loose to hit that low position. Then we hit something fairly fast, but with a very light load. Now we get under a squat bar. We use all safety squat bars. It is a safety bar with a big yoke that comes down, with handles that you can hold on to. The problem with lower backs is that when you get into too much work or you get into too much sheer force with the back forward, the angle is shearing down through the disk. If you sit up straight and squat and put the load straight down, you have pure compression force that the body is built to handle. So we use the safety squats. Again, our guys are long-waisted and long-legged.

Instead of wearing me and them out trying to get them to do it right, because dynamically and physically they can't do it, we just use the safety squat. We get them in there and go, "Racket, handles, grab hold, deep breath, wide knees, sit, and here we go, baby." We just load the weight on them. I do not have to coach technique. I can coach to get stronger. Weights, weights.

We have three players who squat 900 pounds on a safety squat. If it were a straight bar, I would guess it would be about 750 pounds. These guys can really squat. They do not have to work on bending over or technique. They just get in the weight room, and most of them do not even wear a belt. We squat max one time per year in March. We will power-clean three or four times per year. That is because the athlete is in control. If he gets into trouble, he can get away from it. On squats I have to have three men on the same page helping me out of the box. The squat max scares me to death.

After we finish squatting, we go jump up on boxes. If you are a long-jumper, what kills you? It is not the jump; it is the landing. If you are doing jump training, what is going to kill you? It is the landing. So we go over to a box, and we jump up on the box. Now we walk down the boxes. I have to jump high, but I walk back down. I can always jump up.

If we want to work on reactive strength, like cutting and changing directions, we walk up and jump turned to the side of the box. If we are working on starting strength, we will walk up to the box, pause, and then jump. If we want to work on reactive strength on the platform, we do a hang clean or a hang snatch. It is down-up. Stress reflex up. If we want to work on starting strength, we will work power clean from the floor. What are pulls? Pulls are cleans, but we do not rack it. I pull it and let go. Why do I want to pull and then let go? What kills you in a clean? It is not the pull, but the catch. Now I can go a little heavier, pull it, and then let go. No problem. It is the same reason we jump on boxes. We alternate pulls on Tuesday and Thursday. We can alternate those in and out and end up going heavier.

After we finish the squats and finish box jumping, we go out and run. On Thursday we run 130s. It is over distance with a nice, easy stride. How do you do that? It is from one corner to the opposite corner of the football field. We do six 130s. We have three guys across, and we stride to the corners. Then we walk across. When the group gets to the starting point, they go. Then they walk back across the field. We do not run a lot in any one day, but we run every day.

On Monday we are fast. On Tuesday we have power. I have strength, and I have some speed left. Wednesday

 





Page 160

is a recovery day. On Thursday I am strong, and I am lifting weights. What is left for Friday? I can't be fast; I can't be powerful; I can't be strong. All I can do is work.

On Friday we do fitness. We do circuits. We get our freshmen in, or we are in the off-season and they are being red-shirted. We can do some of this three days a week with our non-travel players. We tell our skill players that they must learn to like Thursday. What do skilled players do in high school? They play three sports, usually. They play football, basketball, and baseball or track. When did they ever lift heavy weights? They may have done a little bench press. So we tell our skilled players that they must learn to like Thursday. When we do fitness on Friday, they just fly through it, because they are already in shape. It is easy for them, because they do not weigh very much. The big guys like Thursday. Ha! Dumbbell, bench, squats. Friday, they are sick. They just don't know if they can make it.

Steve Javorick coached our Olympic weight-lifting coach when our Olympic coach was a lifter for Romania. He got a silver medal in Los Angeles. Steve's thing is this: The more fit you are, the more work you can do; the more work you can do, the more quality work you can do. He invented something called dumbbell fitness training. If I had a pair of dumbbells, I would use 10 percent of my weight if I weighed 178 pounds. Now, this will not work for the heavy guys, because they will have more fat on them. They have to go 10 percent of their fat-free weight. We figured that out for all of our players. For most of the young guys, you can give them a set weight, but if I had a partner up here and we were going to do a dumbbell fitness workout on Friday, this is how we would do it.

We put a chart up that shows 8 to 12 exercises. It has quarter 1 and the number of reps. Then it has quarter 2 and the number of reps. Then comes quarter 3 and the reps, quarter 4 and the reps, and the overtime and the reps. If you are my partner, you are up here with me. We are working with 22 1/2 pound dumbbells. I look up at the chart, and it says 10 hang snatches. Now, I have 24 players lined up. We have 12 working and 12 watching. The first man is up. Ready, go! He does 10 hang snatches. As soon as he gets to number 10, he sets the dumbbells down, the other man picks them up, and he does 10. It is just like football. Work, rest, work, rest.

Now, the first man picks up the dumbbells, and he has 10 squat push-presses. He goes to 10 and then sets them down. The second man is up. What does he have? He has 16 step-ups on one leg. He does 16 and then switches legs. He does the 16, and the next man is up. We get 8 to 12 exercises done in the first quarter. It is like a mat drill, only it is with weight and you do not go anywhere. The first time we do it, you can find out who has courage and who doesn't. The ones who do not have courage count like this: 8, 10,14, 16, coach. Then you have to start counting for them. "Two more." "But, coach!" "You have two more." Just start counting their reps. It is easy to cheat. It is toughness training. That is all it is. It is will-power training. You are competing with the man in the mirror. You can make it really hard, or you can make it really easy. Our guys love Friday. The first day that our new coach Dave Roberts, who came from Notre Dame, saw us work out was on a Friday. We were doing fitness work. He though that it was great. If he had come in on Monday with the players doing snatching and doing a few jumps, he would have said that we had too many players standing around. Fortunately he came in on Friday. He thought it was great. We had the players yelling and talking it up. We were doing what we think is great in football. The players know that they must get ready for Friday on Thursday. They know Friday is going to be tough. You can do any kind of movements that you want. It can be side lunges, or lateral step-ups. It goes on forever. We end up with about 300 reps on Friday. You will get some fitness out of this. You can get players bigger in a heartbeat with this. Think of all the reps. What gets you bigger in body building? Reps. That is a bunch of reps on Friday. We may do giant sets that are similar to circuits.

That is what we do for our workouts in a day. We have to finish fast with a similar movement. We always have to finish with running. If you can't run, you can't play. After we finish squatting, we will go out and do six of the 130s. On the first 130, they are running slow because they have just finished squatting. Now, on the

 





Page 161

sixth 130, we have players racing. It takes them about six to get back into it. You have to leave the nervous system with a feeling of speed and a feeling of being explosive. If you don't, the nervous system will be very strong and very slow, or it will be very strong and nonexplosive. It is the total key to everything you do.

What does the nervous system have to do with it? You have to train one body part by taking two days off and training the body part. Yes and no. Yes, if you max out. You would not want to come back the next day and go max squats or heavy cleans. No, that will not work. You would hurt someone.

Let's look at the key to nervous system training. How can women come to college, after five years weigh the same, but lift 100 pounds more in a squat? How can that happen? With guys, don't you have to get the muscle bigger to get stronger? No, it is the nervous system.

We used to get ready for a football season. Around the 4th of July we started getting ready. That is what we used to do. Now, in college, you go to year-round school. In June, you are in summer school. Why? You must have a good graduation rate. You better have players in condition. You better not get players hurt because you have only 85 scholarships. How do you do this? You put them in summer school in June and July. You have eight or nine weeks of lifting and running. You can get them in good shape. Say you go to a camp. They have eight or nine weeks in the summer. Then you have fall, which is 16 weeks long. Then in college, we have bowl preparation, which is another month. Now we are up to 28 to 29 weeks of the 52. Then you get two weeks off. Then they come back, and you are into the off-season program for nine weeks. Now we are at 37 weeks. Then we have spring practice that last four weeks. Now we are at 41 weeks. Then comes May. The older players work out in May for three weeks. Now you are at 44 weeks out of the 52 that players are training. It is year-round training now. In college it is, and it is in a lot of high schools. You have to train year round. Do you think Michael Johnson takes six months n the off-season? He takes about six days off. He starts doing things, not necessarily track work, but he starts working. You have to train year round, but when we come up to play Michigan in the fall, it still comes down to who has the best players. I think we are still working on that part of it.

Now we will get into some practical things. Let's review the work for the week. On Monday we do footwork, we do core work, and we do what we call pre-hab, which we get from the trainers and the physical therapist. Now, if we can do certain exercises before they get hurt, perhaps they will not get hurt, or if they do get hurt, the injury will not be as bad and they will recover from the injury more quickly. We finish our warm-up with loosening. I will cover all of this really quickly. Everyone knows what the speed-agility ladder is. We do that at least once a week. We do all kinds of drills. The players even make up drills. We make it fun for them, but we make it fast. We finish with that type of activity. Then they are jazzed up and ready to go.

Another thing we do is called line hops. We take tape and put it on the floor, or we will take a stripe on the floor or on the field, and we do little hops. It is basic plyometrics, but it is also knee and ankle rehab. We take a spot, and we hop to it. We hop forward, and we hop backward. We hop staying on the stripe. We go zigzag forward and then backward, staying off the stripe. We go laterally. We just keep going back and forth. We do hammer: "Don't touch the stripe moving forward." When they get good, we have them move the hands inside,just as a sprinter does. We are always coaching sprint technique.

When they get bored with that, we go a third of the way down the stripe and put a dot, line, or mark. We go another third of the stripe and put a dot, line, or mark. It is the same drills, but now we have them pause at the dots and stick it or touch it. Why is that important? If I am not a knee bender, I cannot play football. If I am not a knee bender, I can't stop. If I am not a knee bender, that is how I blow out my anterior cruciate ligament. We use this especially with our big guys who have big quads. Really quick, you have heard the statement about the old lineman. He can't play because he can't bend at the waist. We stress posture

 





Page 162

and bending your knee. We want to look as if we are in a racehorse position, or jockey position, ready to kick back and go into our pass set.

We do the drill sideways. Then they get bored with that. Now we do a circle. We have them touch the ground as they go around the circle. We stress space awareness. We have a small receiver who made the best catch I have ever seen because he could make a change from one shoulder to the other shoulder and reach for the ball at the last second to make the catch. That is why we do space awareness. It incorporates those kinds of movements. Those are called tape or line hops.

We do jump-rope drills. In the summer, when we get into line hops and the players get bored, we tell them to get a jump rope. Now we have them do the line hops with the rope. Now it is more difficult. Then they get good, and we tell them to turn the rope backwards. We just keep doing the same things, but we add a twist to make it harder.

Core work is any kind of AB work. We do a ton of AB work. If I am swinging a bat, 50 percent of the power that I generate with my legs will not get to the front of the bat if I am weak in my core. If I am a tackle and I try to reach a defender, this is what happens. How is he going to turn the man and press him if he is not strong in his ABs? Why do we get linemen with back problems? Is it because they squatted too much, too early? It is because they did not do core work. When they try to twist and turn the defender, they get thrown back on their backs. That is why they are getting back problems. It is a combination of a lot of things. We do a lot of core work. We do weighted ABs and nonweighted ABs. We twist and turn and throw medicine balls.

One of the things we do in pre-hab is this. We get on a bench press or a soft-surface bench and take our shoe off. Now the joint is unstable. I have to balance myself on one leg and come down and then come back up. We bend down on the one leg and then come back up. It does not matter if they are 150 pounds or 350 pounds. They have to be able to do this. Why? When they first start doing it, they are weak in the knee area. We have been very fortunate with knee injuries over the last four years.
