Part of that may be luck, but we feel the exercises help.

We do not sit down and stretch before we work out. We get ready, and we loosen up, but we do not sit down and work out. If it is a 6 A.M. workout, we line up, and the first thing we do is this. We go "break down, set, go." We go through our commands, whatever they are. They will change this year, as we have a new coach. Then the first thing we do is walking lunges. Our guys have to change directions down in a low position. That is why we do walking lunges. We come back with walking shuffles. "Squat, shuffle, step; squat, shuffle, step." They are going to get down low and change directions.

Next we go into our sprint skill drills. If any of you coach track, you know what I mean. It is A's and B's and all of that stuff. We do a couple of things differently. We will do skips. They are not grade-school skips, they are sprinter skips. I want the toe up, and I do not worry about the knee. If I do not have the toe up, I am not getting the correct mechanics. We go toe up, and we skip in that position. I want the arms at 90 degrees. It is what we call check to check.

After skips, we do butt kicks. We will do skip kicks or foreleg reaches. We do not do the hurdler stretch. It is fine if you are a hurdler or a sprinter, but these guys are football players. As soon as they start reaching, that is how they run faster. This is not true. You push when you run. That is all you do. If you reach, you land on your heel, and you are walking. It is just push. It is push, push, push. We do skips and butt kicks. We do slide kicks. We slide our ankle up by our knee. We do not do high knees. When kids do high knees, they want to lean back. You can't lean back when you sprint. If you do the high knees, you are teaching something wrong. We do skips, butt kicks, and slide kicks.

We work on arm action by having them sit down. They use the arms at 90 degrees. We go shoulder's height or cheek to cheek. They bring the elbow up high. We gradually increase the speed. We start out in the first

 





Page 163

gear, then the second gear, the third gear, and the fourth gear, and then we go to overdrive. Once they learn to do that drill sitting down, we stand up and do the drill. We may stand behind them, have them put their hands out, and have them touch the elbows to the hands. That is all we do on arm drills.

We have skips, butt kicks, slide kicks. Next we do backward skips. It is for rhythm purposes. The way we teach it is just as if you had just shot a free throw in basketball and you are lazy getting back on defense. You just back-skip. They can learn that very fast.

Now we tell them to get their heel up to their butt, get the hand behind them, and reach back. That is all of our anterior-posterior back stuff.

Then we do our side stuff. This includes shuffle, carioca, and the high-knee carioca by getting it up and over. You can't be low when you do this. You must be tall. We are working on getting the hip and back loose. That is what we do in our warm-up. We do these on Mondays and Wednesdays after we go outside. We work on sprint skill drills. It kind of fades out on Mondays, because of time and because they get bored with it, but we do it every Wednesday. They get bored again on Wednesday, but we still do it, though we may do it one leg at a time. We tell them to skip with the left leg only. Then we go on the right leg only. Then we do the butt kick right leg only, slide kick left leg only, skip backward left leg only. When you get your linemen to where they can do that, they can move.

Another thing we do is to get on a hill. We will kick-step up the hill. We go up the hill diagonally or straight back and power-step down the hill. We are doing the same things you have to do on a football field with resistance, but we are doing it on a hill.

That is an idea of some of the sprint and skill drills we do in warm-up. Agility drills are agility drills. I was talking with the Nebraska people. Do you know how far their offensive linemen run in agilities, conditioning, and sprint work? Everything is at 40 yards. From the time a freshman gets to Nebraska until the time he leaves as a senior, the longest run he will have is 40 yards. I am sure that in football practice they do some other things that are longer than the 40 yards on the field, but in the off-season and the rest of the time with the strength coaches, it is the 40 yards. How can they be so good? They spend two years of lifting, running, and eating before they can ever play. This is true with most of their players. It must be nice to have that situation. Most of us are not that fortunate. Some of our players are a year out of high school, and they have to play. Next year when we come to Michigan, we will have to put three pairs of pants on some of them so you will not be able to see the stains on TV.

On Friday when we go to fitness, we try to unload the body. We do a lot of reps, but with a light load. We do a lot of squat-jump work. Also, we do a lot of circuit work. We will do 110s, and we do the steps.

As our running goes through the week, we will do 50s on Monday. We run across the field, then walk 20 yards. I do it that way because you do not have to worry about discipline because they are always moving.

On Tuesday we do stance and starts. We run straight ahead and laterally. The key on stance and starts is this. They had the Burns Seminar in Dallas a few weeks ago. Vern Burns is the person who taught me the theory on positive shin angle, and we have adopted it. If I am going to run a 40-yard sprint, this is how Burns would teach it. You can take any kid who runs a 40, and if he starts in a football stance, I tell him this: In one hour, I can knock one tenth off his time. Usually it takes about 15 minutes. If I am a personal trainer, it is easy to do. ''Ready, go. You ran a 5.2. Let's go again. Ready, go. You ran a 5.1. Pay me." Electronically, and here is how. If I get into a football stance and I take my first step, the clock is running. If one tenth has gone by, I still have 40 yards to go. I have taken the first step as I am supposed to do, but in the football stance, I still have 40 yards to go. We teach the bunch start. If you are a small player, it is easy. If you are tall player, you have to drop off the line about six inches. We do stance and starts every Tuesday. We do 12 to 16 of them. We just wear them out with it. How do you get better? By reps.

 





Page 164

I will show you the drills, and then I will show you the techniques that Burns taught me. We crowd the starting line and are a fist short of the line. If we have a tall player with long legs, it is going to be a fist and a thumb off the line. It does not matter which foot you put up. Your feet are going to be a fist's width apart, just as if you are in starting blocks. You want to scrunch up your back toe. It has to be turned under tightly. If it is pointed down but not turned under, we do not get the effect we need. Turn the back toe under really tight. If you are short, you need to get the back knee even with the front ankle. If you are long, it will be even with the back of your heel.

Next I want to lay my chest on my thigh. I look like an Olympic sprinter almost. I want to get my weight forward and my butt up. Now I want to get some posture. Here is the key to the whole thing. This is the coaching point. What is the front knee doing? That knee has to be ahead of the toe. When I start, I drive the lead hand shoulder high. I am going to be just like an offensive lineman and get the lead foot on the ground.

How do we teach the drills to do this? It is simple: mountain-climber starts. They get in a push-up position. I want to bring my right foot up even with my left knee, not any higher. As soon as it comes higher, they stumble. We have them come out of the stance in three steps. We say, "1, 2, 3, go." They go three at a time. They are not going full speed.

Next we go to scramble-up and go. They get down in a push-up position. They scramble up and go. We go on sound and movement. This is when I start having fun. This is how we do iton sound: "Ready, go. Next group. Go." Now we tell them that we are going on movement. "Ready, go." About half of them start out before they realize they are going on movement. We teach them to go on movement as well as sound.

The key to strength is the nervous system. This is something I learned from a coach at Washington. He is the assistant strength coach. He is a national or world-class power lifter. It goes back to training the nervous system. We have all done this. You get on a bench and you do 10, 8,6, 4, 2, 4, 6, 8,10. The 10, 8, 6, 4 are on the front side; when you use the same exact weight on the back side, it becomes easier. Why? Because once you get to the heavy set of 2 and you start coming down, your nervous system synthesizes to lifting heavier weight, so the sets of 4 and 6 feel lighter. So we do it on our front side in our workouts on our power lifts, that is, the squat and bench. We use as few sets and reps as we can to get to as heavy a weight as we are going to use that week. Our heaviest set is usually just one set. It does not matter if it is a set of eight at 65 percent or a set of three at 85 percent. It may be a set of two at 91 percent. We use as few sets as possible and conserve as much energy as possible so that we can get to that point. From there, we are going to go to work. Now you can see why Monday takes 30 minutes to bench-press. We will do 10 or 11 sets of bench press.

On Thursday we do 9 or 10 sets of squats, but it follows this pattern. Let's say we are doing a set of three at 85 percent. Next, we come back down and do four sets or five reps at 80 percent. The first two sets feel like nothing. Those third, fourth, and fifth sets are tough.

Now I am going to stay at 80 percent on the bench. Now I am going to double those reps. I do a set of 10 at 80 percent. You can't do that. It is impossible. They will burn out between the seventh and eighth set. That is how I keep track of them as far as getting stronger each week. How many reps did you get on the bench? "Eight, six, eight, ten." He is getting stronger through the month. If it is during the season, I have to adjust his max higher, even though we do not max during the season. But I know he is getting stronger because he did 10 reps. He can't do 10 at 80 percent. That is a max set. It is double what we are working at.

Key To Strength: The Nervous System

Here are the methods to develop strength: (1) max loads, one to three reps very heavy; (2) medium loads, four to six reps as fast as possible, best for high school athletes; (3) burnout set, 7 to 20 reps as quickly as possible with great technique. There is a book by Josef

 





Page 165

Drabik published by Stadion Publishing Co. in Island Pond, Vermont. You can call and get the number. The book is 
Children in Sport Training. 
It tells you the age group that kids can do certain lifts. It is from the study done in the Soviet Union when they were picking their future Olympic athletes. The book is very easy to read. This book tells you at what age kids can start lifting heavy weights. Usually it is at the age of 17 to 18. That is why the medium load is 4 to 6 reps as fast as possible. Again, this is good for the high school players. That is why we do not squat max much.

We have a get-big program. One of the things we do is breathing squats. I trained with Wilbur Miller, who is 65 years old. He is still lifting, and he taught me these. Breathing squats, a set of 20, and you can't do 21. If you want a kid to get bigger, that will do it. It is because you are using so many big muscles for so many reps. Another way to do it is 10 sets of 10. That takes too long, because that is 100 reps. One set of 20 is enough.

When I was at Southern Cal, we opened with Memphis State, and they beat us at our place. After the game, our staff wanted to know what type of conditioning other teams were doing, because our guys got tired against Memphis. So I called about 20 of my friends in the business to see what they were doing conditioning-wise. We found out something that may surprise you. The first game of the season is not won by the best-conditioned team. 
It is won by the mostrested team. 
If you have a group of old kids, they can handle more work, because they have a bigger work capacity. It you have a group of young kids, they can't handle as much work. Just because you run them more does not mean that they are going to be in better shape. It just means you ran them more. This is where the question comes in, Are they ready or not? What we found was the fact that we needed to cut back the running drills 10 days before our first game. We will practice, but we do not do a lot of conditioning after that. You may agree or disagree. That is what we found. On Monday after that first game, we come back and start running again. We do our running on Monday and Tuesday.

This next part is our exercise rep schemes. It is straight from Soviet research. Generally, this is how it works. You make a bell curve: 5 reps, plus or minus 1. A third to one half of all of your reps are going to be 5s. The squats are 4, plus or minus 1. Snatch and cleans and the Olympic lifts will be 2, plus or minus 1. It is automatic.

Exercise Rep Schemes

Bench press: 5 ± 1; use all three methods.

Squats: 4 ± 1; use mainly number 2.

Snatches and cleans: 2 ± 1; use mainly number 2.

Anytime we start our running drills, we go tall, fall, and run. Why? It teaches posture. When I hit the position of leaning forward, I have to run or fall. We want to be tall. That first step is the important one. We want to get the foot on the ground quickly. If we are doing speed work, we start in the sprint stance. If we are doing football drills, we use the football stance.

We do what we call quick foot. We want the foot on the ground now. You cannot accelerate unless you are pushing.

We go out and do speed drills on Monday. We go tall, fall, and run 65 percent across the field. We walk 20 yards; go tall, fall and run 75 percent across the field; walk 20 yards; tall, fall, and run 85 percent across the field; and walk 20 yards. Then we either do one more or start on our five drills. If they are still tight, we will do another one.

The first of the five drills is hands; tall, fall, and run; hands. I want to see my hands. Are they doing the right thing? The next drill is hammer. I can't see it; I have to feel it. It is just like driving a nail. We are driving a nail, driving a nail.

The next drill is toe-up. This is a tough drill. We are standing there, and we tell them to feel their big toe.

 





Page 166

Move it around. Now we go tall, fall, and run toe up. Watch their feet as they go by. They go from hitting the ground and taking a long time to push off to a quick push-off. Why? Because the muscle in stretch contracts faster with more force. Why do linebackers lean forward when the ball is snapped? Why does the tailback move his feet slightly when he starts his footwork on a play? If the muscle is in stretch, he can move faster. Why do baseball pitchers go through the windup? To stretch all of their muscles before they are balanced to deliver the ball to the plate. We have to put the calf in stretch. When they contact the ground, they push off.

The next drill is heel-up. This is good for our linemen. They tend to have a low-heel recovery. They run like a jogger instead of a sprinter.

The last drill is the tall tummy. Look at the sprinters coming down the track to the finish line. You could put a glass of milk on their heads, and they would not spill a drop. They are tall; they are rotating their arms and hips and pushing off with their feet. The clue is tall tummy. If the pro scouts measure them, they can get more money.

Men, I have a videotape to show you. Really quick, two more points. You have to take care of the people who took care of you. First, I want to thank Don Lessner and his staff for taking care of me. This has been a neat clinic. The people of Michigan have been very friendly. It has been fun. I know it will not be this way when we come back in the fall. I want to thank you for paying attention. I hope I have given you something that will help you.

AdvoCare is the company that sponsored me for this clinic. They have some really great products. If you are interested in their product, it is on the handout. It is a quality product, and I recommend it to you. Thank you for your attention.

 





Page 167

Leonard Rush

Developing the High School Offense

Lima Senior High School, Ohio

Thank you very much, Ken. It certainly is a pleasure for me to be here. We ordered our state championship rings a few weeks ago. I told the rep making the rings that I was going to be speaking at the Louisville clinic and that I wanted a ring that everyone could see. I told him I wanted the best thing he had. I hope everyone can see this ring.

We have had a great experience. We were invited to the State House, and we went on the floor of the senate with all of our kids. That was a neat thing for all of us. We did win our league this year; it was the first time ever. We did win the state championship, which was the first time ever.

When Earl Browning called me to speak at this clinic, I told my wife that I had finally arrived. This really means something special to me.

I played at the University of Kentucky under Charlie Bradshaw. Some of the things that I am going to talk to you about today we did years ago. I learned a lot of football, and I learned how 
not 
to treat players and people. I learned how to treat people, and I learned about hard-nosed football. That is what we play in our league. That is why we were able to win the state championship.

I am going to talk with you about some of our coaches. We use some volunteer coaches in our program. I will talk to you later about the volunteer coach and how important that is to our system. I am sure a lot of you have the same situation. We have an open date next year. It is our 10th date, which is October 31. We are interested in a game for that date. I am also the athletic director at our school, so if you are interested, see me. We are in a bind for this date. There is only one school in Ohio that has that date open, and they will not play us. We would be willing to play anyone. If interested, let me know.

If you are not familiar with Ohio high school football, this next section will not mean a lot to you. Some of you from this area will recognize some of the schools we played last year, especially the Cincinnati schools. To put things into perspective, this is the schedule we played this year:

The point I want to make is this. You can see by our scores that we had a lot of close games. You have to win the close games to have a great year. Again, I am the athletic director, and I am the one who set it up. I

 





Page 168

think we play the most demanding schedule in the state of Ohio in Division I, which is the highest. In Ohio you must qualify to get into the play-offs. You have to make the final 16 teams in the divisions to get into the state play-offs. When you get into the top four in your region, you become one of the top 16 teams in the state, and you get into the championship play-offs. I have been at Lima Senior High School for 13 years now. Every one of the teams on our schedule has been in the Division I state play-off over the 13 years I have been at Lima. Three of those teamsSt. Francis, Princeton, and Fairfieldhave won the state championship since I have been at Lima. And Cleveland St. Ignatius has won several state championships. Our schedule has really dominated the play-offs over the last 13 years.

I do want to show you some charts that I think will help you, especially the young coaches who are here. I like them, and they are things that I have stolen over the years. I would be glad to send them to you if you would write me. I have spoken to several clinics recently, and I have mailed several packets to coaches asking about our charts. Write to Coach Leonard Rush, Lima Senior High School, 600 South Pierce Street, Lima, Ohio 45804; telephone 1-419-998-2051.

Let me tell you a little about our school. We are located in northwest Ohio. We are between Toledo and Dayton. We are in the Greater Miami conference. Our league is based in Cincinnati. We play a lot of the Cincinnati schools. We do a lot of traveling. A close game to us is a two-hour bus trip. We added Massillon to our schedule this year, and that was a three-hour bus trip. We had 566 males in our top three grades last year. The Division II break is at 525 males. We are a small Division I school. We are the smallest Division I school to win the state since they started using the setup. In 41 years of football, Lima Senior has had only five losing seasons. Our expectations are to win.

I started talking about our coaching staff. This is how we break our staff down: We have five coaches working on offense and six coaches on defense. Three of these are volunteer coaches. This is how we treat the volunteer coaches. If you come to me and ask me if you can be a volunteer coach, this is what I tell you. You must be in attendance for everything that we do. If you do not want to do that, then you do not want to volunteer for us. So you can see that our volunteer coaches are full-time assistants.

We have three freshman coaches. We have grades 9 to 12 in our building. We have three middle schools that have two coaches at each level. That is the way our program is broken down.

I am going to talk about some of the things we did differently this past year. We give them stars to put on their helmets. Our kids like that type of motivation. Our kids are city kids. If they like that, we will give it to them. We decided this year to give team awards instead of individual awards. If we win, everyone gets a star on his helmet.

Offense

Score 21 points

10 or more first downs

200 yards rushing

100 yards passing

No sacks

10-play drive for score

DEFENSE

Win

Hold opponent to 11 points or less

Eight or fewer first downs

No drives over eight plays

Two quarterback sacks

KICKING

Hold opponent inside 30 on kickoff

Score five extra points or

Kick three extra points

Block any kick or punt

Return all kickoffs beyond 35

We play our starters on our kicking teams. We feel that you will lose if you don't do that in our league. Our quarterback returns punts for us. That is the way we do things. I believe in that, and I will always do it that way. We give special awards to our kicking teams.

I will put up some stats to give you an idea of how we did this past year. This may give you some ideas of the stats we are interested in.

KEYS TO VICTORY

Fumbles recovered21

Fumbles lost8

Interceptions16

Interceptions thrown16

Blocked kicks3

Blocked punts3 (We try to block every punt)

OFFENSIVE STATS (14 GAMES)

Rushing yards3258, avg. 232.7

Passing yards1628, avg. 116.3

Total yards4886, avg. 349.0

Scoring points412, avg. 29.4

DEFENSIVE STATS

Rushing yards1626, avg. 116.1

Passing yards1421, avg. 101.5

Total rushing yards3047, avg. 217.6

Scoring points167, avg. 11.9

KICK SCORING

Pat49 of 52

Field goals7 of 15

Of the eight field goals we missed, four of them were from 50 yards or more. We tried unsuccessfully 52-, 55-, 62-, and 67-yard field goals. We had a kid with a very strong leg, so we gave it a try. He did not make the long ones. The 62-yard try was wide.

You do not have to be really smart to figure out that if we scored an average of 30 points and only gave up 11.9 per game, we are going to win some games.

I felt we had the best defense in the state of Ohio this past year. In our keys to victory, you saw that we tried to block every punt. We do not set up walls. We go after the punt. If we block them, good; if not, our quarterback will fair-catch them.

This is what we do. We hit every day that we are in pads. I am talking about getting after it. I will talk about line play later. But our linemen go over to the chutes and on the boards. We put our first- and second-team players over there every day. We did this in our first game and in game 14. We go one versus one. We practice the same in week 1 as the last week. Our kids are aggressive, and we believe in hitting every day. They like that style of practice. We do not let the seniors beat the heck out of JVs in practice.

We run block 75 percent of the time. We threw for 1600 yards this past year, but we work a lot on the run block. We condition our linemen the same as our running backs and wide receivers.

Here are some things we do not do. I am not saying that what we do is the right thing. We do not lift weights much during the football season. We do not get onto the practice field until 4:15. In our area it is dark by 6:30. Our practice facility is away from our school, so we do not mess around with the weights.

We do not watch videos with our kids. Our kids did not watch tapes on the teams we were going to play during the year. We do not use dummies for our blocking drills. We block people.

We do not teach a backup blocking technique. You will not see us hinge-blocking and setting back in our

 





Page 170

stance to block. We do not do that. We do pass the football, and we sprint out. We do combo protection. However, we do not teach a backup blocking technique, per se. We do not use blocking sleds very often. We have all of the great sleds, but we seldom use them. We probably should sell them all, because we do not use them very much.

Now I am going to tell you some of the things we do that we feel helped us for the entire year. We take a composite schedule of all our opponents. In Ohio you must qualify to get into the play-offs. We put our opponents' schedule down. Each week, we keep track of what they did for their games. If they win, we put a W in that column. In the 13 years we have been at Lima, we have been to the play-offs four times in the last eight years. This is something we start looking at very early. It is a goal for our kids to get into the play-offs. If you want to see what we do, I will be glad to mail you a copy.

When I get with the assistant coaches in the summer, we lay out a master practice schedule for them. Our coaches know each day what we are going to be putting into our system. They can look ahead and prepare for the practice. We plan our offense, defense, and kicking game this way.

Every day that we go onto the practice field, we have a practice plan. Again, this is for some of the young coaches. Our coaches know what everyone is doing at all times. We do not waste a lot of time. We do not have a lot of time, so we want to be organized. We have our practice planner on a computer, and we can print it out very fast after we get it set.

Another thing our assistant coaches like a lot is this duty chart. I put it all down in writing. If you are on my staff and you are responsible for videos and you are to trade the tapes with the opponents, you know that you are to do that. Our coaches like this because they know exactly what they are responsible for.

Another chart that we like is our rating of individuals. We send this rating chart to our freshman coaches to rate their players. They may send it down to the middle schools for them to rate their players.

 





Page 171

Now I am going to talk about offensive line play. We had a big team last year. Our starting left tackle was 6' 4'', 260; the left guard was 6' 2", 290; the center was 6' 2", 255; the right guard was 6' 2", 255; the right tackle was 6' 0", 300; and the tight end was 6' 1", 215. Our backup tackles were 6' 0", 285, and 6' 4", 325. The thing I was proud of was the fact that we had kids with good grades. We are not a school where the teachers just give you grades. You have to work hard at our school. We had some bright kids, and that helped a lot.

I am going to talk about our offensive line. We use the four-point stance on offense for our guards and tackles. We get our rear end up in the air, and we bow our necks, so we can see the defender in front of us or at least see his hands. We have our weight forward. We do not teach backup blocking, and we do not trap a lot. We stress four points in our stance. It is balanced and squared, toe-and-heel stagger, hands under the shoulders, hips higher than the head, eyes up. We want our weight forward. Explode with the lead step (no crossover), short first step, let your weight go, roll the hips. Tackles: inside foot back, head in the hole. Our center's stance is the same stance as the other linemen. He is down in a squat. Extend the ball out, free hand on the ground; explode as you snap; lift ball (snatch); charge over ball; roll off the balls of the feet; lead step, short first step.

Now I am going to get to some X's and O's. When I was here two years ago and listened to Terry Bowden lecture, I liked what he did on the sweep play. It became a good play for us. I want to tell you what we do and why we do it. We play against Cincinnati Princeton High School. They are the best team in our league. Pat Mancuso just retired at Princeton. They were very predictable in their defense. They ran a 50 slant defense. We know that if we get them on the hash mark, they are going to slant a certain way. We run our 33 or 32 dive with fold blocking. It is a hard dive at the covered lineman. Here it is with the nose on the center.

33 DIVE VS. 50

 





Page 172

Down on the goal line, we wedge on the tackle in the gap. We come straight off the ball, and the back hits behind the double team. Against the 4-3, it is about the same blocking scheme.

On our isolation play, we were very basic. We expect our running backs to block.

43 ISOLATION VS. 50

We want to run close to the frontside guard's block. Both backs must feel the play side. The tackle must see the inside defensive lineman move. We want our tackles to take at least a 4- to 5-foot split.

Against Cleveland Ignatius and Cincinnati Elder, we faced teams that played the 4-3 defense. They both played their big tackles down tight, almost in a 1 technique. This is how we decided to block them on the isolation play. We brought the center around on the middle linebacker. Now we could isolate to the splitend side.

We scrimmage Cincinnati Moeller High School each year. I understand that they play Louisville Male High School down at Corbin. They play the 4-3 look as well. They play Princeton, and they want to prevent them from running the split-backs veer. They want to take away that inside against the veer. This is the scheme that we used against 4-3 teams.

We do not teach a downfield block. Our backside tackles never go downfield. If they can get downfield before our running backs can, then they should be playing running back. Our running backs do not cut back against the grain. They run toward the goal line. We scoop-block on the backside. We feel we have to do that against teams that slant their tackles really hard. When we want to cut them off, we do a chop block. It is a legal block. We chop them, and let them roll over the top of us. Our tight end goes downfield, but not our linemen.

Next, I want to show you our 25 and 24 veer. It is our inside veer or triple-option play. We read the first man outside of our tackles' block. We like to double-team or wedge-block at the point of attack when possible. We decided to use the crack-back block this year. We had never used it before. We came down inside and blocked the man responsible for the quarterback at times. It was a very successful play for us this year.

If we run the inside veer against the 50 defense, we read the tackle and pitch off the end. I know most of you are saying, "No one is blocking the corner if you are crack-blocking with the split end." You are right. Our running backs weigh 205 pounds; our cornerbacks are 160 pounds. We will take that match.

25 VEER VS. 50 DEFENSE

Against the goal line, we are going to wedge the tackle on the front side and scoop backside. Against the 4-3, it is very similar blocking. We still wedge on the tackle inside.

This next play was a good play for us. We got the play from Cincinnati Moeller. They ran this against us. I liked the blocking scheme against our 4-3 defense.

45 LOG VS. 50 DEFENSE

 





Page 173

It is an inside counterdive, and it may be run from a split-backs or I formation. The log is made by the backside guard. He pulls around the block of the frontside guard. We do run the reverse-out dive option. We fake the dive option and hand back to the tailback. We can run it on the goal line. The offside guard comes off on the linebacker. It is a good play. I think it is a better play against the 4-3 defense. The center blocks back on the tackle, and we put the guard off the center's tails, straight up the field.

The play we like down on the goal line is our G block. We get a double-team block with the tackle and tight end. It is a great short yardage or goal-line play. The lead back and running back read the guard's block on the defensive end. We have a double team at the point of attack. We pull the onside guard and trap the end. If the end comes down, we log-block him. If he goes outside, we trap him out.

46 G VS. 50 DEFENSE

As I said, I liked what Terry Bowden did with the toss sweep. This is how we started blocking it, and it has been a good play for us. Now, we lead the fullback through on the linebacker and pull the guard. It is our power sweep to the tight end. Again, the fullback blocks the linebacker to the side of the play. The guard pulls and blocks the force man. The tailback steps at the line of scrimmage on his first step. He may run the play anywhere inside or outside, depending on what the end does. We are scooping really hard on the back side.

48 POWER SWEEP VS. 50 DEFENSE

As I said, we do throw the football. We run our combo pass protection for our one-step and three-step passing games. We do not release our running backs on this play. We pack it inside. We want to protect the quarterback. Teams are using the blitz more today. We do lock on man, or big on big. The backs block the first man to show outside of our tackle's block. If we get against a team that runs a slant-and-loop tackle, then we will have to keep our tight end in and let him block the second man outside. Our running back can't block both of them. That is our quick-step passing game.

COMBO PROTECTION

We were a sprint-out team this year because we had an outstanding quarterback who could run, and he could throw on the run. We changed our sprint protection this year. We decided that we would have our linemen take a short step inside and block the area. We used to turn inside, but we were getting too much penetration. We decided to step inside and keep our shoulders square.

We never take a man. We can't take care of the blitzes if we take a man. The backs take the first and second men outside of the play-side tackle.

 





Page 174

SPRINT PROTECTION VS. 50

I am going to give you a couple of drills that we like. The first drill is the board drill. I was watching TV one night, and they had a special on Northwestern and the great job they did two years ago. As they were talking to Gary Barnett, they were running this drill. I decided to try it and found it to be a very good drill.

BOARD DRILLS

APosition 1 down = lineman; position 2 = linebacker; position 3 = end of board.

The man with the dummy is downfield about 10 yards. After the blocker comes off the end of the board, he goes downfield to block the deep defender.

BAngled board (3 positions); offense vs. defense; 12foot boards. I told you we do board drills every day.

I got the next drill from Dick Crum when he was coaching at North Carolina. We call this next drill the North Carolina drill. In Ohio you are not allowed to use names that would upset someone or suggest something as bad, such as hamburger drill. So we call it our North Carolina drill.

NORTH CAROLINA DRILL

(1) Snap count starts it. (2) No penalties! (3) Receiver stalks the defensive back. (4) Run to score! (5) Stay inside the dummies. (6) Three downs to score.

We start the drill with a down lineman on offense and a down lineman on defense. We have the quarterback give the ball off to the running backs. We put our receivers in the middle. We have a defensive back who the receiver is going to stalk-block. The cone gets wide as they go down the field. We put the offense on one side and the defense on the other side of the bags. We used to let the men outside of the bags hit the running back if they come outside of those dummies. We feel that this drill helps us teach our backs to get up the field. It is a 10-yard square for the total area. We make it tough for the offensive lineman to start the drill. He has to get down and drive the man out of the hole so the back can get through. It is a safe drill. We do not do it every day, but you are not going to get a lot of kids hurt on this drill.

Every day that we are in pads, we do a middle drill. We line our cones just outside where the tight end would line up. We set the other set of cones at 5 yards. Our goal is to get 5 yards per play. We go first offense versus first defense. Every day that we are in pads, we go for 5 minutes. It is a live drill for everyone. The only thing that I ask the defense is not to kill our quarterback. We run plays only inside of the tight end. After 5 minutes, we let them have a water break. Our kids like this drill.

 





Page 175

MIDDLE DRILL

I felt that we had a very good defense this past year. I judged our practices this year on how well we did against our defense. Our defense was outstanding. We have 12 starters coming back next year. We only had one senior running back. We have eight starters coming back on defense. When we meet next summer, our goals will be to win the Greater Miami Conference, get into the state play-offs, and win the Division I state championship of Ohio. Our goals will not change. We will be very competitive. I have some of the state finals against St. Ignatius that I want to show. If you have any questions, I will be around. Thank you very much.

 





Page 176

Nick Saban

Pass Coverages in the Eight-Man Front

Michigan State University

Thank you, gentlemen. First of all, I'd like to tell you that it is very much a pleasure for us to come over here and visit with you. I would like to thank each and every high school coach in the state of Michigan. I think you do an outstanding job from a professional standpoint of developing players for college. Anything that we can do to help you develop professionally, we would be pleased to do. Don't hesitate to ask. Come visit us during spring practice.

I'm going to talk about a couple of things here. Any of you who have heard me speak before know I'm hung up on developing the right kind of attitude and chemistry on your team. After that I want to talk about the eight-man front and man-to-man coverage. Most pro teams are playing this coverage now. More and more colleges are going to it, and you could play it very effectively in high school.

I assume you have some of the same problems with your players that we have with ours. The levels are different, but the problems are the same. I would like to talk to anybody here who has any suggestions and can help us in terms of managing young people. We need to get them to do the right things from a behavioral, character, disciplinary, and attitude standpoint. We spend quite a bit of time on that.

We did an interesting thing about a week or 10 days ago. We brought a sports psychologist in to work with our team for three days. He talked to our team about what the team felt and thought. Sometimes we as coaches lose sight of that a bit. This guy had a word that he went through. It was called ''HALT." He had the players write down on a piece of paper what the letters stood for. There were no coaches around. The H stands for, "What are you hungry for?" The A was, "What are you angry about?" The L was, "What are you lonely about?" The last one, T, stood for, ''What are you tired of?"

It was interesting what the players wrote down. You can find out a lot about your team. You need to know what they think and where they are mentally. We are all guilty of relying on personal perception. Sometimes it is good to stand back and view things from the other person's point of view. It did a lot of good for the coaches to see what the players were thinking. It opened my eyes up to a lot of things.

My view of the off-season program is what we need to develop in the players. I want to teach them how to give effort and develop mental toughness. We make it hard. We make them finish every play and give maximum effort. I see all those things as tremendous benefits. But if we don't sell that to the players, all they think is, "I'm tired of running every day." If that is their attitude about running, they are not going to do a very good job of it, because they are not goal oriented in their approach at all. They don't see the light at the end of the tunnel. We didn't make a very good road map for them so they could understand why they were doing what they were doing and how it was going to benefit them.

The last two weeks, I feel we have done a much better job with our team because of our understanding. Does that mean we are compromising our values, principles, and integrity with the way we go about coaching? Not at all. But we can do our job better when we understand where they are coming from. Believe me when I tell you that.

I think the reason we exist as coaches is to help people be successful. If they are successful, we will win games. How do you get them to be successful? The key is consistency in performance. That makes anyone successful in whatever they do. Success is based on performance. What makes people have good performance? They have to want to do it. That is the most important thing.

 





Page 177

As coaches, our perception is that these things are good for you. We are establishing discipline, learning how to give effort, and learning how to finish plays. But to the players, it is a pain in the butt, and it is hard. Therefore, they have no goals or direction. How could they like it, do well at it, and be committed to it? They have no reason to be committed to it, because we didn't sell it to them correctly.

Highly motivated people give consistency in performance. Motivation comes from knowing what you want accomplished and being committed to it. Even if you know what you want to accomplish, what does it take to do it? Your attitude is made up of your habitual thoughts, habits, and priorities on a day-today basis. You can have good or bad thoughts, habits, or priorities, but you control those things. We can change those. If they are bad, we can make them good. That is where it all starts.

Sometimes young people need direction. It all starts with your attitude. The sum of all those habits, thoughts, and priorities is your character. That tells what kind of person you really are. That takes into account the decision you make when the coach is not watching. It takes a lot of character and self-discipline to be committed to something that you want to accomplish. It takes a lot to see it through to the finish.

The first thing is to work and invest your time in something that is important to you. That is critical to being successful in anything. Don James used to say, "You reap what you sow." He was my college coach. He talked about two farmers. One guy worked from sunup to sundown. The other guy sat on his porch all day. When harvest came, the guy who worked hard had the biggest harvest you could possibly have. The guy who sat on his porch had nothing. The price you pay for success must be paid in advance. There is no way you can be successful and not pay the price up front and in advance. It never just happens. You have to make a tremendous investment in it. Invest your time in something. Don't spend it.

The second thing is that you have to stick with it. You have to be persistent and learn from your mistakes. It is so difficult to coach guys right now. They are so self-centered and selfish. So are my kids. It is the way young people are today. It doesn't make any difference how old they are. They have too many choices. They never have to make any commitments to anything. If they are not making any baskets today, they go over and hit golf balls. If the range is a little hot, they go swim in the pool at the country club. If the water is too cold, they go play baseball. They swing three times and miss, so they go do something else. That's what my kids do.

When I grew up in West Virginia in a coal-mining town, we had a company store, and that was it. We had a ball diamond. The company owned it all. When you got up to bat, there was nothing else to do. You kept swinging until you hit it. Most of us hit it pretty good because we didn't have a choice. We made a commitment to doing it because there was nothing else to do. It is not that way now.

As coaches, we always tell our players what they did wrong, but we seldom ever tell them what they did right. I think you have to find ways to reinforce positive performance in the way you coach. You need to be more positive with the players. They always respond better that way.

They have to learn that there is a seed for every good thing in every mistake they make. There is a learning experience in those mistakes. Old Buck Nystrom used to beat our guys with a stick. But they responded to it, and he was one of the better coaches I've ever been around. They used to say to him, "Coach me, coach." That is what he tried to convince them of. They wanted to learn, so he got to them. They took it and respected him. That is what you need to have.

They have to be positive about the negative mistakes they have. They have to see that they are learning from that and growing. In doing that, they learn how to overcome adversity as well.

Our team right now, to me, is right at the choke phase. We are right where we can choke when it counts. That is not negative. I think you have to be damn good to choke. You can't be in a 29-29 game in the fourth quarter with Penn State and not be very good. You

 





Page 178

can't get ahead of Iowa 23-10 going into the fourth quarter and not be very good. But when it got thick in the end, we had too many young players. We didn't have enough prime-time warriors who believed they could make the big play at the end of a game. When they started having that little bit of doubt, they took the apple. They made mistakes they had never made before.

We got a turnover against Penn State on the 14 with less than four minutes to go in the game. We followed that with the three worst plays we had run in the football game. We had 550 yards and got three straight plays with negative yards. After that, we missed the field goal. The guy hadn't missed a field goal inside the 30-yard line all year.

Next the defense came on the field. We had 2nd and long. We doubled the slot receiver. He ran straight across the field. We were playing him inside and outside. The guy who was supposed to take him inside just looked at him. He caught the pass and ran 36 yards into field-goal position for them with less than two minutes to go. We had played the coverage three or four times before correctly. What happened to that guy? I'm not blaming the kid. I'm saying we are not at a level of maturity where guys believe that they can make the play.

In the Michigan game, we were behind 14-10. We went into our two-minute offense with 50 seconds left in the first half. We were on the 50-yard line. We threw an interception, and the guy returned it to our 10-yard line. They scored with 19 seconds left on the clock. They kicked off, and we fumbled the kickoff. They went right in and scored again. With 15 seconds left in the half, they scored 14 points. The second half and the rest of the game, we played all right. Can you overcome that adversity? It is difficult against good football teams. All the teams that I was talking about are good football teams.

I think that all these things come from not believing in yourself, having self-imposed limitations on yourself as to what you can and can't accomplish. They don't have enough confidence in themselves that they can make the play in the most critical time of the game to win the game. That comes from mental toughnessand a lot of self-confidence.

Self-imposed limitations are the doubt we all have. How many times have you as a coach been sitting in the locker room before a big game and had doubts about whether you can win or not? It happens to me sometimes. It happens to your players too.

There was the fisherman in West Virginia. He went fishing. He caught a big fish and a little fish. He threw the big fish back in and kept the little fish. I asked him why he didn't keep the big fish and throw the little one back. He said that he only had a nine-inch frying pan. He put limitations on himself as to what kind of fisherman he could be by what kind of frying pan he had.

I ask our players sometimes how big a frying pan they have. How much confidence do they have? It is important to develop an attitude that you can be successful with.

I can't speak at a clinic without talking about this stuff. I think that's the essence of getting intangibles out of football players. They are simple things like giving effort, playing with toughness, being responsible for your own self-determination, and knowing what you are supposed to do. Those things sound really simple, but how many games do we lose on mental mistakes? How many times do you watch a film where someone is not loafing? How many times do you see your team not demonstrate the kind of toughness, physically and mentally, that you would like for them to play with? Those are all intangibles. It takes no ability to do these things, but very few time do we see them. You have to have a well-conditioned team to be able to do it. If you are not a well-conditioned team, that is when you make mistakes. They make mental mistakes, loaf, and lack toughness when they are tired. Who said, "Fatigue makes cowards of us all"? Of course you know that it was Vince Lombardi.

Let's talk about cover one. The term MOF means middle-of-the-field safety. Anything that has this type of coverage is taught with the same principles and theory. Whether we play three-deep zone with strong

 





Page 179

rotation or three-deep zone with weak rotation, the principles of every position remain the same. Whether we teach man-free cover one with the safety down strong or man-free cover nine with the safety down weak, the principles stay the same. The whole concept of this coverage depends on the area played by the free safety. We feel that a free safety can cover about 35 yards deep and 5 yards outside either hash mark when it is in the middle of the field. For everybody on the defensive field to use the safety's help, they must reroute, funnel, and play leverage on receivers relative to the middle-of-the-field safety.

This coverage starts with the corners. The college football field is a little different from the high school field. The hash marks are 19 yards from the sideline and 13.5 yards apart. The yard line numbers are 7 yards to the bottom and 9 yards to the top from the sideline.

Ten yards from the sideline with the ball in the middle of the field is what I call a divider for the corner. If the receiver is inside the divider of the corner, the corner plays an outside alignment. That is because the safety is in the middle of the field and is relatively close for the post cut of the receiver. If the receiver aligns outside the corner's divider, the corner aligns inside, because the relative distance to the safety where he helps the corner has increased.

The divider determines whether the corner plays inside or outside the receiver. We tell him to play inside or outside relative to the divider. The divider is a plane that runs all the way down the field. If the receiver lines up inside the divider but stems the corner to a position outside the divider, the corner goes from an outside position on the receiver to an inside one.

We tell our safety that if the ball is thrown 25 yards, he should be able to break that far. We teach a little different theory with zone and man coverage.

In three-deep zone, the free safety has to stay as deep as the deeper receiver. When we play man, we settle the free safety at 25 yards. We feel that the one thing that can hurt us is running the post and the dig. If they throw to the dig receiver, there can be no one there unless the corner plays a perfect coverage. The safety needs to jump everything in the middle of the field.

Here is the second concept I want to show you. In a three-deep zone or a middle-of-the-field safety coverage, there are areas on the field that you don't want people to run in. Those areas are the seams of the zone. For the high school field, the seam is 17 yards from the sideline and 3 yards wide. The seam starts at 14 yards from the sideline and ends at 17 yards from the sideline. If you let receivers run down those seams, the safety can't cover those areas. You have to keep people out of those areas if you are playing middle-ofthe-field coverage.

When we play a three-deep zone, someone always drops to the seam and reroutes the receivers out of the seams at 10 yards. If we reroute the receiver at 5 yards, he can run past the defender and get back into the seam. If we reroute him at 10 yards, it is late in the play, and he doesn't have enough time to get back on the seam before he is too deep.

The same thing is true of cover two. We reroute receivers at 10 yards out or in to the seam. The seam in a cover two is the half-defender divider. That creates a leverage field position for the half-field safety. With the three-deep zone, we want the receivers out of the seams, and with two-deep, we want them in the seams.

 





Page 180

Let me show you our cover one. I'll draw this up as an eight-man front. The corners in our defense would be in bump-and-run man on the two wide receivers. The strong safety and three linebackers are responsible for the backs and tight end. The free safety plays over the top in this coverage. The Will linebacker has the split-end side break, and the strong safety has the tight-end side break. Break means "if the formation splits someone else out." If the tailback aligns in the slot to the split-end side, the Will linebacker is responsible for him. That is only for the backs. The corners have the wide receivers all the time.

If the offense brings both wide receivers to the same side, we bring both of our corners over to that side. You may do it differently, but whoever starts out on the tight end has tight-end side breaks, and the Will linebacker always has split-end side breaks. That means we will never have a Will linebacker cover a wide receiver. These two formations are the only ones they can make with normal personnel in the game.

If one of the backs goes in motion to the split end, the Will linebacker covers him. If one of them goes in motion to the tight-end side, the strong safety has him.

Let me get into the techniques of coverage. The divider for the corner is 1 yard on top of the numbers, or 10 yards from the sideline. If I teach a corner to play bump and run, the first thing I want to do is make sure he understands the divider principle. If the receiver is inside the divider, the corner is aligned outside and on his low shoulder. If the receiver is outside the divider, the corner aligns inside and plays high-shoulder position. The receiver wants to get on top because he can go anywhere he wants from that position. We take that away from him. We get on top and cut him off. If the receiver is inside the divider, the corner will be outside and on the low shoulder. If the receiver runs a flag route outside the divider, the corner can easily get to the high shoulder and cut him off.

In the bump and run, the corner should off-hand jam the receiver. If the receiver aligns right on the divider, the corner aligns outside and low. The corner watches the hips of the receiver. As long as the hips are square, the corner is square. As soon as he starts to turn his hips one way or the other, the corners off-hand jams him. If the break is to the inside, the corner jams with his outside hand. If the break is outside, the corner jams with his inside hand. We try to hit between the receiver's shoulder tips and near tip.

Bump and run is the best coverage you can play against anybody, even with marginal athletes. You have a better chance playing guys up on receivers than you do off. The hardest thing to do in man coverage is to play off a receiver and cover him. It is the hardest thing to teach. The corner has to have great feet, be able to redirect, and have a great burst to the ball. Plus, the receiver knows whether he is going, and you don't. When you get up and bump, you are in position to cover the guy, as long as you stay in position and get your hands on the receiver.

Most guys make the mistake of widening their feet and setting back when the receiver breaks. As soon as a defensive back gets in that position, he is beat. To get out of that position, the defensive back has to step back inside his frame to be able to go anywhere. What the back wants to do is keep his feet together, so that when the receiver starts his move, the back can slide

 





Page 181

into him, jam him, and be on top of him. That is the most important thing in bump coverage.

To keep the receiver from walking all over the defensive back, we use a punch at the line to force a release. Just as soon as the receiver moves, the defensive back punches him with the inside hand on the inside tip of the receiver. When that happens the receiver's coach is going to tell him to lean back.

The other thing you can do is to fake a punch. The receiver leans back, and that slows his release. If the defensive back reaches with the wrong hand in bump and run, he is dead. The most natural thing for the defensive back to do is throw both hands up and squat. Either one of those things kills you in bump and run.

We want to protect the middle of the field in bump and run. We give up the out route. Make them throw the ball in the flat, but don't let them throw the ball over your head outside. Those are the hardest passes to complete, so everything is relative to that.

If the receiver takes an inside break, the defensive back jams him the same way. That puts him inside the divide line. The defensive back assumes the low-shoulder position and rides him down the field. If the receiver cuts outside, he is cutting into the defender. If he breaks inside, the defender works down on him, always dragging his back hand so he can hook the receiver. That is the technique in a nutshell.

I could give you a list of drills to do to practice this. You can step and mirror the receiver on release using no hands. Step and slide on the release using no hands. You can do the same drills using the off hands. You have to teach kids to roll over quickly. Kids who play all their life in the off position have trouble turning. If they are turned left and they have to go right, they always slow-turn. When you play close coverage, if you slowturn, you're dead. When the receiver disappears behind the defensive back, the defender has to speed-turn into him. That is the drill we teach. The receiver runs down the field, making the defender roll-turn. Everything you do in close coverage is done with the speed turn.

Emerson Walls ran about 4.9 in the forty. He could not backpedal a step. He could not run a lick, but he always had his man cut off. He always used his help and had 39-inch arms to jam the hell out of people. He was a very effective player and never played in a backpedal. He could half-turn with the best of them, which meant he always had the receiver cut off.

Let's go to the strong safety on the tight end. The first thing we want to know is the alignment of the tight end inside or outside the divider. Most tight ends will be inside the divider. We play our strong safety like a rover back. He aligns 3 yards outside the tight end and 5 yards deep. If the tight end releases upfield, the strong safety runs to his outside shoulder and gets in a low position. He wants to get on the tight end as soon as he can. We never want to play a tight end or a big man in a high position, because he will push off the defender and come back for the ball.

The tight end releases to the outside, and the strong safety plays him on the divider. If the tight end runs into the seam, the strong safety should play even with him. If the safety plays low-shoulder, the quarterback can shoot the ball in there. If the safety plays highshoulder and the quarterback throws at the back pad, he's got the safety too. We run shoulder to shoulder in the seam. Ideally, we want to run the tight end out of the seam. When the tight end runs to the seam, the safety beats him there and makes him break one way or the other. When he breaks, the safety rolls into him and plays his technique.

Let's talk about the three linebackers and the strong safety playing the backs and the tight end. When you are playing middle-of-the-field coverage in man to man, you should never play in-and-out coverage with the tight end and near back. If the tight end runs up the field, the strong safety takes him. If the backs split and go different directions, the Sam linebacker takes the left back coming out, and the Will linebacker takes the right back coming out. The Mike linebacker backs up in the middle of the field to cut any player who comes in the middle of the field. If no one comes into the middle 5 to 7 yards deep, he continues to back up to help on the dig route.

 





Page 182

The Mike linebacker always has the second back out to a side. If both backs flow strong or weak, the Mike linebacker has the second back. If both backs flow weak, the Will linebacker has the first back. The Mike linebacker has the second back. The Sam linebacker becomes what I call the rat. That comes from a rat in a hole. He is the middle player, waiting to cut anything that comes into the middle. The strong safety has the tight end. If the tight end runs a drag across the field, he runs into the rat, who picks him up. The strong safety becomes the rat. That gives us a free guy in the hole both short and deep. We are playing 4 on 3 with all the receivers leveraged off.

If the offense runs a flow or play-action pass to the strong side, the Sam linebacker has the first back out of the backfield. The Mike linebacker has the second back. The strong safety has the tight end. The Will linebacker is the rat. If the tight end runs the drag, the Will linebacker cuts him. The strong safety becomes the rat and could get under the dig route of the split end.

Whoever the free guy is out of the four becomes the rat and cuts off the receivers. The only time the safety gets cut is when the tight end goes across. If the tight end goes vertical, the safety plays outside and the low shoulder. The safety in the sky-force position never has to cover the tight end on an inside move.

Because you are playing 4 on 3, no one is in a run-pass conflict. Everyone can play their gap responsibility on a run and still play pass responsibility without a problem.

Here is a way to work things into your package. Anytime that the safety takes the tight end and the tight end side-breaks, we call that cover one. We could call 0, which means 5 guys rushing with no rat in the hole.

If we call cover nine, the Will linebacker would have the tight end, and the safety would come down weak. It is all the same principle of coverage. What we don't want to happen is to have a linebacker covering a speedster out of the backfield. The strong safety aligns to the split end and has split-end-side breaks. The free safety is in the middle deep but has tight-endside breaks. That gives us a safety playing that speedster out of the backfield instead of a linebacker. If the free safety takes a tight-end-side break, the strong safety rotates to the middle and becomes the middle-of-the-field safety. The three linebackers play the remaining back and the tight end. That leaves us a rat short in the middle.

 





Page 183

The hardest pattern to cover is the double cross by the tight end and slot back. The first thing you do when you get cut off from the receiver who you are covering is to look through the guy who cut you to see whether anything is coming. If there is, it is an automatic cut to the other guy back. That is the first thing. Guys won't do that sometimes. As soon as they get cut off their man, they want to fade back into the hole. What this ends up looking like is a soft zone about 5 to 7 yards deep. When the linebacker gets the cut call, he looks through the guy who cut him and picks up the pattern coming the other way. That way, they don't have to change directions but once.

This coverage will play well in nickel coverage. It plays an eight-man front in the nickel. It will play against four wideouts. It will play against three wideouts and a tight end. In every situation, it makes a front with one more man than the offense has. If you play against four wideouts, you have six in the box and can play the coverage. If they have a tight end, three wideouts, and one back in the game, you have seven guys in the box, and the offense has six to block you. All you need is two guys to play corner. That is why the corners in the NFL always make more money than safeties.

We teach all our coverages the same way. If we play zone, we play cover three and six. When we teach three-deep zone, we teach relationships. We have a flat player who relates to number 1. We have a hook player who relates to number 2 weak. We have a hook player strong who relates to number 3. We have a flat player strong who relates to number 2. We have a corner-corner-free safety in the three-deep coverage. Our weak-side rotation is cover six. Our strong-side rotation is cover three. We teach the drops to everyone.

The progression in zone for us is to drop, reroute a receiver, match the pattern after the pattern distribution, and break on the ball. Matching the pattern after the pattern distribution is another talk. We number the receivers from the outside in on both sides. The flanker strong would be 1, tight end 2, and the back to that side would be 3. The split end weak would be 1, and the running back to that side would be 2. The flanker does a curl, the tight end hooks, and the strong back runs a flat. After the pattern distribution, the back is 1, flanker 2, and tight end 3. This coverage is like man to man, except you don't have a man. If a receiver comes into your zone, he is your man. That is pattern matching. This is good because you don't get picked off.

Fellows I really enjoyed being here. Come to see us any time you get the chance. You guys really do a great job of coaching. Keep it up. Thanks.

 





Page 184

Joe Sarra

Defensive Line Drills and Techniques

Penn State University

Thank you. It is a pleasure to be here at the clinic. I was born and raised in Pennsylvania. This clinic has outlasted many others that have come and gone. You are a tribute to this clinic for coming out and supporting it.

Coaches, this is your clinic. If you have questions, ask them, and we'll answer them. I don't know all the answers. When I talk, I talk with the beliefs of what I am coaching. I'm going to talk about what I know. But anytime you have a question, stop me and ask it.

I'm going to move along rather quickly but at the same time be thorough. It doesn't matter what position you coach or the defense you run, this clinic should help. That includes offense. I'm going to talk about a few of the intangibles that help win football games. One of the most important things in football is to get the team ready. You have to teach and motivate for a team to perform. The key is to get players to play at the highest level of their ability. We have to push our kids to the point where they don't think they can do it any more. These players don't know what their highest level is because they haven't been pushed that hard. They have to learn to fall down and get back up.

If a player has feet and courage, that is what it takes to be a good defensive player. I ran into one of our players up in the hall before I came down here. He was our manager. We were getting ready to play Notre Dame, and our long snapper got hurt. This manager lined up and long-snapped the ball against Notre Dame for us.

If a kid has courage, you can teach him to be a big hitter. You can make him the best tackler on the team. If you have courage to hit somebody and feet to get to the ball, that's all defense is. If a kid has those talents, it is hard to keep him on the bench.

This next point is important in football but is also important in life's critical situations. You have to 
control what you can control. 
You can determine whether your team outhustles people. You can determine their getting to the ball. You determine whether your team is in shape. You determine whether your team is in condition. We play two halves of football. The first-half score means nothing. The final score is what counts. You determine whether your team outhits the opponent. We can control the fact that we outprepare people.

We control conditioning. In the winter program, if they won't pay the price and condition, they will get you beat on the goal line. They will lose on the goal line. We rate our players in several areas. We consider conditioning, the weight room, academics, and other areas.

When I have a meeting with my players I put on the blackboard, "Today is today. Make it count." However, it's more than that. It is the moment that counts. Any one of us could walk out of here and drop dead. Any tragedy could happen to any of us. You have to cherish the moment.

Here are some things that you build your defense around. You have to be a productive defense. We talk all the time about forcing turnover. Coach Paterno will come into our meeting and tell us that we need more interceptions and fumble recoveries. We have to force turnovers. The defense must have pride. They have to dominate and defend their area. They have to feel as if no one can run the ball at them. They will defend their area, and no one can run into their area.

We have a saying that is on the board in the linebackers' meeting room: Pay the toll. That is the linebacker area. It is just like the Pennsylvania Turnpike. To use it, you have to pay a toll. Anyone who comes into the linebacker area gets his head knocked off. If a guy crosses the linebacker's face, if he doesn't deck him, he gets a -3 on the grading scale. They are to dominate and control their area.

 





Page 185

You have to play smart, with poise and pride. They have to have confidence in themselves. There may be a guy with less ability who can play because he is confident that no one can beat him. Toughness is what wins the game. You have to think team, be committed, and be unselfish. Those are things that a lot of people don't have today.

You have to do whatever it takes to win. If you want to win, you have to work harder. People can say that they want to win. That is easy. They have to prepare to win through hard work. You can outhustle people, but execution wins.

This next point is a big one. Coach Paterno does this great. We meet every morning. We look at our personnel board. We move people up and down that board. Who should be moved up a number? Who should we move down? You have to get the proper people in the proper positions. We have moved guys from defense to offense. We had a kid who came to Penn State as a linebacker. Now he is a running back. If you get the proper people in the right positions, they make the plays.

You have to talk to your team. They have to understand your goals and what they are working for. We as coaches are going to work hard but smart. We are working to win. Some people work hard, but they don't work smart.

You have to punish people. You won't win games unless you punish people. You have to get your kids ready to punch somebody in the mouth. You have to get people on the run, and when you get them down, put them away. This is a physical game. We want to beat our opponent physically. How fast you get to the ball only matters if you punish people when you get there. You control gang tackling. It is not how fast you are; it is how physical you are. When the running back is getting pounded, he starts asking the coach to spread the ball around a little.

What are the qualities we look for in the defensive line? The number one thing is toughness. I never recommend a guy for our coaches to look at if he is not tough. When I say tough, I mean the tougher the challenge, the better he plays. We want a kid who is a competitor. You won't see size on our list. You can't worry about size. In the line, it is a street fight. You don't talk about weight classes in a street fight. It is the same way with the defensive line. You can't tell the guy that you can't fight him because he is out of your weight class. You can work harder than anybody else to get better. You can go to the weight room and work on your strength. You can work on your hand and foot quickness. The game of football is played with the hands. If you have a guy with quick hands and feet, he can play. We work with martial arts to increase the speed of the hands.

A defensive lineman has to be aware of what is going on around him. He has to shed blockers. He has to be able to change direction effectively. That is important in defensive football. The lineman must stay focused. He has to be able to get off a block. He has to be just downright mean.

In the defensive linemen's meeting room on the left-hand side of the bulletin board is "loyalty and trust." It never comes off the board. We trust each other, and we are very loyal. We never criticize our teammates or talk about them. If I can't trust a player, he is not going in the game. When all the guys are working together, they share that trust in one another. You can't talk out of both sides of your mouth. You have to talk straight.

We evaluate our players. We have a sheet that we use to evaluate them. The strength coach evaluates the players. The trainers and coaches evaluate them. Their academic advisors evaluate them also. We give them a 5, 4, 3, 2, or 1 grade. We want to get the winners on the field. These guys control the tempo of the game. They are the ones who can make big plays and turn the game around. If you have a horse, make sure he gets in the race. Make sure the good back gets his hands on the ball. I'm not talking about some prima donna. I'm talking about guys with character who won't get you beat.

There are three types of guys. There are players who are winners, players who won't get you beat, and players who will get you beat. Forget about potential,

 





Page 186

and get them to execute. If you have an excuse player, don't play him. He always has an excuse for why he made a mistake. Don't play him. He'll get you beat.

I give our kids a checklist. Defense is our game, and winning is our aim. That is a catch phase for the defensive linemen. I give them a form. On the form are things that we are going to grade and work on all year long. I have the kids grade themselves. I grade them and write down comments for them.

I give them drills to do over the summer to improve on. It is a job description for them. Included on the sheet are the following: change of direction, stance, alignment, assignment, delivery of a blow, shed, pursuit, tackling, and a good base. We also include backpedal, opposite reaction, explosion, read keys, mental alertness, and second effort. I grade the players every night after practice and give them the sheet the next day.

Next are fundamentals. A player has to move and control his feet. He has to shed the high and low blocks. If a person can shed, he can make plays. Some guys can't get off a block. The tackles have to pass-rush, make tackles, pass-react, pursue, and be tough.

I'm going to talk about stance. We use a three-point stance in the defensive line. If I am teaching a young player a stance, I'll put him in a four-point stance. A four-point stance keeps the lineman down. You can't play with your shoulders turned. You want your pads square and your feet under you. A four-point stance helps the kid to do that. We get into a three-point stance, but 98 percent of our weight is on the ground. We have a slight stagger.

In our alignment we play head up, shade inside and outside, nose up, wide or loose alignment, and gap. We key the ball or anything that moves. If anything moves, we are going.

Our responsibility is whatever defensive technique we are in. I'll cover that later. The reaction is to what the defensive lineman sees. He has to see blocking schemes. He must understand his pass-rush lane. Let's go back to the mechanics of the stance. We don't use a wide base. That will get you in trouble. We want our feet under our hips. I don't say anything about shoulder width. Every kid thinks his shoulders are wider than they are. Good backs run with their feet under the hips. They don't get their feet outside the body. They are skaters. They work within the framework of their hips. When we start to get down, we put our hands on our knees. If I get in a right-handed stance, my right foot is back, and it is my push foot. If I get in a lefthanded stance, it is just the opposite. My knees are bent, and I have some weight on my hands: not too much, but just enough. The off hand is close to the ground. We get in the four-point stance and then pick up the other hand. We are a takeoff team. We read on the run. When the ball is snapped, we take off. In our bent-knee position, we get the power angles we want. We step and explode off the ball.

The first thing we tell our linemen is not to get knocked off the ball. If they get knocked off the ball and back into the linebacker, we can't move. If the lineman is in an outside shade on a guard, his inside hand is down. He steps with the inside foot. By stepping with the inside foot, the lineman can keep his outside leg free. If the stagger is too much, it cause the lineman to rise up. It also doesn't allow him to get into the blocker as quickly as having a shorter stagger. We want to get to the blocker quickly, make contact, punch with the opposite hand, and get off the block.

When the offensive blocker rises up to block, we don't rise up. We strike through him. We learn how to use both right- and left-handed stances. We have to play some techniques that require using the opposite hand down. Make your players line up straight. Your feet determine where the lineman lines up.

When we run drills, we film them. We watch the footwork of the linemen. If they don't step with the proper foot, they get a -3. That is discipline. We do it one way. I never stand behind the defense when we are practicing. I stand behind the offense. I want to see the defensive linemen's eyes. I want to see what they are seeing.

 





Page 187

I want his toes to be straight ahead. I may say, ''Feet!'' That means they are too wide or too narrow. I could say, "Stagger!" That means his stagger is too big or not big enough. If I say, "Tilt," that means their footwork will cause them to get reached.
