We do something differently than a lot of people do. I have done this both ways. It doesn't matter what you call techniques, as long as your players know what you are talking about. We letter our gaps. The center-guard gap is the A gap. The guard-tackle gap is the B gap. The tackle-end gap is the C gap. Outside the tight end is the D gap. Head-up the guard would be B. If we want our lineman on the inside eye, that is a B minus. If we want him to straddle the inside leg, that is called tight B. The B plus is the outside eye of the guard. If we get into the B gap, that is called wide B.

We work hard on responsibility. I quiz my players on their assignments. You would be surprised at the number of kids who leave the huddle and don't know the defense or what the snap count is. They don't pay attention in the huddle.

Here is another thing we could talk about anytime. I'm not here to show you alignments, but we'll talk about them. They have to know the alignment, assignment, and reaction. Those things are more important. Never try to tell a kid that the defense will win a game. A scheme is only as good as the players playing it. Execution wins games. The front does not win games.

People ask us about flip-flopping people. We have done both at Penn State. If you don't flip your people, the guy playing on the right gets to play the trap the same way all the time. He uses the same steps and footwork all the time. But if you flip the guy playing the trap, he has to learn both ways, and it adds to the assignments he has to learn. If you flip your people, you have to worry about jumps. If they are flipping, they have to get set in their alignment. If you don't flip, all they have to do is get ready to play. We don't flip our people anymore.

We run a drill about three times a week called "early work." We take our inside people. We work best on best and run traps, reach, scoops, and cutoff blocks. We do this drill before practice. After we work on those things, we put the offense and defense together and work seven on seven.

Let's get into the practice schedule and what we do on the field. Nothing fancy goes on out there. The first thing we do is stretch. I work with the snappers and kickers, but they do their stretching on their own. We work on our flexibility. The guys may be big, but the game is played with bent knees. I think we take too much time from practice to stretch. I tell them to get stretched out. I don't care when they do it, walking across campus on the way to practice or coming out to practice, but I'm not going to take practice time for them to stretch. You hope they don't do this, but it could happen. A guy gets into a fight. He can't say, "Hey, John, wait five 5 minutes; I've got to stretch and get loose." We get loose during our drill work.

The first drill we do is a bag drill. They step over the bags and stretch. At the end of the bags, I have them do some kind of football-related finish. The first time, I may have them recover a fumble. The second time, I may roll the ball, and they have to pick it up and score. The third time, I may throw the ball to them. The fourth time, they have to tip it to someone else. Then I may have someone there to form tackle. There are all kinds of things that you can do during the warm-up period.

There are ways to recover a fumble. If you are not getting done what you want to get done, keep your players after practice and make them do it right. When you are trying to recover a fumble, never get on your back. Grab the ball, lock it down with the arms, and pull up the knees and hips. When they come in on the ball, if they don't bend their knees, they'll kick the ball. We work on this every day.

You can make your players bend their knees and run quickly over the bags. To teach our kids to bend their knees, we do a drill called hand-touch knee bends. The player gets his butt down and face up, bends his knees, and puts his hands on the ground. We run three or four players at a time. We start them out with their feet straight up the field. You can't push off and have good

 





Page 188

acceleration and power with your feet out to the side. We start them running. When I yell "touch," they touch their hands to the ground then. They don't run two or three steps and then touch. In a game, your reaction is not when you want it. It is when it occurs. On defense, you have to gain ground on every move. After the touch, they come out low, hard, and accelerating. When a player comes out high, he is not gaining ground. Whatever drill you do, make sure they are gaining ground on the first step. I don't tell them to go. They touch and go.

Every day we do stance-and-starts. We put them in their stance and make them come out low and hard. We tell them which foot to move first. We emphasize elbows in tight and pumping. We are looking for explosion.

We do a grass drill to teach players how to fall. If you feel yourself fall, what do you do? You put your hands down to break your fall. If you fall and want to get up quickly, you use your and hands and feet. We teach our kids how to fall. You have to get up on the way down. We do this drill. They start to chop their feet. I say, "hit." They hit the ground, get their hands and feet under them, and come up running. Make them get up and down two to three times and make them tackle to finish the drill. This is the second-effort drill. How many times have you seen guys come off the ground to make tackles, sacks, or recover fumbles? I've seen it lots. If a guy can't give us a second move, he can't play for us.

We use this drill for linebackers and down linemen. The first way we teach this drill is in an up position. We teach them how to slide and change directions. You have to be able to defeat the low block. If you can defeat the low block, you can beat the high block. If you are playing the high block and the low block comes, you can't beat the low block because your legs are split. You cannot shed the low blocker. The feet have to stay under the hips at all times. If the feet get outside the hips, the player can't make a second move. If he keeps his feet under his hips, he can go right, left, forward, or backward. The knees have to be bent, and the back foot must be active in the slide. In the change-of-direction drill, the toes have to remain straight ahead. If the toes turn, the shoulders turn. We want the pads parallel. We have three guys in the drill. There are six guys behind them in what we call the bull pen. They are practicing the drill. They are moving their arms and practicing. I say, "switch," and the second line becomes the first line and continues the drill. The third line moves up and practices while they wait for the next "switch" call. You don't become a better player by standing around.

The next drill is the lateral run. We don't slide our feet, we cross over. We don't want a big, wide step. It is a short movement like a carioca step. We keep our toes straight up the field as we cross our feet. My butt is low, and my knees are bent. If we turn our feet, the shoulders turn also. We can't shed anybody or change directions if our shoulders and feet are turned. Don't let them rise up when they change directions. If you do, you will get beat. When your eyes see something, they don't tell you to rise up.

This next drill is not a good drillit is a great drill. We do it every day. We have two long, flat dummies. We have two guys facing each other. One guy is going to his right, and one is going left. They have their hands on the dummies. They slide up and down the dummies. While they are sliding up and down the dummies, they can't take their hands off that bag. When they get to the end of the dummies. They turn and sprint. You can do the same thing with a board instead of a dummy. The main thing is to make them move laterally and keep their hands down.

We take the drill one step further. We have an offensive player and a defensive player. They do the same drill, but as they get to the end of the bag, the offensive player tries to cut the defensive player with a low block. The blocker is trying to get to the knees of the defensive player. The defensive player has to keep his hands down and shed the blocker. If the defensive player gets to the end of the dummy and rises up, he is blocked. We work on movement, shedding, and keeping down. We slide down with the toes straight and then cross over with the toes straight, always keeping our hands on the bags. It makes them bend their knees and stay down.

 





Page 189

We look hard in our game films for guys who do these drills in actual game situations. This drill is one you can find all the time. That is why it is so good. We set up a series of bags and make the linemen step over the bags using the form they worked on in the hands-down drill. They step over the bags going right and left. At the end of the bags to the right and left, we have a tackling lane with a ball carrier. We go through movement, change direction, see the ball carrier, explode up into the lane, and make the tackle.

This next drill is a million-dollar drill that you can get on credit. Go down to the hardware store. Buy some rubber tubing, and make a circle. Even when you pass-rush, you have to maintain good body position. We put two guys across the circle from one another, one in a right-handed stance and one in a left-handed stance. On movement, they run around the circle. They have to keep the hands down on the tubing. They have to lean to stay on the circle. The head is up, and the knees bent. When they get all the way around, they change directions and go the other way. This gives them the idea about coming around the horn in the pass rush. It teaches them to lean in and make the play on the quarterback instead of running past him.

 





Page 190

Bill Sexton

The FSU Offensive Package

Florida State University

Thank you. I'm going to get started and cover as much of our package as I can. Every year when we start spring training, we start in the I formation. We start in the I formation, run the football, and throw the playaction pass. Once we get that established as well as we can, we go to split backs, with two wide receivers and a tight end. From that we are going to throw and run the ball as well as we can. Then we go to three wide receivers, no tight end, and two backs. From there we progress to four wide receivers, no tight end, no fullback, and one tailback. I'm going to touch briefly on each one of these personnel groups. If I have time, I'll mention something about our goal line and shortyardage package.

We have come to the conclusion that we have to be diversified in college football. We cannot be a onedimensional football team. We have to be able to run and throw the ball. That is what we strive for every spring and fall.

We want to be able to do equally well, whether we are in the I, split-backs, or one-back set. We don't want to give a defense an advantage by personnel group. We don't want the defense to know what we are going to do from a certain set. The same is true of the formation we are in.

Each spring, we start in the I formation, and the first play we try to establish is the toss sweep. We have been blessed with some great tailbacks. The tailback for us has to be a multidimensional player. He has to be a runner first and a pass receiver second. In the I formation, we put him 8 yards deep. One great thing about the I formation is that it gives the I back a chance to see the field. He is 8 yards deep with his shoulders square to the line of scrimmage, and he is in a twopoint stance. He has the chance to use his God-given ability of vision to run where they aren't.

The fullback's feet are at 5 yards. That puts his hands down at approximately 4 yards from the tip of the football. Our splits are two feet between the center, guard, and tackle. We have a three-foot split between the tackle and tight end. If the ball is in the middle of the field, our wide receiver's split will be halfway between the hash mark and the sideline.

I'll draw up an eight-man front to show you this play. More and more people are starting to crowd the line with their safeties. They are getting their safeties involved in run support. That is another reason to be diversified and not run the ball every time. We always put our tight end on the defensive end, no matter what technique he is. The tight end blocks the defensive end in the 6, 7, 9, or wide 9 technique. He takes his steps according to where the defensive end is aligned.

If the tight end has an inside shade, he takes an inside zone step. He squares his hips and tries to knock the defensive end back off the football. We try to establish a new line of scrimmage. The tackle always blocks the defensive tackle. It doesn't matter if he is a 3, 4-eye, 4, or leverage technique. He wants to prevent the tackle from spinning back into the play. He knocks him upfield as much as he can. The tight end and tackle need to get squared up as much as they can so that they can knock their men back toward their goal line as opposed to knocking them down.

The guard pulls and looks for the run support. He tries to cut the outside hip of the strong safety. If the strong safety comes up on the line and blitzes, the tight end gives the guard an alert call. The guard knows the strong safety is blitzing and kicks him out. Also, the tailback can see that.

The center has the strong A gap. He gets a full scoop into that gap. The split-side guard hard-scoops the knees of the 1-technique shade tackle. The split tackle comes hard inside for the backside B gap. We flip-flop our offensive line. We have a tight guard and tackle, and a split guard and tackle.

 





Page 191

The backside 5 technique is controlled by the naked bootleg. With every run we have at Florida State, we have a naked bootleg. The split end releases hard inside for the free safety. If he can't get him, he turns back on the off corner. We have a three-step read for our flanker, or what we call the Z receiver. He is reading the corner and free safety. If the free safety comes, he cracks on him. If the free safety stays back and the corner hangs, he blocks the corner. If the corner comes in a hard corner or cloud coverage, he blocks the corner.

The tight guard pulls up the alley. The fullback's initial takeoff point is the inside leg of the tackle. That is strictly a takeoff point. He starts toward the inside leg of the tackle until the guard clears. From there, he tracks the onside linebacker. The fullback probably will find the linebacker in the 7-technique area. If the linebacker has a blitz on, the fullback finds him and chops his outside hip. If you can get the linebacker on the ground, it is easier for the tailback to get to the next level. When you block them high, they have a better chance to come off and make the play.

The tailback takes a lateral flow step or hitch step so that the fullback can clear. The quarterback takes the snap, seats the ball in his belly, reverse-pivots, and pitches the ball to the tailback. The tailback concentrates on catching the ball. The secret here is not to get in a hurry. He wants to let the blocks develop. The guard and tailback key the tight end's block. In this case, the guard goes outside the tight end who is coming down on the 7 technique.

The tailback takes a flow step and heads downhill. We want him coming toward the line, not laterally. The tailback sees the down block on the 7 technique and bounces the play outside. The play now depends on the blocks of the guard and fullback.

Let me draw this up against a leverage front, or 50 front. A lot of people don't think this is a good play against this kind of defense. We played Texas A&M in the 1991 Cotton Bowl. They ran this type of defense. They had the noseguard in a 1 gap, a defensive tackle in a 5 technique, and a defensive end in a 9 technique. To the back side, they were in a 1, 2, or 3 on the guard. They played hard corners with the safeties free. We had a tailback who became the MVP in that game. He gained almost 180 yards rushing. We ran the sweep 15 or 16 times on a cold, rainy, sleety afternoon in Dallas, Texas. The defense has leverage in the 5 and 9 techniques. The tight end and tackle have their work cut out for them. They have to block players who already have them outflanked. They take a small zone step and cross over with their next step to the crotch of their assignment. They have to knock them back. They entangle their blocks and knock them back at whatever angle they are.

The tackle and tight end take the outside arm and punch through to the outside number of the defender. They grab the inside number of the defender and turn him. They have to accelerate their feet. We talk about two things in blocking. We talk about the initial lick or contact and accelerating the feet on contact. We get physical with our feet. The man who accelerates his feet on contact wins the battle.

The tight guard pulls and reads the tight end. He is still on the strong safety but reads the tight end. If he doesn't see the numbers on the tight end's jersey, he goes around him. If he can see the numbers of his jersey, he pulls inside the tight end to get the strong safety.

The center has to scoop hard to block the shade gap player. We have had good centers at Florida State, but we have had average centers who could handle a 1 and 2 technique. It is hard, but still they can do it. Unless you are playing against a totally dominating football player, that block is not as hard as it seems. If the noseguard moved all the way to a 3 technique, the tight guard would give a "stay call." That means he stays in and blocks the 3 technique. The tailback will have to make a move on the strong safety. We block the line of scrimmage first. We want to block the inebackers second and the safeties third. You can't

 





Page 192

block them all. The tailback knows that. He has to outrun them, run over them, or make them miss. If he can't do that, we need to go recruiting again. The split guard and tackle are scooping hard. The 5 technique is held by the naked-bootleg threat of the quarterback.

The fullback aims for the tackle's inside leg. He blocks the linebacker. The only way he will block the linebacker inside the tight end is if he is on a blitz. The fullback lets the guard clear and tracks the inside linebacker. He usually goes into the same hole as the guard. He junction-blocks the linebacker. If the defensive end runs out to the sideline, the success of this play is whether the tackle and fullback can make their blocks. It is still a very good football play.

The tailback takes his flow lateral step. He runs downhill, keying the block of the tight end. The tailback generally hits up inside the end. If the defensive tackle is playing super-hard leverage, the tailback comes inside his block. When that happens, the success of the play is dependent on the blocks of the center and backside guard. I've seen plays where the defensive tackle and end skated hard to the sideline. The tailback turns the ball up inside for a good gain.

Your most powerful blocking position is when the defender is lined straight up on you. Your hips and his hips are aligned, and that is where most of your leverage comes from. When a defender has leverage, the blocker tries to take some kind of position step to square up his hips so he can knock the defender back off the ball. The first step is the short zone step. The crossover is the power step. When the blocker can cross over, he can deliver the blow.

Probably the next most frequent defense we face is the 4-3 defense. Now you are faced with a little different problem. By alignment, 4-3 teams take their defensive end and align him in a "ram technique." They align their defensive end at a 45-degree angle and run him through the V in the neck of the tight end. They have the tight end really outflanked. When we get a 4-3 defense, the first thing we have to do is get the wide receiver in the blocking scheme. We signal him to crack on the first safety inside. In the 4-3 defense, they have taken their linebackers and shifted strong. They shift the front to the weak side.

The tight end takes a drop step to square his hips and turns out on the defensive end. The wider the defensive end is, the more of a drop step he has to take. He probably can't hook the defensive end, but he can in certain situations. What he is trying to do is knock him back at the angle he is charging. The tight end does not want to absorb the blow. He attacks the defensive end. He wants to create as much distance between the end to the defensive tackle as he can.

The tackle blocks the tackle. He blocks down on the 3 technique and tries to knock the tackle back at an inside angle. The guard pulls inside the end for the Sam linebacker. The fullback comes off the tackle's leg and turns inside for the Mike linebacker. The reason we do that is because the center can't block the Mike linebacker on that fast-flow play.

The center, split guard, and tackle are hard-scooping on the backside. The tailback sees a wide 9 technique. He takes a lateral flow step and heads downhill at the butt of the tight end. Seventy-five percent of the time, he ends up going inside the tight end's block. The further the tight end can knock the defensive end off the ball, the more alleys the tailback has to run the ball.

This play has been outstanding for us on the goal line. I'll draw it up against a 6-2 defense. There are some people who don't like to pitch the ball on the goal line. We have not had a problem with that. We coach our men to catch all pitches with eyeball concentration. This is not a fast play. The tailback takes his flow step

 





Page 193

and catches the ball before he runs with it. This gives the read and blocks a chance to develop. We can run this from two tight ends and be in a situation where we can run it both ways. The 6-2 defense will be a leverage-type defense. The defense is aligned in 1, 5, and 9 techniques to both sides of the ball. One of the toughest things about running this play in short-yardage situations is that the defense can get more people to the ball than you can block. This is a play where you can give the ball to a great back and give him a chance. There is a leverage player in the 5 and 9 technique. Defenses like to pinch in short yardage. This is a great play against that stunt. The closer you get to the goal line, the tighter the defense gets. This play is a great play on the 1-yard line. Especially if you can run a little inside zone play to the fullback or the quarterback sneak.

The play that helped our short-yardage offense is the quarterback sneak. When you can run that play, the defense has to tighten up inside. That lets you run the sweep on the goal line. The guard's rule doesn't change. He pulls for the support. If they play bump and run on the goal line, we tell the wide receivers to outside-release and run to the back of the end line.

If they play a zone, the wide receiver has to block the corner. The center hard-scoops the strong A gap. The fullback aims at the inside leg of the tackle. He lets the guard clear and tracks the inside linebacker. The backside is in a full scoop. That includes the second tight end. The tailback has the same read as he did on the previous defense. He reads the 9 technique.

We are blessed with outstanding athletes at Florida State. We take our plays and break down their averages. We know what they average in short yardage as well as the open field. The toss sweep averaged between 6.5 and 8 yards a carry. That is a play you can hang your hat on. This is a play that we will establish.

The next play is the sprint draw. This play averages between 6.5 and 8 yards a carry. Let's start with the eight-man front. The tight end's rule is to release on the outside support when the tackle is uncovered, unless he gets a 4-3 call. The tight tackle blocks the defensive end. The tight guard blocks the 3, 2, or 1 technique. The techniques they are going to use will look like the sprint-draw pass. They are going to show their pads. They try to lift the defensive men out of their shoes and short-step them off the ball. That is the same technique we use in our sprint-draw protection.

The center and split guard combination block the defensive tackle and backside linebacker. The center squares his hips, knocks the tackle over to the guard, and gets off on the inside linebacker.

We want the defensive linemen to think 
pass. 
The split tackle pass-sets, engages the defensive 5 technique, and tries to run him upfield. If we can get them rushing upfield, this is a great play. The split end releases for the rover. If the rover is on the line, he goes up on the free safety. We can establish the sprint-draw pass, and it makes this play really go.

Let's draw it up against the leverage defense. The defense is in a 9, 5, and 1 technique to the tight side. They are 3 and 5 technique to the split side. The tight end and tackle have the same blocking technique. He shows his pads to the defensive end, engages him, and runs him up the field. If the defensive end sets on the line, the tight end short-steps out, engages him, and drives him off the ball. The tight tackle shows his pads, engages the defensive tackle, and works him upfield.

 





Page 194

We want a running lane inside. When the tailback hears the tackle-technique call, he knows he is going inside. He has to feel the blocks of the linemen and run to daylight. We don't want him running the ball outside to the tight side. A tailback can't start downhill with his pads square to the line and beat a leverage player to the outside. The fullback climbs the Sam linebacker. He takes him down the middle, not to the side. We are not thinking inside or outside with his block. We don't want an edge player.

The fullback fires through the hole. When he gets 2 to 3 yards from the linebacker, he widens his base from a staggered run to short steps. He becomes an offensive lineman. He gets his shoulder pads down on his knee, with his cage up. We hit with the face mask and shoulder pads up. He brings his hands up and tries to lift the linebacker out of his shoes. He hits him as hard as he can, accelerates his feet, and drives him off the ball. If the Sam linebacker is sticking his nose in there and making it tough on us, we fake the ball in there and throw the pass behind him.

The center and tight-guard block make the combination block on the noseguard and Mike linebacker. The tight guard blocks down on the noseguard with the center. The center comes off for the Mike linebacker. The split guard and tackle show their pads on the line of scrimmage. Hopefully, they get a pass rush from the 3 and 5 technique. They engage and drive them past the ball.

The split end drives hard on the corner and blocks him. The flanker does the same thing.

Against the 4-3 defense, this is an outstanding play. As Coach Bowden says, you can blind-call the sweep. We want to limit our running game. We are very sophisticated in the passing game. But we are as simple as we can be in the running game. We are going to have a lot of passes. That does not mean we deemphasize the running game. It only means that we have more people involved. We want to simplify the running game to the point where the linemen and backs are doing a few things but doing them well. The tight end shows his pads and tries to force the defensive end wide or downfield.

The tight tackle and guard come hip to hip and roadgrade the 3 or 4-eye technique back into the Mike linebacker. They work a combination for the 3 technique and Mike linebacker. The center and the split guard are doing the same thing with the 1 or 2-eye technique. They combination-block for the tackle and Will linebacker. The split tackle sets and works the 5 technique upfield.

This is where the play changes in the 4-3 defense. The difference is the hardball play by the four inside people. There is no pass set. We set on the outside and drive off on the inside. It is a defensive recognition for us and a technique change for the four inside people. That is something we evolved to. It became a better play when we did it.

The fullback isolates on the Sam linebacker. We tell the fullback to block him on his side of the football. We do not want to block him on our side of the football.

We have eight people making the sprint-draw pass look like the sprint draw. If the defense wants to get their safeties involved with run support, we'll beat them with the sprint-draw pass. If the defense commits safeties to run support, the wide receivers are in man coverage. What else could you want in the passing game?

 





Page 195

Let me show you the weak-side sprint draw. It is a combination hardball play and a pass set. The number situation against the eight-man front doesn't look good, but we've had a lot of success with this play. We get a "rover call." That identifies the defense for us. The rover call puts the fullback on the rover. The center knows that on a rover call he works with the split guard to push the 2 technique up into the playside linebacker.

The tight guard and tackle do not scoop. The tight tackle tries to get his hip square on the 3 technique. The tight guard chips up on the backside linebacker. The tight end cuts off the 7 technique. The unblocked man is the strong safety. The tailback is looking to run on the outside leg of the split guard. It has the potential on a heavy 5 technique to break outside. On the back side, we are trying to get square, not cut off. We want to have the ability to cut back to these blocks. The tight end takes an inside zone step and squares his pads on the 7 technique. He tries to push that man upfield because the ball could break back to him.

Against the leverage defense to the strong side, this is an outstanding play. The tight end and tackle short-step, square their hips, and work the defensive tackle and end back downfield. The tight guard and center combination-block the 1 technique back into the Sam linebacker. The split guard and tackle short-step and work their men back. The fullback runs through the A or B gap to block the Mike linebacker. The tailback has a different look from the eight-man front. There is a natural alley outside the 5 technique. He wants to get out there if he can. If the 5 technique plays too heavily on the tackle, the tailback takes the outside alley. I've seen the play break back in to a little home-run alley created by the overplay of the Sam linebacker. If we get the noseguard blocked, that is a great alley.

Quickly, let me put up the 4-3 defense. It is blocked like the strong-side play. We hardball with the split guard and center into the Mike linebacker. The tight guard and tackle do the same thing with the defensive tackle into the Sam linebacker. The fullback leads up on the Will linebacker. The tailback takes the play to the daylight. He has the natural alley outside the 5 technique on this defense also. The tight end shows his pads and runs the defensive end past the ball.

The next personnel grouping we work into is the three-wide receiver group. It is a strong passing formation. We take a 250-pound tight end out of the game and replace him with another fast receiver. Now, you can really penetrate the field with three great speed guys. You can get vertical and run curl-flats and flood routes. It also gives you the ability to run the football on the defense. The defense has to make a decision. The defense has to take one of their linebackers from the inside and walk him out to try to disrupt one of those wide receivers. If you get a six-on-six in the box, you are in good shape. We try to run the isolation to both sides.

Our third wide receiver blocks the walked-off linebacker. The tight tackle short-steps his defensive tackle. The tight guard and the center work a hardball combination on the noseguard to the backside linebacker. The split guard and tackle short-set the 3 and 5 techniques. The fullback isolates the first inside

 





Page 196

linebacker. It is the same steps as our sprint draw. The aiming point is the outside leg of the tight guard. From there, the tailback runs to daylight.

When the tailback sees seven in the box instead of six in the box, he knows there is no cutback for him. The play becomes a strictly play-side run. We have to leave one guy unblocked. We leave the man who is the farthest away from the point of attack.

The play to the weak side looks like the schemes we ran with the sprint draw to the weak side. They are primarily the same play.

The outside play from this set is the toss sweep. We crack on the inside linebacker with our slot receiver. We reach and overblock with our line. The fullback blocks on the support. The tailback flow-steps as he did on the downhill play, but he is trying to get wide on this play. There is no tight end, therefore there is no read. This is a full-fledged sweep to the sideline. The tight guard is overblocking on the inside linebacker. If the linebacker runs through, the guard blocks him. If he doesn't, the slot back cracks him. The wide receiver three-step-reads the corner. If it is a hard corner, he blocks him. If the corner retreats, he cracks the strong safety.

I'm out of time, but I would like to show one pass before I go. Our goal every year is to average 200 yards rushing. As you can see, we don't have many plays. We run sprint draw, isolation, sweep, and the reverses off those plays. Our goal in the passing game is 250 yards. We are trying to get to 450 yards of total offense. If we can minimize turnovers, we can win most games. The big thing that we are doing out of the I is the sprint-draw pass. We had Bill Parcells down for a visit about 10 years ago. I asked him, If he could run only one pass against all defenses in pro ball, what would it be? This is what he showed us.

This play has been outstanding for us for years. People call this a dig route. The tight end releases upfield at 10 yards and works inside off the linebacker. If it is man coverage, he separates from him. If it is zone, he finds the open area. The outside receiver runs the same pattern at 16 yards. The fullback blocks as if he is running the sprint draw. The tailback fakes and comes through the line. He runs an out at 3 yards.

The quarterback sets 9.5 to 10 yards behind the tight guard. We want the linemen to keep their pads square. It is not a turn protection. The linemen must remain square as long as they can.

If the free safety comes up on the 16-yard dig or 12-yard tight end's pattern, we are going downtown. We try to hit the post cut to the split end. If the free safety hangs deep, we come to the strong-side linebacker area. If the linebacker flies out of there, we deal the football to the tight end right now. If that window is closed, we look for the 16-yard pattern. That pattern takes a little longer to run. If it is not open, we go to the last read, which is the back in the flat.

 





Page 197

Jackie Sherrill

Punting Drills and Techniques

Mississippi State University

It is a pleasure being here. I know this morning is kind of early for some of you guys. I see of lot of coaches here who are taking notes and seem to be organized. It is important to be organized. I went to speak at Coach Bryant's clinic in Alabama one year. I talked to them about being organized. That reminds me of a story.

This fellow was extremely organized. He wrote down in his notebook everything in every category. He got a visit one day from a guy who was inquiring whether he had been in Louisville on the 21st of February. The guy got out his notebook and began flipping through it. He looked under the category marked ''cities'' and told the fellow that he was in Louisville on the 21st. The fellow asked him if he was there on the 21st and 22nd. The guy looked in his book under "dates." He found out he was there on those dates. The fellow then asked him if he was at a Coach of the Year Clinic. The guy looked in his book under "clinics." He told the fellow that he was. The fellow asked him if he stayed at the Galt House Hotel. He looked under "hotels." He told the fellow that he certainly did. The fellow then ask him if he went to a place called O'Malleys. The guy looked under "watering holes." The answer again was yes. The fellow asked him if he met a lady named Mary. He looked under "names." Yes, the guy had met a lady named Mary. The fellow than asked if he spent the night with her. The guy looked under "entertainment." His answer was yes. The fellow jumps out of the chair and says, "Damn it, I don't like that; she was my wife." The guy looked in his book under "opinions." He told him, "You know, I didn't like it either."

Let's move along, and let some of these guys get in here. I'm going to spend some time this morning and go through the kicking game. But I'm also going to talk about the lost art of actually punting the ball. I remember the first job I had as a head coach. I was setting up the staff and doing all the little things. I asked my staff who could coach the punters. There wasn't a person on my staff who could coach the punter. At that time, we had a player that was a great punter. He was an All-Pac-10 punter. I decided to be the kicking coach. I wasn't going to let anybody screw him up.

Great players teach us a lot more than we can actually teach them. Over the years of coaching punters and kickers, I have derived some things. Ever since then, I get every book that comes out on the kicking game. It pleases me to know that what I have been teaching the punter was in all those books. The techniques remain the same. In the very short time today, I will be able to teach you how to teach your kids to punt. That doesn't mean they will be able to punt. That takes a certain amount of ability.

I have developed the four P's for punters. You 
prepare 
a young man to do something. You 
practice 
hard at it. You 
position 
him to do something that he has to 
perform. 
If he can't perform, you are not going to be able to do it.

Since the guys are still coming in and we are in Louisville, Kentucky, that reminds me of a guy who had just won the lottery. He went to a financial advisor. The advisor told him the hottest thing to invest his money in was race horses. It had the greatest returns on his money. He bought a race horse. The racing season was over in the United State, so he sent the horse to run on the European circuit. The first time his horse ran, he got a telegram. It says, "SSFF." He looked at the telegram and says, "Started second, finished first." He was excited. The horse was on the way back, so the guy decided to get a brass band and meet the trainer and horse. When the trainer got off the boat to the playing of the brass band, he looked at the owner and asked him if he had gotten his telegram. The owner said, "Yes, started second, finished first." The trainer said, "Hell no! He started, stumbled, farted, and fell."

 





Page 198

I collect quotes. I have about 500 or so, and I'm going to give you a few of them. "By failing to prepare, you are preparing to fail." We all talk about this. It is spring, and it's time to get ready for spring practice. The quote I keep on my desk is, "I'm third." We all know who is first. One thing that I tell our coaches is that if our players don't come above a lot of things in their personal life, I won't have them around. If you are going to be a coach, the most important thing you have to understand is that ''players winyou don't." If you want to have a job and be a successful coach, you better make sure that you end up doing what you have to do to develop those players. You need to have a good relationship with them, because they do come first in a lot of things. "Life is in sessionare you present?'' We all go through life. The older we get, sometimes we don't feel that.

"Happy are those who dream dreams and are willing to pay the price to make them come true." We all have dreams. At our clinic this year in Florida, I probably had more former players who are now coaches than any other coach there. I draw these three circles on the board all the time. One is for wishing, the other is wanting, and the third is doing. It is important to make your players understand that everybody is wishing and wanting, but very few people are doing. I take it a step further and draw a little circle inside the "doing" circle. That means the people inside the circle are doing it 24 hours a day.

We all have to do things with our football team to help them understand. I want you to write this down. I want you to write down "14 days." I want you to circle that. Years ago in the 1960s, there was a boom of people going around giving motivational talks. They would get two to three thousand people going to a clinic. These people would come out of those clinics and were going to beat the world. They would go to an insurance seminar. They would come out of that meeting ready to go sell a million dollars of insurance. It got really expensive, and a lot of people began running those clinics. There is one guy on the west coast who is good, but he charges $20,000 for a 30-minute talk.

I'm going to give you about a $20,000 talk in about 15 seconds. You won't remember what you hear here for an average of 14 days if it is not a part of you. If it is not part of your philosophy or understanding, you won't remember what was said here. It is the same way with your players. It doesn't make any difference what you tell your players as long as it's part of them.

I want to play a coach who jumps on a table, screams, and hollers before a game. He tries to get his players so fired up that they want to tear the locker-room door off. The emotion lasts until the first play. When the guy across from him slaps the crap out of him, he doesn't care what the coach said in the locker room. What I'm trying to say is that it doesn't matter what any of us say. Unless you are willing to take it in and digest it to where it becomes a part of you, whatever we say is going to last about 14 days.

In the third circle on "doing," I would put names and leave it on the board. Every few days, I would put a name in there of someone who was actually doing it. He was doing whatever you asked him to do. Then I would take the circle, tighten it down, and put some more players in there. You are not going to have but a few players on your football team who do it 24 hours a day.

"We are not often what we think we are, but we think we are." When you think about things and talk to players about them, make sure your players are on the same wave length that you are.

I like this one very much. It fits all of us. "Take out your brain and jump on it. It gets all caked up." We think that we are really intelligent. Age make you wiser sometimes, but sometimes it makes you dumber. When I was a young coach, I spent a lot of time going to clinics. I took my summers as a young coach to go visit schools. I went to Texas A&M, Texas, Houston, and Oklahoma all in one summer. The reason I did that was because the wishbone was the popular offense in the country. Being a defensive coach, I wanted to understand the wishbone from the offensive side, so I could stop it.

"When the little bird tells you something, make sure it is not a cuckoo." Don't start or believe rumors. I have a rule. I do not respond to the third party. I am probably

 





Page 199

the most-publicized guy in college football over the last 10 years. I'll give you a good example. When I was at Texas A&M, we had this guy who was worth $800 million. He was the chairman of the board. The newspaper kept going to him because he took credit for hiring me. He didn't hire me; I came for some other reason. But he took the credit, and the newspapers would talk to him. Every time he would say something, they would come to me for a comment. I would tell them that I would not respond to that question. They would ask me in 20 different ways, trying to get me to respond. I finally told the newspaper guy to go get the chairman, bring him into this room, and I'll respond to your question. You will find that will never happen.

"You cannot leave footprints in the sands of time by sitting on the beach." You need to write this one down because it is really true: "The road to success is always under construction." Here is another one that really applies to athletics: "It is better to fail at something than to succeed in doing nothing." This is probably my favorite one: "If you must cry over spilt milk, condense it.'' We all have problems. It is our responsibility to convince the players that their problems are not as big as they think they are.

The thing that I'm concerned about in high school these days is the resurgence of pot smoking. I've studied in the Arizona drug-testing committee. They put drug testing in college programs. I was the first coach in college football to test all our players. I believe in it, and I've saved a lot of players' lives from crossing over that line. Research tells us that it is being grown inside under lights and is two to three times more powerful. If you don't or can't test your players, you need to be constantly aware of what is going on. Players cannot play and perform if they are doing those things.

I am here for you, so stop me at any time if you want to ask a question. When we talk about motivation and how to motivate people, that is a misused term. The more mature players you have, the better football team you are going to have. I've been around probably the best college team ever as far as talent. Every player from our starting 22 played pro football. On the 1980 team that I had in Pittsburgh, we had 27 players; 3 were drafted in the first round, 11 in the first seven rounds, 18 others got drafted, and 22 went to camp. We went through a span of four years where we went 11-1. If it hadn't been for the dumb-butt coaches, we probably would have won the national championship at least two times. Coaching is just like the Kentucky Derby. All the horses in the Kentucky Derby have a chance to win, or they wouldn't be there. All those horses can run.

What is motivation? The best example I can give you is playing for Coach Bryant. There was no coach in college coaching like Coach Bryant. He forgot more about winning than we will ever know. We were playing Tennessee. It was fourth down, and we had the ball on their 4-yard line. Coach Bryant had me by one arm and David Ray, our kicker, by the other arm. He sent us on the field. Suddenly from the press box someone yelled, "Clock play." Stabler came up to the line on fourth down and threw the ball out of bounds. He stopped the clock, but we lost the ball on downs. The game ended in a tie.

We went back to our locker room after the game. The manager had left the keys to the padlock on the door out on the field. Coach Bryant knocked the lock off the door, and we all filed into the locker room. About that time, all the coaches started coming in. Coach Bryant told all the coaches to get out of the "damn locker room!" Here we were in the locker room with Coach Bryant. He told us that in all his years of coaching, this was the first time he, as the coach, had lost a football game. He always talked to the press. That day he left the locker room, talked to no one, got in the police car, and left. He usually had a meeting with his coaches on Sunday morning to find out who played well. That meeting didn't happen.

On Monday morning, all the coaches were sitting around the big conference table in his office. They were all early because that is the way Coach Bryant wanted it. He looked at his watch and said that he was five minutes early, but he wanted to get started anyway. We were playing LSU the next week, and they had a great football team. He told all his coaches if we didn't beat LSU to pack their bags. He didn't talk to any of them for a whole week and never had another staff meeting. We went to play LSU and won 35-6.

 





Page 200

They scored the 6 at the end of the game. That's motivation.

Let's talk about the punter. There are two types of punters. There is the slicer and the conventional type. You do not want a slicer. If you are a slicer, you can't control the ball.

Let's talk about the grip of the ball. There are four panels on the football. It doesn't matter how you number the panels. I start with the one to the left of the strings as you hold the ball. A right-footed kicker's right thumb goes on the first panel of the ball. The index finger and middle finger go on the second panel. The ring finger and little finger go on the third panel. On a Wilson football, there is a trademark. It's a circle with an R in it. When I talk about the dot, that is what I'm talking about.

The ball sits in the fingers. Don't palm the ball. It is just as for the quarterback: I want to see air under the fingers. We want the ball back in our fingers so that we can drop the ball down to our side and be able to hold it. It is almost a natural placement of fingers on the ball. I want to be able to place the ball in my fingers and walk, talk, or swing the ball. That means I've got the right pressure on the fingers and no pressure on the palms. Have your punter drop his hands to his sides. Walk up and place the ball in his right hand. It automatically goes in the proper place.

Don't put the ball in the fingertips. To hold the ball in the fingertips, you have to squeeze it with the hand. If you squeeze it with the fingers, you have no control over it. Also, if you squeeze with the fingers, your elbow will go inside, and we certainly don't want that. I have them grip the ball. If I can place my fingers on the ball and take it away from him, he doesn't have control of the ball. If the punter puts the ball in his palm, it is easy to take away from him. The grip is on the back third of the ball.

If he grips the ball, swings it up to the middle, and extends his left hand, that is where it goes. His left hand may be adjusted up on the ball somewhat, but it is a mirror of his right hand. We want a small bend in the back of the kicker. What I am trying to teach you is how to get the ball in the perfect spot every time.

As we swing the ball up and stop it, we can see that the ball is not straight. It is automatically three to five degrees inside and three to five degrees down. If you take the ball in the fingertips, it pulls your wrist and elbow inside, which is not natural.

The next thing is the stance of the punter. We want a parallel stance. That doesn't mean that you won't stagger your stance a little bit. That depends on the punter. We always start off parallel. If the punter is kicking the ball downfield, the only way that can happen is if the body is going downfield. If my hips are pointed outside, I cannot punt the ball downfield. It is impossible to do unless the punter is a slicer. Everything the punter does is going downfield. His shoulders, hips, and feet are all going downfield.

We want no weight on the toe or heel. We want a comfortable stance. We don't want to be leaning forward on the toe. I want a slight bend in the leg. I do not want a straight leg. If the punter is straight legged, that means his butt is out. He wants his butt forward but a slight bend in the legs. We want him to be relaxed in his stance.

The hands are relaxed. When the punter receives the ball, he does not want his hands locked. I don't want those hands extended forward with the elbows locked. I want them relaxed. After he relaxes, he moves his hands up into position. If he locks his hands, that will force him straight up. He has about four inches between his elbow and his body.

The target area for the center snap is just below the chest and just above the crotch. A good drill for the snapper is the one-man sled. Place the sled at the punter's depth, and let him practice snapping the ball at the sled. A lot of people talk about the speed of the snap. It is not speed; it is accuracy. We talk about .7 for the snap.

I've had about five pro coaches call about our punter. I asked them who coaches their punter. The response

 





Page 201

from all five teams was "no one." The most important play in the game is the punt, and no one coaches it. That doesn't make sense to me. They told me that the punters kind of coached themselves. Every day, I spend at least an hour with our punter. That is prepractice, during practice, and after practice. I film every time that he punts the ball. I film two angles. I film from behind and the top. He watches those films every day. I want him to understand in the game if I say, "Drop the ball."

When the punter kicks a bad kick in a game, he does one of two things. He goes over and hangs his head, or he wants to know what happened. You, as the coach, better have something to tell him. You can teach yourself how to coach punters just by watching film.

If the hips are turned to the right or left, the first thing that happens when I step is that my foot is going in the direction of my hips. All of a sudden, my hips are pointing to the right, and I want to kick it to the left. That means I have to slice the ball.

I want the conventional style, where the punter strikes the ball and his foot finishes above his head. I want that finish on the right eye with his foot above his head. That is why I take the film from the top. If I don't see his foot above his head, I know he did not finish the kick. If I see his foot to the left of his shoulder or below, that means he had some bad steps, and his plant foot and body were turned.

To get your punter kicking down the field, draw a chalk line down the field. Just simply make him step down the line. He is stepping down the line and kicking down the line. We want his foot right on that chalk line. If you don't want to use the chalk line, put him in a tunnel. Form a tunnel with dummies or cones. If you have a punter who is crossing over, he will kick the dummies. That will get him out of crossing over more quickly than you know. If you have a punter who is overstriding, pile dummies up in front of him. However, watch him. They want to stop and jerk their knee up when you do that.

There are two drills that I want to show you really quickly. The first drill is called the seat drill. The punter

is on his knees. You can't do this enough. I make our punters do this a thousand times. Have a manager or another punter spin him the ball. The punter catches the ball, seats it, and drops it. They present the ball and drop it. I don't want him to fart around with the ball. The more he adjusts and fiddles with the ball, the less likely he will be a quick kicker. As a punter, you are trying to get your time between 1.3 to 1.5 seconds. I time the punter from the time he catches it until it comes off his foot. If it comes off in 1.6 to 1.7 seconds, the kick is going to get blocked.

During pregame, I watch the opponent's snapper and punter. That is when I decide what we are going to do in punt receiving. I watch the center to see how he presents the ball. I watch the punter to see if he can get it off in 1.5. I check to see how many steps he is taking. I look at the distance it takes him to kick the ball. Most punters are 15 yards deep. If the punter takes more than 5 yards to punt the ball, it will be blocked just about anytime they want to come after it. We can't protect it. That is where I put the dummies to keep the punter from overstepping the 5-yard mark. If the punter can't get the kick off before the 5-yard point, the block point is 7 instead of 9 yards. Even if he can get it off in 2.0 seconds, it will be blocked.

In the seat drill, the punter catches the ball. He grips the ball and presents it. He then drops the ball. If he drops the ball correctly, it will bounce straight up and back to his right. If the nose of the ball is down, it will bounce back to the punter. If the nose of the ball is up, it will go away from the punter. If the ball is turned one way or the other, it will bounce the opposite way from how it is turned. He is not kicking the ball; he can practice these things, and you don't have to be there.

The other drill is called the flex drill. I do this to get the players to understand how to explode through the

 





Page 202

ball and finish. If your player is doing this the correct way, there will be times when he falls right on his butt. His power will take him through the kick. The punter presents the ball as if he were going to kick it. As he presents the ball, the coach or manager takes the ball, but the punter continues his motion as if he were going to kick the ball. When the plant foot is put down, there should be about a 30-degree bend in the knee. The power leg or punting leg will actually be almost touching his butt as he fires up through the ball. As the leg comes from almost touching his butt to the explosion, contact will be made around the knees. This lets the punter experience the full extension of the leg. This is like swinging a weighted bat. He is really going after the ball, but all of a sudden there is no ball there, so he finishes pretty high.

Next, let's look at the placement of the ball on the foot. When the punter's foot is locked and straight, there is only about four inches of the ball that actually makes contact with the foot. That is because the ball is not flat. The ball is dropped on the laces of the shoe. Because it is dropped slightly down and inside, there is more surface to hit.

I am not concerned about the spiral of the ball. I want the ball 40 yards downfield every time it is punted. I don't care if the ball doesn't shoot out like a gun. We would like to have that, but if it doesn't, that is okay. As long as the punter's mechanics are all right, the ball will go downfield 40 yards. If you want the ball to turn over, the punter has to drop the nose of the ball more. By dropping the nose of the ball, the fit on the foot is better, and the angle is better. If the ball is kicked high, the punter is overstriding and has his weight back.

If the ball is kicked to the right, that means the ball is dropped on the outside of the foot. If the ball goes left, the ball was dropped on the inside of his foot. When I watch punters from the front, I can tell you by the way the ball comes off the foot whether it went left or right.

Let me go over some steps for you. Punters are generally a 2-, 2.5-, 3-, or 3.5-step punter. You don't want to be a 3- or 3.5-step punter. If I am a true 2-step punter, I am going right, left, and punt. If I am a 2.5-step punter, I have a jab step to get going. I punt with a right, left, and left step pattern.

[Question] The question was about a bad snap? The first thing that we tell the punter is to get the ball. If the ball is left, he moves his whole body behind the ball. Don't just reach. If we are going to kick the ball downfield, we have to get straight behind the ball. If he doesn't move behind the ball, his momentum will be carried the way the ball is snapped.

Here is a drill to teach a punter how to punt. Get two

punters facing each other about 5 yards apart. Have them play pepper with each other. When I talk about pepper, it is the game you play in baseball. In punting, all you are doing is teaching your punter how to control the ball. Don't worry about the spiral. All I want to do is control the ball. Work on presenting the ball and dropping it. I'm watching the ball. If it goes left, it came off the inside of my foot. If it goes right, it is the outside. It is one step and drop. After we develop some skill with the 5-yard distance, we start to increase the distance apart up to 20 yards.

To make a point, I make the punter close his eyes. With his eyes closed, I make him kick the ball. If he can't do it, his drop is not consistent. He can do it if he steps down the line, has a consistent drop, and swings his leg down the line.

During the seat drill, the punter gets so good that he can stop the ball as it is being snapped to him and not have to make any adjustments to it before he kicks. He has to focus and concentrate on the ball, but his hand-eye coordination will let him do it.

We watch the punter's head. We draw a straight line from his head. As he receives the ball, seats it, and steps forward, his head should not go up and down. It should remain level throughout the approach. As he presents the ball, he does not pull it up or down. If the head goes up on the first step, it means his heel is out. I stand to the right of the punter. As he receives the ball and presents it, if he raises the ball up, I'll slap it out of his hands. The ball is not raised or lowered throughout the kick. They work on the level presentation during the pepper drill also.

 





Page 203

The slicer can spiral the ball, but there is no power. I want to kick with power and forget about the spiral.

Next, I want to cover placement of the ball. A regular kick is one that is kicked straight downfield. If I want to directionally kick the ball, there are some techniques involved. Remember, we want to kick down the line. If the line is a directional kick aimed at the sideline, we still use the same principles. If he wants to kick the ball to the right, the punter moves to the left before the snap. The hips have to be turned to the right sideline. If I'm going to kick it left, I move right before the snap. Our rule is he never moves outside the foot of the center. You see some people line up over the guard. If you do that, you are asking the center for an awful lot. We don't punt outside the tackle box. We never punt outside our guard box.

To get the punters to understand height, I make them kick over light poles. I make them walk up within 10 yards of the pole and kick over them.

When they directionally kick the ball, they need a target. If they punt downfield, there is the scoreboard. When they kick for the sideline, I want them to find a target in the stands. It may be a big section number in the stadium or something like that. He can't see the pylons in the end zones. The kicker's target is in the stands.

The next type of kick is when you are backed up. Never allow the punter to be closer than six inches from the end line. That has to be practiced. The desperation kick is critical in some games. When the punter has a desperation kick, he has one step to kick it. At the end of the game with 11 people rushing is when the punter has a desperation kick. He gets one step to get it away.

The punt formation we use is the spread formation. We have two gunners on the outside to go to the ball. We have two slot backs and a personal protector for the punter.

The kicking look for mismatches is just as if you were looking for a mismatch on offense. If there is a weakness in the kicking game, find it. The punter is just as important as the quarterback. The quarterback gets the chance to throw the ball 20 to 30 times a game. The punter gets six times a game to punt the ball. You have to spend time with him. It is the single most important play in any game you play. Don't take it for granted.

Gentlemen, my time is up. Thank you for your attention.

 





Page 204

Frank Solich

Running Back Drills and Techniques

University of Nebraska

If you listened to my introduction, you may have noticed that I played fullback at Nebraska. How would you like to be an I back and have me blocking for you? It would be a tough go. I will do everything I can to let you know basically what we are doing drill-wise with our running backs. I will cover the techniques we are teaching and what we are trying to get accomplished with them. Also, I will talk to you about some general things that I think are extremely important in terms of football and winning games.

I have been very fortunate as a coach at Nebraska. I went to Nebraska in 1962. That happened to be the same year that Bob Devaney went there. We started winning when he got there, and we have had a winning season every year since. In our worst two years, we were 6-4, both years.

After I graduated from Nebraska, I got a chance to be the head coach at Holy Name High School in Omaha. I took the job, and without ever coaching a down, I thought I was the best coach that there ever was in the state. I had come through a CYO program in Cleveland, Ohio, where I grew up. Our CYO program there was very successful. Our high school team won the city championship.

I thought I knew everything there was to know about football when I left Nebraska for Holy Name High School. I was very confident when I went into my first game as a coach against what happened to be our arch-rival, Cathedral High School. I gave the team a great pep talk before the game, and I knew we could not lose. We came out on the field and got beat 48-3. It did not take me long to realize that I was not the mastermind of football that I thought I was. I realized that I had a lot of things to learn and a long way to go.

Fortunately, I was around some great people, and I learned a lesson there. If you surround yourself with good people, you are going to have a chance, no matter what you are doing, regardless of whether you are a coach or not. If you surround yourself with good people, as a coach, you will have a chance. If you are a player and you surround yourself with good people and you hang out with good people, you will have more than a good chance.

I was never so dejected in my life as I was after that first loss. I was walking across the field to shake hands with the opposing coach. I had my head down and was asking myself if I was in the right profession. I was wondering how things were going to go for me in the future. About that time, I bumped into a little old lady leaving the game. I said, "Excuse me, ma'am, no offense." She said, "That is right, coach, and you did not have any defense either." That is about the way it was at Holy Name High School. We did not have an offense, and we did not have a defense. We did have a kicking game. We scored 3 points in the kicking game.

At the University of Nebraska, I have been very fortunate to coach in a program where, generally speaking, we have had a good offense, a good defense, and a good kicking game. Can you win the conference championship without all three of those elements? You can, but it is tough to do. Can you win the national championship without all three of those units? You can, but it is very, very tough. We gave it a shot in 1983 against Miami. At that time, our defense was ranked 78th in the country. However, we had a tremendous offense and a tremendous kicking game. We lost to Miami by one point in the national championship game. Other teams may have won the national championship without all three phases of the game. Generally, if you have all three phases, it gives you your best chance. However, having all three phases does not make it automatic.

I want to talk about some things that are very important for all coaches. I feel it would be worthwhile for you to go through a process here that is going to

 





Page 205

get everyone in your program moving in the same direction. It will get everyone in your program on the same page. Coach Tom Osborne has our staff sit down at the beginning of the year, and we hammer out a mission statement. This past year, we came up with this statement. It is very short and very concise. We wanted "excellence through teamwork." That is how we wanted to get things done. We wanted to be an excellent team, but we wanted to do it by having everyone working together and by being unified. What we did was to list items under our mission statement that would be the way the coaching staff would operate. We decided that we were going to be hardworking, thorough, professional, and loyal. The coaching profession demands that you be hardworking. If you are not, you are not going to be around very long. The other items are issues that come up during the season. Being loyal is very important. There will be times when you will be tested on this issue. If the defense plays great and turns the ball over to the offense in good position, but the offense cannot take advantage, it can cause some tension between the offensive and defensive coaches. You must be in the game as a team.

Next, under the mission statement, we have a list of governing values. It tells us how we will operate as a staff. We want to promote behavior that reflects well on the university, the football team, and each individual player. We want to promote the character of each player. That is something high school coaches do a great job with. I think we need to do a good job in this area. We all need to visit with our players about things other than X's and O's.

We want to promote unity, pride, unselfishness, and confidence in our team. I think this is very important. I do not care how your team looks on paper in terms of their height, weight, or speed; it makes no difference. If you do not have them unified, if they are not unselfish, and if they are not a confident team, you will not get the job done the way you want it done. You are not going to win as many games as you want to win. I feel that building these aspects on the team are very important.

It is great having the staff all working together and on the same page. But more important is to have everyone who comes in contact with the players going in the same direction as well. What Coach Osborne does, after we have completed our mission statement, is have a meeting with everyone who has any contact with our team, in any shape or form. He gets the equipment managers, trainers, team doctors, strength coaches, ticket office staff, and anyone who comes into contact with our team together in a meeting. He goes over our mission statement with them. He tells them, "This is what the coaches have decided we are going to be all about. This is how the coaches are going to operate this season. We want you to operate in this fashion." Nothing will destroy you more than to have someone in your own organization who is bitching and complaining all of the time. If you have that, you will have a brush fire that will turn into a forest fire immediately. An example would be your trainer taping ankles before practice. He is all upset because the star running back hurt his back doing squats in the weight room. The trainer complains to the other trainers about lifting weights. He has six or seven other players who are waiting in line to be taped. Now, you have a problem, and you may not even know about it. You need to address those issues with everyone involved with your program. Let them know how you want to operate and handle the players. If you can get everyone working on the same page and you have a good offense, defense, and kicking game, you have a good chance of getting the job done.

There are two ways a player can operate. He can say, "What is in it for me? How many touchdowns can I score, how many yards can I gain, or how many tackles can I make?" Or he can say, "How can I contribute to the success of this football team?" The more players who have the second attitude, the better your chances for success.

It doesn't just happen that your team is unified. It does not just happen that everyone is moving in the same direction. You have to work just as hard at this as you do coaching the X's and O's. You have to talk to your players about unity and unselfishness, or else you will

 





Page 206

not have it. Coach Osborne has a theme for the week. One week it may be character. He will have posted some quotes from famous people that the players will recognize. Every time we have a team meeting that week, he will talk about the theme for the week. He will start the meeting off by talking about that theme. That is what he starts out talking about that week in all of our team meetings. He starts addressing a lot of issues that are going to be important to your players, now and later, after they are out of the university. He stresses the cohesiveness of the team throughout the year. We have been tested. The last couple of years, we have been tested. We have had a few players get into some trouble. Men, let me tell you, if you have any of your football players get into trouble, it goes coast to coast in a flash. So, our unity and togetherness were tested a great deal the last two years. By addressing the issues, talking about them with your players, and telling them how you expect to operate, I think you have a chance to be successful.

Now I want to talk about the drills that we use with our running backs. These drills have been used over the years. We do not change our drills just because our players get bored. I bore my players to death with the drills we use. We have a very select number of drills that we use, but we get a tremendous number of repetitions on them in practice. Basically, we get daily repetitions on them. I think you can see the carryover value in the games by doing the drills daily. On each drill that I cover, I will show it on videotape, and I will show it to you used in a game film. You will see everything we do drill-wise. Also, I will go over our techniques as far as carrying the ball. I will cover the drills that we use in dealing with one man and the drills that we use when we are dealing with more than one man. Hopefully, I will have time to go over our blocking techniques.

I believe drills are designed for a couple of purposes. One, the drill should fit into what you are doing offensively. They should fit the type of plays you are going to run. I will show you a drill that helped us a great deal on the inside zone play. Another reason for drills is to develop some confidence in your backs about going into contact.

A lot of the players who come to us are ready for contact. They will run into anyone without hesitation. There are some who are not really sure, and they are not confident about the contact phase of the game. We do a lot of close-quarters, one-on-one type of drills. We do these drills at a fairly fast pace, and I feel that the repetitions will help build their confidence in going into contact.

If you are the tailback in our system, you are going to get the ball 25 to 30 times out of the 80 snaps we will have on offense. We can't have that back having any doubt about how he is going to operate in a game. We cannot have a timid player out there carrying the football. There has to be an attitude that is formed. There has to be an attitude that is developed within your backs. There are no collisions like the ones running backs have in football. This is where you get the major collisions. The linemen have collisions from two to three yards apart. But the running backs are going to have major collisions with defenders who have angles on them and are running full speed. There are no collisions like the ones they have. They must develop an attitude that they can perform at a high level. They have to be able to do it game after game and, basically, play after play.

We are trying to develop an attitude and a skill in the drills that we perform. We have a warm-up period at the start of practice. All of our players are out there. They go through a series of things in a 10-yard area. The do the high knees, carioca, and some other warmup-type drills. Then they do some partner stretching drills. After that, all of our players break up into group work.

All of the running backs know to come to the corner of the end zone with me. When they come over, they all grab a ball. Let me talk about that first. They all have a ball in their hands. We have them go over bags with a ball in their hands. The reason I do this is that if they are going to play for us, they are going to carry the ball a lot in the game. If the ball hits the ground, our chances of winning are not very good. I want our running backs thinking good ball technique right at the start of practice.

 





Page 207

Good ball technique to us is this: The finger is over the point of the ball. The elbow is ready to pin the back point of the ball to the rib cage if you are in the open field. If you are sprinting for a score, you can have great arm action. Anytime they are in a dangerous situation or at linebackers' level or stepping over a player, we ask them to roll the ball in front of their breast plate a little. What we are doing is removing the surface of the ball from the defender. Now the defense does not have anything to grab. The linebacker can get his hat on the ball, but it is not going anywhere. You cannot see the ball from behind. We never have two hands on the ball going through the line of scrimmage. We have one hand on the ball. You are not the same back when you run with two hands on the ball as you are with one hand on the ball, where you still have great balance. It is basically the same position or area where you would carry the ball with two hands, but we want only one hand on the ball in front of the chest plate. I can secure the ball here with one hand. I can control the ball from that position. Our backs get confident in this position. I am not going to tell you that every time our backs get the ball, they have the ball on the chest plate or tucked in on the side. Ideally, that is what you want to happen. That is not always going to be the case. We know that, but that is our job, to talk to the backs about good ball techniques. We remind them to roll the ball forward, keep it high and tight, and use some terms that they will be able to identify with. So we always start out our drills working on good ball techniques.

I always watch team work from behind the offense. If I see a lot of the ball from that position or if he is flagging the ball as he comes back to the huddle, I give him some reminders to keep the ball high and tight. I will suggest that they roll the ball forward a little more. I want to make positive remarks to them about ball techniques. I do not want to talk to them in negative terms. They are getting hit up about good ball technique all the way through practice, from the start of practice with drill work all the way up to team work. We were in the top five in the country in turnover ratio this year, and we run an option offense. Two years ago, we were pressing to be number one in turnover ratio in the country. This tells me that we do not put the ball on the ground that much. We take good care of the football. When you are running an option offense, you have a lot of concern about turnovers because there is a lot of risk in running the option game. If your backs start putting the ball on the ground, then you have major problems.

When they come to start the drills, we have 10 rectangular bags that are flat on the ground. The backs come over and get a football. The first thing we are going to do is to step over the bags using good ball technique. This drill takes at the most a minute and one half to go through, but we do not miss a day of this drill. We are going to get three or four things accomplished in this drill.

I had one coach visit our practice. He asked me what we did with our backs in our drills. Before I answered him, he told me that he did not like bag drills. He went on to tell me that he felt they were a waste of time. But I disagree. If you are a basketball player and you go three or four months without bouncing a basketball, you will be rusty when you come back to the game. It is the same as catching a football. If you are used to catching the football 20 to 30 times a day in practice, and then you take a month off, it will not be as natural to catch the football if you take a month off and then come back out to catch those passes. We think running with the football is the same.
