We go over the bags every day. We have them go through them three times. First, they step through each bag with one foot. I am not concerned about them getting from point A to point B as fast as they can. I am interested in high knees going over the bags. I am interested in ball techniques as they go through the bags. As soon as they go over that first bag, they get high knees, and that ball gets rolled forward in front of their breast plate. That is a dangerous situation at this point. That ball is high and tight. We are stepping over a man on the ground. I can show you on tape 30 or 40 times a season when our backs have to step over someone. It may be a defender, or it may be one of our players. We are working on ball techniques and high knees.

 





Page 208

As we get the ball up high, our vision is going to be focused down the field. I want them to look downfield and focus in on something. This is where peripheral vision comes into play. I want to see the field. I want to know where people are coming from. We do not want to be looking down at the bags we are stepping over. I want to work on my peripheral vision in the drill.

As they come over the last bag, we have a manager about 8 yards from the last bag. As the back comes over the last bag, he wants to beat the manager with a speed move. He must accelerate hard at an angle to beat the manager, who is simulating a safety. After he passes the manager, he accelerates up the field for another 7 to 8 yards. We are working on ball technique and high knees, and we are working on our vision. We work on a cut, and finally we are working on acceleration. I have seen drills where the players would get over that last dummy, turn, and jog back to the end of the line. We do not want that. The difference in your backs going for a long run may be a result of acceleration, so we work hard on this at the end of the bags. As soon as the back makes his cut, he accelerates as hard as he can. As soon as he has the man beat at the narrowest margin, he is straight up the field. He does not want to continue at an angle toward the sideline, where he gets pinned out of bounds on the 3- or 4-yard line. We want to try to beat the pursuit angles immediately after we beat the defender upfield with hard acceleration.

In the next drill, we turn sideways and go over the bags so that both feet hit between the bags. As we come off the last bag, I move the manager up about 4 yards from the last bag. I have to give him a head fake and a shoulder fake in one direction to get him leaning one way, and then I have to beat him. He is too close to beat him with a spin move.

On the third drill, we face the opposite direction stepping over the bags. I have the manager almost on top of the back as he comes off the last bag. The manager pops him with a push-back dummy. As the manager pops the back, he gets in a really tight spin move. We spin so that we gain ground on the spin move. We want to spin the ball away from the defender. We want to spin, gain ground, and get up the field. The reason we work on the spin move is this. There are times in a ball game when you have a breakdown up front. You get the ball, and the defense is right in your face. You do not have a chance to do anything else but spin and turn to try to get upfield.

The next day, we do three bag drills. This time we are going to do the stutter drill. This time we go over the bags and stutter our feet in between the bags as we cross the bags.

This drill teaches body control. When they first start this drill, they have problems. As they stutter the feet, the rest of their body is moving forward. That is the way most backs are. They get the ball and want to score. This helps control their body as they go over the bags.

The next time, we will stay sideways and step over the bag with both feet. On the next bag, we step over with one foot and then twist over the other bags.

The third time, we shuffle over the bags and then accelerate as we come off the bags. We do some bag work every day except the day before the game. These drills take only a couple of minutes.

The next thing we do is to go through what we call our three-step cuts. The reason we do this drill is because I want our backs to be upfield runners. I want them to think about making the quick, hard, sharp cut. I do not want a lot of stutter stepping. I want them to make the quick, hard move up the field. We set cones about four steps apart. We start off running three steps and then plant, three steps and plant, up and down the field. We want hard, quick, slashing moves.

 





Page 209

We do not want our backs getting caught going laterally. We want a slashing-type runner. We want to get upfield. We want a 2nd and 4 call instead of a 2nd and 9 call. We do this every day with our backs. That gets the idea in their mind of the type of runner we want. They go up through the cones and then back through the cones. That does not take but a minute or two.

Next, we go to what we call the balance drill. I think it is a great drill to get the backs used to having their hands on the ground. I want our backs to understand that they are backs who finish off runs. I want them to be able to come off the ground with that hand and to be able to accelerate as they come up, so we put them in that position. You will see this in the film of our drills.

We put them down with one hand on the ground. The arm extends down from the shoulder to the ground. It is not way out in front of them. I want as much pressure on the hand as is on our foot. I want the back leg up above the butt. We want to roll our eyes up high. I put them into this position for at least 15 seconds with a ball in their hand. On the ''hut,'' they hop forward for about a yard. They keep the same hand and same foot down. They go for a series of four or five of those moves, then they come up running out of there. That is what we call the balance drill. They must have the same hand and same foot down, for example, right hand and right foot. As they hop, they switch hands and feet. When they have the same foot and same hand down, it becomes much more difficult.

We tie that in with a gauntlet drill. We have a line of players form the gauntlet. The back starts out 5 or 6 yards in front of the gauntlet, does four or five balance moves, then accelerates into the gauntlet. The players try to knock the ball out of his hand. He must put the ball in front of his breast plate, high and tight. We never go through the gauntlet with two hands on the ball. As the back comes out of the gauntlet, I will be at the end of the line. He must make a move on me and then accelerate up the field again. We do this drill every Wednesday.

We also have the wheel drill. We have four dummies in a circle. The backs step over the dummy with a ball in their hands. The back will have one hand down on the ground. As he crosses the bag, he goes all the way around the bag and then goes to the next one. As he gets to the last bag, he comes out and accelerates for 8 to 10 yards.

We use the wheel drill to change things up with the balance drill. We do the balance drill most of the time, and we do the wheel drill occasionally, but we do not miss those three drills. We do the bags, three-step cuts, and the balance drill every day. We get through them in a short period of time.

Another thing that the balance drill does is give your backs the stretch in the shoulder and hip areas to get them ready for practice. I think it is a good way to start

 





Page 210

practice. We try to simulate game situations with what we are doing in these drills. The first 10 minutes of practice is just that; it is warm-up. When they come to me for drill work, they have to be ready to go. Some backs are notorious for wanting to stretch through half of practice. Some of them want to go through group work at half speed. Then when it comes time for team work, all of a sudden they are ready for practice. Our backs know when we go to drills, we are thinking of playing football. We are not thinking of it as continuing to warm up for team work. That may be an attitude problem that you may want to work on with your running backs.

Next we run a drill that we call react and cut. We get the same bags and stand them up. We have a manager holding those bags. We have a line of players behind the dummies with a ball. I will put a cone at the side of the bag and one down the field about 10 yards. We have the players come smoothly to the bag. They press that point of attack. Then the manager leans one bag one way or the other. The player shuffles away from the bag and accelerates through the hole. We do not want him to shuffle too wide.

This drill is working a little on our inside zone running play. On that play, we tell the back this: Smooth to the hole, press the point of attack. If he has an onside crease that he knows he can get to and make a big play, then he is to accelerate through the onside crease. If there is no onside crease, then he must think about taking the ball to the back side. It is imperative on the inside zone play that the back comes to the hole smoothly. They cannot be coming so fast that they cannot cut the play back to the back side. They must operate in a smooth fashion coming to the hole. What we are doing in the drill is forcing the back to find the backside crease. He has to find the hole, and then he has to accelerate through the hole. This drill ties in very well with our inside running game. We do not want them to come to the line of scrimmage and stutter-step. He finds the hole and works on acceleration.

We have one other drill that we do. We do not do this drill every day, but we make sure that we work on it a couple of times a week. We line up four cones in a straight line less than a yard apart. The back takes the ball in his right hand and starts toward the dummy. He shuffles through the first three cones. Then he sprints to the next cone and spins around the next cone. He puts the ball in the other hand. Then he accelerates to the next cone. We have a manager there with a hand dummy. He slams the back with the hand dummy. What we are doing is trying to make sure that we get every inch we can along that sideline.

Nothing bothers me more than to see a running back drift out of bounds when contact is made. We want to make sure that we get every inch we can on each play. We are working on the shuffle step, we are working on putting the hand down, we are working on switching the ball and accelerating, and we are working on getting every inch we can on the sideline. If you will notice, we are trying to combine two or more techniques into the drill.

 





Page 211

Another drill we call the jump drill. Most great backs have quick feet. They are not plodders. You do not hear them coming. They put the foot down, and they are gone. We put four bags down. We lay the bags in a Z-like design. The runner faces the first bag. He jumps over the first bag with both feet together. He keeps his shoulders parallel to the line of scrimmage. He jumps over the next bag sideways. We want him to go over the bags with light feet if possible. Then he jumps the next bag, again keeping the shoulders square. He jumps over the last bag and then accelerates through the cones.

At the end of the drill, we have two players with pushback dummies at the cones. The back comes off the last dummy, accelerates, and tries to split the two players with the dummies. They jam the runner to give him some resistance. He will lower his shoulder, split those two dummies, and then accelerate. We always finish the drill off with some kind of acceleration.

I want to show you the drills on tape. [Video]

In the off-season, we do a lot of jumping rope. We do a lot of rope skipping where we are moving. We do not do a lot of static rope jumping. We are working on getting our feet up and down as quickly as possible. We do not want the feet coming up high. We want really quick feet. We jump with two feet and then jump back across the line with one foot. We work on quick feet movement. Then we jump or skip sideways. First, we do one foot, then we do two feet sideways. Then we do both feet backwards.

Next I want to get to what we are doing in terms of contact drills and blocking drills. Our philosophy is no different from most others. If our back can make the defense miss him and he can run for a touchdown each time, that is great. But you and I know that is not the way football is. If you can make them miss and you can put it in the end zone, then do it. If you get pinned in with one man, we want to use the lift drill. If we are pinned on the sideline and we do not have the opportunity to cut back, we know that we are going to make contact with the defender, and we are going to look him up.

When we see that contact is obvious, we are going to look him up. We are going to turn back into the man. We are going to go look him up. We drop our arm and our fist as if we were going to plant it into the ground. We want to get our shoulder pad under the defensive man's shoulder pads. As we come into contact, we want to turn our hips in to the man. As contact is made, we want to come up with our elbow in tight and a really hard punching action with our upper body. We try to lift through the man and try to get yards after contact.

We keep a chart called "after-contact yards." We score them with a plus or a double plus. If our back makes contact with the defender and he gets 4 1/2 yards, we give him a plus YAC. The YAC is yards after contact. If he gets 7 or more yards, we give him a double-plus YAC. If he only gets 1 or 2 YAC, that is only average. We expect more. If he gets no yardage, he gets a minus. Our backs set a goal going into each game of how many YACs and how many double YACs they want to get in a game. They are thinking about breaking tackles. They are thinking about yards after contact.

To simulate the drill, we will put another running back down on all fours. The back with the ball will be 3 to 4 yards from him. On the sound of "hut," the defender comes up to simulate a tackler. The back turns and gets into the tackler. He makes slight contact and brings the fist up into punching action. He wants to work through the lift. I do not want to get underneath the man and punch up, I want to punch through. Punching through and at the right angle is where you are going to get the YAC. In the spring we do this drill every day. In the season we do it through Wednesday, making sure that they get at least one lift with each arm. The key is to get into the middle of the defender

 





Page 212

and not out on the shoulder pad. You have to turn back in to the man.

If you have more than one man to deal with, we just cover up with both hands on the football, get our pads lower than their pads, try to split the area, and try to get YAC. In our backs' minds, this is what they are thinking, and we get enough reps on it. If they can beat a defender with a one-on-one move, they do it. If they are dealing with only one man, they use the lift. If they are against more than one man, they do the blast. To teach the blast drill, sometimes we use the blaster. We will put two men with hand dummies in front of the blaster. If he can split the two dummies, then he will come on through the blaster. If he can't split the first two men with the dummies, he does a spin and heads back up the field. It is a great drill for backs to get their pads down and to develop good running angles. After we teach the drill, we run it at a high tempo.

We teach four blocks in our system. We have what we call an attack block, an attack-and-adjust, a pass pro block, and a cut block. It does not matter what they are doing on the field, but they should be using one of those four blocks when they are called on to block. This is true even when they are blocking out of the punting game. We are still an old-fashioned punting team. We still have two tight ends, two up backs, a fullback, and a punter. Our up backs set in the hole between the center and guards. If anyone comes in that gap, he attack-blocks him before he goes down to cover.

If you are on the kickoff return team and you are the fullback, you come up and block using what we call an attack-and-adjust block. If you are on a special team, one of those four blocks will come into play.

An attack block is when the ball carrier is directly behind us and the back is going to break off our block. They are used on power and iso plays. We want to execute the block by running in a natural motion. We do not want the arms swinging hard. We do not want the feet to be in a wide base. We want the feet up underneath us. Again, we want to make sure that our pad level is under the defender's pad level as we do the striking. We do not snap the hips up on the striking. All that does is to raise you up and bring your pad level up. We never snap the hips forward on the contact.

It is one thing to block a defender who is standing still and another thing to block a linebacker who is moving. We start out working on our pass pro block. We have each man take three blocks, and then on the fourth block I will critique each individual. It is a quick drill. We go to the attack block after that. Each player gets four blocks each.

I talked to you about the attack block. The attack-and-adjust works this way. As the blocker approaches the defender, the defender shuffles a couple of quick steps. Now the blocker must adjust his body by shuffling. He get his hips squared up with the defender and then attack-blocks him. The key is to get the feet underneath them and get back in front of the defender. After he adjusts his path, it becomes an attack block.

I will take you through the different running and blocking drills on the tape. [Video]

On the blocking phase of the film, there was some good blocking. You are not going to knock people down all of the time, and you are not going to win all of the battles. They have to understand that they do not win every battle on the football field. If they are trained right in terms of your drills and techniques, you have a chance to win most of your games. The key is to have your backs go into the game with a tremendous amount of confidence. If you are a running back in our system, you are really involved in play after play. I want them confident about their technique and confident about their ability in terms of going into the contact phase of the game.

I want to thank the high school coaches because you do a great job. We have to depend on your films a lot. We have 38 states that we cover, and we do not see everyone. But where there is a player, we try to see him. We appreciate the high school coaches for their cooperation. If there is anything we can do for you, let us hear from you. You are welcome to come to visit us. Thank you very much. I appreciate being here.

 





Page 213

Fred Von Appen

Concentration and Finish

University of Hawaii

There is one thing Blaine Gaison failed to mention in the introduction. If we do not get our ass moving in the right direction, we will be looking for a new job soon. I appreciate your supporting the Nike Clinic. It always amazes me that football coaches would be here this late to hear a lecture. It is after 9 P.M., and we still have a good crowd remaining in place to listen to a 210 coach. The coach is up here because he is trying to pay a debt back to Nike for shoes for the team. It amazes me that you would sit in here this late. We have locked the doors, so you can't leave if you wanted to anyway.

Frank Solich of Nebraska just spoke. I listened to his lecture. I have been on the other end of five Cornhuskers games, and I can assure you that they knock your ass down. Frank said they don't run over people, but they do.

One thing that amazes me is that you guys will pay your money to come to a clinic, come from a neighbor island, or drive across the state to come to listen to someone lecture on football just to improve your vocation. That is unique to this business. It makes you unique. You are listening to a 2-10 coach speak at this time of night. I appreciate the opportunity to swap ideas about football with you. I appreciate your participating in this clinic and in a game that is very special to all of us. It is a time in our society where we represent the last bastion of discipline in that society in many respects. If we do not kick them in the rear and straighten them out and give them some direction, I do not know who in the schools are going to in this day and age. Looming litigation keeps the school administrators from doing it, and classroom teachers will not do it, so coaches are the last hope. If you are not setting the example in private and public schools, I do not know who is going to. Young people need more guidance and direction today than they ever needed. The challenges in coaching are greater than they have ever been before. That is because young people today are being reared differently. There are a lot of temptations out there today. There are things other than football for them to do.

Football is a difficult sport to play in that it is not a natural thing. Practicing football is not always pleasant. There is a lot of tedium and monotony in it. It challenges us to stay on top of the game and to give them plenty of guidance and direction.

I am going to talk about the role of concentration and finish. We have to get it right because my contract is running out soon.

This husband and wife went out on the golf course to play a round of golf. He had to hit from the back tee each time, and she would tee off from the ladies tee that was closer. On the fourth hole, he teed his Titlist ball up, and-bang!-he hits his wife right in the head. Mrs. Liebusch goes down, and she does not get up. He calls the EMS, and a doctor came and pronounced her dead right there. He felt horrible about what had happened. They decided that they would have to do an autopsy. They did the autopsy and called Mr. Liebusch up to discuss the autopsy. This is what the doctors said: "Mr. Liebusch, we found out that when the Titlist golf ball struck your wife in the head, she sustained a skull fracture. As a result of it, she died. However, the puzzling part of this whole thing is that we found a Dunlop golf ball in her anus." Mr. Liebusch said, "That is not very puzzling to me. That was my Mulligan."

I want to talk to you about our approach to our philosophy, administration, and organization. Before we can get anything done in football, we feel we must be able to capture our players' concentration and give them a concept of finish. So much of what we do requires that we have total concentration by our players. How do you do that? How do you build a concept of finish? Everyone knows that you have to

 





Page 214

start things, but frequently you do not know how to finish them. One of the things that helped us when I was with the 49ers when we were successful was this. Our players knew how to play to a standard of play, they knew how to concentrate through out, and they knew how to finish.

There are three musts for our program to be successful. First, we must be able to teach and maximize concentration for our athletes. Second, they must know how to practice with intensity. Practice basically is a dress rehearsal for the game. Practicing is critically important. In our sport, in this day and age, we only have 20 hours to prepare our athletes. Three of those hours are devoted to the game, so we have only 17 hours to practice. If we do not maximize their concentration, get them to practice with intensity, and build some concept of finish, then we are in trouble.

One of the places we start is in our team meetings. Let me stress the importance that we put on the reaction to the meeting setting. We tell our players that if we have a meeting, there is a reason for it. If we step before you, we have something of importance that is germane to the sport that we need to get across to you. It is imperative that we get your attention. I have neatness as the number one thing. We are talking about conduct and decorum in the meeting setting. If you do not get what you ask for in that setting, you are not going to get it on the practice field.

It all starts with meeting and how you practice. That will determine how you are going to play in the game. When you come into a meeting, the room is going to look a certain way. When you leave, it should look the same way. We do not want a lot of spit cups, apple cores, or other garbage left behind. If we had the room set up a certain way, we want you to leave it that way. It is the same in the dressing room. We cannot have jocks, shoes, helmets, cups, and other things left all over the locker room. We are going to police the area. Basically, it is a reflection of the players' attitude. If we do not police it properly and pay attention to it, then we probably are not getting the results in other areas as well. Football transcends the field. It goes into the locker room, the training room, the weight room, and the meeting room that we use.

When we go into a meeting room, we expect the players to come in and seat themselves, put their feet on the floor, sit up straight, rivet their eyes on the speaker, and listen and hear. There is a difference. Those of you who are married know that your wives insist that you do not know the difference in listening and hearing. "You hear, but you do not listen!" Kids are great at this. They must give up their eyes. Posture tells us something about their learning attitude. It should be no different from the classroom sitting. We expect our players to sit up. We do not want them to put their feet up on the chair in front of them. We do not expect them to drape their feet over the shoulders of the man in front of them. We do not want them in a posture that is not receptive to learning, so, "Park your rear end in the chair, sit up straight in the saddle, rivet your eyes on the speaker, listen and hear, look and see." You are not guaranteed that you are getting the player's attention all of the time just because he is looking at you or just because he appears to be hearing you. He may be daydreaming about another coed somewhere on campus, but you will not know. But you have a chance, and it is important that you police that chance. That is part of our responsibility. If I have a player whose eyes are wandering around the room, he may be looking at something else, and he may be asleep.

Neatness is another thing that is important. It should not look as if the volunteer fire department just responded to a call after the meeting is over. We reset the chairs, pick up the garbage, and put the room back as it was. Then they can get their happy butts out to practice. During that setting, they should have given us their eyes and ears. It is important that we police that and get it. You get what you ask for. Anytime you are in a meeting setting and you are not comfortable with that setting, you are the one at fault, not the players. If the players' eyes are not on you, get their damn eyes on you. If you do not think a player is listening, get his attention. Turn the volume up, and get his attention. It is important not to lose any one of those swinging Richards in that room during that meeting. What you are imparting in that meeting they are going to put into practical application on the field a little later. It is important that you get what you want in that meeting. It is the same way on the field. Make

 





Page 215

certain that what you are asking for you are getting. That is your responsibility to your program, school, and the players. You are dealing with a collective group, but you are dealing with them one at a time.

Locker room, training rooms, and offices are areas where there should be a certain decorum. There is a certain demeanor that the players should assume. We do not want the training room to be a place where players just hang out. Coaches need to pass through the training room to see how things are going. "How is that ingrown hair on your butt doing, Mortimer? Is it getting better? Hey, you can't afford to miss any more practice, pal. You need to get out there and go to work."

When players come to your office, they need to feel that the door is open to them. It needs to be a place that is warm and receptive, but it comes time when you must take care of business. "What can I do for you?" Get the problem out on the table, and then get him out of there. It should not be a place where they lounge around.

Locker room settings can be interesting places. They can become sociological pockets. One thing that we did when I was at San Francisco with Coach Walsh was to go through the locker rooms, both pre- and postpractice, to see how things were going. Every once in a while, you have this little pocket of players that may be planning a coup d'état with your squad. You need to walk right in the middle of that and spoil t. There can't be any sanctuary for the players. They need to know that you are every place. They need to know that you are interested in how they are doing. "Is everything going OK up on campus?" They need to see you as someone other than a policeman of their rights and wrongs on the football field. I think it is important for the coach to pass through the locker room, not only to police the area, but to take a look to see what is going on. Some of you may be surprised at what goes on in some locker rooms. There may be a cure for AIDS in some of these lockers. If you do not get by and take a look at them once in a while, the players will think you do not care and will not make an effort to keep the place neat. Now, do not misunderstand-we do not have standby inspections. But players notice if you come around or if you care how things look. We need to take a look to see how things are going. If you don't, they start to think you do not care. That will start to reflect on the attitude of your team that you do not want to have. How they behave in the training room, how they treat the training-room people and equipment people, how they treat one another, and how they treat the facilities you have are important. Ours need improving, and I am sure most of yours do too. But what you have is what you have. Until it gets improved, it is the best that you have, and their attitude is reflected in how the players take care of it.

It is important for the coach to be present in all areas of the program. They need to understand that there is a modus operandi in the locker room, training room, meeting room, offices, and on the field. This is all part of capturing that concentration. It is all part of building the finish to the football team.

There is always danger in rumors and innuendos. You can defuse a lot of that by being present. You can learn a lot by taking 15 minutes to walk through the locker room after practice. "How did it go today, Mortimer? Are the towels soft enough? How is the soap?" When you get around in the locker room and visit with them, they know you care.

Football is a family affair. A lot of things that go on with a football team are confidential. We have guys, and they have them in pro football, that will call home with a problem. "He called me an SOB in practice." Now, we do not want to do that, and it may happen, but you would like to keep some things as confidential as possible. You do that by talking to them in this manner. "This is a manly sport, and we interchange on a manly level. We want to keep things within the family setting." Meetings are very important, and that is why we have them. We do not want to call meetings just to be meeting. We want to keep them short, concise, and to the point. We do not want to waste their time, and we do not want them to waste our time. If we have a meeting, we have something to tell them. When we are through with the meeting, we want to break. We always want to finish the meeting with a special flare. You may think this is corny; we think it is important.

 





Page 216

We break all meetings just as we break the huddle. When we have finished, we say, "Ready, break." We all break and get out of the room. They should get sucked out of the room just as if there was a vacuum pulling them out of the room. They may visit for a minute, but they should move on to the next venue, which is probably the practice field. The sooner they can get out there, the better off you are. You only have a limited amount of time.

We want to be ahead of time, not on time. What is on time? If time were not important to us, we would not have clocks. We have clocks and calendars. If it is important, they need to be on time and ahead of time.

Next, I want to talk about systems, styles, and standards of play. A premium is placed on explosive movement and finish in football. This is true for offense or defense. It is the same with the kicking game. We need to be explosive, and we need to know how to finish. What is finish? How do you practice finish? You have to build this into your practices. It is as simple as that. People do not naturally finish things. They do not finish things in the classroom or any other venues that we have talked about unless you insist that they finish them.

We play a lot of situational football. So do you. Everyone does in this day and age. That is true for special teams, offense, or defense. In our concept of football, we are going to play a lot of players. Last year we had to. Some of them did not want to stay on the field. "Get your butt back out there! I don't care, we are behind. You stay out there and play." We have a lot of guys to play. We think that is healthy. We think it is healthy for the general mental health of our football team. Situational football gives you an opportunity to use a lot of players. You can build them a role. When you do that, they are less inclined to detach from your program or feel as if they are not making a viable contribution. Everyone wants simplicity and flexibility. Those are by-words in football. The good coaches are the ones who can simplify things and take the complexity out of things. They reduce it to the simplest terms and impart it to their players. Timing is of the essence. Time is of the essence in the game, of the essence in preparation.

We do not compromise on work ethics. We stress intensity and finish. We do not compromise work ethic at all. We can't afford to, even if we were a great team-and we all know that we were not-but if we were a great team, and I have been around some great teams. The difference in two teams' playing is that the great team knows how to maintain concentration throughout and finish what they start. They have to practice those things. They have to understand the importance of those things. These points do not come naturally. It isn't natural to finish. It isn't natural to practice hard and to work hard in the practice setting. That is a dress rehearsal. Practice should be harder than the game. We want machinelike proficiency. We must be concise. Unit play is critically important. The team comes first. There is strength in numbers. The team will always come first. Any player who insists on a guaranteed start is taking the first step in getting put out of our program. There are no start guarantees in our business. You are a better team when you have competition between positional candidates. If someone wants a start guarantee, that is not the spirit of the business.

The team always comes first. It always has and always will. That is the thing that is unique about the game of football. We are the only sport that plays with that many people. You may have between 75 and 110 players on your squad. We have between 105 and 108, counting the walk-ons. We have 85 on scholarship. It is a unique game. That is a lot of people to manage and to get to concentrate and finish collectively. It is a lot of players to get to play together as a team. They must put the team as the number one priority and not themselves. It is important for you to home in on that and for your players to understand that.

When you have a meeting and you feel uncomfortable with one of the players, get that crap straight right away. "What is the problem, Mortimer? You have a funny look on your face. You look like you just had a crap sandwich and did not like the taste. We can't have that. I can't talk to you about line stunts with that look on your face. So, what the hell is the matter?" We will sit down and talk about it. I have to find out what the problem is.

 





Page 217

Our edge should be in tactics, in our belief, in our intensity, and in our mental acuity. It all has to be built in, once again in meetings and practice. You have to draw their attention. They must be able to maintain concentration and finish.

We believe in a starting rotation concept in our program. If you come out and practice hard and practice to the extent we expect of you, you should have a right to feel that you are going to be able to function in some capacity on our football team. You are going to be in the starting rotation. Now, we know the program indicates that 11 players will start. That is fine. But that is not the way it shakes out for us. We are a better football team if we play with 15 or 18, but not at the same time. The more people we can play on one side of the ball, the better we feel we can be. The more players you play, the more it will affect the stamina and the tempo of your team. It is also going to affect the mental health of your team. You may say that Mort can only go to his left. Well, put him in the game and let him go left, and then take him out. Put a guy that can go over, right, and left. Then put Mort back in at another time. Mort will feel good about himself, you will feel good about playing Mort, his folks will be happy, and all of his relatives will buy tickets. Now Mort will not go over and park his butt on the bench and detach from the game. He will not be able to afford to, because the next time it is 3rd and 1, his happy butt may be back out there going to the left again. It is important for the starting rotation concept to kick in for us.

If you invest, you play. That does not mean that you play all of the time. I did not say that. If you invest, you play. How much you play may be related to how outflanked you are. If you invest, you play, and we have a deal. There is nothing worse than the player who goes over on the sideline and tells others that he is not going to play. What does he do? They are looking up in the stands and waving at people. They are listening to the band and checking the hindquarters of the rally squad. They are not involved in the game at all. You tell them to get into the game, and they want to know where to line up. They are not ready to play. They are the first players to bitch about not playing after the game. Don't let them detach. I would rather have a lesser athlete in the game who is fresh than a premium athlete who is exhausted. We would rather play with 14 to 15 men.

If you play, you have accountability. We had a tough year last year. We were not a very good team. We had some great kids, and they played hard and worked hard. If they invested in practice, they got to play. They were always accountable. They did not complain about a lot of things. They knew that if we came up short in a game, the coaches were accountable, and so were they. It was an agonizing year. We were able to get through the year because they believed that they would get an opportunity to play and contribute. They knew by their involvement that they were going to be held accountable.

In talking about finish, think about this. No matter what you are doing in a practice session, you need to develop a finish. I learned this a long time ago, when I was at Virginia Tech. I was coaching the offensive line. We brought in a coach by the name of Phil Cutchins, who was the head coach at Oklahoma State, who got fired. It happens to us all sooner or later. Phil went into the banking business and was happy as hell. But our head coach brought Phil in to observe our practices because he knew football. He watched us practice as individuals and looked at our blocking schemes. The thing I got from one of those sessions with Phil was this: "Be careful what you say to your players." He said to talk to them about bouncing off the ground, not getting off the ground. Talk to players about exploding their eyes and hands. That has a more visual concept to it than just "Put your hands here." Get off the ground! Hurry up! Be quick, but don't hurry! There are a lot of word pictures that you can paint for your players that are important.

We apply this to the concept of finish. We try to finish every drill. We want them to get the idea that they do not gradually stop, they do not walk. The reason we came to this concept was this. If you go out and watch other teams practice, you will see a lot of players standing around doing nothing. Only a few players are being coached. The important thing about the time constraints is that we cannot afford to have anyone not being coached. If we have eight men in a line and

 





Page 218

one man on a sled, then that man is getting the hell coached out of him. We have eight men in a line and one working. Are the men standing in line in a manner that denotes that they are maintaining concentration? Are they allowing themselves to be coached even though they are not physically taking a turn? There needs to be some methodology for how they stand in line. Do you want them in line as the military does? Do you just want to make certain that they are watching the man taking the turn? When he is being coached, they are in turn being coached. The others should learn from any mistake he makes. They should learn from anything positive he does. If you do not pay attention to their concentration and finish and its relationship to the drills, you will have eight players do different things. It is our job to home in on concentration and finish.

As soon as the player concludes with the sled, he does something to finish the drill. He doesn't just slam the sled and work his feet, you blow the whistle and tell him to stop working his feet, then he walks his happy butt back to the end of the line. The others in line are watching that event. He is not getting any finish out of that. If that happens, you have really lost the essence of what you are trying to get accomplished in the drill. In everything they do, we make a big deal that there must be a finish to it. We do not want to glide or walk away from a drill setting. What is a drill? It is a microcosm of the game. You are taking some segment of the game out, and you are working on it. I want to be certain that if I am coaching Mort on the sled, the other guys in line are paying attention to what I said. As I work Mort, I want to look at them to see whether they got the right idea. I do not want to repeat everything I said to Mort to each player.

The same thing happens in a meeting. You need to make certain that you are finishing the meeting the way you want to. Did I capture their concentration? I don't know, because I can't look inside of their heads. But if they are looking at me and it appears that they are listening, then we have a chance. That is all we ever want in this sport anyway. I have a chance to hold that man. Now, when he goes out on the field and puts that into practical application, I will see whether he learned it or not. I want to make certain that if I can only coach one or two at a time, the other guys are homed in on what we are doing. You get the point.

These are points that we can expect from one another. It needs to be clear-cut to the players what they can expect from the coaches and what we expect of them. Put it down. It can be a list of 10 things or 20 things, whatever. We reduce our list to as few as we can and then tell them that this is what we expect of them. "This is what you can expect of us."

What can the players expect from the coaches? We should be consistent. We are going to expect them to meet a certain standard of play, concentrate, and finish strong in everything we are doing. We are not going to relax that standard for a player just because he is a talented player or because we are doing something that may not seem to be important. It is all important, or we would not do it.

They must feel the difference between urgency and whatever you use to caricature your football team. You do not want a player to be a fanatic. That is not a good posture. He needs to be urgent. It means that a thing is sudden, and it is happening right now. "Let's make this thing urgent." It is an urgent proposition. It needs to be done at a certain tempo. We have prescribed the tempo, and it has to go that way. As coaches, we are not paid to be satisfied. If we won a game 75-0, we would still find something to gripe about. We are trying to play to a special standard.

We are going to give them leadership and direction. It is going to be firm, and it is going to be on our terms. It is going to be with the team orientation in mind. We want to create an open dialogue. If you have something stuck in your butt, come in, and let's talk about it. We try to find out what the problem is.

Coaches are accountable. If you pick up the paper, you will find out that we are accountable. They should expect us to have technical command of what we do. We should know what the game is about. We do not have all of the answers, but we do have a lot of them. We have to respect the player for what he is going

 





Page 219

through. It is not going to be an easy street. We respect the fact that they are involved in a demanding sport. We must have a sense of humor. You bet!

What do we expect from players? They must be urgent. They must concentrate. We have to work to get them to concentrate. They have to cooperate in the concentration effort. Some of them may say, ''Why do we need to concentrate?'' Some players have a tough time concentrating. You need a mechanism to help them concentrate. Get those things in a formula, and you may have a chance.

We expect full compliance. "Why did you do it that way? How come you called that play?" "Because." "Get your butt in there and block." They need to have a will to prepare. Everyone wants to play in the games. Are they willing to prepare to play the games? Preparing is a bitch; at least it should be. They should think the game is a lark.

They must be accountable. They need to commit to something. This is tough for the youth of today. They must be willing to play a role. "What is the best role for you on this team? What do you think it is?" No; it isn't that. Here is what we think it is. We try to work with the players, but sometimes they will not be satisfied with what we decide. Coaches coach and players play and that is it, unless you are in the NBA. That is one of the reasons I only coached in the NFL for eight years. I have coached at every level except Pop Warner, and that may still be coming. This doesn't make me an expert on anything. One of the reasons I stepped away from the NFL was this. It was fun, but I did not make as much money as the players. That did not make sense to me. You have players who are making a million dollars, and you are only making 65 thousand or something like that as a coach. You have to understand that you are responsible for directing the traffic, and that is the way it is.

Finish. Put a circle around 
finish. 
This means that our players will do something special when they get to the end of a drill. He may sprint to a cone, do a forward shoulder roll, then get up and run to the end of the line. He is going to do something special to finish that drill. He does not just step over the large bags and walk back to the end of the line. He is going to move. We tell them that we want them to run or stand still-no walking. We can go out on the street to walk. They are young, and so they can run.

 [Editor's note. Coach Von Appen went on to discuss th
e 
role of concentration and finish in relation to the pass rush. However, due to technical difficulties, we were unable to reproduce the last part of the lecture.]

 





Page 220

Tommy West

Developing a Philosophy and a System

Clemson University

The first thing I want to talk about is what we have done at Clemson. We have found over the years that, regardless of what you do, you had better have a plan. You had better stick to your plan. We have developed some things at Clemson that we stick to. We have an overall philosophy, we have a football philosophy, and then we have a system. Our philosophies are set in stone. They do not change. We will always keep those philosophies. Our system will change.

When we first started at Clemson, our staff started talking about philosophy. We said that we were going to build upon a philosophy that will win. That is the bottom line: We wanted results. We decided to break down the philosophy into two areas. We have an overall philosophy and a football philosophy. We all know what wins. Generally, about 90 percent of the time, the teams with the best players win. We have an advantage over you in that we can recruit. However, at times, that may not be an advantage. We all know the best players win. What if you do not have the best players? You cannot just throw in the towel and say that you do not have players as good as the opponents. In our overall development, we have developed our philosophy so that talent does not have anything to do with winning.

The second part of our philosophy is that we feel we must sell our program to the players. Our players must believe in the overall and football philosophies. We started from day one three years ago selling our philosophy of how we were going to win games and eventually win our conference.

The third part was to be committed to the philosophy. I feel this point is critical. You and your staff have to be totally committed to the way you are going to win. That may sound good, but we really are committed. It does not matter whether you are the head coach, assistant coach, trainer, or manager, you have to be totally committed and understand what our philosophies are. "This is how we are going to do things, and this is how we are going to win football games." People in our program know our philosophy, are totally committed, and believe that we will win. Our team believes they will win.

In our overall philosophy, we said our first goal is toughness. That is number one in our overall philosophy. I will tell you why it is that way. I truly believe that this game has never changed. The game that we have been a part of all of our lives and the game that we love has never changed. It has never changed. People have changed, players have changed, coaches have changed, but our game has never changed. It is still a game of blocking, tackling, and fundamentals. There is no way around toughness in football. There may be in some other sports. We are in the only sport where contact is not a penalty. In our sport when two players knock the heck out of each other, the fans stand and cheer. In any other sport, it is a foul or penalty. There is no way around toughness in our game. We preach to our team that we are going to outhit our opponents for four quarters. That is a big point for us. This does not guarantee that we are going to win the game. This is only going to guarantee us a chance to win. That is the mental part of the game. We talk about mental toughness and physical toughness. We preach this every day. That is how we are going to win. Toughness is a key part of our game and is what we believe in.

I believe toughness is a part of consistency. If your kids are tough and they believe that they are tough, they will practice that way. I think that breeds consistency. You do not have games where you are up high for one game and down low for the next game. If you are tough and your kids believe it, I think it breeds consistency. Toughness is number one in our program.

The second point in our overall philosophy is conditioning. We are totally committed to conditioning as a staff and as a team. Our team believes in conditioning. They did not believe in it at first, I can

 





Page 221

promise you that. They thought the staff was about half crazy. If I am going to ask you to hit for four quarters, you cannot wear down at the end and be successful. We have to be the best-conditioned team. If we are going to be the most physical team in our conference, then we have to be the best-conditioned team. We continue to tell our players the value of conditioning. We tell them that we will be there in the fourth quarter.

The third phase is the attitude part. We feel that toughness is somewhat of an attitude. Conditioning! You have to have the right attitude for conditioning. We talk a lot about enjoying what we do. I enjoy coaching. That is what I do best, and that is what I enjoy. At our first team meeting, I went in front of the team and asked them what they do best. No one said a word. I asked them again. Again, no one said anything. I said, "If I hit it, raise your hand. Cook? Sew? Rake leaves? Play football?" When I said "play football," some hands went up in the air. I said, "Everyone who thinks playing football is what they do best, raise your hand.'' Everyone in the room raised their hand. I assured them that they were right. "That is what you do best.'' If you cannot enjoy what you do best, then you will be a miserable person. Everyone does something well. Everyone does something better than other things. I sold them on the idea of doing what we do best. We enjoy coaching football and playing football. If you do not enjoy it, you should get out of it. We talked a lot about the attitude of it.

I feel that our overall philosophy will never change. We all know that the team with the best players usually wins. How talented do you have to be to be tough? You do not have to be the most talented to be tough. How talented do you have to be to be the best-conditioned player on the field? It does not take talent to be in shape. You do not have to run a 4.4 to be in shape. You do not have to be able to bench-press 400 pounds to be in condition. How talented do you have to be to have a good attitude? How talented do you have to be to enjoy your work? How talented do you have to be to put in a day's work? That does not have anything to do with talent. You can see why the overall philosophy is set the way it is.

We are not the most talented team in our league. Florida State is the most talented team in our league. How are we going to catch Florida State? Get better players than they get? Well, good luck. They are going to be number one, two, or three in the country every year. Their recruiting class this year was rated number one in the country. What if you can't recruit better players than Florida State? Are you just going to play for second place in the league each year? I do not believe in doing that. We have to find a way to overcome them. We have to be tougher, be in better condition, and have a better attitude than they have. Sooner or later that may give us a chance to beat Florida State. I feel that we have closed the gap some recently. The gap when we got there was a 57-0 defeat the year before. The gap is closing, but we are not there. The overall philosophy is set. Again, those philosophies will not change.

Now we get to our football philosophy. First, we want to run the football. We are totally committed to the run. We totally believe in the run. We have to sell this to our program, to our players, and we have to have them committed to this. We have to be able to run the football. It matches with toughness, conditioning, and attitude. That is what we have to do.

It is tough to stop the run in practice if you do not see it in practice. I have coached in places where we did not run the football. We have to be able to stop the run. Those are two things that we spend a tremendous amount of time with.

The third thing that is a part of our football philosophy is that we must be on the plus side on turnovers. We have broken this down in the three years that we have been at Clemson. We have lost one football game where we won the rushing game and were on the plus side on turnovers. We have lost only one game when we could win the rushing game and the turnover game. That was the game at Duke my first year, when we got a punt blocked for a touchdown that beat us. In our system this works. If we do not win the rushing game or we do not win the turnover game, then we have a hard time winning. That is our football philosophy, and that is it. That does not mean that we

 





Page 222

do not want to throw the football. We start out by building the running game.

Now, we are in a position where hopefully we can move forward. We have stuck with our system the past three years. In our second year there, we were fourth in the country in rushing the football. This year we were about 14th. This year we were between 10th and 15th. The first year at Clemson, we led the nation in turnover ratio. We have been in the top 15 in the nation in that category each year. We spend a lot of time working on holding on to the football on offense and trying to knock it loose when we are on defense.

If you will go back and look at all of the teams through the years that were successful, you will find that they all ran the football. When I was growing up, the winning teams were Michigan, Ohio State, Texas, Oklahoma, Nebraska, and Alabama. They all ran the football. At that time, a lot of them were wishbone teams. They were all able to run the football. They all played good defense. They were all on the plus side on turnovers. They were consistently good.

Let me give you some great stats from the NFL. They are from 1995. If your quarterback threw for over 300 yards, you would win 47 percent of the time. If he threw for over 400 yards, you would win 52 percent of the time. If you had a 100-yard rusher in the game, you would win 89 percent of the time. If you just ran the ball 40 times, you won 92 percent of the time. Those are impressive stats to me.

In our conference in 1995, it was amazing how close our stats were to the NFL. In our league, if you passed for 200 yards, you won 52 percent of the time. If you passed for 300 yards, you still won only 52 percent of the time. If you rushed the ball for over 200 yards in our league in 1995, you won 94 percent of the time. If you rushed for under 200 yards per game, you only won 26 percent of the time. Again, those are convincing stats.

After we leave our philosophy, we get into what we call our system. This is true for the offense, defense, or kicking game. It must fit our philosophy. We feel the system must be flexible. A lot of things dictate what we do in our system. Some teams have systems, and their personnel must fit their system. We are not really that way. We take our personnel and make it fit within our philosophy. It must fit our team. In our system now, we have more of a power game. That is because of the tailback that we have. He is not a slashing type of runner. We have to take our quarterback and do what is best for him. We try to tailor our system to fit our talent. Again, our system must fit our philosophy. We must be able to run the ball. We must be flexible. We must be willing to change the system at any time during the year to fit our team.

In our offensive system for our running game, the first thing we want is the power game. We want to be able to take the fullback out of the I formation and go straight at the defense. We want to be able to take the tailback out of the I formation and go straight at the defense. This is where we start. We start with just a simple belly play to our fullback and a simple power play to our tailback.

The second part of our running game is that we want to run the option game. We are not an option team, but we will always have an option in our attack. We will always have one option play, regardless of our offense. If a team does not have an option in their system, the defense can do a lot of different things. They can play unsoundly and get by with it. If the option does show up, the defense must be accountable for the dive, the quarterback, and the pitch. It only takes one time for the option to burn a defense. We will always have an option play. We keep it very simple. We do not read the option, but we always have one option. When the defense breaks us down on Sunday, they are going to see an option play.

The third part of our system is that we run the misdirection. That play can be a number of things for us. The reverse may be the misdirection play for us. We use different things for our misdirection in our offensive system. We may use the naked reverse and the bootleg plays as our misdirection.

In our running game, we face the 4-3 defense. We are not a 4-3 team; we are a 3-4 defense. Most people in college football have gone to the 4-3 defensive

 





Page 223

scheme. We see this about every week. We may see some eight-man fronts. Everything in our running game is set against the 4-3 defense. We believe that the teams will take their best players and put them at defensive ends. They place them there where they can get up the field and cause problems for the offense. They match up their most talented player against our least talented player. That would be our offensive tackle. We have tried to come up with a system where we do not have to put our worst player up against their best player.

We are a formation team; we are not a motion team. I will show you what we try to do with our formation. We are basically not a tight-end team unless we are going to come into a power game. We go three wide and four wide to run the ball. We want to spread the defense out. We are three wide or four wide, depending on which linebacker we want to get rid of. We know if we go three wide, we can remove the Sam linebacker and make him play the strong safety. If we go four wide, we can make the Will linebacker play the eight-man front outside. We may want the Will linebacker to stay in against our running game because we think our fullback is better. What we look for is match-ups. If we can't match up with one of the linebackers, we will take a wideout and move him outside. That is the basis of what we do in the running game.

BASE FORMATION

THREE WIDEOUTS

FOUR WIDEOUTS

TRIPS

I want to show you what we have done with a couple of plays. We took these two plays and made a series out of them. It is the same old belly play that we have all run for years and years. Against the 4-3 defense, we feel that we have had an effect. What we do differently is this. We block the play the same way most of you do. The thing we do differently is to send our tailback opposite. For years, the belly play was off the sweep with full flow to the play side. We send the tailback opposite for two reasons. We are trying to hang the Will linebacker. We would love for him to be keying the tailback on the play. If we can get him to do that, we have a good play. His alignment will determine the block of our tackle. When teams true-stack against us, our tackle can shift through the 5 technique and get to the Will linebacker. Again, it is not a fullflow play.

32 BELLYREGULAR

32 BELLYTWO WIDEOUTS

 





Page 224

32 BELLYFOUR WIDEOUTS

We come back and run the option game off the belly play. Our quarterback will turn out and make a quick reverse. We will run the belly until we feel that we can get the tackle on the Will linebacker. We do not have to block the 5 technique on either of these plays. All that our option game is on the back side is a sweep without the fullback. Now, we do not have to reach a 5 technique who is a better athlete than we have to block him. The ball is going to be pitched, so we do not have to block the tackle. We want to get our tailback out in the alley. We will get to the Will or Sam linebacker by formation.

If we can't get to him, we can go to three wide receivers. We can move the tight end outside and get the Sam linebacker to come outside. We can get leverage on Sam to get the ball outside if we want to run the play to the strong side.

If we want to run the play to the weak side but we can't run it weak, we will run it out of four wide. Now we have a wide receiver or our fullback with leverage on the Will linebacker.

We can also run it out of a trips set. That will guarantee that we will get leverage on the linebackers.

19 BY FORMATION

19FOUR WIDEOUTS

19ONE BACK

We do run the combo with the play. We can call 32 or 19, especially when we are going out of trips when they start kicking the defense over. We may take it out the back side. What we want to be able to do is to get our tackle on the first inside linebacker. If we can't do that, then we have to get into a formation where we can get him. We can use the wideout, we can use our fullback, or we can use the tight end to get to the inside linebacker. It will clear itself up more if I can get this videotape going.

COMBO EXAMPLE: 3219 CALL 1919 AT LINE

COMBO EXAMPLE: 1819

 





Page 225

Off this play we had the fullback belly, which was a power play straight at the defense. Then we put the option play in off the belly play. We felt that we could get into formations and have an advantage blocking. We would not have to block the 5 technique. Then we do it one step beyond by adding a reverse play off the option as a misdirection play. It is a simple game that we feel gave us an advantage with leverage blocking.

That is how we attack the defense. We are going to have a power play that comes at you. We are going to have an option play off the power play. Then we have the play-action pass off the option play. It is a very simple scheme. This is especially true for our offensive line, where we want to keep it as simple as we can. We were able to run a power, option, reverse, and play action; get in the shotgun formation; and do the same thing. But the thing our offensive coaches did was to create leverage both inside and outside for our linemen. That is a big advantage, as anyone knows.

I appreciate the opportunity to be here. Come to see us practice. Again, thank you.

 





Page 226

Dave Wiemers

The 4-3 Defensive Line Package

Saginaw Valley State University

I appreciate the warm welcome. I appreciate your help. We are trying to get established in Michigan. We feel like our team is an up-and-coming team in our conference. We are trying to get over the top in our conference and at the national level. We had an outstanding year last year but it was also disappointing on some points. A lot of people would be happy with a 7-3 record. But we collapsed in the end a little. The injury bug got us. We feel like we are building an outstanding program. We are still recruiting. If you have a player, let me know. At this level the recruiting never stops.

We went to the 4-3 defense two years ago. We had been playing the 4-4 for a number of years. After the first year we felt we needed to get the run stopped a heck of a lot better than we were doing. The biggest reason we went to the 4-3 was to stop the run. A lot of people will tell you the 4-3 won't stop the run. There are too many cutback lanes in it. We flatten out the run and that is what we wanted.

Today I'm going to talk about our fronts. Our main disguise is what we do up front with our front four people. We want to be as simple as possible. I'll talk about defensive line play. I coach the linebacker now but I am a former defensive line coach. We feel like that is where the success of the defense starts. I'll talk about linebacker play a little and a couple of coverages that put us over the hump. To finish up I'll talk about some things that give us problems. In any defense you have problems in certain areas.

The thing that has made us a good defense is playing an attacking front. I can't describe how much an impact that has made with our defense. It lets our defensive lineman come off the ball and try to make plays. Our best players are our defensive linemen. They get in a three-point stance, with their tails up a bit, and they get their heads in a crack and play. That has been the number one thing to improving our defense. This is our philosophy. Here is our identity. (1) We want to be the most aggressive defense in Division II football. (2) We want to develop our style of play. The biggest thing we want to do is try to get our kids to trust one another. Resiliency is what we call sudden change. That is when things go bad on offense and the defense has to hustle back into the game. A big key to defense is; BENDDON'T BREAK. In the sudden change situations we want to be at our toughest. We have to play with effort, have a good attitude, execute, and know our assignments. (3) We want to be technique oriented. We want to teach and play the best technique we possibly can. We want them to be athletes and play. We don't fill their heads with so much that they can't play for thinking. (4) We want to be great tacklers. We teach tackling every day. (5) We want to force turnovers.

Our defensive goals are posted for everyone to see. We developed these goal back at Pittsburg State when we were one of the top Division II teams in the country, year in and year out. I'll go through them briefly for you. (1) Scoring defense less than 12 points per game. We finished this year at 14 points a game. (2) We want to force 3 turnovers per game. (3) At least 5 TANGOS per game. A TANGO is a 3-and-out series. Those are not all our objective but they are the main ones we stress with our kids.

There are a lot of 4-3 teams out there. We have gotten help from the University of Miami. They were really helpful. Everybody's stuff is copied and that is where we get ours.

As far as tackling, the only thing I'm going to talk about is our little creed. Our kids memorize this. It is a saying. Here is what we want to do when we tackle people. These are fundamentals of good tackling. The first thing is to SEE WHAT YOU HIT. The next thing is KISS THE FOOTBALL. That leads up to cross-the-bow tackling. The third thing is TO CLUB UP AND GRAB. The

 





Page 227

last thing is RUN THROUGH THE MAN. The biggest problem in tackling is dead feet on contact. If you can run your feet through the contact, you have a chance to win the battle.

When we talk techniques up front. The zero technique is head-up the center. The shade is on the center. The 's, 2's, and 3's are on the guard. The 4-eye, 4, and 5 are on the tackle. The 6, 7, and 9 techniques are on the tight end. When we talk gaps with our line, it is A, B, C, and D. With linebackers we number the gaps.

In the defensive line we want to screw with the heads of the offensive linemen. Our disguise in our defense is the front four people. We line up in different places. We line up in 8 to 9 different fronts with the front four people. Because of that we are starting to get splits n the offensive line about 6 inches wide. We feel like that is a positive for us. Eventually it generates more tackles for other people.

Our first front we call "BASE." We have our tackle in a 3 technique to the tight end. The nose tackle is in a backside shade on the center. Our weakside defensive end is called "Animal," and aligns in a 5 technique. Our strongside end is in a 9 technique on the tight end. Our Mike linebacker has his heels at 5 yards and is shaded strong on the center. Our Sam linebacker lines up in the C gap with his inside foot aligned on the crotch of the offensive tackle. The Will linebacker is a floater. We )lay him in a lot of places. That is our base front and what we are going to play about 50 percent of the time.

We play this defense all spring long. We won't play anything else. We learn how to play BASE first and play it well. Then we move on to something else. We show a two shell in the secondary. BASE is a good front or us. The problems come from the trap inside and the block-down kick-out on the end. We play him wide because he makes plays, but the tight end gets on our Sam linebacker pretty good sometimes.

The next front is called "G" front. The only difference in alignment is our nose player moves to the 1 technique on the inside shoulder of the weakside guard. We can call "G" with almost any of our other fronts. It is an adjustment. If we get two tight ends we go to automatic ''G." If we are getting wider splits between the guard and center it is automatic "G." We leave a lot of the adjustment up to the nose tackle. If there is a strong guard, we'll move over and make him block the nose tackle all the time. We want to take advantage of the weaker players. At this point and time in our conference, center play is a little down. We are finding smaller kids at center. Therefore we are playing quite a bit of BASE. Playing "G'' is obviously best for the weakside running game. When you play a 4-3 defense, offenses are going to attack your Will linebacker

At Saginaw we run the option and our defense is getting pretty good at stopping it. Our 5 technique is always on the dive. Our 9 technique is always on the quarterback. We plug the Sam linebacker on any flow at him. The Sam linebacker is playing at 5 yards and is plugging the C gap on any flow at him. That means Sam s the dive player on the option his way. The end has the quarterback and the pitch is covered from the secondary depending on coverage. If we are going to win the Division II National Championship, we have to stop the option. Most of those teams are option teams.

The defensive end always goes to the strength of the set and the Animal to the weak side. Since they have moved the hash mark in, there are really no boundary and field positions any more. We always call the strength to the tight end. The reason we do it is personnel. The nose tackle always goes with the Animal and the tackle is always to the end's side. Strength call is important to us and we have to see it early. The first thing the Sam linebacker does at the beginning of the weak is to know who the tight end is.

 





Page 228

That can be a problem with quick or no-huddle teams. We run BASE and "G" 60 percent of the time. The other 40 percent we run different alignments with our tackles. We run something that will create some confusion.

The first one we go to is called "22 KNIFE." Knife is our slant technique.

It is something where we want to get the inside people moving. The 22 means that the nose and tackle are aligned in 2 techniques. Everyone knows when we go to head-up alignments, we are going to slant. But they don't know which way we are going. That is the confusion we want to create. The nose tackle and tackle are slanting back to the BASE defense. The tackle has the B gap strong, and the noseguard has the A gap weak.

In our knife technique we step laterally and fight to get their head in the crack of the gap. We call it "Hat in the Crack." That generates penetration off the slant. It can be good for you if your tackles have a good quick first step. They fight the inside arm, rip up trying to go low to high, and getting in the crack.

We can run the same stunt to the weak side. We call it "22 KNIFE WEAK." It is the reverse stunt from the strongside stunt. The difference is when the end hears a weak call he slants into the C gap. This is a good change-up for us. We create doubt in the offensive linemen's heads when we get in the head-up position. When the splits start to get tight, we know we are causing problems. We knife the short motion at times. The Mike linebacker calls the stunt on the field and we knife the motion. This is a great defense for teams with big splits in the offensive line.

I get excited talking about this next front because it has been real good for us. We call it "11." It is a wing-T stopper. It is an anti-trap front. The inside tackles have one thing in mind. We want them to get two yards of depth into the backfield. We are not seeing a lot of A gap football plays these days. What A gap plays there are is the quarterback sneak and trap. When we get in this defense they have to trap the 5 technique. The wing-T kicked our tails for two years. This defense stopped it.

The tackle and nose line up on the inside shoulders of the guard. When our linemen are playing inside techniques, they are ripping hard with their outside arm right through the ear hole of the guard. They are thinking penetration. The strong end plays a 7 technique. The Animal is in a 5 technique. The guy who loves this alignment the most is the Mike linebacker. The Sam linebacker is covered by the end so he has a chance to run a bit.

Here's how we played against the wing-T. The Mike linebacker takes care of the fullback. He is the midline player. The Mike linebacker spends the whole day tackling the fullback whether he has the ball or not. The Sam and Will linebackers key across unless they have a halfback in front of them. In this case the Will

 





Page 229

has a halfback offset in front of him and that is the man he keys. The Sam linebacker has no back in front of him, so he keys the offset back also.

If the set was a double wing the Sam and Will linebackers keyed the motion man. Anytime there was motion both of them keyed it. We only gave them two places to go. They got flow toward, and flow away from them. On flow toward, they scraped into their gap responsibility. If they got flow away, they got in their stack position inside behind the tackle or nose tackle, and waited for the counter coming back or the fullback on the midline trap.

This defense is our version of a stem front. We don't want to stem too much. We don't want to give them that kind of freedom. They seem to get tired quicker if they are moving all over the place and they are going to forget. When the ball is set we line up in "22," with 3 linebackers in the box. When we go from 3 linebacker to 2 in the box that is good for us. The Mike linebacker calls, "Move." When he does that we stem to what we call ''JOKER.'' We coach the Mike linebacker when to make his call. We do it from the cadence of the offense. The strong safety rolls up to the tight end side. We play cover 3 behind it. The Will linebacker walks to the outside and becomes a true outside linebacker. We don't want to play this defense all the time. It is there to confuse the offense and get stem movement in our defense. The tackle and nose move one technique toward the tight end. They are in a "31" defense to the tight end. We like the personnel we have playing this defense, particularly our strong safety. He is a good off-the-edge player. He is good on the blitz and just a real physical player.

When we go to "JOKER," the blitz package and calls are the same. We couldn't do this unless we tied it all together and used the same words. All we are doing is lining up in different places on the field. The key to doing this are the Sam and Mike linebackers. They have to understand gap changes. When we play three linebackers we play them vertical. There is not a lot of shuffling. The two linebackers look inside; they have to shuffle a little bit.

We have five different fronts. We script those fronts for games. We put all the different looks in the first 15 calls. We feel like the offense has to go in at halftime and go over every look they have seen. They may have seen them the week before, but they don't know the one we are going to run the most.

A huge advantage for us was playing our defensive end in a 7 technique. We kept it simple and brought the 9 technique down into the 7 and it really helped us.
