He is stepping in and ripping through with the outside arm. If he does the job the ball will bounce to the outside. Our outside linebacker and strong safety love that, because they make tackles.

If we put the defensive end in the 9 technique and slant him to the C gap it is called "CRASH." If the tackle comes out to B gap it is called "SMASH." If we do the same thing to the weak side we call it "GAP" and "SNAP." This is option slip stuff.

We feel like our fronts give the offense a lot of different looks. It is very simple. It gives us something to line up in.

 





Page 230

I'm coaching linebackers now but my heart is with the defensive line. That is where you win games. We are winning football games with our defensive front-four people. The key to us is the attack front. You need the right people to do it. You can't play with a 290-pound slug in there. We have tried to recruit quicker players.

Stance is key and alignment is crucial. We don't vary from a different stance or alignment. You start where you are aligned. Never get beat by bad alignment. We never have our defensive linemen with their helmet within the framework of the offensive lineman. If the offensive line splits start to get tight, it looks like we are sitting in the gap. It doesn't look like we are shaded on anything or anybody. But that is the way we coach it. We are still lined up on the man we are shaded on.

In our stance we get down in a heel-to-toe relationship. If we want to slant, we base up a little and bring our feet closer together. The inside hand goes on the ground. That is the hand closest to the ball. We put the hand down in front of our nose and even with the back foot. We want our punch hand and first step going in the same line. We don't talk about weight distribution but we want the weight on the hand with their butt a little higher than their shoulders. When kids can't get off the ball, it is generally because they get too flat in their back. That comes from being tired also. Don't get so high that you can't see the man you are shaded on. We want our inside foot on the outside foot of the offense.

Our first step is going to be right where our hand used to be. The step ended up being about a 2-foot step. We punch through the hip of the offensive lineman. We take a thumbs-up half-closed fist and punch out nice and firm. We step down and replace our hand. This is if we are in outside techniques. We do this for pad level. High pads lose battles. If we are going to win we have to keep the pads down. When we punch the hip we literally want that offensive lineman to go home with a bruise on his hip. We use this as a feeler to get the shoulder turned to the play.

If we are playing an inside technique like the 1 and 7 technique, we crowd the ball as tight as possible. Place your outside foot on the inside foot of the lineman you are aligned on. He gets his rip arm ready. As the ball is snapped, all the linemen are thinking about is ripping his outside arm. He is not going to punch down to the tackle. That would be a 5 technique. All he is going to concentrate on is stepping and getting his outside arm through. That keeps us from getting in a "Wash. " This has been huge for us. That is the same for a 1 technique.

If we line up in an "11" defense, our tackles have their inside hand down. On their first and second step they are thinking outside arm and ripping it as hard as they can. They rip right through the ear hole of the offensive lineman. He is coming on all fours. He wants to get to the second key as quick as possible. All we have to teach is outside and inside technique with all four of them.

We only talk about six blocking schemes with these guys. The first one is the base block. He punches the hip and works upfield to get his hat in the crack. Do not get stretched, and they find the second key. It is hard to tell a base block and a zone block. In the zone look, the offensive lineman stretches for an instant.

The next one is a reach block or zone block. We step upfield with the first step and punch the hip. Make a quick second step away from the offensive lineman and explode back upfield. We want to end up 1 yard outside and 1 yard upfield. Next, we want to find the second key.

The next thing is the veer block with a cutoff. We step straight upfield and punch the hip. On the second step, we step down with the down block and rip the backside arm through the cutoff block. Look for any trapper. If we see the trapper, we wrong-arm him or

 





Page 231

what we call "Run the Hoop." That is the hoop circle we use in practice. We feel it takes a tackle two years to learn how to play the trap by himself.

On the double-team block, step straight up the field and punch the hips to avoid the hips of the offensive linemen coming together. On the second step continue upfield, splitting the double-team and find the second key. If you start to give up movement, drop the outside knee and tackle the outside lineman. Do not drop to the ground unless you absolutely have to.

On the scoop block step straight upfield and punch the hip. On the second step, step down with the lineman and rip the backside arm through the scoop blocker while still penetrating.

With the down block we still step straight upfield and punch the hip. On the second step, feel the pressure of the down block and "Run the Hoop" finding the second key. This is running around the block, but if you stay tight this is a good technique. If you get pinned by the down block, fight across the face of spin over the top of the down block.

When our linebackers line up we want them to use a progression to make the play. We key the backfield for direction and flow. We play and secure the primary gap first. We want to get in the gap and play vertical. If he doesn't make the play, make it go somewhere else.

We read four flows. The Mike linebacker shades strong most of the time at a depth of 5 yards. If we get double tight ends, he shades weak. That is for vision so he can see the midline player. The Sam and Will linebackers are at 5 yards deep, sneaking up to 4.5. The Sam and Will linebackers line up in 50 techniques. Their inside leg is splitting the crotch of the tackle. They always key the near back. We pick the five best plays our opponents run and that is all we look at for the week we are preparing for them. From those five plays we key the guy who gets the football the most. In split backs the Mike linebacker keys through the quarterback for directional opening to the nearest flow. His first step is at the directional opening of the quarterback. After that he goes to the immediate flow. If we see the isolation play, that is a full-flow play for us. The Will linebacker is taking on the isolation block with his outside arm free. We force the iso back to the inside. The Mike linebacker cleans up in the B gap. We number the gaps 1, 3, 5, and 9. The Will linebacker is a 31-gap player. If the ball comes at him, he plays 3 gap. Ball away from him, he plays 1 gap. The Mike linebacker is a 13 player. Ball at him, he plays 1. If it goes away, he plays 3 gap. The Sam linebacker is a 51 player. Ball at him, he plays 5; and ball away, he plays 1 gap.

We tell the Sam and Will linebackers on full flow toward to go fill the hole. On flow away, stack on the nose or tackle. There is no shuffle. It is a 45-degree angle at the tail of our nose tackle. The trouble area is the tackle getting on the Will linebacker on plays away. We want no shuffle to prevent being cut off. On a play away from the linebacker, he rips inside the tackle and squeezes the guards. Full flow has the threat of a cutback.

Fast flow has no threat of a cutback. That is the toss sweep, speed option, and plays like that. We turn the linebackers loose and let them run. Any vertical seam they see, they get in it and go after the ball. The Sam linebacker on flow to him, blows the C gap. The Mike linebacker picks the first vertical seam and fires. The Will linebacker runs over the top and goes vertical only when he has to.

I'm out of time but there is one coverage I want to show you. It has made a huge difference for us. It is our 2-back coverage, which we call COVER 8. We sold out to stopping the run when we went to the 4-3 defense. We wanted a coverage that gave us an extra player. If the offense has two backs in the backfield, 90 percent of the time we are in COVER 8. The safeties align outside the number 2 receiver in an outside shade. They are going to do what we call "patting their feet." The safeties are 10 yards deep and the corners are 5 yards deep. We start every defensive coverage at these depths. That is our disguise.

The safeties line up at 10 yards and sneak up to 8 yards before the ball is snapped. They pat their feet and key the number 2 receiver. Obviously we are thinking run.

 





Page 232

In a Cover 8 the Will linebacker can go over the top and a safety fills in behind him. If flow comes to the safety they run the alley like crazy. The backside safety is keying the backside tackle through the near back. He is keying the tackle for direction and run or pass. He pats his feet and immediately goes vertical to replace the Will linebacker, if he reads run. He is the guy the offense cannot account for. He can react and tackle the play for a 3-yard gain.

The strong safety is called "BARON." On flow away from him he is replacing the Sam linebacker. If there is outside flow away from him, he reacts down the line at 8 yards depth, looking for the immediate cutback outside of the tackle. He helps fill in behind the playside safety.

If the number 2 receiver goes vertical, it becomes a matchup zone. The corners are aligned inside. They take the post away and force the fade.

Coaches, it's over. I'm out of time. I'll be here quite some time and I'll talk to you about anything we do. Good luck to you. 

 





Page 233

Greg H. Williams

Zone Defense from Goal Line to Goal Line

University of Georgia

This topic came up as a result of a conversation with one of the foremost high school coaches in Georgia, Dan Pitts down at Mary Persons High School in Forsyth. When Coach Ray Lamb asked me to speak at this clinic quite a while back, he asked me to pick a topic. I was fortunate enough to speak at the Georgia Athletic Coaches Association last summer. I did an hour-and-one-half lecture on individual drills and techniques. I will talk at our clinic about the two-deep and some other things. Again, I am speaking to a lot of the Georgia high school coaches. I am a little perplexed, because I want to give you something to take away from here.

Coach Pitts came up to visit with me one day. He asked me about zone coverages. He felt he needed to blitz more, but he wanted to play man to man. I started showing him the type of blitzes we run. Every time I showed him a blitz, we were playing some type of man coverage, or we were in a combo or nickel package. Coach Pitts just sat there shaking his head. He did not like what he was hearing. We talked about running the blitz with zone coverage. We agreed that this was nothing new to NFL teams because they have been doing it for a long time. They can do that by having great personnel.

I coached at Maryland for 10 years, and we played Penn State each of those 10 years. They played a lot of zone coverage with the blitz. Coach Pitts liked that idea a lot better, because he liked to play zone coverage. We solved some of his problems, and he went back to Forsyth. I sat there later and decided that I would make my topic zone defense.

will cover a number of zones. The first thing we must talk about is this: What do you want to get out of playing zone coverage? There are a lot of different zones. What does the zone give you? What do you get out of it? I listed the things that we want out of the zone.

Ten Commandments of Zone Defense

1. There is more than one defender to the ball.

In a zone defense, everyone looks at the ball. That is the number one thing you must do in defense, regardless of the level you are in. People who play good defense always have more than one person to the ball. That is the way we start out everything in the secondary. There are four of us back deep, and there is only one ball. We have a number of drills that we used to teach our players to get to the ball. That is the first thing we want to do. We want more than one defender to the ball. That will help us stop the deep pass. That is the second thing you want to do. If we get more than one defender to the ball, we will not get the deep ball. The third thing we want to do on defense is to stop the yardage after the catch. This has brought about the West Coast offense, which is throwing the 5-yard pass to Jerry Rice over the middle and having him run for 50 yards down the field. After every game we chart yards gained after the catch. That is significant to the outcome of the game. We do not want to give up a 5-yard pass and a 50-yard run. That is 55 yards. We want them to earn those yards. We do not want them to get any yardage after the catch. Other things in these 10 commandments are associated with our philosophy of yardage after the catch.

I listened to John Madden comment in a play-off game after our season was over. He said that most NFL teams were defending the West Coast offense by staying in their zones. They let the receivers catch the ball underneath and then knock the hell out of the receivers. That is what the zone defense was designed for in the first place.

2. Defend areas of the field. 

We are going to put our players in certain areas of the field. Why? Because we want to defend that area. That is what we want to defend. If we play a three-deep zone, we are going

 





Page 234

to put a man in the middle and a man in each outside zone. That is where they are going to stay. They stay in that area of the field. If we want to play a two-deep zone, we will set those corners outside of the wideouts, and that is where they are going to stay. If we are anticipating short passes on 3rd and 15, we may drop our linebackers back to certain spots on the field. We have a limited rush and five certain people in areas of the field, and we still have three deep.

3. Receivers do not know where the lick is coming from. 

Receivers love man-to-man coverage. When the defender gets up tight on the receiver, that receiver can't wait to run his pattern. Do you know why? Because he knows who is going to hit him. He knows where the lick is coming from. He can take the lick where he wants to take it. When that receiver comes outside and the defense scatters, he does not know who is going to hit him. That is another reason we play the zone.

4. Different zones change receivers' routes. 

The wide receiver comes outside, and all of a sudden there is a man in front of him and one behind that defender. Now he is reading two deep. Whatever was called in the huddle, they are going to convert. Now you get into the communication process between the quarterback and the wideout. The receiver has to change his route because he can't run the hook. He has a defender in that area.

If we are in a two-deep look and all of sudden we switch to a three-deep look, the receiver has to change his route again. By using different zones, we make receivers change their routes. That leads us to the next commandment.

5. Different zones change the pass patterns. 

Is it a five-short zone; is it a three-deep zone? Is it a two-deep zone? Is it a strong zone or a weak zone? The offense will have to change what they are doing, or we will be successful against them.

6. Zone coverages dictate where the ball will be thrown. 

When we line up our defenders in certain areas of the field, we take those areas away by our alignment. It is called an alignment advantage. We take away certain patterns. We want to force the offense in play selection. To be successful, they have to try to get to the flat to get some yardage. They could try to run to the seam and hope that the quarterback can drive the ball into the receiver. We want to dictate where they throw the football. In practice, we work to see whether the offense is throwing the ball where we want them to throw it. If they are not throwing the ball to the weakest part of that zone, then we are not executing the defense the way we should. You cannot let them throw the ball into the teeth of your zone. If you do that, they will beat you to death. We want to make them go the long road. We want to make the offense put the ball where we want it put.

7. Changes in zone coverages confuse the quarterback. 

Defend the pass from the eye of the quarterback or the man throwing the ball. Don't defend the pass by looking at the pass patterns from your office on the screen. Defend it from the eyes of the man throwing the football. That is why there are only five or six great quarterbacks in the NFL. There are only five or six of them who have both the eyes and the arm. Lots of them have the eyes, but they do not have the arm. A lot of them can see where the ball should go but can't get it there. On the other hand, you have some guys who have the arm and can get the ball down the field, but they can also read or determine on their drop where the ball should be placed against that defense. We look at things in college by the kind of throw the man can make. This year, we had to play against two quarterbacks who have it all: the quarterback at Florida and the quarterback at Tennessee. We did not have much success against them. We had some success against some of the other teams because their quarterbacks were not able to see the opening and then do something about it.

8. Blitz and defend with zone coverages. 

Attack the protection scheme. That is what I was telling you about in my conversation with Coach Pitts. He was not comfortable with man coverage. I showed him a couple of simple blitzes that would give the protection he was playing against problems, yet he could still be secure in what he liked, which was the three-deep secondary. I will go over this later. Anyone in the NFL that blitzes and plays zone coverages gets a big raise or a title.

 





Page 235

That is the deal right now. The reason they do that is because it attacks the protection scheme. The offense has problems because they do not know who is coming or who is dropping. When they have problems protecting the quarterback, they also have problems with the throwing lanes for the quarterback to throw the ball out of. The quarterbacks throw out of a vision lane. When those lanes change, those quarterbacks have to change. In the NFL, if you can rush the quarterback, you can stop them. If you can't rush them, you are not going to stop them.

9. The zone defenses offer advantages as the field shortens. 

This is something I have talked about for years. When I first started coaching, we were not very good down deep. The reason was that we did not practice defending the red zone. We talked about it, and we discussed strategy, but we just went over it. We did not spend time down in the red zone. The reason that the zone defense is good in the red zone is because the offense only uses certain passes down deep. You can get the interception down deep, and you can get your safeties involved in run support. You can actually be in a nine-man front without subbing for the nine men.

10. Zone personnel is less important. 

You just do not have to have as good a player to play the zone. This is especially true if you get good at playing zone. f you are going to play man to man, you have to have one thing and one thing only: That is makeup speed. Sooner or later, you are going to get beat, and you better have someone lined up back deep who can make up for it. If you have four players who can fly, you can play a lot of man-to-man coverage. I have some players on defense who can fly, but they can't fly as fast as those guys from Florida. That is why we have to play some zone. We also have to play some man. Zone is a personnel thing. You have a better chance of getting a good cover with the personnel that you have in the zone than you do in man. When you go man to man, you have to depend on the blitzes getting there to cover up for your coverage problems.

Those are the 10 commandments of zone defense. I am going to go through 10 zones. All of these facets are going to make up these coverages. You can take three or four of these and make up your own coverages. You can pull out two or three of these and make them your cover two, or whatever you want it to be. I will explain these to you as best I can in a short period of time.

The first cover is universal. It is what everyone plays. It is three deep, four short-strong. This was played back about 20 years ago. There never was such a thing as a weak zone. Basically, what we want to do is to go three deep. We are going to put the two corners and free safety in the deep thirds. Now I am going to give you an overview of all zone coverages. I will not be able to go over every detail of each coverage. I have the diagrams drawn up for an odd alignment, but I did not put the linemen down.

THREE DEEP, FOUR SHORTSTRONG

Also, I have them drawn up for an even alignment, which has three linebackers. Basically, all this on the diagram is a three-deep zone. We can go through all of the commandments again and see that we are three deep. We are taking away the deep areas and the underneath areas of the field by lining up in those areas. We do not want to give up a completion in those areas. We are not going to give up a deep ball.

We have the same three deep with four short strong with the four-man front. We have three deep, we have four short, and we are in a strong zone. We have the even front.

 





Page 236

THREE DEEP, FOUR SHORTSTRONG

The other thing you must understand is that the offense will attack you two ways. They will attack you horizontally or, as Al Davis says, vertically. When you are thinking about zone coverages, those are the only two words you should think about. When you watch your opponent, you ask this question: Are they attacking us horizontally or vertically? In the old days, when Al Davis was coaching the Raiders, he did not have a horizontal pattern in his play book. He was verticalboom! If you watch the West Coast offense, it is all horizontal. Three deep and four short is a good defense for the vertical patterns. However, it is tough when they attack you horizontally.

Next is our three deep, four short-weak. Now we are going to take the zone, and we are going to roll it to the weak side. We are going to put that corner in that weak flat. We bring the free safety over to play the outside one third. The strong safety comes over to the middle, and the strong corner drops back to play the outside one third. We drop the strong backer and the two inside linebackers.

THREE DEEP, FOUR SHORTWEAK

You can disguise these looks. That is a whole can of worms in itself. They have three down linemen, they have four linebackers, and they are in three-deep coverage. That is one hell of a defense from an eightman front. That defense came into being with Monte Kiffin and the eagle defense when he was at Nebraska and Arkansas. What they did was to slide the line. Now, the strong safety who played the strong flat before is going to come back and take the deep middle. The corner who played the outside one third is going to play the flat area.

THREE DEEP, FOUR SHORTWEAK

You can see that the free safety will play the outside one third on the weak side, and the corner to the field is going to play that outside one third. That is a weak zone.

In the strong zone, before, we had no way to harass the wide receiver. Now, with this coverage we are going to harass the wideout. We could not do that in the strong coverage. Now we are going to dictate what pattern they are going to run. Before, they have been hurting us by dragging their tight end, hooking or curling the X end, or putting the back out into the flat, when we were in our conventional strong three-deep. That would be a horizontal pattern. At the time, they do not have a pattern. The minute we harass the split end and cover the flat, we have three over two.

That is the other thing you must understand about pass defense. It is either three over two or two over

 





Page 237

one. That is how you defend the pass. Those combinations depend on whether it is a vertical or horizontal pass route that you are facing.

In the four-man line, we drop the backers to the strong side. Now where does the offense have to come with the ball? They have to come to the strong-side flat.

Next, we will look at three deep, five shortstrong. We are going to set in a strong zone. The corners have the outside one thirds. The free safety comes to the deep middle. The strong safety takes the strong flat. We are going to attack the horizontal and vertical passing game. This is the defense that the NFL uses to harass teams that pass for the 5 yards. They use a three-man rush and play five short with three deep. This is very good to stop any horizontal pattern. On the other hand, we have three deep. Most teams would send the two wideouts on a deep route. The NFL defensive teams started using this defense to slow the two wideouts down. That enabled them to stop any receiver from going deep. This is the defense that changed the rules of the NFL.

THREE DEEP, FIVE SHORTSTRONG

When Green Bay got New England in long-yardage situations, they went to this package. The ran the twist stunt up front and shut them down. They had no place to throw the football. You can run the same scheme with the three linebackers, but it is not worth going over. It takes too much teaching to get the job done.

Next we go to our three deep, four short, with a spot defender. Remember when I talked about taking an area of the field away from the offense? What happened is this. Good offensive coordinators started lining up their good receivers in different positions. They would move them around. The 49ers did this with Rice. You have to come up with something that will combat this move. We came up with the spot defender. We want to do it in relation to the formation or the motion or what was going on at the time.

When I was at Maryland, we had to play against Herman Moore. They had a good offensive scheme. They moved him all around to get him open on the passes. You never knew where he was going to line up. We did not have anyone as fast as he was, and they still had a great horizontal passing game to the other receivers. This is where we used our spot defender. We would line up our spot where Herman would line up, ust as if we were in two deep. As soon as the motion started, the spot moved to the area outside the tight end at 12 yards. We moved the two corners back to the outside one thirds. The free safety came back and played deep middle. The four linebackers dropped off, and we played four short with a spot defender.

THREE DEEP, FOUR SHORTSPOT DEFENDER

We had a choice to drop or blitz the strong inside linebacker. We would mix it up: drop him or blitz him. We still had the two tackles and nose to rush. Early in the game, this is what we would do. We would bring the strong safety to the spot. The corner would drop. The strong-side backer would drive to the outside. The

 





Page 238

reason we went to that particular spot was because that is where Herman Moore was catching all of his passes. He would line up at different spots, but he would still end up at that spot. That is where we had to get in order to stop the pass.

The next zone is what everyone is using now. It is our two deep, five short-hard. We are going to play the two corners hard. The word after the underneath coverage tells the corners how to play. Hard means they are going to play hard on the run, play collision on the wide receiver, and play that area. If we are in a 50 defense, this is how it looks.

TWO DEEP, FIVE SHORTHARD

We keep the two safeties deep, and they set in the middle of the field, with each man responsible for one half of the field. It is a two-deep with five short, and we are playing hard. Why are we getting tricky? Because we have the horizontal game shut off. We have five short defenders underneath. They should not be able to complete anything in the 10- to 12-yard areas. We are going to collision the two wideouts. This will make them alter their pattern. Now the dilemma comes. What is open? What is open? The vertical game. Now they are going to attack the area between the two safeties. Also, they will attack the areas to the deep outside. I call the area to the outside the side pocket. Have you ever played pool? That is where they want to put the pass. How are they going to get it there? They are going to get it there two ways. They can have the wide receivers release inside and then come back on a corner route. The other way is to come straight out, make the corner miss on the collision, and then get down to the pocket for the fade route. Those are about the only two ways they can get deep against you.

What are we going to do in the secondary when we are in this defense? We have to work the heck out of defending those patterns. We have to work hard to get a good collision on the two wideouts. We have to be able to collision and still get back on the corner. The safeties have to be ready to break on the ball.

The other route we face is the tight end coming down the middle. The linebacker has to get a piece of that tight end as he comes downfield. That is easy to do if it is a drop-back pass. If it is play action, it is a problem for the linebacker. We can change it up as far as who and how we drop our linebackers. This is the way the offense is going to attack the two-deep, five-short, hard defense. They are going to try to get three-deep receivers on your two-deep defenders. On the back side, we have three defenders on their one receiver. On the front side, we have four on their two receivers. I know that there are a lot of one-back sets today. I do not have time to go over that, but this is the basic way we play that zone.

On the 4-3 look, we have three linebackers in the game. Now we have two deep, five shortsoft.

TWO DEEP, FIVE SHORTSOFT

 





Page 239

We still have to defend the side pockets. The corners are going to collision the wide receivers, except this time we want them to work back at an angle so that they are at the bottom of that side pocket as the ball is thrown. This allows our two safeties to be tighter and stronger in the middle.

We get a good hit against the tight end from the linebacker on this look. He can read pass or run from his position. This 4-3 front is more conducive to handling the man down the middle. Now the corners come back and pattern-read. They form a visual triangle from the wide receiver to the near back in the backfield. They slide out of the area and get back to the bottom of the side pocket. They must be ready to react to anything coming in their zone. They should be able to help on the fade route. The two safeties know the corners are sagging. They know the fade has to come in deeper, and that gives them more time to play the fade. They will stick more to the inside and help us there.

This defense is really big when they move the tailback into the slot on the back side. Now they have two receivers, and we have three defenders. We have to win. We've got to win. On the strong side, they have two receivers, and we have three defenders. We have to win. The reason I like two deep is because we force them to run to certain areas and in certain patterns. We continue to get more than one person to the ball. We can practice and practice and practice and get good at it. It takes a lot of the passing game away from the offense. It gives you great perimeter support. In essence, it gives you nine men up front. If they run the screen pass, you have it turned in. If they run the toss or the option play, you have a corner outside. In 1972 and 1973, no one played two deep. They did play two deep with man coverage underneath. Now you can see this coverage about 90 percent of the time. You see this coverage and a weak zone coverage. Those are the two predominant coverages in college football today.

Let me get to the last coverage. This is the zone blitz with a three-deep coverage. I took this off one of our game tapes. It is what you see from the Carolina Panthers. They defend the pass from the eyes of the quarterback. So do we. The quarterback comes out, and what does he see? Two deep! He sees two linebackers inside and a five-man line. In essence, it is a run defense. From this look, the quarterback thinks he can throw the ball deep. He thinks he can throw to the flat. He thinks he can do a lot with the ball.

ZONE BLITZTHREE DEEP

When the ball gets snapped, it is just like a covey of quail. Our defenders are going all over the place. As soon as the ball touches the quarterback, he looks up, and now he sees a three-deep secondary. The nose and tackle stunt on the strong side. Now, on the short side, the backer is in the A gap. The tackle drops off the line to the hook zone. The outside linebackers are rush and contain players. They make contact with the man on the line, keeping the outside arm free. They are going to keep the quarterback in that pocket. By doing all of this switching, they have disrupted all of the passing lanes that the quarterback has worked against. It no longer is a basic five-man rush or even a basic four-man rush. He doesn't know where the passing lane is with everyone moving around. This is only one blitz. There may be 50 of them.

What happens if you are playing man to man and you get beat deep? Everyone looks at the secondary coach and says, ''He is a dumb ass.'' Now, this zone blitz made me a hell of a lot smarter. It may have kept me in coaching 10 more years. Now when the defensive coordinator calls that blitz, I am up in the press box drinking Coke. Why? I have three deep. The

 





Page 240

quarterback does not know who is dropping to cover deep. When the receivers come out, we have people in the zones that they have not accounted for. That is why sometimes the quarterback will throw the ball right to a tackle. The quarterback sees the linebacker come and thinks that area is open. You can see the dilemma this has brought into play.

A few years ago this is what Penn State ran. They would bring their strong safety up close to the line and blitz him. They called him the hero.

HERO BLITZ

Teams would see the hero up tight, and they would check off and throw the quick route. Penn State would send the linebacker outside and pick off the pass. To me, this was the first zone blitz. It is amazing what you can do with this defense. This is something you can do in high school football. You can play three deep. Some of you may have been using this same type of defense. Remember the 10 commandments: personnelyour personnel! You may not have had the personnel to play man coverage. It does not have to be out of a 50 front. Carolina Panthers just so happened to be running that front. We watch a lot of tape. This is something that can help us. That is our zone blitz.

I am going to put the videotape on. I will point out the 10 commandments on defense. [Video]

 





Page 241

Gregg Williams

Linebacker Drills And Techniques

Houston Oilers

I appreciate the opportunity to get a chance to visit with you. I want to make this as informative as I can. I want to make this as fun as I can. I understand that it is a poor meeting when I'm doing all the talking. I feel that same way when I am involved with the players. A meeting has to be an extension of the field. I don't play anymore. They do. They play the games on Sunday, Saturday afternoons, and Friday nights. They have to know as much about what you do so that they are better able to play the game.

One of the most complimentary things that can be said about me is, "You still think like a high school coach." That is still said about me when I get into the personnel meetings on whether a guy can play or not. Right now we are going through all that with the college draft.

If the kid makes plays at the high school level, he'll make plays at the college level, if given a chance to play. If the guy is a play maker at the college level, he will make plays at our level, too. It will happen that way because play makers are play makers. Some of the best football coaches I have every coached with to this day were the high school coaches that I played for, the high school coaches on my staff, and the coaches that are still there in Kansas City.

It was easier for me to get on with the Houston Oilers than it was for me to become a head football coach in the state of Texas. I do think that there are some great high school football coaches in Texas. Some of the worst coaches I've been associated with are at the pro level. There are some weak coaches in pro ball. There are some baby-sitters here. They think the guys can go ahead, line up, and play. All they have to do is keep them out of trouble and make sure his car gets washed. It doesn't happen that way. These guys still have to be coached. They crave to be coached. We are going to coach them from when they get out of the Lexus until they get back into to it at the end of the day. That s the kind of staff Jeff Fisher has put together. Before I get long and drawn out with a lot of things dealing with linebacker play, I'm going to hit this really quick. I'm going to talk about philosophy. I would much rather be sitting where you are than doing this. That is what I have missed for the last 10 years since I've been at this level. I miss the clinics, fellowship, and the interchange of ideas. Understand, at the pro level we don't tell anybody crap. We don't tell each other anything, because that is taking food out of my kid's mouth. The guys that will get me fired are the guys at the Chargers, Lions, and Cowboys. We are not telling anybody anything. When I sat out there, I used to hate all that philosophical bullcrap. I will be brief with this.

I do believe this. If you are a young football coach, the most important thing in coaching is your philosophy, not your scheme. It is more about changing the behavior of that young man than it is about that scheme. I do believe it is important. That is why I'm going to talk a little about this to start with. After that, I'll get into some drills. I've got a film at the end that I'll show. After you watch that film, you're going to say, "He ain't any better than me." I am you. I have coached junior high, high school, college, and pro football. The reason I'm still at this level is because I'm going to coach the players every single day as hard as I can coach them.

I had a guy say to me, "You need to understand: I'm an individual. You need to treat me as an individual." He needed to understand to do it our way. We had to give him more than one way to be successful. But if he thought about being an individual, he needed to go to an individual sport. This is a team sport.

Let's talk really quickly about linebackers. I've done this at every level I've been at. As soon as the game is over, I come in and turn the film on. I ask myself two questions. The first question is, "Am I coaching that specific technique?" I ask that question especially if there was a bad play or something negative that

 





Page 242

happened. I'm going to study that play and find out how and why it broke down. If I'm the problem, I have to change it. I have to go back and find a better technique to allow that kid to be successful. If the answer to question number one is no, then I ask question number two. If I'm not coaching that technique, I must be allowing it to happen over and over again because I'm seeing that same stuff week after week. That's it. If you will live by that creed, you will start to see a change in your players.

I talk in clinics across the country. I talk to corporations, businessmen, salesmen, coaches, and players about goal setting and success. I talk about what you have to do in order to improve yourself from poor to average, from average to good, from good to great. To have those good years, you have to be able to effect change in the people around you. You are an average coach if you can't effect change. You are a baby-sitter. You are monitoring those players for two or three hours. There are lots of ways to go about effecting change. Some of these things are positive, and some are negative.

Individual drills are important to linebackers. I want to talk to you about my beliefs. Some of the exhibitors in the back aren't going to like this. It is my philosophy on coaching aids. Jack Pardee taught me this. I came from high school to the college level coaching linebackers. Jack was a Hall-of-Fame pro linebacker, and he was looking over my shoulder at everything I did. My philosophy is this, and he taught it to me: I am not really big on a lot of coaching aids during the season. You have to have certain dummies. You have to have a few cones and sleds. I understand that, but that sled is not going to knock his jock off on Friday night. Jack told me to get all that stuff done during the off-season. He told me I could have one drill with the coaching aid. "Anything past that, and you are out of here." He said he wanted to see them tackling, shedding, striking, covering, and running. He wanted to see them do those things the way they were going to do it on Saturday afternoon. I still use a few things, but not many, and only one a day.

We have a 20-minute, live, get-after-your-butt, special-teams period. That pretty much has our players warmed up before we start the individual periods. Still, there are a few guys each day who are not involved in the special-teams period who need to be warmed up. I'll start the individual period out with some sort of a warm-up. I'm going to emphasize stance and the first step as much as I can at the starting point of that warm-up period. I coach eyes, stance, and first step as they gradually warm up.

From there, I am going to some kind of full-speed, ballistic footwork drill. I want a change-of-direction and quick-movement drill to get all the muscles loosened up before we get into the contact period.

Every day, we are going to do certain things. It may be in a different order, but we do it every day. We do an upper-body strike-and-shed drill. We do a lower-body strike-and-shed drill. They learn how to get in a fit position and how to strike with their upper body and shed. They learn how to play the cut block. They strike and shed that block. They are going to learn how to play football through a few coaching aids and a few other bodies.

If you want to know whether your team was ready to play in a game in which you just got your butt kicked, look at the tackling. The number one indicator of whether your team was prepared and ready to play that night is tackling. If they tackled like crap, they weren't prepared. If you are a defensive football coach, you know that doesn't come naturally. Tackling is not natural. It is something you have to work on every single day. I am at the NFL level, and we tackle every day. I'm 38 years old. I've got guys as old as I am, but we are going to tackle every day. You have to emphasize the same points in tackling over and over.

As a defensive football coach, our number one thing is "don't let them score and get the ball back for the offense." That is it. It is not a hard game. Turnovers don't happen by accident. You have to teach guys how to strip the ball and knock it out. You have to teach them how to intercept the ball. Players in our three major position areas are not allowed to come out of the individual period unless the head coach sees a turnover drill every single day.

 





Page 243

I'm very proud that our linebackers are recognized by the league as being the best cover linebackers. I think the reason for that is that I coached in the secondary for a number of years. I couldn't understand why the linebacker coach couldn't get his linebackers to do the same things that our strong safety was doing. The outside linebacker is the same cover man as the strong safety. The linebackers and secondary should be doing the same drill.

A speed turn is used to save time in coverage. When the defensive back is running on the hip of a receiver, he is inside and playing on the upfield hip. The receiver will club, throw the defensive back up the field, and come underneath him. If the defensive back takes the step where he got clubbed, takes another step, then opens up and drives on the receiver, that is three steps. What we teach is to take one step, make a quick head turn, and then come right back on the receiver. We teach our linebacker to do that, and we practice it.

When I came back to the linebackers after coaching the defensive back, I started teaching these big-bodied guys those techniques. They thought I was crazy. They looked like cows falling out of the back of a pickup truck. They were horrible looking. Now they rival the defensive backs in their technique.

We do a catching drill of some type. How many times does the linebacker get his hands on the football and not finish the catch? Most of the time, the guys in the secondary are receivers who got kicked off the offense. They couldn't catch the damn thing in the first place. The linebackers need the work even more than the defensive backs. Most of those linebackers wished they were tight ends catching touchdown passes. They got moved to defense because they couldn't catch a cold running buck-naked in a rainstorm. Because of that, we have to work on their hands every day.

We will pass-rush every day. Those are the drills we do every day. Our individual period every day starts out being 20 minutes. You are bullcrapping yourself if you think we ever get 20 minutes. The first drill that the head coach cuts so that you have time to add whatever he is putting in is the individual period and special-team period. To get all your individual period in, you have to do your drills in combination. Don't do single, one-specific-coaching-area drills. Do two or three specific areas in one drill. Jeff Fisher tells me I have 20 minutes in individual period today, but eight minutes later when he blows the horn to go to the next period, I've got my individual drills all done. I'm still going to do those things that I told you we do every day in eight minutes.

When we get finished, there is a lot of hard breathing going on. We go 100 miles an hour. Let's say we are going over bags today to work on agility. We work on our step-overs and -ups and changes of direction. We work on their eyes while they are going over the dummies. Get in a position where you can see their eyes. If you stand behind them and watch their butts, you are stupid. You have to coach through their eyes to know what they are seeing.

They go down the bags stepping over them. Then they go up and down them changing directions. They get on the back side of the bags, and they work on protecting the near arm. They play same arm and same leg protecting against the cut. When they get to the end of the bags, there is a guy there who jumps at them like a frog and tries to cut them. The linebacker plays his hand on the head and turns it. Where the head goes, the body follows. After that, there is a cross-the-bow tackle. I work on agility and cut-block mechanics, get a live rep on the cut block, and make a cross-the-bow tackle. But he still doesn't get an "attaboy" unless he strips the ball on the tackle.

On the tape, you will see us working on the low part of a seven-man sled. We work on upper-body strike. If I work on upper-body strike, I have to concentrate on the hand positioning also. The man who works his hands inside will win the battle. The thumbs have to be up. We have the big muscle groups of the upper body involved. If the thumbs get inside and the wrist rolls back, the wrists get jammed. The plexus of nerves across the tops of the arms shuts off, and you have no upper-body strength. We extend on the dummy with our hands and violently shed it as we go to the next dummy. At the end of the sled, I'll give the guy coming

 





Page 244

down the sled a live cut block. He has to play the cut live by turning the head, playing the back pad, and flattening him out. The last thing he does is the tackle. We work on strike, explosion, hand placement, and getting his hip across.

You have to be creative. Do combination drills. Don't tell me you don't have enough time. You don't have enough time if you are stupid. There is no place for stupidity in the game. You have to think to stay ahead of the head coach who is going to cut your period short. He is going to fire you if you can't coach. He'll call you in at the end of the year and tell you, "We are going in a different direction." I must get the things taught, regardless of whether he gives me enough time or not.

Here is some philosophical BS for you young coaches. These are the principles of good defense. I believe this and have done it from the junior high level up. (1) You have to have great communication in your defense. I'm going to show you something here in a minute on game planning that will make you shake your head. You won't believe we do it. Communication is vital to defensive football team. You have to communicate, and everyone has to be on the same page.

(2) We coach techniques and give them different types. However, the players have to make the decision on what specific technique to use on a particular down. As an example, I have a 225 linebacker. He tries to play "hat and hands" and gets his hands inside on a 340-pound, 7' 9" offensive guard. He gets engulfed and taken down. The officials only call holding when it gets really bad. I'm not going to teach him to play a "hat and hands" technique in this situation. I'm going to teach him a flipper technique. He delivers the flipper and knocks the crap out of that guard. He gets under his pads and gets his body in the hole. He may get buried, but at least the ball is cutting back.

(3) Can you effect change? If you want to know the difference between being a good coach and a great coach, this is it. You can't affect their size or speed, but you can effect change. If you can't, you don't need to be coaching. You decide who plays. If he doesn't play at the effort level you want, why in the hell is he playing? That is one thing I want said about me. When people see my linebackers play, they say, "Those guys play all out." If they don't, you will see the next guy going in. I'll get a fresh one in there who is going to bust his butt to get to the ball. The guy I took out comes over yelling and MFing me about his incentive clause for his number of plays. I tell him that if he wants to play and make that incentive clause, he better bust his butt to the ball. If you take a lazy step, you are over by me. He is not going to play unless he plays all out.

In 1993, we were the number one special team in the league. We were very simple as far as scheme. I had seven rookies on that team. We were number one because of effort. They flew to the ball with reckless abandon. You can make that happen. It is a sin if you allow them to take lazy steps.

(4) Intelligence is what we would all like. We would like to have smart players, but I've coached some dumb ones, just as you have. If the dumb guy can play, you've got to find a place for him. Chuck Knox told me this, "If that kid is good enough to play, then you better be smart enough to coach him." It has nothing to do with the kid's intelligence; it has to do with yours. For the ones who aren't very smart, you have to find a way for them to succeed. If they know their own element of what will make the defense successful, they have to do it.

We are a pressure-package defense. We are the only defense in the United States at any level that has the last say. Most offensive teams say they have the last say because they have the automatic system. I'll tell you really quickly how that comes about. Blitzing pressure teams are selling tickets right now. If you have an offensive-minded head coach, he wants you to get the ball back to him in a hurry. I don't want a 14play drive going on while the All-American quarterback stands on the sideline with his thumb up his butt. The instructions are clear from the head coach: Get the ball back.

If you are a good blitzing team, you have to do these things.

 





Page 245

(a) You have to disguise. You don't want the quarterback to know what you are in before the ball is snapped. Make sure your pre-snap is not what you are going to be in after the ball is snapped.

(b) Timing is important to the blitzing team. You want to be able to hit the line of scrimmage on the run. If the quarterback sees you creeping, he will chicken out and get rid of the ball. Get the snap count to help with timing. Teams that use motion usually snap the ball at certain points in the motion. Get that from the scouting films. You can use that as a tip-off. Start your blitz when the motion is getting to the area where the ball is snapped.

(c) You have to collapse the pocket with inside heat. Buddy Ryan would fire you if he saw an inside pass rusher use a swim move. He wanted the gap ripped and the pocket collapsed.

(d) Speed pressure comes from the outside. If the quarterback can step up in the pocket, that is the place where he can escape. If there is no collapse of the pocket, the blitz breaks down. If the speed blitz comes from the inside gap, the ends better be containing.

(e) You have to take away the quick inside passing game. Force the offense to run outs and fades. Don't let them throw inside posts and curls.

We are a 4-3 football team, and we like to get into the eight-man force game. We are predominantly an over-and-under shifted team. If we are playing a shade technique on the center and a 3 technique away from the tight end, that is an under front. If we play a 3 technique to the tight end and a shade on the back side, that is an over front. The only way teams get us into a true 4-3 look is if they balance up.

Offensive coordinators who see a team that kicks the front one way or the other balance up to see if the defense will balance up. This game is not hard. Coaches make it hard. I guess we want to promote our intelligence. If the defense doesn't balance, the quarterback simply counts right and left and runs to the side with the least number of people on it. They use the "check with me" call. What we do is balance up and force you to run to a personnel mismatch rather than a numbers mismatch.

Ninety-nine percent of the time when we break the huddle at the Houston Oilers, I don't know what we are going to play. If I go back and coach at the high school level, I will run this package and system. I'll have to simplify it somewhat, but they can do exactly what we do. We have one of four calls in the huddle. Our game plan is the most important thing we do. We have a two-back formation page. It has every two-back formation known to man on it. We have terminology that describes our communication. The number 212 to us means two wide receivers, one tight end, and two backs. The first number is how many wide receivers, the second is how many tight ends, and the third is how many backs. Red means split backs. Flop is what Buddy Ryan called the slot set, and that stuck with us. Brown is a weak back tilt in the backfield. Brown-up is moving the weak back up into a tight slot position off the weak tackle. I-over is taking the fullback out of the I and offsetting him strong. I-under is moving the fullback the other way. If we moved the fullback up into the slot position, it was I-under flop or I-over flop. Blue is a strong back tilt, and blue-flop is moving him into the slot position. These are all the adjustments you can make with two backs in the backfield.

Across the top of our game-plan sheet we have AFC. That stands for automatic front and coverage. We have a computer system. I brought one of the charts we use. The one I brought is for the red set, or splitback look. On this sheet we have every run from this formation and the hole they run into. The passes are recorded at the top of the sheet. A split-back formation in our league is primarily a passing set, but they may run a little lead draw strong and weak.

This set is mainly a pass and not a lot of run. We play a seven-man front to it. We are going to take away your wide receivers and force you to beat us with your tight end or your backs. If you run to the open side, we are going to kick the front weak. If you like to run to the tight side, we kick the front strong. We have decided whether you run the opposite of the scouting report; we have won that down. The offense is doing something they really don't want to do. If an offense

 





Page 246

is multiple, it has tendencies. We do a five- to seven-game breakdown on people we play. In the secondary, we are going to roll the coverage to your best receiver or where you like to throw the ball.

If the offense comes out in a running set, we get into the eight-man front and stop the run. The quickest way to get beat is not being able to stop the run. We have been number one, two, or three in the NFL since I've been at the Houston Oilers in rushing defense. If you are a tight-end-running team, we kick over. If you run to the split end, we kick under. We have one more than you can block either way, and we will kick your butt.

Our BF stands for blitz the formation. I call a coach who just picks a blitz a Rolodex coach. If you happen to call the wrong blitz, you have a corner somewhere on that field trying to cover their best receiver all over the field. You have lost. We want to blitz and guarantee a hit on the quarterback.

We study your protection. If we know that you are going to get three out to the strong side, we use that to our advantage. The third receiver strong is a hot receiver. We know that the center is blocking weak and that the split back is check-releasing weak. We know that the quarterback is reading the fourth rusher strong as his hot read. Our blitz is coming from the fourth strong attitude. The lesser corner can take away the inside. He knows that all he has to play is a 6-yard stem. The ball is coming off; otherwise the quarterback is on his back. The defense has a free rusher.

We know whether you have the potential to block eight. When you do that, we bring nine. We have to have a free blitz man coming so that the corner is guaranteed heat and only has to cover the short route. We get there in a hurry, or we get beat in a hurry. We don't have to die that slow death with the quarterback standing back there baking a pizza.

When I was in high school, we double-called our coverage. We played one or the other, depending on the set. Ten of the players got the check, and one didn't. All he listened to was what was called in the huddle. He played the first call and not the second. What we do is this: All 11 break the huddle, and none of them know what is going on. Here is the huddle call: "AFDC, ready, break." They have to listen to the communications that are going on as the offense comes out to set up. They have to hear the Mike linebacker and free safety, and they all have to be on the same page. Otherwise, we are going to have a really crappy down. The thought process isn't that far out of whack. We put the best front-up that we can when you are going to run the ball and the best coverage when you throw it.

Tuesday is the off day for our players. That is in the collective bargaining agreement. That is when we put together the game plan. That is when we put together the formations, tendencies, runs, and passes.

On Wednesday we work on first- and second-down calls. On Thursday we repeat the hard first- and second-down calls and work really hard on the third-down calls. On Friday we throw all the crap at them, which includes goal line, short yardage, and that kind of stuff. On Saturday we review, and we play on Sunday.

In the drill part of linebacker play, we use these types of drills. We talk about defeating a block. We talk about defending-area responsibility against a run. We drill them in gap control. We give them a pass coverage key, zone or man to man. The last thing we talk about is pursuit.

Two things before I start the film. I always felt that if I could take one thing home from each guy who spoke, I had had a hell of a clinic. I learned a lot of things, even if it was just a different way to say the things I was doing. If it makes the kid understand because the new way is more descriptive, that is worth it. Here is the first thing Jack Pardee taught me. A linebacker is an in-between player. He has to be tough enough to play the run and have enough finesse to play the pass. The in-between player has to be coached this way. He lines up to stop the run and reacts to the pass, or he plays pass coverage keys and reacts to the run, but he is not going to do both. You are doing that linebacker an injustice if you are not coaching him that way.

 





Page 247

Your defensive call on first down is a run call. We are in an eight-man front, playing run as a linebacker and reacting to our pass coverage. That gives the linebacker a jump on making a big play. I want a play maker, not a robot. If I have to coach that linebacker very much, he is probably not a linebacker. The game is easy. Find the guy with the ball, and go splatter that sucker. Go intercept the ball when it is thrown. If he has instincts, don't screw them up. Don't give him so many things to think about that he can't play. Give him a run key, and let him run to the ball. If the offense gives him a "high hat" (that is pass blocking to us), he reacts and goes to pass coverage. The same thing is true of the pass key. The linebacker gets his pass key and gets into his coverage. If for some reason he were to get a run key, he reacts to the run.

The second thing is pad level. How many of us have said this over and over? "The guy with the lowest pads is going to win." You win with pad level. You have to get your pads underneath his pads. As the player gets down in his stance, he is thinking about whether his pad level is under his opponent's. The next thing he knows, he is on his back. Kids don't make a mistake on purpose. They make a mistake because they don't know what to do. They are trying to do what you tell them to do, but they don't know how. So you turn the film on. If you are not using a film every day in practice, you are stupid. The reason you watch the film is to see the body position. His line of sight is down, and he thinks his pads might be under the opponent's. Give him a horizontal look. Now, you ask him, "Are your eyes at his throat?" If the linebacker's eyes are not at the blocker's throat, he is too high. He can look straight ahead.

I talked about exploding their eyes on contact. Watch their eyes as they tackle. Do they explode the eyes through the contact, or do they cringe on contact and blink? Get to a point where you can see their eyes. If the linebacker can always see the throat, he is going to win at pad level.

We play a lot of eight-man front and get a lot of zone scoop blocking. We never allow both parts of the scoop. We are going to hold the man on the line of scrimmage and allow the linebacker to run downhill into the gap. If the blocker gives an avoid step and tries to play wide on the scoop to get to the linebacker, we use the momentum for the blocker to pull us away from the backside scoop block. We grab the hip of the first blocker and pull through to beat the back scoop block. They may get the down lineman blocked or the linebacker blocked, but they are never going to get both halves of the scoop blocked.

If people do a good job of getting the front seven blocked, the last guy they are going to block is the trail defender on the back side. If they leave him unblocked, we'll chase you down from there. We plug the hell out of the plays on the front side and make you cut back. When you do, that backside defender will knock the hell out of you and might cause a fumble. In our league, they use these pencil-necked wide receivers to block the back side. We never hold force on a wide receiver. When they come across in motion, they bring a corner with them in our scheme. The corner replaces the linebacker in force, so he can wrong-arm the wide receiver, get inside, and come laterally down the line of scrimmage.

Let's talk really quickly about pass rush. Study the great speed rushers. Who do you think told me all this stuff? Nobody told me this. I study films. You better get the film of the best team in your league and find out why they are kicking your butt. They probably know something you don't know. I get films of guys who are great speed rushers off the edge and watch them. He can fight centrifugal force. He is able to dip and rip and shorten the distance to the pocket. They have the ability to fight the pressure inside. They have the ability at the hip joint to plant and get the toe pointed to where they are going. If the toe is pointed outside and I get blocked outside, that is where I am going. It doesn't matter whether I am bigger and stronger. To fight the pressure, I have to get my cleats in the ground, get under his pads, and be able to run the line I want to. He has to fight with pressure to get his toe pointed and his hips turned in. With each step of my front foot, I have to get more cleats in the ground. We buy three eight-foot sections of PVC pipe and connect them in circles. We practice rushing

 





Page 248

around them. We make them chase one another in the warm-up drills. We use these circles and incorporate them into our sack-and-strip drill.

We have dummies with Velcro arms on them. We normally put a ball in them. As the linebackers are speed-rushing, they take the inside, upfield shoulder and wrap up the tackle. The downfield arm is stripping the ball. If they get the ball stripped out, they are scooping and scoring with the ball. If. the numbers of our players to theirs are even, we want to scoop and score. If the numbers are in their favor, get on the ball and recover it. We are working on a half-moon speed rush: knocking the ball out, scooping, and scoring. We try to pick the ball up and get it in the center of our body. We don't want them to try to pick the ball up outside the frame of the body. They straddle the ball when they pick it up. Then we have a race to the end zone. Make sure you spike it at the end. That is simple stuff, but they have to work on it too.

To teach the linebacker how to play over a tight end, I put him in a fit position. I put him in a position where his hands are on the inside and the tight end's hands are outside. I start with their arms collapsed. That is to simulate the tight end firing off and engulfing the linebacker. The linebacker's eyes are at the tight end's throat. His hands are inside, and his thumbs are up. He wants his feet in a good power base. From this point right here, I have to teach them to separate and shed. We call the drill "fit, separate, and shed." From this point, I work on extending his arms, getting his hips back, and getting his head to the outside. If the tight end starts to walk on the linebacker, the linebacker keeps a firm arm in the direction he is going. He takes three steps with the tight end and violently sheds him. From there, he gets in position to tackle.

The next thing we do is to put the tight end's hands down and let him come off. The linebacker has to fight for the inside hand position, get his hips and body away, and power-clean the tight end. Then he repeats the first drill.

I have added to the drill because we got a new tight-end coach who is working as hard as I am with his tight ends. I had to develop another part to this drill to work on beating the tight end's hands. I put two guys on a line. They are going for a three-second count. One guy starts with his hands inside. When I say go, the other guy tries to get his hands inside. They are pushing against each other and regaining inside control. We work on base, short steps, and control. If one guy loses his base, the other tries to throw him off the line.

We work on shedding a guy down. If a tight end is walking on us and has good body lean, we have trouble shedding him with the rip or throwing him to the side. The only way to get off a tight end who has stood up the linebacker and is walking on him is to give ground and shed him down. We also incorporate that in the fit-and-shed drill.

When we talk about pass rush, it takes a lot of off-season work. We work with a karate expert. It helps keep the hands of the offensive linemen off us. If a guy is trying to get his hands on the linebacker, we want the linebacker to strike and clean the offense's hands, elbow to wrist. Long-armed tackles are what we face. We make the contact on them elbow to wrist. You have to learn how to practice against each other. We don't want to hurt our guys. We try not to have any bull rushes. We put them 3 yards away from each other as a running back on a linebacker. We work edges and finish with rips and swims.

If we reach the level of the quarterback, we want to be a contain rusher. At the same time, if we can beat the guy inside to the quarterback, we do. The first time we blitz, I want the linebacker to bull-rush over the back.

"Time is up!"

Fellows, this always happens to me, whether it is Coach Fisher cutting my individual period or the moderator telling me to stop. It's been great being here.

 





Page 249

Dan Young

The Trap and Trap-Option Package

University of Nebraska

It is my pleasure to share with you our trapping game and the option off those traps. Last Wednesday, when I left Nebraska, it was snowing. Coming over, I kept thinking how good my nine iron was going to be. It is a great experience coming to Hawaii. The coaches here understand football and make a great effort to learn.

I know that some of you do not run the option. I am sure that most of you run some type of trap. If you don't do it, you can at least learn what we are doing at Nebraska, and hopefully that will help you. We have had a lot of success in the last four years. We won the national championship in 1995. For those last four years, we have a 47-2 record.

I would like to comment on the job Earl Browning has done getting this clinic started. Also, Tom Cheaney and Ed Roach have helped put the Hawaii clinic together. Rick Sakata has done a lot of work behind the scenes. I know it takes a lot to get these programs started. Getting everything organized takes a lot of effort. I want them to know we appreciate the effort.

I want to go over three of our traps and two of the options that come off the traps. The first trap is the one we run off tackle. We trap the first offensive defender outside of our offensive tackle. The fullback aims at the butt of the offensive tackle and reads the tight end. The fullback gets the ball, although we do run the play from a one-back set. We give it to our I back and run the same blocking scheme. Against the 50 front, we are going to have our onside tackle step with his right foot and chip the defensive tackle. Then he comes off on the linebacker plugging, back to the noseguard running, to the backside linebacker. We have the tight end come down and aim high because he is getting help with his offensive tackle. Then the end works upfield.

Against the 50 front, the onside guard is going to pull. He is really going to climb the hole. He is going to try to get the rush end kicked out. What happens is this. As the tight end squeezes down, the defensive end closes down with the end. We really have to climb the hole to get it kicked out. If we face the even front, we pull our guard a little flatter. If it is an odd front, we really climb the end to get him kicked out.

Again, the fullback is going to come off the butt of the tight end who is off his down block. The quarterback will reverse out with a three-quarters turn. We are going to run option action with the tailback and quarterback. We hope to get underneath the onside linebacker if we run the option play enough. Hopefully, he will widen, looking for the option. We set the play up by running the option play a number of times. On the 50 front, we have to fool the frontside linebacker and get underneath him. On the back side, we pull our guard around and try to get our center to cut off to the back side.

If the frontside tackle has a defender on his inside, we have him communicate this to the tight end. The tackle will down-block, and the tight end will block the tackle. We will have the wide receiver go in motion and block down on the first linebacker. He has a double read. He can block on the first linebacker to show or the strong safety coming up.

TRAP VS. 50 FRONT

Against the even look, say, a 6-2 or 4-4 front, we come down with our tackle. He steps down with his lead foot

 





Page 250

toward the man inside. The big trouble we have with our tackle is getting in too wide a stance. When he takes the lead step, he has to bring his back foot underneath him to get balance. It is just enough time to give the defense a chance to react. We want to shorten up the stance and step directly at the defender. We will get some help from the center in case the man slants back inside. The tackle can aim a little higher because he knows he has help coming from his center. The onside guard pulls and traps the first thing outside of our tackle. If we have an eagle look, we are going to trap the 7 technique lined up on our tight end. On the option, if we get a 7 technique on our end, we will have the end block him. When we run the trap, we are going to trap him with our guard.

TRAP VS. SPLIT 6 (4-4)

I know that it looks like an easy block with the tackle on the inside man, but that man can do a lot of things that make it difficult to block him. That is why we have our center help the tackle.

If the tight end has a 9 technique, he will release underneath. If he has a 7 technique on him, he goes to the outside. When the tight end has to go outside, the pulling guard hugs the line a little closer. He breaks off the block of the tight end. Again, at times the play will break, and we do not even have to touch the linebacker because he is running outside to play the option.

Against the 4-3 front, the play is very similar to the 44 front. We pin block with the tackle. We pull the center to help on the down defender. The onside guard kicks out on the end.

TRAP VS. 4-3 FRONT

If we want to bring the flanker back in motion and have him crack back on the linebacker, the tight end comes down inside. The flanker is also involved in the passing game and runs several routes from this motion.

TRAP WITH MOTION VS. 4-3 FRONT

That is how we would run our fullback trap off tackle. What happens at times on the play, especially on the 4-3 front, is that the defensive end will slant down inside, with our tight end releasing inside. What we do then is to have the tight end jump the end on his slant. Then we just bring the guard around for the first linebacker. If the defensive end lines up inside our tight end, we will release around him. We do not have our end block him on the trap if he lines up inside.

TRAP VS. 50 SLANT

 





Page 251

We have run the same play with the I back when we were in a one-back set. There is nothing unique about having the fullback. If we see a 4-3 front, we can run it just with the I back. We like it with the fullback. We have the blocking scheme set so that we can run it with either back.

I-BACK SETTRAP

If we are seeing a steady dose of the 4-3 with the end on the 9-technique end playing on the outside of our tight end, we will change up the blocking between the tight end and pulling guard.

The tight end will base-block the run end, and the pulling guard will block the outside linebacker. We will use this change-up against teams that like to play their rushes wide. The center is assigned the middle linebacker. He will not help the offensive tackle on his pin block.

CHANGE-UP TO OUTSIDE TRAP

If we call the play and the defense does not give us the 4-3 look, we will change back to our inside zone blocking scheme. This is nothing new for us. It is the same blocking scheme that we use to block a lot of plays. This is how we would adjust our blocking if they came up with a 50 front.

32 ROLL VS. 50 FRONT

Next is our option. Our option off the trap is one of the best options, year in and year out. Our belly option starts out very similar to the trap. The main difference is we block a 7 technique with our tight end and pull the guard around for the linebacker. We will trap to the 7 technique with our guard. When we run the option, we release our tight end inside and hope the rush end squeezes down inside. If he does, we log-cut the rush with our guard. If we get the big cut by our guard, the fullback bounces outside and blocks the play-side linebacker. We always option the force man on this play. If the rush end comes upfield, we kick the end out, and the fullback comes underneath the kickout block and looks for the onside linebacker. We tell our guard to pull flat on an even front and to climb the hole against an odd front.

Against a 4-4 front, our tackle will pin-block, and the center will protect against a slant, then go up for the backside linebacker. If the tight end has a 7 technique on him, he will block the 7 technique, and the guard will pull around for the linebacker. Since the guard is now blocking the frontside linebacker, our fullback is free to go block the safety.

OPTION VS. 7-TECHNIQUE RUSH

 





Page 252

Against a 50 front, the frontside tackle will chip the man on him by stepping with the right foot (option right) and proceed upfield, checking for a frontside plug by the onside linebacker, then proceed to the backside linebacker. The pulling guard will climb the hole and hope to get a log cut on the end man. The tight end blocks down on the man on our tackle. If we can get a log cut on the defensive end, the fullback bounces outside and blocks the onside linebacker. We option the force.

OPTION VS. 50 FRONT

If the rush end comes upfield, we will kick the end out, our fullback will come underneath the kick block, and the quarterback will duck up underneath as well.

50 FRONT-RUSH END UPFIELD

Against a 4-3 front, our tackle will pin-block. Again, we will either kick the rush end out or log-cut him. The fullback and quarterback will again read the guard's block to determine whether to go inside or underneath.

9-TECHNIQUE RUSH

If the rush end slants inside, our tight end jumps the slant and blocks the rush. Our guard will now block the man who the tight end was to have blocked. Our fullback will still bounce outside and block the playside linebacker.

DEFENSIVE-END SLANT

This play is our best option on the guard log. In our power set, we will assign our wingback to second deep. Against the 6-2 guard log, we will have our center scramble the 1 technique on the guard. We use the same technique on the defensive tackle as we did against the 5 technique on a 50 front. The fullback and quarterback will read this block on the defensive end and either go around or come up underneath if we have to kick the end out.

VS. 6-2 FRONT, GOAL LINE

 





Page 253

A lot of teams in college football try to run this play but have problems with it. The reason we have had success with the play is because we spend a lot of time on the play. Most teams are not willing to spend the time to perfect the play.
